THE
PARLIAMENTARY DEBATES
(OFFICIAL REPORT)
IN THE
THIRD SESSION OF THE FIFTH PARLIAMENT OF NORTHERN IRELAND
APPOINTED TO MEET AT STORMONT, ON THE NINETEENTH DAY OF DECEMBER, IN THE
FOURTH YEAR OF THE REIGN OF
GEORGE VI.
HIS MAJESTY KING
VOLUME XXIII.
THE SENATE.

---

Tuesday, 19th December, 1939.
The Third Session of the Fifth Parliament of Northern Ireland appointed
to meet on 19th December, 1939, in the
Fourth year of the Reign of King
George VI, was opened by the Governor
(His Grace the Duke of Abercorn, K.G.,
K.P.) in person.
The House met at Twelve of the
Clock, Mr. SPEAKER (The Right Hon.
Viscount Bangor, O.B.E., D.L.) in the.
Chair.
KING'S SPEECH.
His Grace the Governor, being seated
on the Throne, commanded the acting
Gentleman Usher of the Black Rod
(Brigadier-General H. G. Young, C.I.E.,
D.S.O.) to let the. Commons know "It
is his Grace's pleasure that they attend
him immediately in this House."
The Commons being at the Bar with
their Speaker, his Grace delivered the
following Speech from the Throne:-
Members of the Senate and of the
House of Commons.
When I last addressed you our Country
was at peace. Since then the United
Kingdom has been forced into what may
prove to be a prolonged campaign in
defence of universal liberty and justice.
From the day war was declared
Northern Ireland has given proof of her
willingness to play her full part in this
great struggle.
To me it is a source of deep satisfaction
that the young men of the Province have
offered themselves in such large numbers
as volunteers for service in His Majesty's
Forces. Their rally to the colours has
been fully up to the high standard
expected, and the women of the country,
too, are loyally carrying out their duties
in many spheres of activity, both on
Service and in their homes.
My Ministers have devoted constant
attention to Civil Defence and I am
gratified to learn that the Act passed last
Session has enabled precautions to be
taken for the protection of our people
from the worst effects of attack by hostile
aircraft. These arrangements are being
improved and developed day by day with
the cordial co-operation of local authorities, and, in fact, all sections of the
community. The training of volunteers
continues, and those who have been
enrolled are discharging with diligence
their allotted tasks.
Food production is one of the most
essential national services at this time. My
Government has, therefore, laid plans for
the ploughing of a quarter of a million
additional acres of grassland in 1940, and
every effort is being made to meet the
needs of farmers by the provision of
tractors, ploughs, and other implements.
A
King's Speech
King's Speech
THE SENATE
No less important than the production
of food supplies is the provision of
essentials required for the successful
prosecution of the war, and I am glad
to know that in this sphere, as in all
others, our industrialists are making an
important and growing contribution to
the national effort.
I am further pleased to observe that
the importance to the nation of maintaining overseas trade is fully realised,
and that my Ministers are working in the
closest co-operation with United Kingdom
Ministers in order to facilitate the
development of Northern Ireland export
trade, compatible with the requirements
of the home country. My Government is
also actively engaged in assisting industry
to surmount the many difficulties with
which it is faced, while, at the same time,
ensuring that the interests of employees
are duly safeguarded.
The necessity for borrowing large sums
of money for the purpose of prosecuting
the war has been the subject of legislation at Westminster, and an intensive
campaign covering the United Kingdom
as a whole has been launched with the
object of obtaining the maximum
response from small investors. I am
gratified that my Government has created
a National Defence Ulster Savings
Certificate Issue, the greater part of the
proceeds of which will be made available
for National Defence purposes. I am
confident that this appeal, which has met
with an immediate and ready response,
will continue to receive the whole-hearted
support of the community.
Among the Measures which will be
introduced will be a Bill dispensing, for
the period of the war, with the necessity
for holding Local Elections and preparing
Registers of Electors.
A Measure will be laid before you for
the amendment of the law relating to
hire purchase, and you will also be
invited to consider proposals for the
further postponement of the raising of
the school leaving age, for the provision
of increased grants to public mental
hospitals, for the amendment of the law
with regard to Building Societies, and
for the more effective prevention of
fraudulent dealings in investments.
It was not practicable to proceed in
the last Session with the Bill for the
This
postponement of the general revaluation
due to be made in the year 1941.
matter, however, will be brought forward
for your consideration in the forthcoming
Session.
on
The Report of the Committee
Maternity Services has now been received
and will be given full and sympathetic
consideration by my Government.
Members of the House of Commons.
The Estimates for the Public Services
will be laid before you in due course.
My Ministers are fully alive to the
need, in the national interest, for the
most rigid economy, and it will be their
policy not to sanction any new services
or extensions of existing services, which
are not essential for purposes connected
with the war or for the supply of very
urgent needs, and I am also gratified
that our local authorities are taking
measures to ensure that a like regard for
economy is observed in the administration
of local government, although it is
regretted that this policy necessarily
involves the suspension of many useful
public works.
Members of the Senate and of the
House of Commons.
I am pleased to learn that, notwithstanding the unavoidable changes in our
industrial organisation, there has been a
substantial reduction during the year in
the number of persons unemployed, and
I trust that, for many of those who are
still out of work, the expanding demands
for labour required to meet our national
need will afford improved prospects of
employment in the future.
I am glad to find that the principle
of Holidays with Pay is spreading and
that in the coming year over one hundred
thousand workers will profit as a result of
its adoption.
I am gratified to know that work in
the schools throughout Northern Ireland
is proceeding normally. Effective
machinery has been provided under the
Education (Evacuated Children) Act
passed last Session for the education of
any of our children whom it may be
found necessary to transfer to country
areas.
I reiterate my admiration of the
magnificent response of the whole people
King's Speech
Papers Presented
19 DECEMBER 1939
to the calls made upon them in defence
of King and Country, and I pray that
the blessing of Almighty God my rest
upon your deliberations.
Then His Grace was pleased to retire,
and the Commons withdrew.
House adjourned during pleasure.
House resumed.
Papers Presented to Parliament.
Ministry of Agriculture:
The Chrysanthemum Midge (Northern
Ireland) (Revocation) Order, 1939.
The Bacon Industry (Registered Agreements) Order, 1939.
The Bacon Industry Act (Northern
Ireland) Order, 1939, appointing days
for the operation of certain provisions of
the Act.
The Bacon Industry (Curers Levy)
Regulations (Northern Ireland), 1939.
The Bacon Industry (Exempt Sales)
Regulations (Northern Ireland), 1939
(Revocation) Order, 1939.
The Bacon Marketing (Additional
Licence Fee) Regulations (Northern
Ireland), 1935, (Revocation) Order, 1939.
The Bacon Industry Act (Northern
Ireland), 1939, appointing days for the
operation of certain provisions of the Act.
The Bacon Industry (Exempt Sales)
(No. 2) Regulations (Northern Ireland),
1939.
Ministry of Labour:
The National Health Insurance
(Emergency Payment of Benefit) Regulations, 1939.
The National Health Insurance (Joint
Committees) Regulations, 1939.
Laid before the House (pursuant to
Act) and ordered to lies on the Table.
Address in Reply to His Majesty's
Most Gracious Speech.
Mr. SPEAKER having read the
King's Speech,
Sir THOMAS DIXON (in morning
dress): I beg to move, "That an humble
Address be presented to His Grace in the
following words :-
"Your Grace,
"We, His Majesty's dutiful and loyal
subjects, the Senate of the Parliament
of Northern Ireland, beg leave to offer
our humble thanks to your Grace for
the Speech which your Grace has
addressed to both Houses of Parliament."
The Gracious Speech begins with a
reference to the war which our Empire is
engaged in at the present time. This war
has been forced upon us simply because
Hitler seemed to want to dominate
Europe. He over-ran Czecho-Slovakia,
and when he attacked Poland it was quite
time that those nations which wanted to
see liberty and freedom upheld decided
that this aggression must stop. We in
this part of the British Empire value our
freedom and our liberty, and naturally
we will give all the assistance we can in
carrying this war to a successful conclusion. The Gracious Speech refers to
the response of our men and women to
the call for recruits for the various services of the Crown, and I am sure that
if more men and women are required
later on the response of the men and
women of Ulster will be equally prompt.
The Gracious Speech goes on to refer
to the responsibilities of the farming community. I am sure the farmers of
Northern Ireland will do all they can to
increase production and to till the extra
quarter of a million acres of land the
Ministry of Agriculture has asked for. I
understand the Government is going to
fix minimum prices for various farm products, and, if that is so, I think that in
fairness to the farmer the Government
should state what those minimum prices
are to be as soon as possible. I also hope
the Government will see that there are
plenty of tractors in the country, as well
as a sufficient supply of seed, which is at
present scarce. You cannot sow if you
have not got the seed.
Reference is also made in the Speech to
the business man and overseas trade. I
am sure business men will do all they can
to carry on and to increase overseas trade,
and if they can it will be a great help
to the currency question which we all
hope may improve in the future. The
present position makes buying from
America and elsewhere so much more expensive. As regards the finances of our
country every one can help in this. The
Address in Reply to
King's Speech-
THE SENATE
[Sir Thomas Dixon]
small man can save something and can
buy Savings Certificates, and the bigger
man can buy Defence Bonds and in that
respect they will get no finer security in
the world. There are not many Bills to
come before this or the other House and
none of these are very controversial, but
there is one Bill I am glad to see is
coming before us, and that is the Bill to
improve the hire purchase system. I
think it is high time this Bill was brought
in. I beg to move.
Sir JOSEPH DAVISON (in morning
dress): I esteem it a very high honour to
be asked to second the address of thanks
to His Grace the Governor for the
Gracious Speech from the Throne, which
he has delivered to both Houses of Parliament. I heartily agree with everything the right hon. Senator has said in
proposing the Motion.
The Gracious Speech makes mention of
many matters of moment which are
uppermost in our thoughts to-day, but
the principal of these is the reference to
the campaign into which our country has
been forced in defence of universal
liberty and justice. In accord with our
professions of loyalty and attachment to
Great Britain, this Province has given
proof of its willingness to play its part
in the great struggle. We offer our respectful homage and devotion to His
Majesty the King in this time of anxiety
and uncertainty, and pray that victory
in battle will be given to the forces of
our country and of our great ally. In
the ranks of our enemy, leaders have
changed, but, unfortunately, it is the
same spirit of ruthless greed and dominance which prevails. While we all long
for peace it must be a peace that will
make for enduring security amongst the
nations of the world.
The Gracious Speech refers to the
feeling of great satisfaction that the
young men of the Province have offered
themselves in large numbers. Our young
men have responded nobly to the call of
the country's danger, and have offered,
and are still offering, themselves for service with the fighting forces of the
Crown. Older men, many of whom
bore the brunt of battle during the
Great War, are offering themselves for
home defensive service. To-day in the
streets of our city and towns and villages
Men
can be seen vast numbers of men who
are wearing the King's uniform.
of the Navy, the Army and the Royal
Air Force, and with them I can link
the men of the mercantile marine, who
are carrying out their duties under conditions of extreme difficulty and danger.
Our women, also, in the same spirit
of loyalty, have joined the several
women's services and are doing their
part in this gigantic struggle. In Air Raid
Precaution service, I am given to understand that the men and women who are
associated with this movement in city
and counties are pulling their full
weight. All over Northern Ireland a
generous response has been made, and
there are large numbers still volunteering.
His Grace the Governor has himself
shown a very sympathetic interest in the
work of this protective service by attending demonstrations held in Belfast
during recent weeks. His presence has
been a wonderful encouragement to those
engaged in Air Raid Precaution work.
These demonstrations have been very
successful, and have shown where improvement, if necessary, can be effected,
and training is being continued to improve and develop the service.
As a city dweller I do not feel competent to judge of the many difficulties
that confront the farming community in
the matter of increased tillage, so as to
increase food production for man and
beast. I have read correspondence in the
columns of the Press, and reports of
meetings of farmers. These are generally
critical of the ways and means by which
greater production may be. brought about.
They are also critical of many of the
restrictions imposed upon them, but
these restrictions are war measures,
mainly the result of Imperial legislation,
and the Minister of Agriculture in
Northern Ireland has a very difficult task
reconciling the different interests
involved. In many instances guarantees
have been given, but more guarantees are
demanded. I feel that if those who are
engaged in this, our chief industry, and
responsible representatives. of the
Ministry of Agriculture could come
closer together and each examine the
viewpoint of the other, a great deal of
criticism would disappear, and the
farmer, I honestly believe, will do hist
utmost to live up to his responsibilities.

Prime Minister, right hon.

The will to win may compel us to suffer
many inconveniences such as the blackout, which is, undoubtedly, hurtful to
traders and shop-keepers, and which is
becoming increasingly dangerous for
pedestrians. Great care and watchfulness
is necessary on the part of both pedestrians and drivers of mechanicallypropelled vehicles in order to avoid accident. Safety first should be the motto
during the black-out period.
Our beloved King has just returned
from visiting the British forces on the
battle front. His presence must have
been a great encouragement to the troops,
who are suffering the hardships of war
and maintaining the fight for freedom
and justice.
We are justly proud of our gallant
British Navy, which has given such a
brilliant account of itself since the
declaration of the war, but particularly
of its gallant exploits within the past few
weeks.
Captain Kennedy, of the Rawlpindi,

of himself and his men when he fought
against overwhelming odds, but the latest
achievement of His Majesty's cruisers,
Exeter, Ajax and Achilles, fills us with
wonderful pride. The Graf Spee has gone
to an inglorious end.
The exploits of the Royal Air Force
have also given us a thrill of pride. The
old fighting spirit still lives, and when
the British Expeditionary Force gets to
real grips with the enemy, right will undoubtedly prevail over might.

Mr. LESLIE: As apparently it falls

duty is a distinct compliment, and partly
on the manner in which that duty has
been discharged. The right hon. Baronet,
the Lieutenant of Belfast, who moved the
Address, is, if not an original Member
of this Chamber, at all events one of our
earlier Members. His family, as we all
know, has been long and honourably connected to some extent with Belfast, but
also to some extent with the County of
Antrim, to which I myself belong.
I remember it is a good many years
ago now-being present at a meeting in
Belfast, and it may be that some of those

at that time. The meeting was called in
order to consider in what manner the
great services of the greatly honoured
Baronet's father, the late Sir Daniel
Dixon, could fittingly be commemorated.
Someone spoke at length about his services to the City of Belfast. But an old

been a

the right hon. Baronet now represents.

Now I pass to the Speech itself.
Speech opens, as we have been told, with

There are one or two smaller matters in
the. Speech to which I will refer for a
moment or two before passing to the two
greater items on which I do want to claim
the attention of the House. These small
items are the miscellaneous Bills referred
to on the fourth and fifth pages of the
Gracious Speech as printed.
We are told there will be a Bill dispensing for the duration of the war with
the necessity for holding local elections.
That is a Measure of a class which we
have met before, and which we have
always received with considerable satisfaction because we know that
satisfaction is merely a reflection of the
satisfaction that is felt outside. In times
of war the ordinary person does not want
to be troubled with local government
elections.
our
The second is a Measure to which the
Mover of the Address referred in terms

Measure for ameliorating the law in
reference to hire purchase. I imagine the
Bill will follow in the main the lines
of the Measure recently adopted by the
Parliament at Westminster. If that be
so I think it will be very welcome, particularly perhaps in Belfast, although
certain evils attending that system of hire
purchase are not confined entirely to the
city of Belfast but are widespread
throughout the Province.
Again, there is a suggestion for the
further postponement of the school
leaving age. For that I can have, nothing
but approval, and in that matter I am
speaking rather perhaps for the country
than for the town. The other small
matter is the suggestion that an increased
grant will be made to public mental
hospitals. With that proposal I feel
myself very much in sympathy. One or
two of the Members here may remember
that our late colleague, Senator Harold
Barbour, and I voiced in College Green
in the very early years of this Parliament a very distinct claim that more
consideration should be given to the
public mental hospitals in view of the
alteration in the burden and requirements which were even then cast upon
them. A proposal to that end was also
made by the committee which dealt with
the financial relations of the State and
local authorities some years ago, but I
am afraid that nothing very material
came out of it then. Let us hope something better will be provided for us in
the future. The claims on the public
funds of the mental hospitals, which are
in the main maintained at the charge of
the county rates, are to my mind very
cogent and very urgent.
.
I pass from those smaller matters to

additional ploughing of a quarter of a
million acres of grassland. It is too soon,
speaking on 19th December, to say yet
whether that somewhat ambitious
programme of 250,000 acres of extra ploughing is or is not going to be realised. I
think it will do no harm to reflect
for a moment on the greatness
of the task which it is suggested should
be undertaken. It was originally suggested that England and Wales should
supply two and a half million extra
tillage, but references in the papers circulated in this Province last week showed
that the suggestion had fallen from twoand-a-half to two million acres. The
suggestion as regards Scotland was that
Scotland should plough 260,000 more
acres. That is 10,000 more acres only
than it was hoped that Northern Ireland
would be able to bring under the plough.
If you look at that suggestion for a
moment in another way it would rather
seem to involve the suggestion that the
area of Yorkshire is equal to twenty-five
twenty-sixths of the area of Scotland.

THE SENATE
[Mr. Leslie]
That is a matter of figures, and perhaps
not a theory that can necessarily be
translated into practice.
But the question is, are you going to
get this extra 250,000 acres in Northern
Ireland, and, if you are, what are you
going to put in it? The right hon.
Gentleman who moved the Address put
his finger on the point when he asked,
will there be seed available to sow that
area if you can get it ploughed? Can
you get that area ploughed without
bringing back into agriculture many
acres of land which their owners have let
out of agriculture, as properly understood. There is a great deal of grass
land in the Province which can no doubt
be properly broken up under certain
conditions. Are those conditions likely
to be fulfilled?
If you cannot get your 250,000 acres
where is the blame to be put? Is it not
on the system or lack of system in agriculture in Northern Ireland no less than
in the United Kingdom, because in that
matter, at all events, Northern Ireland
is only part of the larger problem?
What I am saying is not intended as
any reflection on the Northern Ireland
Ministry. But is not blame to be put
on that lack of system which has allowed
agriculture to fall and fall and fall from
the high place it occupied in the early part
of the last century and from the position
to which it had almost been again at the
conclusion of the last war to the state
in which it is to-day?
What are we faced with? You are
asked to plough an additional quarter of
a million acres, and of that quarter of
a million acres, how much is properly
drained? Have you not, so to speak,
put an unnecessary obstacle in the way
of draining a large portion of the land?
Grants have been given during the past
few years for draining, but these grants
have been limited to farmers who had a
valuation not exceeding £200 a year.
Surely it is obvious that if you want the
extra grass land ploughed it is the over
£200 valuation man you have to look to
SO as to be able to make your contribution. There is very little use
ploughing undrained land and there is
no use whatever in ploughing land which
you will not be able to sow. Whether
the Government-I mean the Governin
ment of the United Kingdom, because in this matter again our
own Ministry act merely as agent
-whether the Government of the
United Kingdom will be able to get seed
to issue to the farmer at a reasonable
rate is rather at the moment, I am
afraid, on the lap of the gods. Nothing
can be accomplished if adequate supplies
of satisfactory seed cannot be provided
for the farmer, and it is idle to talk of
a quarter of a million acres of additional
tillage.
There is one thing which can be done
next year to give us the position this
country has lost. Next year again we
shall require the fullest production of
food materials which can be got from our
own soil and then we can revise more or
less the hasty procedure which has been
adopted for the 1940 crop. One thing
might be done. The £2 ploughing grass
subsidy, subject to income tax and so on,
is at the moment limited to the breaking
up of land which has been out under
grass for seven years. Looking to
the ordinary scale of rotation, particularly on small farms, the farms which
have got regular rotation, it does seem to
me you might possibly get more tillage if
you reduced the seven years to six or
perhaps five. That is a matter which
calls for consideration next year. This
year-I mean 1940-I do not think it is
material because of the extent of rough
grazing of which I have already spoken.
Unhappily some of the rough grazing is,
as I have said, undrained.
Passing from agriculture, I think there
is nothing more one can say as regards
the future. There is no use in going
back on the mistakes of the past. That
will not help us. There is no use talking
about the price of food stuffs.
There
seems to be an ineradicable notion abroad
that the price of food stuffs is controlled.
It is strictly true to say the price of food
stuffs is controlled, but it is not true to
say the price of any particular food stuff
is adequately controlled, the difficulty
being, of course, that nearly all these
food stuffs are made up of an aggregate
of various ingredients. The price of each
ingredient has to be controlled first
before you have the basis of the final
ration, the dairy ration and so on. We
have not got power really to control
supplies of that
of that nature. We cannot
provide for the incoming to our ports of

To turn for a moment to the necessity
for economy, I was glad to see it was
distinctly suggested that local authorities
should exercise economy.
If one may
take it out of the somewhat stilted phrase
appropriate to the Gracious Speech and
come down to brass tacks, does it not
mean that our local authorities are to be
encouraged to abstain from those
grandiose and unnecessary schemes which
in the past have been adopted? The
ordinary ratepayer will welcome anything of that sort, because, after all, any
expenditure of the local authorities is
reflected in the rates, and the ordinary
ratepayer does not want to pay any
more rates if only because he well knows
he will have plenty to pay in taxes. The
suggestions as regards economy are
doubt admirable. Can they be translated into practice? And in suggesting
one method by which they can be translated into practice I am aware I am
treading on dangerous ground.
aware that what I am going to say will
not be entirely popular.
no
I am
Economy can best be promoted by
fighting that spectre of inflation which,
from its cloven hoof to the horned head
which, as its natural apex, always sticks
forward during periods of financial crises,
and that spectre of inflation owes its
origin to a great extent to the evil which
I am about with some trepidation to refer
to.
We have already begun to hear too
much as regards the necessity for raising
wages right, left, and centre to meet the
rise in the cost of living. Figures have
been presented to us showing, if I remember rightly, that the cost of living at
the moment has not risen by 12 per cent.
It has also been suggested by one or two
economists that if you find this or that
article in use rising in price it is not impossible to secure a substitute, and these
methods are preferable to rushing headlong to the ultimate folly of a general rise
in wages.
It cannot be too often pointed out that
every rise in wages entails a rise in cost,
and if you are going to allow cost to rise
and rise you have no protection whatever
against inflation, and if inflation is
allowed to become the watchword perhaps
of the United Kingdom I think most of
us who have looked into the subject at all
know where it is likely to take us. One
would, therefore, so far as one can,
appeal in the interests of economy to the
people not to press for a rise in wages,
but as
an alternative to search for an
alternative economy. One would venture
to urge on local authorities and other
large employers of labour that they
should be chary of granting any unnecessary increment in wages, because by that
means both sides will, I think, be doing
all that is within their power, in that
respect at all events, to aid in the successful prosecution of the war in which we
are unfortunately at present engaged, and
from which we expect to emerge victorious, but after which we do not wish
to face a peace which may be more disastrous to the country than war. I have
great pleasure in supporting the Address
in reply to the Gracious Speech.

Mr. SAMUEL CUNNINGHAM:
reading this Speech I think there is one
point that we as a House might wish to
stress. We speak about volunteers going
to the front. I think we should also show
sympathy to those mothers and wives who
stay at home and cheer those going to
the front. It always strikes me that perhaps the man in the field has less trouble
and less sorrow and less anxiety than the
mother and wife at home. I would have
liked the Speech to have brought out our
sympathy with them.
There is another thing I would like to
point out, and it is this. We talk about
taking good grass land and turning it into
arable land. I think that is all to the
good. But there is another aspect of the
matter. When in the last war food was
running short for our armies in France
they were able then with a great deal of
push and care to ship over a great many
cattle which were raised on this good
grass land. They were rushed over, and
the army was properly supplied with
food. It was an emergency and it passed
away afterwards. We speak about wanting good land to plough. I will just give
an instance which came to my knowledge
a few days ago. The National Trust of
Scotland got as a legacy from Campbell
Blair a large tract of country in the island
of Mull. A great deal was useless on
account of bracken. They commenced
Debate on the Address
Debate on the Address
THE SENATE
[Mr. Samuel Cunningham]
and broke up that land and in two years
there was a splendid crop of oats and rye.
It is held up as an example in Scotland.
Now I think that instead of picking out
for ploughing good land which is useful
to supply cattle and butter and milk, we
should select land which we all know as
moorland. Some of it looks beautiful in
early summer. That land is covered with
bracken, and it is good land on which
bracken flourishes. If that land could be
brought into cultivation-and I do not
see why it should not as it did in the
island of Mull-it would bear corn. That
would be adding wealth to this country.
We only add a small difference if we
plough good grazing and put in oats or
wheat. But in the other case
really adding the whole value as wealth.
I hope that our Ministry of Agriculture

Mr. ROBB (Leader of the House): I,
too, would like to say how greatly I
appreciated having the honour of transmitting to the Mover and Seconder of

to discharge those respective duties, and
how gratified I was with the manner in

just tell of one little incident that touches
on the point made by Senator Samuel
Cunningham regarding the brevity with
which the Mover of the Address can say
what he has to say. I was once speaking
at a luncheon. It began at one o'clock,
and there were so many speeches that it
did not finish until four o'clock. As I
was coming out I met Sir Thomas Dixon
who said, "I congratulate you." I was
very pleased, but he added, "You were
the shortest."
Coming to the Gracious Speech, and
the speeches that have been made upon
it, there are one or two things I would
like to say. With regard to the difficult
question that has been brought to notice
by different speakers, namely, the provision of seed oats, I am empowered to
say that everything that can be done in
that regard is being done by our Ministry
of Agriculture. They recognise, as every
farmer apparently does I am
am not so
familiar with that side of life-the difficulty there is possibly going to be with
regard to the provision of seed for the
acres that are to be ploughed. That is
not a matter that is being lost sight of,
and whatever can be done will be done.
With regard to the extent to which
the ploughing campaign has already progressed, I am able to state that
applications involving the ploughing of
127,000 acres have been received by the
Ministry up to the moment. Those are
applications in respect of the grant of
£2 per acre for ploughing this land. That
is a good long step, over half of the extra
250,000 acres aimed at. In this connection I would like to direct the attention
of the House to the announcement made
at Westminster recently that the scheme
regarding the grant for the ploughing of
land is being amended so that holdings
of one acre will now be able to apply for
this grant. That is a matter of importance everywhere, but it is a matter of
very great importance in a country such
as ours where the agricultural holdings
are, as a general rule, so small. The
rate of progress already attained is a
great achievement on the part of an
industry which came through such a thin
time for SO many years, but it is
imperative that the effort now being made
should be carried on intensively.
I am asked to stress the interest that
the Ministry of Agriculture takes in the
efforts that may be made, and in the
endeavours that may be put forward, to
maintain the cattle and butter supply in
this area. That is not only a direct pro-
Debate on the Address
Debate on the Address
19 DECEMBER 1939
vision in itself, but it has the effect of
lightening the ever increasing and very
difficult burden on our mercantile marine
in the matter of imports. Everyone can
easily see that the more we produce at

burden upon our ships and the lighter,
therefore, will be the duties of protection
by convoy or otherwise.

With regard to War

about 27,000 certificates, a very creditable result. In comparing that record
with the number of certificates sold in
England there are. two things to be
remembered. In England they have more
spare cash, and, in addition to that, they
have developed in England a thing that
started but did not develop so quickly
in this area, namely, savings clubs.
They were started here and they
functioned, but not very strongly,
whereas in England
in England they not only
functioned but increased from year to
year. One of the objects of the Savings
Committee over which the Lord Chief

am quite satisfied that it will be a

a
There are one or two other things referred to in the Speech. There is
the postponement of local government
elections, and I do not think anyone will
object to that. With regard to the provision for mental hospitals, it has been
announced, as the House is probably
aware, that the grant has been increased
from £38,000 to £60,000-quite
substantial increase. The Speech also
refers to the Report of the Committee on
Maternity Services. Although all the
recommendations in that Report have not
yet been given effect to, one important
step has already been taken. The Government has taken action in respect of
an important recommendation touching
the training of pupil midwives.
The Ministry of Home Affairs has told
the Royal Maternity Hospital, Belfast,
that it is prepared to make grants to the
hospital ranging from £40 to £72 10s in
respect of each nurse trained at the hospital, up to a maximum grant of £1,000
a year. The payment of this grant in
respect of pupil midwives will be of
assistance to the hospital itself, and it
will check the tendency of nurses to go
to England and Scotland, where these
grants are already payable, for maternity
training. Pupil midwives went over
there in order to get the advantage of
these grants, but now that the grants are
to be paid here this training will be
given here, greatly to the advantage of
the nurses and the Maternity Hospital.
Senator Sir Joseph Davison referred
to the difficulties of the "black-out." I
am sure everyone will sympathise with
and support his suggestion that people
who have to be out at night should exercise the greatest care. The increase in
the number of accidents to pedestrians
during the "black-out" is a fact that
has many times been brought to my
notice by the Attorney-General, and it is
a fact that must disturb everyone. I do
not want to apportion blame as between
pedestrians and motorists, but I would
like to appeal as strongly as I can to
every person whose duty takes him out
at night to take the greatest possible
care. I do not know that speed limits
are of any use. In the case of the only
accident I have seen the motor was not
going at four miles an hour. It is a
matter entirely of care on the part of
both motorists and pedestrians.
In connection with the "black-out" I
might mention that when the modified
form of street lighting which has been
promised in England has been proved
to be effective it will be the rule here.
It has not yet been tried, and no one
yet knows what amelioration will flow
from it, but so far as it is a help it will
be adopted here.
Senator Leslie referred to a matter
with which I am not familiar, the necessity for extending the scope of the
drainage grant, but I will bring it to
the notice of the Ministry of Agriculture.
Prima facie, Senator Leslie made a case
for extending the grant to larger holdings, and I will see that his suggestion
is brought to the notice of the Ministry
directly responsible.
Debate on the Address
Adjournment
THE SENATE
[Mr. Robb]
On the subject of inflation I am afraid
I am a little bit of a heretic. But I do
not think that this is perhaps the place
in which to start a general discussion
upon the merits or demerits of printing
money. I would like to say that while I
sympathise very strongly with the view
expressed that a rise in wages does involve a rise in cost, and every rise in cost
induces a rise in wages, and you have a
vicious circle and it is very hard to stop
the ball rolling once it begins-but, as I
say, I am not quite sound on the question
of inflation from the hon. and learned
Senator's point of view.
With regard to the Bills that will come
before the House. Bills touching hirepurchase and building societies and share
pushing will follow Bills recently passed
at Westminster. It is a curious fact that
when some systems, such as hire-purchase
or such as the provision of sums for the
building of new houses by building
societies, begin they are excellent in
practice and in theory, and in the
beginning they work splendidly. But it
is the universal experience that not very
long passes over until some unsuspected
evil arises in respect of the manipulation
of these systems. In connection with
hire-purchase and in connection also with
building societies these difficulties and
evils have been brought to the notice of
the public generally, and of the Government, in particular, in recent years.
Steps have been taken in England, which
it is hoped will remedy the evil, and we
intend to follow them here.
Whether somebody will find a method
of driving a coach and four through our
new Act of Parliament we do not know,
but we are going to try it, and if somebody does find a gap we will try to stop
that gap.
I can assure the House that
in both these two main matters the
example of England will be followed.
After all, England has much wider experience in these matters than we have.
We have found some of
of the things
happening here that happened there, and
if these remedies are effective we
apply them. Our first step ought to be to
follow in their footsteps. I have much
pleasure in supporting the Address.

ORDERS OF THE DAY.
Hire-Purchase Bill (S.).
Order for Second Reading read.
Mr. ROBB: I move the Second Reading of this Bill. The hire-purchase
system of acquiring goods is not of very
long standing. But it developed very
quickly and it developed very quickly
especially in America, and with it there
grew up a great many evils, and it is
possible that the hire-purchase system,
so widely spread in America, was one of
the causes of the great financial slump
there in 1929. We in the United Kingdom did not have such a wide development of this system, and we in Northern
Ireland did not have such a wide development of the system as exists in Great
Britain. But here, too, we have found
some of these evils superimposed upon
the system that grew so rampant in
England that legislation was called for to
check them. Legislation in England was
ultimately through a private Member's
Bill, but all parties joined together to
work out what was regarded in their considered opinion as the best that could be
done to remedy the evil that had been
found to exist. And the Bill that I am
now introducing is a copy practically
verbatim of the Bill that was passed in
England in 1938. It does not apply
universally. It applies only in certain.
classes of hire purchase and credit sales
agreements. It applies where the hirepurchase price does not exceed, when the
agreement relates to a motor-car or railway waggon or railway rolling stock,
£50;
where it relates to livestock, the
sum of £500; and in any other case the
sum of £100. I may say I made some
inquiries, not, perhaps, very extensive,
with regard to the existence in this
country of hire purchase with regard to
livestock, but I could not find anybody
who had had any experience here.
as it might happen-it apparently has
happened in Great Britain-that there
would be agreements relating to livestock,
it was thought well to follow in this
regard the English Bill.
But
Now the methods and the provisions
are not very difficult really to follow, and
the simplest plan, I think, would be to
go through the Bill. In the first place
under Clause 2, where a person is going
to enter into an agreement to acquire
goods under the hire-purchase system,
that person must be informed of the
price at which he could get these goods
if he paid cash. I have had a curious
personal experience with regard to this
matter. I went into an establisment in
Belfast out of curiosity some few years
ago and asked what was the price under
the hire-purchase system of a particular
piece of goods, and I was told that I
could acquire by the payment of instal-
Hire-Purchase Bill-
Second Reading
THE SENATE
[Mr. Robb]
ments, which in the aggregate came to
£170. Out of curiosity I said, "How
much in cash will you take?" "Oh,"
was the reply, £69." That is a state
of affairs of which the hire purchaser
surely ought to be informed of the
amount he would have to pay cash down,
and the amount he would have to pay if
he took the goods on hire purchase.
I may say incidentally that although I
talk about hire-purchase agreements, this
Bill applies to credit sales, which is
defined as a sale in which the purchase
price is to be payable in instalments of
five or more. The first thing then is
that the purchaser or hirer is to be told
how much he can get the goods for cash,
and then he knows that. The next
thing the Bill provides is that in either
a hire-purchase agreement or sale there.
shall be the terms put in writing, and,
accordingly, sub-clause (2) of Clause 2
provides that there shall be a note or
memorandum made and signed by the
hirer and by and on behalf of the other
parties to the agreement. That includes
the seller and any person who guarantees.
The next sub-clause says what is to be
in this agreement. It is to contain a
statement of the hire-purchase price and
the cash price; secondly, the amount of
each instalment, and thirdly, the date
at which the instalments are to fall due,
and a list of the goods to which the
agreement relates, sufficiently clear to
enable any person to identify them.
Further, there is to be in this
memorandum a notice, in the form in
the Schedule to the Bill, more or less
specifying the rights of the hire purchaser. In addition to all these, the
memorandum is to be executed and a
copy is, within seven days, to be given
to the purchaser. Those are the provisions under that Clause, but it does go
on to provide that in any proceedings
founded upon this agreement for hire
purchase, if the court before whom the
proceedings are taken comes to the conclusion, in all the circumstances of the
case, that the hirer has not been prejudiced by the absence of any item, the
court has a dispensing power.
Clause 4 makes provision for the possibility of the hirer wishing to terminate
the hiring agreement. It provides that
any time before the final payment is
made, without prejudice to the right to
recover anything that may be due, the
hirer may determine the hiring and the
agreement upon payment of the amount
by which one half of the hire-purchase
price exceeds the total of the sums paid
and the sums due, or not less than some
sum set out in the agreement.
Clause 5 makes provision for certain
things that are not allowed to be put
into or made part of the agreement. They
are altogether in protection of the hirer.
The agreement has been made, and the
goods have been handed over. Clause 6
provides that at any time the hirer may
demand from the seller or owner copies
of the documents relating to the goods
and a statement of how much has been
paid and how much is due, and if the
owner does not do that he is liable to a
fine. In the next clause you have a
corresponding liability put upon the
hirer. It is the duty of the hirer to keep
the goods in his possession or control, and
if the owner asks where the goods are
the hirer is bound to tell him. If he
does not do so he is liable to a fine.
Clause 8 deals with certain warranties
which are implied in every hire-purchase
agreement. They are expressed in legal
language more or less. There is an
implied warranty that the hirer should
have quiet possession of the goods. That
means that if any person disturbes, under
claim of right under the owner, the position of the hirer, the hirer should have a
right of action against the owner. There
is an implied condition that the owner
shall have the right to sell the goods, and
a warranty that they are free of any
charge or encumbrance. Unless the goods
are second-hand there is an implied condition that the goods are of merchantable
quality and reasonably fit for the purpose
for which they are sold.
Clauses 9 makes an important change in
the law. If the hirer has two or more
hire-purchase agreements with the same
owner, when he comes to
him any
pay
sum of money which is not a payment in
full of all the instalments then due, the
hirer can
say, "Appropriate what I
am paying you now to Agreement No. 1."
If the hirer does not do that then the
payments are to be applied proportionately to the amounts due on each instalment at that time.
15 FEBRUARY 1940
Hire-Purchase Bill-

Second Reading
case the cost of installation is a first
charge and does not come under the
general provisions. The hirer must pay
that in any case.
Clause 21 is a series of definitions, but
under sub-clause (3) there is a provision
which I should, perhaps, mention.
Where the owner has agreed that any
part of the hire-purchase price may be
discharged otherwise than by the payment of money, any such discharge shall
be subject to the provisions of the Act.
Clause 10 I do not think I need deal
with. It is not very important except
from a lawyer's point of view. In Clause
11 we have a new provision which provides that where goods have been let on
hire-purchase agreement and one-third of
the hire-purchase price has been paid, the
owner is not to enforce any right to recover possession otherwise than by action.
Once the hirer has paid one-third of the
hire-purchase price the owner cannot, as
he can under the present law, walk in and
take the goods back; he must go to the
court for an order. Without going
through the details of this and the next
clause I may explain that the court has
power to do what in the circumstances is
equitable. It can order delivery of all or
part of the goods, and payment of the
particular amount due on the goods that
are allowed to be kept. It may also
award damages if the goods have been
damaged, and things of that sort. Where
the court makes such an order the next
clause restricts the rights of the owner to
such rights as the order gives him.
Clauses 13 and 14 are virtually expansions
and completions of that.
Clause 15 deals with an evil that did
arise. Where a man has bought on hire
purchase a certain number of articles,
and then buys some more, it was the
custom to provide that they should be
lumped together in one agreement, and,
no matter whether the hirer had paid
off the cost of the first lot, they did not
belong to him until he had paid for the
whole lot. This clause provides that
where goods have been let under a hirepurchase agreement and after one-third
of the hire-purchase price has been paid
or tendered the owner makes a further
hire-purchase agreement with the hirer
comprising those goods the provisions of
Sections 11 and 12 shall have effect as to
the further agreement.
Cluase 16 deals with what is to happen
on the bankruptcy of the hirer, and
Clause 17 is purely a legal matter. Under
Clause 18 proceedings in respect of these
hire-purchase agreements are
to
be
brought in the county court where the
hirer lives, and sub-clause (2) deals with
the right of appeal.
Clause 19 refers to another matter.
Sometimes a hire-purchase agreement is
made on condition that the owner or
seller shall install machinery, and in that
These are the main provisions of the
Bill. Reports indicate that even during
its short life in England a similar Bill
has worked well and is doing a great deal
of good. As I have already indicated,
the English Bill was founded on a
Private Member's Bill, and all parties
combined to make it as good as it could
be made to carry out its object. The
Bill now before the House is on the same
lines as the Imperial measure, and I beg
to move, "That the Bill be now read a
Second time."
Question proposed, "That the Bill be
now read a Second time."
Mr. LESLIE: Before I begin to deal
with the Bill before us I think the House
will be with me in the expression of the
pleasure which it gives us to see you, my
Lord Mayor, occupying the Chair this
afternoon. Those of us who are original
Members of this Senate-and I am sorry
to say there are a bare half-dozen of us
left-recall that your predecessor in
office was our first Chairman by Statute,
and we are very glad to see the tradition
continued.
Now I pass to the business more particularly before us on the Paper. I may
say that I am sure we all welcome this
Bill. It was stated by the Leader of the
House, when speaking on the Address at
the opening of the Session, that there was
a general desire, even an anxiety, in
Northern Ireland to do something to
mitigate the hardships and the injustices
which had centred around what at first
sight appeared to be a natural development in the direction of hire purchase
of furniture and things of that class. We
can only hope that the efforts to which
this Bill is directed and the provisions of
the Bill-some of which are rather of a
drastic character, although I am not going
Hire-Purchase Bill-
Second Reading
THE SENATE
[Mr. Leslie]
to suggest they are in any way too drastic
-we all hope that those efforts will prove
successful, and will bring into effect the
sentiments which have inspired the Bill
before us.
-
It
As the right hon. Gentleman told us,
the Bill itself is based on the English Act
of 1938. It follows that Act very
meticulously; in fact, I think I may say
there are only three variations from that
English Act. The first variation I
observe is in the first Clause, where three
very short words of correction-on
which I should like to congratulate our
draughtsman have been introduced.
The second is in relation to Clause 18,
where we have substituted a Clause
necessary for our own requirements.
does not in the least agree with Clause 18
of the English measure, because Clause
18 gave a certain measure of jurisdiction
to the inferior courts, and in that respect
we have not thought fit to follow the
English example. Perhaps I may explain that I am saying "English"
deliberately, because the Act of 1938 is
limited to England and Wales. It does
not apply to Scotland. The third variation is some necessary adaptations to
bring legislation applicable to Northern
Ireland into the measure instead of
English legislation which does not
apply.
SO
In Clause 18 we introduce a very
guarded reference to circumstances which
might, perhaps arise, but of which, indeed, it is very hard to visualise the
Occurrence.
The English Bill was, as we have been
told, a private Bill, and as to that
matter I may, perhaps, ask leave to refer
again to it in a moment or two. Both this
Bill and the English Act, of course, deal
with two important classes of transactions the transaction relating to hire
purchase and the transaction relating to
what are called credit sale agreements.
Those two classes of agreements differ in
this respect. Under the hire-purchase
agreement the property and goods do not
immediately pass to the hirer. In the
credit sale agreement the property does
pass, and that is a distinction which we
have constantly to keep in mind in
endeavouring to consider the Bill before
us. The fact that this particular Bill
follows the English Act has its own difficulties and perhaps to some extent its
disadvantages. However, we have to
take that as we find it.
The first point, indeed the main
point, to which at the moment I
want to draw attention, is the jurisdiction under which this Bill 1s to
be brought into force, and I have to say
that gives me a good deal of difficulty even
as to points which are of relatively minor
importance, not as to the objects but as
to the administration of the Bill. Let us
consider what is the jurisdiction. It is
obvious that the effect of this Bill will
be to extend the jurisdiction or equity
jurisdiction of the court, and it is abundantly plain that the intention is that the
court referred to shall be the County Court.
I think that is absolutely certain, but
we do not in the Bill expressly say so,
and I believe precedent requires that we
should say so. The precedents I am
thinking of are partly the Act of 1877,
the Civil Bill Act, which is familiar to
Members of this House; the County Court
(Ireland) Amendment Act, 1882, and
our own Workmen's Compensation Act,
1927. All the precedents there point to
conferring the necessary jurisdiction in
express words, and I suggest to the
House that those precedents gain point
when we observe, as we do from this
Bill, that there is a proposal to extend
what I may call the normal jurisdiction
of the County Court to sums amounting
to £500. The House will see that in
Clause 1, as it now stands, there is a
reference to the possibility of transactions in livestock, the amount of £500
being concerned.
Well, the normal jurisdiction of the
County Court in Northern Ireland
is very considerably below £500, but
there are, as hon. Members are aware,
various statutory exceptions in which the
jurisdiction does extend to £500, but, as
I have said, where that extended jurisdiction is conferred it has been the practice to say so very expressly, indeed. And
still under the jurisdiction there is this
question of what I may describe as the
venue of the proceedings or the court in
which such actions are to be brought. I
suppose I am right in saying that the
normal procedure in a county court action
is to bring the action within the jurisdiction in which the defendant resides. That,
broadly speaking, is the normal procedure
in practice. In Clause 12 of the Bill
Hire-Purchase Bill-
Second Reading
15 FEBRUARY 1940
some variation is introduced, and it leaves
us in a little doubt as to what is the intention of the Bill before us as to matters
of that kind. That will require attention
later on.
Going through the Bill I found some
words or phrases which gave me some
difficulty. I do not say that any of these
matters are of great importance, and
certainly they do not affect in any degree
the principle and general provisions of
the Bill, but they did give me a little
difficulty, and I think they are matters
for discussion at a later stage. Perhaps
the most important difficulty that occurred
to me was the omission of any distinct
reference to the right on the part of the
hirer, or the duty cast upon the hirer in
the event of the determination of the
hire-purchase agreement to return the
goods which are the subject of the agreement. I cannot find any definite requirement in the Bill to the effect that that is
to be done. It would seem to me to be a
normal matter of equity, but the Bill
does not directly say so, and it does seem
to me to be one of those cases in which
we ought not to rely on innuendo using
the word in the popular sense.
have to put that in because otherwise the
Leader of the House will, perhaps, frown
upon me. I think the point is good, and
we should be precise on it also.
I
Passing to a more important matter as
it seems to me, perhaps wrongly, the provision under Clause 3 of the Bill where
we are not dealing with hire-purchase
agreements. May I for a moment remind
the House again of what I said that the
credit-purchase agreement is the agreement under which the property passes
directly to the purchaser and does not
remain with the seller. Now it is provided apparently that the provision as to
making a credit sale agreement shall be
limited to articles or goods in which the
credit sale price exceeds £5. Looking back on past experience of cases
brought before me I cannot for myself
agree that that limitation of £5 is what
is required in the interests of those poorer
classes of the community for whose benefit
this Bill is, as I think, particularly
directed. I do not see why we should
insert that somewhat arbitrary limit. It
seems to me that a person who is in the
habit of selling goods on credit sale terms
is not unlikely to be a person relatively
in a somewhat extensive way of businesswho is doing that kind of business with a
good many people, and there is no reason
why he should not have a printed form
to get him over any difficulty as regards
expressing the agreement and article.
I do suggest to the House that it
might be advisable for the protection of
the poorer classes that that limitation of
£5 should be removed. It may be that
the right hon. Gentleman at a later
stage will be able to confute me, and
may be compelled reluctantly, I hope, to
give a good reason why we should not
change the limitation I suggest when the
time comes, but for the moment it is not
important, and I commend to the consideration of individual Members of
the House that there is no real need to
insist on that limit of £5. The House
will remember that when we come to
figures of that sort we necessarily deal
with the poorer classes. And it is somewhat similar when we come to Clause 9.
Is there any particular reason why we
should limit what I am going to call the
benefit of appropriation to the hirer?
The man acting under the hire-purchase
agreement would seem to be rather the
white-haired boy of the Bill. Why
should the credit sale man not have the
same benefit as regards appropriation? It
may be that he has one or more credit
sale agreements in which he is concerned.
It may even be he is concerned in a
credit sale agreement and hire-purchase
agreement. Is there any definite reason
why we should limit this concession, because it is a distinct concession, to the
man who is concerned with the hirepurchase agreement? But, again, it may
be that the right hon. Gentleman, still
with reluctance I hope, will be able to
confute, or be driven to confute me at
a later stage. For the moment I am
rather inclined to stick to what I may
describe as my own view, that we should
consider these two classes of people not
exactly share and share alike, but at all
events on the same footing.
Passing from that matter to the question of the installations to which the
right hon. Gentleman referred for a
moment, while I am ready to believe that
the provisions in that Clause were
necessary in England, I am not certain
that there is any great demand for its
reproduction in Northern Ireland. If it
is necessary to reproduce it, I am rather
inclined to wonder whether it goes quite
421789
Hire-Purchase Bill-
Second Reading
THE SENATE
[Mr. Leslie]
far enough. At the same time, it is not
a matter to which I should be disposed
to suggest any variation from the English
Act, because I am inclined to think that
in Northern Ireland the matter will be
found to be of very small importance,
but, again, it may be that there are
others present who may be able to give
the House further information than I
possess dealing with the matter, and
they may be able to explain whether
there is or is not any real necessity for
the introduction of this Clause.
I come now to the time for the commencement of the Act, that is to say, the
1st July next. I am quite certain there is
no Member here wants to delay for one
moment the operation of the Act which
will result from this Bill, but I feel constrained to point out that we are now at
the 15th February, and also that time.
lagged so to speak as regards the English
Act from the 29th July, 1938, to 1st
January, 1939. It may be that we may
find difficulty in getting this Bill through
both Houses in reasonable time to allow
it to come into operation on 1st July.
I shall not at a later stage, at least in
Committee stage, make any suggestions
as to alteration, because I think that is
a question which may be left over to the
final stage in this House-the Third
Reading and afterwards-or to those
who sit in another place.
We may
find, as time goes on, there is not
sufficient time between the Bill receiving
the Royal Assent and its coming into
operation. If that is found or becomes
imminent, then I think in another place
at a later stage any necessary Amendment might be introduced. It seems
to me, if I may say so, that this Chamber
will perform its duty if it draws attention.
to the possible difficulty that may arise.
As regards most of the matters which
I have mentioned, or to which I have
alluded, I shall put something on the
Paper, as the phrase goes, so that they
may receive in Committee stage the consideration which I think is necessary.
There is one matter I may mention
particularly. I reminded the House-I
think the right hon. Gentleman the
Leader of the House mentioned it-that
the English Act was based on a private
Bill. That may be the reason why Clause
1 of this Bill is in its present position at
the head of the Bill instead of, as is the
more modern practice, towards the end of
the Bill, next door to the interpretation
clause, which would be the more natural
place for it, if I may say so. The more
modern practice is certainly to bring the
application clause in a Bill towards the
end, and that practice has certain advantages. The older practice still survives in
local Bills, but even so where it does
survive the interpretation clause usually
immediately follows the application clause
and that, perhaps, has its advantages.
In the present case Clause 1 is separated
from the necessary interpretation clause
by the whole length of the Bill-some
twenty clauses-and, personally, I found
in reading the Bill that that was very inconvenient. Indeed I am almost inclined to
suggest at another stage that we transfer
Clause 1 from where it stands now to
where I regard is its proper place, down
to the stokehold, if one may be sufficiently
irreverent to describe it so. I cannot
refrain from saying that putting the
wording of Clause I where it is now is
singularly unfortunate. The right hon.
Gentleman is an ally, perhaps an unintended ally, of mine in this respect, because in speaking on the Bill and searching
for an example he referred to the piano
and the sofa.
ture.
When anyone speaks of hire purchase I
am inclined to say the first thing he
thinks of is the hire purchase of furni-
He certainly does not think of
railway waggons or livestock. Really I
think that a sense of humour might have
prevented those people in England who
put Clause 1 where it is now, in the forefront of a Bill dealing primarily with
hire purchase. If we examine Clause 1
we are struck with the point I have been
endeavouring to illustrate. We shall
find, I think, if we go through the Bill
that that reference to livestock is going to
get up and hit us in a place where
perhaps the English authors of the Bill
did not altogether expect.
Passing from that, there will be some
other matters which I shall bring before
the Committee. They are not matters,
as I have said before, of very great importance. They are not very vital, but
they are matters which, I think, ought
to be discussed in this House, because it
must be remembered that in the case of

Hire-Purchase Bill-

Second Reading
Then there is another practice about
which I was told and which, I think, is
covered by this Bill. I refer to the buying of a suite of furniture. A person
bought a suite of furniture at £30, and
according to the agreement signed the
buyer was to pay £3 down and £1 a
week afterwards until the £30 was paid.
Payments went on pretty well for twenty
weeks.
the Bill before us we are acting as a
Chamber of first instance. We are not
revising a Bill sent to us from another
place. I do not think for a moment that
the discussion of these matters will arouse
anything approaching controversy; in
fact, the Committee stage is likely, as I
see it, to be of a conversational order
more than anything else. But I do
think it is necessary that we should look
rather carefully at some of the proposals
contained in the text of the Bill. I wish
the Bill every success, and I have no
doubt that its passage through this
House will be as swift and as easy as
circumstances permit.
Mr. BARCLAY: I welcome this Bill
and support its Second Reading. Some
few weeks ago I raised this question of
the hire-purchase system on the floor of
this House. Since then I have had
several conversations with men and
women who have been victims of this
system. They were all anxious to know
if the Government of Northern Ireland
would bring in a Bill on the same lines
as that passed by the Imperial Government. They told me that if the Government did so it would be a great benefit
and blessing-in fact, one man said it
would be a godsend-to the workingclass people of Northern Ireland.
On reading the Bill over and listening
to what the hon. and learned Senator
(Mr. Leslie) has said, I have come to the
conclusion that this Bill deals with all the
complaints that I received during the
past few weeks. For instance, there is
the blank cheque which, I understand,
has come lately into more prominence
than before. The way the blank cheque
is operated is this. When a buyer goes
to buy some goods the seller takes a
notebook out of his pocket and writes
down the articles the purchaser wants to
buy. Then giving her a blank form he
says, "Sign your name at the bottom of
that. I will fill in these articles in ink
and send the form on to you." That is
done and when the form reaches the buyer
she is surprised to find several pounds'
worth of goods on it. The form has her
signature, and there is no alternative but
to receive the goods and pay the money.
If she makes a protest the seller simply
says, "These articles will be up in price
in a week or so, and I thought I would
let you have them now that they are
cheap."
On the twenty-first week the man
was unemployed and so was unable to
pay. According to the agreement which
he has signed if he misses one instalment
the seller can come and seize the furniture and take it away. That is what
happened. On the twenty-second week
the seller came to the house, lifted the
furniture, and took it away. He had the
poor man's £23 and the suite of furniture
into the bargain. As far as I can gather
from the Bill that point is covered.
There was also put to me the case of a
wireless set. The price was to be £16.
The condition on which it was got was
that there were to be instalments of £1
a week, and the buyer signed an agreement to that effect. He got along very
well for five weeks, but the sixth week he
failed. Of course the wireless set was
seized. I should have said that in the
agreement it was stipulated that if he
failed to pay the full amount he made
himself responsible for half the money
that lay between. There were five weeks
paid, which left eleven weeks to pay. This
poor man was taken to court and a decree
was obtained against him for £5 10s and
with the £5 he had paid the sum he paid
the seller for the wireless set was £10 10s.
The seller, of course, had the wireless set.
I think it is time that something was
done to call a halt to this evil. As far
as I can understand, this practice has
been going on for some time. It has not
been carried on by Ulstermen or
Britishers, but by foreigners who came
into the country and started up business.
I do not condemn all those men who do
business in this way, because I know that
amongst them are honourable men who
would not be found doing anything
shady. They have, in fact, helped the
buyer when hard times. came. They
have let him keep the goods until such
time as he got employment or recovered
from sickness and was again able to pay.
But it is the others I wish to get at, and
I am very pleased that this Bill covers
practically all the complaints made to
Hire-Purchase Bill-
Second Reading
THE SENATE
[Mr. Barclay]
me during the past few weeks. Therefore, I have great pleasure in supporting
the Bill.
Major BOYLE: I am very pleased to
welcome this Bill. I would like to congratulate the private Member in another
place who had the pluck and the temerity
to succeed in getting such a Bill through,
as I am very strongly of opinion it has
been an Act of very great service to the
poor man in preventing him from being,
if I may say so, robbed by some of these
very clever business people.
There is one thing in the Bill which
I would like the right hon. Gentleman,
the Leader of the House, to take into
consideration, that is the limit of £100
in the first Clause for anything except
a motor car, railway engine, or livestock.
I would like to see that figure increased
to at least £200, and I will give the
reason. In the country-I am not now
referring to the town, because I know
nothing about it-a great many of what
I might call the smaller people are
building houses, bungalows, and somewhat larger houses, for themselves; and
these houses, I understand, are largely
paid for by means of loans. The people
have not got sufficient money to pay for
them down, which is probably very much
the cheaper way.
When they get a four, five, or sixroomed house they have got to furnish it,
and the furniture dealer, who is probably
the greatest scoundrel of all, comes along
and provides the furniture. But £100 is
not a very large sum with which to
furnish a five or six-roomed house. These
people want a dining-room suite, a drawing-room suite, kitchen utensils, bedroom
furniture, a piano, and a wireless set.
The furniture man sends all this, but
£100 really will not cover the amount. I
should very much like the Government to
consider the question of increasing that
figure. It would do no harm to raise the
amount, and it would be of very great
assistance to the people concerned. I
have great pleasure in supporting the
Bill.
Mr. ROBB: The points that have been
raised are practically all Committee
points. With regard to the order of the
clauses, I may say that my own personal
preference in reading Acts of Parliament
is to get the application and the interpretation at the beginning. I like to find
it there; it is, perhaps, a matter of practice. My experience is it is easier to have
it at the beginning. It is there in the
Conveyancing Act, with which I am, perhaps, more familiar than with any other
Act. I have no great prejudice one way
or the other on the point.
With regard to the other points raised,
the hon. and learned Senator (Mr.
Leslie) spoke of this measure impliedly
and unexpressedly extending the jurisdiction of the county court. I had not
thought of that point, and I will consider
it. Then he mentioned the duty on the
part of the hirer, in the event of the
determination of the agreement, to return
the goods. Possibly something might be
put into the Bill on that point. As to
the time when the Act will come into
operation, I think that question might
very well be left to the last one of the
two Houses which deals with the Bill. I
should like to thank Senator Barclay for
the illustration he gave, and his support
of the Bill.
Major Boyle raised the question of extending the limit, and on that subject
may I say two things? First of all, the
limit was fixed in England, after very full
discussion, and, secondly, it is of great
advantage to us in this country to
have our laws the same as they
are in England, so far as we can.
As to the figure, personally I do not
think I have any prejudice one way or
the other, but I am very strongly in
favour of having our laws the same as
those of England. It is a matter of importance to any person who is practising
the law, and who has, in addition to his
other difficulties, to watch out for differences between the law here and the law
in England. This Bill, in the main,
follows the lines of the Bill in England,
and it is a tremendous advantage to have
the same figures and limits. But, as I
say, I have no prejudice in the matter,
and if the House should think it worth
while to alter this, I am not sure that I
would be willing to offer any very strong
opposition. It is a matter for the
House.
On Question, Motion, "That the Bill be
now read a Second time," agreed to.
Bill committed to a Committee of the
Whole House for Thursday next.
45 Education (Leaving Age) Bill-

Adjournment
Education (School-Leaving Age)
Bill (S.)
Order for Second Reading read.
Mr. ROBB: As the House is aware, it
was originally intended that the raising
of the school-leaving age should become
effective on the 1st of July, 1939, but it
became necessary to postpone it until the
1st of July, 1940. In the meantime the
war has occurred. I think it must be
obvious to every person that this change,
involving problems of organisation and
in many cases of extending the accommodation, is not a suitable one to introduce
at a time like this. Accordingly, the
Government have decided that the raising
of the age must be deferred. We do not
fix the date, because, frankly, we do not
know how long the war will last. I beg
to move.
Question proposed, "That the Bill be
now read a Second time."
Mr. LESLIE: Nobody, of course, will
have any objection to the principle of
this Bill. The only point is that it may
be said, though I am not going to say it,
that the decision as regards the new date
will be taken out of the control of
Parliament. I do not think there is anything in that, because, when the time
comes, we cannot presume that Parliament will want to go back on existing
legislation that is to say, that they
will not desire to make any change
as to the extension of the school age; but
the thing that remains is that if we give
the power to fix a new date by Order in
Council we ought to ask for an undertaking from the right hon. Gentleman
that the date so fixed will be fixed with
due regard to the convenience of the
public, that is, there should be something like six months' notice.
Mr. ROBB: I had intended to say so,
but I forgot.
Mr. LESLIE: I am sure we would get
it fixed much better by doing it that way
rather than by an amendment in the
Bill, a practice which the House dislikes
when dealing with an Order in Council.
In other respects I have no objection to
the Bill.
On Question, Motion, "That the Bill
be now read a Second time," agreed to.
Bill committed to a Committee of the
Whole House for Thursday next.
Adjournment.
"That this House do now
Resolved,
adjourn."-(Mr. Robb.)
Adjourned accordingly at
twenty-five minutes before Four o'clock until
Thursday next.

Question to Minister.
AIR-RAID PRECAUTIONS.
The following question stood on the
Order Paper in the name of Major Dobbs:
To ask the Leader of the House
whether he can now state what steps are
being taken to make the poorer houses
in Belfast structurally safe against airraid attack.
Major DOBBS: When I tabled this
question in December last the subject was
a matter of importance. I am sure it is
of even more importance now. No one
can deny that the situation in Central
Europe is of a very serious character.
When we remember that we are a very
rich nation, that we have a very prosperous Empire, and that we hold in our
possession the greater part of the raw
material of the world, we cannot be other
than the object of great envy, and, may I
a very desirable and toothsome
morsel to the octopus in Central Europe
that is throwing out its tentacles and
bringing the surrounding nations into its.
grasp.
say,
We must be prepared for war, and
though we cannot look with anything but
pride and gratitude to the fact that our
Oral Answers
Financial Provisions Bill
21 MARCH 1939
fighting forces, both in personnel and
material, have been enormously increased
during the last few months, I would like
to ask if we have been equally careful in
looking after the safety of the civilian
populations of our large towns.
I am
convinced that if at any time Germany is
at war with ourselves she will go for a
knock-out blow in the first few hours;
that she will try, by incessant and
organised air bombardments on our great
towns of over a quarter of a million inhabitants, and by the murder of the
civilian populations of those towns, to get
in that knock-out blow. She will hope
that the proletariat will rise and demand.
peace, however ignominious.
I have no fear for the civilian population after the first few weeks of war,
when their tempers are roused, and when
they have got their tails up, but I do
greatly fear mass phychology in the first
few hours of war. I am asking this
question purely with regard to Belfast.
I think Belfast is the only probable target
in the whole of Ulster. I beg to ask the
question standing in my name.
Mr. ROBB (Leader of the House): In
reply to the hon. and gallant Senator,
I may say that the whole question of
the safety of the civilian population from
attacks from the air in time of war is
receiving the most careful consideration
of my right hon. Friend, the Minister of
Home Affairs, and in this matter he is
in close touch with technical experts who
have studied the matter in Great Britain.
I think it would be misleading if the
public got the impression that it would
be possible to make dwelling-houses generally structurally secure against air-raid
attacks. This, I feel sure the House will
agree, would be quite impracticable. It
is rather the duty of the Government
to consider the best means of ensuring,
by structural means or otherwise, in conjunction with the local authorities concerned, the safety of the lives of the
citizens, and it is this aspect of the matter
which is being most carefully studied at
the present time, both by the Governments and local authorities.

Exchequer and Financial Provisions Bill.
Brought from the House of Commons
and read a First time.
Mr. ROBB: I do not think I need deal
with this Bill at any great length, for
the reason that the provisions of this
purely financial Measure are covered in
some detail in the White Paper which has
been circulated to Members, who will not
therefore wish me to detain them with
lengthy explanations.
In regard to the power sought in
Clause 1 to enable the Ministry of
Finance to issue an additional £1,500,000
from the Government Loans Fund, I
should state that this will not empower
the Ministry to lend money up to
£11,500,000, as they have no power to
lend moneys repaid. Although the limit.
of £10,000,000 authorised by Parliament
to be loaned from the Fund is almost
reached, the amount outstanding is about
£6,000,000.
In regard to the powers sought under
Clause 9 of the Bill, I should remind the
House that the Ministry of Finance were
empowered by the Transport Act of 1935
to guarantee necessary borrowings for
capital purposes by the Northern Ireland
Road Transport Board during the first
five years of the Board's operations. The
Board has SO far borrowed about
£2,600,000, to be repaid at call or short
notice under this guarantee, and the
House will agree that the Consolidated
Fund should not be liable for a sum of
this magnitude borrowed on short-term
loans.
