Education Bill [S.].
Order for Second Reading read.
Mr. ROBB: This is a small Bill dealing
with difficulties which have arisen in
connexion with residences provided for
teachers in public elementary schools.
deals only with those which were built
partly by aid of a Government loan under
the National School Teachers' Residences
(Ireland) Act, 1875. Under that Act
teachers' houses were built by various
trustees with the aid of a loan limited
to £250. This loan was repayable by
instalments over 35 years. The loan
charge was usually £12 10s, and towards
this the Commissioners of National
Education paid one-half annually, £6 5s,
the balance being provided locally. The
Commissioners' rules allowed the local
trustees to charge the teacher 21 per cent.
of the loan charge, and, as a matter of
fact, in 95 per cent. at least of these cases
the teacher paid this balance and the
local trustees paid nothing.
At the end of the 35 years there was
the house, the Commissioners' rules no
longer applied, and two views were
taken. On the one side were the trustees
who in law owned the house, and who
said to the teacher, "Pay us a full rent
for the house you are living in." On
the other side was the teacher who said,
"I agreed to pay one-half of the loan
charge, and now there is no loan charge,
and I am not bound by agreement to
pay anything; you never, in fact, paid
a penny for this house; either the
Government or the teacher paid it all;
I will pay no more rent." The Ministry
has in fact had examples of both these
cases, and we think it equitable that
while the teacher should pay a rent which
is generally the only fund the trustees
have for keeping the house in repair,
yet that that rent should not in the
ordinary case exceed what the teacher
used to pay, namely, 2 per cent. on the
amount of the original loan.
In some cases, however, these houses
were, or even now are, old-fashioned and
defective. They have no bathrooms, and
have bad sanitation generally. In any
case where such defects have been made
good it is proposed to allow the trustees
to charge the teacher such sum as the
Ministry may approve to cover interest
on such outlay.
The first sub-clause of Clause 1 of the
Bill carries out these intentions. It does
one other thing which is very much
a lawyer's point. The form of agreement
between trustees and teacher in the Commissioners' rules was what in law is called
a caretakers's agreement. Under such
agreements no rent is payable. As rent
will be payable in future provision is
made for substituting an ordinary
tenancy agreement for the caretaker's
agreement.
The second sub-clause deals with a
different matter. There have been a few
cases where a school has been transferred
to the local education authority, but the
teacher's residence has not. Sub-section
(9) of Section 14 of the Education Act of
1923 did provide that after the loan
period had expired a teacher's residence
should continue to be held on the same
trusts as before. But in one case that
came before the courts the argument was
put forward that the school in whose
favour the trusts of the residence was
created was no longer the same school
when transferred. This argument
succeeded, and the teacher was ejected.
Now, it was obviously never intended
that a house built as a teacher's residence
with a loan from public funds, repaid
half by the Government and in most cases
as to the other half by teachers' contributions, should cease to be available as a
"
*སྐ
ta
ag
Education Bill-
Second Reading
21 MARCH 1939
house for the teacher of the same pupils
in the same building merely because
under the 1923 Act the school was transferred to the local education authority.
Accordingly, without of course disturbing the existing judgment, sub-clause 2
provides that when a school has been or
shall be transferred, the teacher's
residence shall continue to be available
for the teacher, and this notwithstanding
that the school building may be altered,
rebuilt, or even removed to another site,
so long as its identity can be reasonably
preserved, though not when this identity
is merged by an amalgamation.
Clause 2 deals with another aspect
altogether. There are cases in which the
teacher's residence provided, as I have
mentioned, is no longer used as such. It
is still impressed with a trust, and the
income received from it ought to be
applied to some charitable purpose. But
there is no convenient machinery for seeing that this is done. This Clause
provides that the Ministry may call on
trustees to account for these profits, and
in case of misuse they will be in a position
to see that the proper steps are taken.
Clause 3 enables loans to be made for
nursery schools as for any other class.
These are the three main provisions
embodied in this Measure. I beg to
move, "That this Bill be now read a
Second time."
Mr. MCALLISTER: I suggest to the
Leader of the House that the further
stages of this Bill should not be taken for
some little time, so as to give Members
an opportunity of considering the Bill
thoroughly and in detail. I think it is
an important Measure, and the principles
involved are more important than the Bill
itself. I would like to have an opportunity to examine the various Clauses to
ascertain how far they affect the present
law as regards the right of ownership of
property. I assume that in the cases of
these privately built school houses that
have been transferred to the Ministry
through the Regional Education Committees all the instalments of the loans
originally obtained have been paid off,
and, therefore, no further payment of any
description should be demanded.
There is another point that strikes me,
and it is this. In his speech the Leader
of the House said that where trustees had
carried out sanitary or other improvements to a teacher's house the trustees
should be reimbursed in respect of the
expense. He did not say that where a
teacher, of his own volition, had made
such improvements he or she would be
entitled to be reimbursed. Is it only the
trustees who are entitled to recover expenditure made on improvements?
Mr. LESLIE: It seems to me that this
is a very useful little Bill, and that it is
going to provide a solution for sundry
difficulties that have arisen about the
country. I seem to recognise one of the
Clauses as dealing with a point that arose
in my own district, and which I brought
to the notice of the Ministry, although I
do not remember what conclusion was
arrived at. The only point I want to
make sure about is this. I take it from
paragraph (b) of Clause 1 that it is the
intention that after this Bill becomes an
Act those responsible may enlarge or
improve an existing teacher's residence,
and then get the benefits conferred by the
Act; that is the right to charge the teacher
the rent provided for.
Mr. ROBB indicated assent.
Mr. LESLIE: I gather that that is so,
and I think the wording of the Bill
sufficiently covers that point. On the
whole I support the Bill, and I think it
is a useful Measure.
Mr. ROBB: There is no wish to hurry
this Bill, and I am quite willing to undertake that no further stages of the Bill
will be taken this week.

(Winter
Unemployment Assistance
Adjustments) Regulations (Northern
Ireland), 1938.
Mr. ROBB: I beg to move—
That the Regulations made by the
Ministry of Labour on the 19th day of
September, 1938, under Sections 33 and
46 of the Unemployment Assistance Act
(Northern Ireland), 1934, authorising
the adjustment to meet winter conditions of the assessments of the needs of
applicants for unemployment assistance
be hereby approved.
Unemployment Assistance-
Winter Adjustments
THE SENATE
[Mr. Robb]
In moving this Resolution, I should
like to explain that with the onset of
the winter of 1937-38, the Unemployment
Assistance Board for Northern Ireland
decided, like the Board in Great Britain,
that, as the cases of persons in receipt
of unemployment assistance came up for
review, regard should be had to the
circumstances likely to arise in many
households as a result of increases in the
prices of certain commodities combined
with the approach of winter conditions,
and that assessments should be adjusted
to meet those seasonal circumstances.
Additions by way of winter allowances
were made in approximately one-half of
the total number of claims to unemployment assistance. For the usual family
group of man, wife and children the rate
of increase was 2s 6d or 3s a week, and
in a small number of families the addition
did not exceed 2s per week. In the
aggregate these additions amounted to
about £32,000.
When these winter allowances were
granted in the winter of 1937-38 it was
thought that the principal Regulations
conferred the power to authorise them,
but it subsequently appeared open to
doubt whether those Regulations did give
sufficient authority.
To put the matter beyond doubt, therefore, the Ministry, in exercise of its
powers under Section 46 of the Unemployment Assistance Act (Northern Ireland), 1934, made the Unemployment
Assistance (Winter Adjustments) Regulations (Northern Ireland), 1938, dated
the 19th September, 1938. These Regu
lations provide explicitly for the adjustment of assessments to meet winter
conditions, and in this respect are
identical with the corresponding Regulations made by the authorities in Great
Britain. In Great Britain and in
Northern Ireland these winter allowances
are payable between mid-November and
mid-April.
Section 46 provides that any regulations
made by the Ministry under this Section
shall be good for only twelve months unless, before the expiration of twelve
months, they have been approved by a
Resolution passed by each of the Houses
of Parliament.
On Question, Motion agreed to.
Resolved, "That this House do now
adjourn."-(Mr. Long.)
Adjourned accordingly at a
Quarter before Four o'clock.

Infanticide Bill.
Order for Second Reading read.
Mr. ROBB (Leader of the House):
This Bill is another stage towards the
lessening of the severity of the law with
regard to those cases where a woman, by
some wilful act or omission, has caused
the death of her child. Originally the
law was that where a woman had caused
the death of her child by wilful act or
omission she could be charged, and
charged only, with murder. If the
facts were proved the jury were bound
to find her guilty, and the judge was
bound to pass on her sentence of death.
But in the progress of national science it
was recognised that there were circumstances under which a woman newly
delivered of a child was not entirely responsible for her action. The difficulty
was formerly met by a reprieve, and the
sentence of death was never carried out,
but in the year 1922 the law was
amended, and an Act was introduced
and was applied to Northern Ireland by
which the law said that in the case of
a
woman being proved to have caused
the death of her child newly born by
reason of the disturbance of her mind
consequent upon childbirth, the jury
would be entitled to find her guilty not
of murder, but of infanticide. The
words used in that Act were "newly
born," and the court came to have to
determine what was the proper interpretation of the term "newly born."
was decided, for good or ill, that in that
Act "newly born" meant born within
one month.
It
The progress of medical opinion and
the development of the general intelligence of the community led to dissatisfaction with that, and accordingly, last
year in England an Act was passed
whereby it was enacted that "newly
born" should mean born within twelve
months. Therefore, if a case be brought
against a woman within twelve months
of the birth of her child, and she is
charged with having caused the death of
that child by wilful act or omission, the
jury might find her guilty, not
murder but of infanticide, with the consequence, of course, that she was not
sentenced to death. The judge did not
assume the black cap, and all the paraphernalia of the death sentence was not
gone through, but she was sentenced to
a term of imprisonment or other punishment corresponding to her crime.
of
That Act was passed last year in
England, and the first two Clauses of
the Bill now before this House enact the
same thing for this area. Sub-clause (3)
of Clause 1 reads as follow:-
Nothing in this Act shall affect the
power of the jury upon an indictment
for the murder of a child to return a
Infanticide Bill-
Second Reading
THE SENATE
[Mr. Robb]
verdict of manslaughter, or a verdict
of guilty but insane, or a verdict of
concealment of birth, in pursuance of
section sixty of the Offences against
the Person Act, 1861, except that for
the purposes of the proviso to that
section a child shall be deemed to have
recently been born if it had been born
within twelve months before its death.
The proviso in the Act of 1861 that is
referred to reads as follow:-
If any person tried for the murder
of any child shall be acquitted thereof it shall be lawful for the jury by
whose verdict such person shall be
acquitted to find in case it shall
SO appear
in evidence that the
child had recently been born, and
that such person did by some
secret disposition of the dead body of
such child endeavour to conceal the
birth thereof, and thereupon the court
may pass such sentence as if such
person had been convicted upon an
indictment for the concealment of the
birth.
That Section said that when a person
was charged with the murder of her child
which had been recently born, and was
acquitted upon that charge, such person
might be convicted of concealment of
birth. But the same difficulty about the
interpretation of "recently born" arose
in that Act, and the term was interpretated as meaning born within one month.
This sub-section of the Bill now before
the House extends that period of one
month to twelve months, corresponding
to the Clause referring to the crime of
infanticide. That is all this Bill does.
It extends the meaning of "recently
born" to having been born within twelve
months. It is an amelioration of the law
in this respect, and I commend the Bill
to the House. I beg to move, "That the
Bill be now read a Second time."
Mr. SAMUEL CUNNINGHAM: I
am very glad to see this Bill introduced.
I think civilisation has been very slow in
rectifying this matter, and I would like
the Government to go even further and
improve upon what the Bill proposes to
do. I think it is a great pity that a
mother, who is really out of her mind
on account of the suffering she has undergone, should have to be brought before
a public tribunal to be tried. It must
I
be very bad for a mother to have to
stand her trial under such circumstances, and it would be a good thing if
the Government would agree to accept
the certificate of a doctor that the woman
was unfit to be tried for causing the
death of her child. In that case she
should not have to be tried, even though
it is certain she will be acquitted.
think it is terrible that a mother, who
has passed through such trouble and
suffering should have to appear before
a judge and jury and the public to be
tried for an offence committed under
such circumstances, and I hope the
Government will see their way to insert
in this Bill a Clause enabling a doctor to
certify that a mother was unfit to stand
her trial under the circumstances set
forth in this Bill.
Mr. LESLIE: Like, I imagine, every
other Member of this House, I welcome
this Bill which, as I think the right hon.
Gentleman told us, follows word for
word the Statute enacted in England.
Formerly, by which I mean before 1922,
when the first Act was introduced, there
was the greatest difficulty in getting
juries to convict, however clear the
evidence might have been. If they did
convict the consequences, as illustrated
by the right hon. Gentleman, had to
follow. But that duty was most repugnant to the Judges,
the Judges, the Crown
Prosecutors, and to everybody connected
with the case. Indeed, it is recorded
that the late Mr. Justice Hawkins, as he
then was, when on one occasion he was
called upon to preside over a trial of that
description, definitely refused to assume
the black cap, and told the unfortunate
woman before him that she need not
listen to what he said, because it did not
concern her in the least. Of course,
he
passed the appropriate sentence, but in
words that were practically inaudible,
and the woman was reprieved.
This Bill goes a little further in one
way; that is to say, that the causes of
disturbance admissible in extenuation extend to what is described in the Bill as
lactation consequent upon birth. That
really goes, I think, a little further than
the actual delivery. Again, the Bill does
not reproduce the provision in the Act
of 1922, that as an alternative a verdict
for an offence under the well-known
Section 12 of the Children Act might be
brought in. That particular form of
Infanticide Bill-
Second Readin
22 MARCH 1939
verdict, I imagine, was found to be of
no use in practice, and, therefore, it was
dropped, and I cannot myself see any
reason to regret its disappearance. The
Bill does not interfere in any way with
the general application of the Section of
the Offences Against Persons Act, which
deals with the ordinary cases of concealment of birth.
The right hon. Gentleman in front of
me (Senator Samuel Cunningham) suggested that perhaps it might be possible
for Parliament to go further. I am
afraid I cannot follow him there. I do
think it is necessary, in the interests of
the preservation of human life, that we
should take steps for the preservation of
infant life, and I do not think that we
ought to further relax-I was almost
going to say the facilities, but such
facilities as public inquiry does afford for
the protection of infant life. Nowadays.
I do not think that the situation of a
woman, sad as it is, is so extreme as to
justify a departure from the old established principle.
Before a woman can be charged under
this Act, or with any offence of which
this Act takes cognisance, that woman
will have the advice of legal people. It
may possibly be only her solicitor, or she
may be able to engage counsel or have
counsel assigned to her, but I think a
woman charged with an offence of this
sort cannot enter the court under any
doubt whatever as to what is likely to
happen. She must, nowadays, be well
assured that the death sentence, so far
as she is concerned, is a thing of the
past. I do not think there would be
any justification for going further along
the path of leniency, attractive though
no doubt at first sight it is. I need
scarcely say, as I think I said at the
beginning, that I support this Bill, and
I hope it will have a speedy passage
through this House.
On Question, Motion agreed to.
Bill committed to a Committee of the
Whole House for to-morrow.

Mr. ROBB (Leader of the House): I
beg to move, That Standing Order No.
188 relative to Local Bills be suspended
for this day's sitting for the purpose of
taking the Third Reading of Plumb's
Divorce Bill." I may explain that under
the Standing Order, where an amendment
has been made in Committee to a Bill, the
Bill must lie on the Table for three days
before the Third Reading. This Bill has
been before Parliament since last
December, but there was a small amendment made to it in Committee, and it
was only returned to this House on
Tuesday. Consequently, this is only the
second day, and if the Standing Order
were not suspended it would mean that
the Royal Assent to the Bill must be postponed for a considerable number of weeks.
In order that the Royal Assent to the Bill
may be given on 29th of this month I am
moving the suspension of the Standing
Order.

Laid before the House (pursuant to
Act) and ordered to lie on the Table.
Question to Minister.
BY-PASS ROAD-PROVISION OF A
TUNNEL.
The following question stood on the
Order Paper in the name of Dr.
Gibson:--
To ask the Leader of the House
whether it is the intention of the Government to have the new by-pass road
connected with the city centre by a
tunnel from Middlepath Street to
Queen's Square, thus giving work to
the unemployed, and when completed,
relieving the traffic congestion
Queen's Bridge, providing a bomb-proof
shelter for many hundreds of persons
living in the vicinity in case of air
raids, and a means of communication
in the event of a high explosive bomb
destroying Queen's Bridge, or the
Albert Bridge, or both.
on
the
Dr. GIBSON: Judging from
number of messages and letters I have
received since putting down this question
I conceive it is of more than ordinary
public importance. Only this morning
I received a letter on the subject from
one of the most prominent public men
in Ulster. He writes:--
I am very much interested to see
that you have a question on the Paper
this afternoon as to how the by-pass
road can best be connected up with
the County Antrim side.
I see that you contemplate a tunnel
from Middlepath Street to Queen's
Bridge. For some time now I have
been much interested in this, but to
me a plan whereby the Holywood bypass road should be continued down
Sydenham Road, and then come under
the river by a tunnel underneath the
Liverpool steamer, running along
Great George's Street, and emerging
at Royal Avenue, has always appealed
very strongly.
By this route the heavy cost of
building bridges across the County
Down Railway to get from the Sydenham Road to Middlepath Street might
be saved.
Further than that, a tunnel both
on the County Down side and the
County Antrim side would be a wonderful A.R.P. provision for the mass
of workers in the Queen's Island and,
also, for the workers at Gallaher's in
York Street.
A question of this magnitude cannot
be settled very readily, and I have no
doubt the Government are considering
what is best to be done in this connection. The by-pass road is being
made, and in the ordinary course of
events it will be completed in twelve or
eighteen months, but I do not think it
will be of much use unless you have a
connection with the city centre. There
is no use hoping that this by-pass road
will give any relief to traffic if that traffic
has to cross the Queen's Bridge. As I
have said, the by-pass road will be completed in twelve or eighteen months,
and a tunnel such as I suggest would
take at least twelve or eighteen months
to complete, so that I think it ought to
be put in hands at once.
There may be engineering difficulties
in the way I have no doubt there are.
There may be financial difficulties in the
way of that I feel certain, but it is
just as easy to face your engineering and
financial difficulties to-day as it will be
in twelve months' time. My anxiety.
is that something should be done
immediately. The matter should be put
in hands without delay, and unemployment should be relieved to the extent of
the number who will be employed on the
work. From the point of view of
101 Provision of a Tunnel

29 MARCH 1939
Education Bill
A.R.P. such a tunnel is very important.
I very much regret there is not greater
progress being made with A.R.P. in
Northern Ireland, but it would be
helped very considerably if you had a
tunnel such as I suggest where thousands
of people could find shelter in case of
an air raid.
Mr. ROBB (Leader of the House):
The question of how the by-pass road.
should be connected with the city centre
has not yet been decided, work on the
road being concentrated on the Tillysburn end of the road, which can be proceeded with without prejudice to the
question to which the hon. Member
refers.
Even if a tunnel were built it is very
questionable whether it would be suitable
as an air-raid shelter, as any penetration
of the tunnel below high-water mark or
in the under-river section would result
in the flooding of the tunnel, and it
would therefore be unusually vulnerable.

CLAUSE 1 (Amendment of provisions with respect to teachers' residences).
The following Amendment stood on
the Order Paper in the name of Mr.
Robb:
Clause 1, at the end of sub-clause (2),
add the following new sub-clause:
(3) Where a teacher's residence is
provided in connection with a school,
then, notwithstanding-
(a) The removal of the school to other
premises; or
(b) the reconstruction or rebuilding
of the premises in which the
school is carried on; or
(c) the amalgamation of the school
with one or more other schools; or
(d) in the case of-
(i)
a boys' school, its amalgamation
with a girls' school, or a girls'
school and an infants' school;
(ii) a girls' school, its amalgamation
with a boys' school, or a boys'
school and an infants' school;
(iii) an infants' school, its amalgamation with the boys' school, or
a
boys' school and a girls'
school;
such residence shall be deemed to have
been provided in connection with the
school so removed, reconstructed, rebuilt or amalgamated:
Provided that this sub-section shall
not apply in a case (not being a case
coming within the provision of paragraph (d) of this sub-section) where
the school forms one of a group of
two or more schools which are replaced
by a new school, or are amalgamted
with and carried on in an existing
school other than the first-mentioned
school).
Mr. ROBB: The sub-clause to which
this particular Amendment refers is subclause (2), which reads-
Where, at any time before or after
the passing of this Act, a school in connection with which a teacher's residence
is provided, has been, or is, transferred
to an education authority the school
shall, notwithstanding such transfer,
continue to be the school for which
that residence was provided, and the
residence, if not transferred to the
education authority, shall, subject to
the provisions of sub-section (1) of
Education Bill-
Committee
THE SENATE
this section, continue to be held by the
trustees upon the same trusts and conditions as it was held immediately
before the transfer of the school:
It was pointed out to the Ministry
that in the Education Acts sometimes
the word "school" is confined to the
actual building; sometimes it refers to
the institution and the activities that
are being carried on there; and sometimes it has been urged that a
school is not the same school as the old
building that was pulled down for a new
one
new
to be erected. Considering that
there might be some ambiguity regarding these matters if the Bill is left as
it is, the Government has put down this
Amendment to make it clear what is
meant. The Amendment says:-
Where a teacher's residence is provided in connection with a school,
then, notwithstanding-
(a) the removal of the school to other
premises;
That is where a school is transferred to
other premises.
(b) the reconstruction or rebuilding of
the premises in which the school
is carried on ;
That is where the building is pulled down
and a
new school erected on the same
site.
(c) the amalgamation of the school
with one or more other schools;
That is where on that site another school
is brought in.
(d) in the case of—
(i) a boys' school, its amalgamation
with a girls' school, or a girls'
school and an infants' school;
(ii) a girls' school, its amalgamation
with a boys' school, or a boys'
school and an infants' school;
(iii) an infants' school, its amalgamation with a boys' school, or
a boys' school and a girls'
school;
That deals with cases which frequently
occur. We have had all over Northern
Ireland many cases where we have had
separate boys', separate girls', and
separate infants' schools practically in
the same building and when the number
of pupils in those schools falls below a
certain figure the two or three schools
may be amalgamated. This part of the
Amendment says that in such cases a
teacher's residence shall be deemed to
have been provided in connection with
the school so removed, reconstructed, rebuilt, or amalgamated. The subsection is not to apply in the case of a
school which forms one of a group of two
or more schools which are replaced by a
new school or are amalgamated with
an existing school other than the firstmentioned school. I move this Amendment in order to make clear our policy
with regard to this particular matter.
Amendment agreed to.
Clause, as amended, ordered to stand
part of the Bill.

Question to Minister.
RE-ESTABLISHMENT
OF NORTH
IRISH HORSE.
The following question stood on the
Order Paper in the name of Captain
Perceval Maxwell:-
the
To ask the Leader of the House
whether in view of the need for widening the opportunities of service in the
armed forces of the Crown, consequent
on the introduction of conscription in
the United Kingdom, and to enable
Ulstermen, who may be called on to
undergo military training in
mechanised or artillery arm of the
Army to be trained in an Ulster regiment, the Government of Northern
Ireland will inform the appropriate
Department of the Imperial Government that it is the desire of the
majority of the people of Northern
Ireland that the North Irish Horse
should be re-established as a mechanised or artillery unit.
Mr. SPEAKER: Before calling upon
the hon. and gallant Member to put his
Oral Answers.
Housing Bill-
THE SENATE
(Mr. Speaker
question, I should like to point out that,
although he is entitled to
put the
question, any debate on this subject
would be entirely out of order, because
this being a matter of defence it is outside the powers of this Parliament.
Captain
PERCEVAL-MAXWELL:
Having regard to the ruling which you
have now given, I am of course unable
to give this House my reasons for having
put this question on the Order Paper, but
I would ask your permission to explain
why I, and not another Member of this
House, am putting forward this question.
I did not have the honour to serve in the
North Irish Horse at any time, and as
hon. Members know, there is a distinguished Member of this House (Sir
Emerson Herdman) who was also a distinguished Member of the North Irish
Horse, and had he been here it would
have been for him to have raised this
question. I am putting the question at
the request of the hon. and gallant Member, who is unable to be present, and of
several of the old members of the North
Irish Horse and other persons who feel
it would be desirable that the North
Irish Horse should be re-formed. Having
explained the reason why I am putting
this question I will not say anything
further.
Mr. ROBB: The Government appreciate the widespread desire in Northern
Ireland that there should be more varied
opportunites here for service in the
armed forces, and when, some time ago,
representations were made through the
usual channels to the appropriate Department in Great Britain for the reestablishment of the North Irish Horse
as a mechanised unit they were made
with the full approval and support of
the Government.
So far as I am aware the Imperial
Government have not yet intimated their
desire in the matter.

Bill ordered to be read a Second time

Housing Bill.
Resolved, "That this Bill be now read
a First time."-(Mr. Long.)
Mr. ROBB: I beg to move, "That this
Bill be now read a Second time." The
main purpose of this Bill may be simply
stated. It is to facilitate the provision
of improved housing for the poorest class
of the population.
Mr. MCALLISTER: On a point of
order, may I suggest that this Bill is
entirely out of order, as there is no reference to taking the Second Reading of the
Bill on the Order Paper for to-day. I
submit that the Second Reading of a
Bill should not be taken when it does not
appear on the Order Paper.
the
Mr. SPEAKER: It is quite in order,
because the Second Reading of
Housing Bill is actually mentioned on
the Order Paper.
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: Had I noticed
that it was mentioned in the Motion to
suspend the Standing Order I should
certainly have objected to that Motion.
Mr. ROBB: Although I am sure hon.
Members, who are well aware of the great
room there is for such improvement, will
have no fault to find with the purpose of
the Bill, and although it is very largely
a financial Measure, I think Members
will wish me to give an explanation of its
provisions.
The first part of the Bill is not
designed to increase the number of houses
available, but to assist the rehousing of
those town dwellers who are living in
Houses which are not fit for habitation.
The erection of new housing estates on
the outskirts of the towns has the con-
Housing Bill-
Royal Assent
2 MAY 1939
siderable disadvantage that the persons
who move to them generally move at
the same time further from their work,
with the consquence that travelling expenses bulk too large in small family
budgets, and that they lose touch with
the churches, schools and other social
centres with which they have been associated. It is proposed, therefore, where
unsatisfactory
houses have to be
destroyed, to replace. them with good
houses on the same site. A novel feature
of the Bill is the provision enabling local.
authorities to provide temporary accommodation for persons displaced by rebuilding schemes; accommodation from
which they will move back to a new house
in their old district.
It is clear that the poorest section of
the population, which the Measure is
intended to help, cannot afford to pay
what would be an economic rent
for a good house built at existing costs.
Therefore it is proposed that, up to a
maximum of £150, one-third of the cost
of each new house erected under the
first part of this Measure shall be met
by the Government. The Government
contribution will only be made provided
the schemes and their carrying into effect
are subject to the approval and supervision of the Ministry of Home Affairs.
The rent where the cost of a house
does not exceed £450-that is the
greatest amount upon which the Government grant of one-third of the cost of
erection is payable-must not exceed 4s
a week. Where the cost of a house exceeds £450, the maximum rent chargeable will be 6s a week.
The first part of the Bill also enables
Housing Associations, non-profit earning
bodies, to obtain Government assistance
on the same scale and on the same basis
as those on which it is offered to local
authorities. The maximum rent which
may be charged for houses provided.
under the Bill by Housing Associations
is 6s 6d.
Part one of the Bill deals only with
housing in urban areas. Part two is
applicable to both urban and rural areas.
Its purpose is to enable local authorities,
with Government assistance, to Cooperate with the owners of condemned
houses in converting them into satisfactory dwellings. The proposal is not
new. A similar Measure, the Housing
(Rural Workers) Act passed at Westminster in 1926, has worked very well in
England.
Assistance can be given where it is
estimated that the value of the reconditioned house would exceed £450 and
the maximum cost of repairs allowable
for the reconditioning of a house is £150.
Assistance can be given in the form
of either a grant or a loan at a low rate
of interest, or a combination of grant
and loan. In so far as grants are concerned, the owner, the local authority,
and the Government will each pay onethird of the cost, provided it does not
exceed £150. If it should exceed £150
the owner must pay the excess without
assistance. The owner will be able to
add to the amount to be charged for
rent before the reconditioning
effected an amount equal to 4 per cent.
per annum on his share of the cost.
was
The purpose of Part III. of the Bill
is to enable certain additional classes of
persons to reside in cottages built under
the Labourers Acts.
These classes are:-Firstly, widows
and unmarried daughters of agricultural labourers; secondly, agricultural
labourers working outside the rural district in which the cottages in which they
live are situated; and thirdly, persons
living in overcrowded houses, or in unhealthy houses which cannot be repaired
at a reasonable cost.
Part IV. is mainly consequential. I
should state, however, that it provides
that local authorities shall not accept
persons born outside Northern Ireland
as tenants of houses provided under the
Housing Acts or Labourers Acts unless
they have resided for at least seven
years in the United Kingdom. This
provision, of course, does not apply to
persons displaced by rehousing schemes.
It also provides that the Fair Wages
Clause should be applied in respect of
housing by local authorities. I should
perhaps mention that similar provision
in respect of building by housing
associations is made in the first part of
the Bill.
These are the objects of the Bill, and
I am sure they are objects with which
the House will be in agreement. I do
not think there will be any serious
criticism of the method by which it is
endeavoured to carry out the assistance
the Government have planned for the rehousing of the poorest class.
Housing Bill—
Second Reading
THE SENATE
Question proposed, "That this Bill be
now lead a Second time.'
Mr. JOSEPH CUNNINGHAM: I
would like to support the Second Reading of this Bill because I think it is very
necessary not only in the larger districts
such as Belfast and other towns in Northern Ireland, but even in country districts. In the country there are
deplorable dwellings in which human
beings are existing, and this Bill provides
a good opportunity to improve their
dwelling accommodation. I must say the
Government has been very generous in
its financial assistance in this direction.
Sir JAMES WILTON: Question.
some
Mr. JOSEPH CUNNINGHAM: My
view is that it has, and I think that
generosity should induce the local
authorities to operate the provisions of
the Bill. There will be some difficulty
in deciding who are proper and suitable
applicants for houses let at a rent of
4s a week, because there are a large number of people who will be very anxious
to become occupants of houses at such a
rent. The lowest rent at which we have
been able to let houses in Belfast is 7s
a week, and they are only very small
houses. A rent of from 4s to 6s a week
is comparatively small, and I am afraid
we will have considerable difficulty in
selecting the most suitable tenants. I
hope the local authorities will be pressed
to put this Bill into operation when it
becomes an Act, because, while we have
no very bad slum property in the City
of Belfast, there are a considerable number of houses that are not fit for human
If
habitation. They are built too close
together so that adequate air and sunshine do not get round the dwellings,
which are bound to be unhealthy.
the provisions of this Bill are put into
operation by the local authorities the
housing conditions of our people will be
greatly improved, and they will be more
healthy and contented as a result.
Sir JAMES WILTON: I do not think
anybody can dispute the fact that a
Housing Bill is necessary in Northern
Ireland. It may be more necessary in
some cases than in others but, generally
speaking. I do not think there is any
section of the community that will not
welcome some sort of Housing Bill. But
I would like to join issue at the outset
with Senator Cunningham when he conpliments the Government on their
generosity. We are asked to build
houses at a cost of £450, of which the
Government will give us £150. That
means the houses will cost the local
authority £300, and we are asked to impose a rent of not more than 4s a week
on those houses. I am sure this Bill was
intended in the first instance to provide
houses for the very poor, but who would
for one moment attempt to suggest that
the very poor should be put into inferior
houses. There can be no doubt that no
house of which the economic rent is 4s
a week is fit for anyone to live in.
Take the house referred to in this Bill,
which will cost the local authority £300.
If the local authority got the best terms
possible, a loan for a term of 60 years at
3 per cent., and they let the houses at a
rent of 4s a week, they would be losing
£15 a year per house. What encouragement is there for a local authority like
my own, with rates of almost 14s in the
£, to build from 200 to 400 of these
houses with the prospect of losing £15
per house per annum.
The
I support the Second Reading of this
Bill because I support the principle of a
Housing Bill, but I very much regret
that the representations that were made
from time to time to the Government,
particularly by the Council of Municipal
Authorities, bore so little fruit.
question of rent is one of considerable
importance, because if there is to be any
encouragement for local authorities to
provide what is undoubtedly necessary,
namely, better housing, there is not the
slightest doubt that the rent of the houses
must be at least almost economic. I do
not say the local authorities should not
pay something, but fancy asking them to
build houses at a net cost of £300, for
which they are allowed to charge only 4s
a week rent, and, in addition, receive no
rates in respect of those houses.
There is another point I would like to
mention and that is the type of tenant
who will get these houses. When the last
Housing Bill was passed and subsidies.
were given to local authorities, the
authority which I represent built between
600 and 700 houses, and the economic
rent which had to be charged to meet the
expenditure was anything from 12s to 14s.
a week. Now, if we do as we should do
and build another 200 or 300 houses

Second Reading

The
under this Bill to be let at a rent of 4s
a week, what is going to happen?
people who are in the original subsidy.
houses will ask to get one of these new
and cheaper houses. The tenants of the
subsidy houses are unable to pay the
rents of 12s, 14s or 15s a week unless
they take in a couple of sub-tenants who
pay
rent.
fairly substantial
The
moment the Corporation takes advantage
of this Bill those sub-tenants will at once
leave and ask to be provided, as they are
entitled to be, with one of these new
houses. Thus the original tenants of the
subsidy houses will be unable to pay the
rents, and the houses will probably be
left on the hands of the local authority.
a
Another important question is the
proper supervision by the Government of
the building of houses under this scheme.
The houses that were built as subsidy
houses are now known as subside-y houses
by reason of the fact that the Government supervision over their erection was
not sufficient to ensure that good material
and good workmanship was put into
them. If the local authority has to spend
£300 on each house it is very probable
they will take good care it is a substantial
house. I support the Second Reading of
the Bill because no one can deny that a
Housing Bill is necessary, but I reserve
the right to criticise particular Clauses
in Committee.
Mr. LESLIE: Certainly I am entirely
in sympathy with the other speakers in
welcoming any attempt to improve housing conditions where those housing conditions do in fact require improvement.
Although there are undoubtedly instances
throughout Northern Ireland of very
indifferent housing accommodation
think we may fairly claim that, taking
our area as a whole, we are not very far
behind other parts of the United
Kingdom.
I
Passing from that to the Bill before
us, the Bill appears to me to be purely
permissive in character. It is entirely
for the local authorities to say whether
they will or will not put the Measure
into operation. Most of the local authorities at present are somewhat oppressed
mentally, if not actually physically, by
the burden of their rates, and they are
perpetually being subjected to pressure
on the part of their constituents, on the
one hand to get the rates down, and on
the other to carry out some improvement
which appeals to the particular constituents. They are in that way in a very
difficult position.
As regards the assistance which the
Government is prepared to give, £150
out of £450, that is the assistance
towards the lump sum cost of building
houses, I am not quite sure that I shall
not be disagreeing with the Mayor of
Derry about that, but I am afraid I must
say that a contribution of £150 out of
the taxpayer's pocket is a very generous
contribution. The incidence of clearing
slums, closing uninhabitable houses, and
replacing them has, since the Housing
of the Working Classes Act of 1890, been
a burden cast in the first instance on
the local authority, the idea being that
it was at the cost of the rates that these
improvements should be, effected.
But although I hold that view as
regards the lump sum, I agree that something might be done in the way of increasing the rent charged for these houses.
I am afraid I have to think that the.
people displaced in these unhealthy
houses are not, speaking generally, the
most deserving class of the community.
I am also not satisfied that in the. Bill
itself any provision has been made for
preventing the evils of sub-letting and
consequent overcrowding. Whether the
individual local authority will be able
to protect itself by its own by-laws or
by the provisions of other housing enactments I am not prepared to say.
That is a matter to which the Government should direct attention and give

a later date the point of preventing the

lodgers. Whether that is a true story

Passing to the question of rates, I cannot agree with Clause 5 of the Bill, if

Local Officers Superannuation Bill.
Order for Second Reading read.
Mr. ROBB: Hon. Members will remember that the Belfast Corporation last year
adopted a scheme under which all officers
in their employment must retire at the
age of 65. Under the existing statutes
dealing with the superannuation of Local
Government officers, such officers, unless
their retirement is due to ill-health, are
only entitled to a superannuation allowance when they have attained the age
of 60 years and have given at least twenty
years' service.
As a result of the imposition under the
new scheme of the age limit to which I
have referred, several officers of the
Belfast Corporation have been, or will
be, compelled to retire before they have
given the twenty years' service necessary
for superannuation. The Corporation
have taken the view, with which the
Government concur, that it is unfair
that these persons, who entered the.
service before the introduction of the new
rule, should be deprived of any pension
by it.
This Bill is introduced in order to
enable the Corporation, and any other
local authority who may adopt a similar
scheme of compulsory retirement for its
officers, to superannuate persons affected
in the way I have described. The Measure
will apply only to officers who were
serving on 31st March last. I beg to
move.

Evidence Bill.
Order for Second Reading read.
Mr. ROBB: This is a Bill which is,
of course, fairly technical. Some people
call it a lawyer's Bill, and so it is, but,
after all, the law exists for the good of
the general public. The object of this
Bill is to simplify the procedure and to
render less technical the difficulties that
beset litigants.
The first Clause deals with the admissibility of documentary evidence as to
facts in issue. It provides that in any
civil proceedings where direct oral evidence of a fact would be admissible, any
statement made by a person in a document and tending to establish that fact
shall, on production of the original document, be admissible as evidence of that
fact under certain conditions. The rule
of law in general on the question of
evidence is that the best evidence must
be given of any fact, and the best
evidence of a fact is not the matter of
the document itself, but oral evidence,
and that is the only evidence the court
accepts in ordinary cases.
It is a curious rule of law that a statement of the fact, although made at the time
by the person giving evidence, cannot be
shown to the court. The witness can refresh his memory from it, but the document itself is not admissible in evidence.
Take a simple case. Supposing a merchant in Derry sues me for goods that
he says I bought in his shop on a particular day. I say I did not buy them
there at all; that I was not in Derry
on that day; and that in fact I was
playing golf at Newcastle. I go into the
Evidence Bill-
Second Reading
THE SENATE
[Mr. Robb]
box and swear I was not in Derry that
day, and that I was playing golf at
Newcastle. I am asked, "How do you
know you were playing golf at Newcastle
on that day; how do you remember it
was on that particular day?" I reply,
"Well, I keep a diary, and my diary
shows me that I was at Newcastle playing golf on that day." The first thing
any man hearing me swear that would
say is, "Well, let me see your diary."
But I cannot show my diary to the court.
It does not seem common sense, but that
is the law.
Suppose I call the caddy master and
he says, "I issued a ticket to play on
that particular day to Mr. Robb, and I
know him quite well." He is asked,
"How do you know?" and he says,
"Well, I have my book of tickets, and
it shows that I issued a ticket to him
on that day." Although he may refresh.
his memory by looking at the tickets,
the court cannot see this document.
That may seem rather ludicrous.
That
is what happens where a witness is alive.
Supposing a witness is dead, and has
made a contemporaneous record of facts
which are in issue in any trial. The law
says that that record is admissible as evidence, but only under two conditions.
The first is that what is put in the record
must be against the interest of the person putting it on record, and, secondly,
the court would allow it to be read if
it was the duty of the person making
the record to enter that particular fact.
Both in the House of Lords and in
another place in this country a curious
example of that is given in the books.
There was a case being tried where, the
question turned upon the date of the
birth of a particular person. The real
question in issue is whether that man was
born upon 2nd April or 22nd April.
There was no direct evidence upon the
point, but there was produced to the
court a document, a copy of which is to
be found in this book I have here, which
was a record in his books made by what
the report of the case calls the man midwife who attended at the birth of this child.
It was written in Latin, and it set out
that he attended William Jones's, junior,
wife at the birth of a son; that that boy
was born on the 22nd April, and that
he charged the sum of £1 6s ld.
Had the document stopped there it
would not have been admissible in
evidence, but luckily for justice in this
case the man midwife, although writing
the rest of the document in Latin, had
written the good English word "Paid"
below that. The court held that this was
an entry by a man which was against
his own interest, because if it had not
been paid he would still have sued for
the fee, but because he had put "Paid"
it was against his interest, and accordingly that document was held to be
admissible.
as
Not only was it held admissible
regards the fact of payment, but the
whole document was held to be admissible.
as evidence of all it contained, and as it
contained the fact that the birth was
upon the 22nd April, it established to
the court's satisfaction that this man
was born on the 22nd April. That is an
example of the class of case where a
document is admitted as evidence in
court, because it is said to be against the
interest of the person who made the
document, and the. whole of the document is admissible as evidence.
The other class of case where a document made by a dead person is admissible.
in evidence is where a person makes a
statement in writing in the course of the
execution of his duty in the office that
he holds. One of the examples in the
book is that of an entry by an incumbent of a church that he had baptised a
child on a particular date. The question
in issue in that case was again the date
of the birth of the child. Although he
had made an entry in the book that he
had baptised the child on a particular
day, and that the child was born on a
particular date, the court held that the
only part of that document admissible
was that part which recorded the doing
of his own duty and the date of doing
that duty, and it was not admissible as
showing the date on which the child was
born. Those are two examples of
anomalies in our law.
The first Clause of this Bill says that
the conditions for admitting a statement
in evidence shall be:
(a) if the maker of the statement
either-
(i) had personal knowledge of the
matters dealt with by the statement; or
Evidence Bill-
Second Reading
4 MAY 1939
(ii) where the document in question
is or forms part of a record purporting to be a continuous
record, made the statement (in
so far as the matters dealt with
thereby are not within his
personal knowledge) in the performance of a duty to record information supplied to him by a
person who had, or might
reasonably be supposed to have,
personal knowledge of those
matters; and
(b) if the maker of the statement is
called as a witness in the proceedings.
case
In the first
I instanced my
diary would have been admissible in
evidence, and the court could have looked
at it. The book of tickets of the caddy
master at Newcastle would also have
been admissible in evidence, and the
court could have looked at it. To
resolve difficulties of that sort the first
Clause of this Bill is enacted. There
you have a document which sets out a
series of circumstances, and the person
making it has either personal knowledge
of the fact or has made. it in the course
of his duty on information supplied to
him by a person who had knowledge.
Those are cases where the person making
the record itself can be called as a witness,
but it goes on to provide that the con--
dition that the maker of the statement
shall be called as a witness need not be
satisfied in a number of specified
instances first, if he is dead; second, if
he is unfit by reason of his bodily or
mental condition to attend as a witness;
third, if he is beyond the seas, and it is
not reasonably practicable to secure his
attendance, or if all reasonable efforts to
find him have been made without success.
At the present time. you could not use
in court a document made by a man if
he is still alive unless you can call him.
In bringing forward this Bill the
Government is seeking to have enacted
for this area what has been since last
year the law in England. Might I here
interpolate that it is a great advantage
to practitioners of law in this country
that as far as possible our law should be
kept the same as the law in England.
We are a small area, and it is difficult
for any person to publish books on any
branch of the law, because they could
not make it pay. It is a great advantage
that text books published for English
practitioners should be equally applicable to practitioners here.
Sub-clause (2) of Clause 1 goes a little
further. It says:
In any civil proceedings, the court
may at any stage of the proceedings,
if having regard to all the circumstances of the case it is satisfied that
undue delay or expense would otherwise be caused, order that such a
statement as is mentioned in subsection (1) of this section shall be
admissible as evidence, or may, without any such order having been made,
admit such a statement in evidence-
(a) notwithstanding that the maker of
the statement is available but is
not called as a witness;
(b) Notwithstanding that the original
document is not produced, if in lieu
thereof there is produced a copy
of the original document or of the
material part thereof, certified to
be a true copy in such manner as
may be specified in the order or
as the court may approve, as the
case may be.
That is simply and solely for the purpose of saving expense. There are many
cases in which a litigant is put to enormous
expense in producing a witness
from England, Scotland, or abroad to
prove a document that everybody knows
is a good document. If the court is
satisfied that it would lead to undue expense to bring that witness here the
court may say the document may be
admitted. The third sub-section is rather
an important one. It says:
Nothing in this section shall render
admissible as evidence any statement
made by a person interested at a time
when proceedings were pending or
anticipated involving a dispute as to
any fact which the statement might
tend to establish.
or
It has been universally recognised that
once litigation has been begun
threatened, what people put on paper is
not a disinterested record, and it is only
in respect of documents made before any
such dispute arises that this Act is to
apply at all. The fourth sub-clause only
states that the statement must be in the
man's own handwriting, or typed, and
Evidence Bill-
Second Reading
THE SENATE
[Mr Robb]
signed by him, or verified in some way,
and the fifth sub-clause says the court
may draw any reasonable inference.
Now we
come to the second Clause.
We have allowed the court to see this
document, and this Clause deals with the
weight to be attached to such evidence.
The first sub-clause says:-
In estimating the weight, if any,
to be attached to a statement rendered
admissible as evidence by this Act,
regard shall be had to all the circumstances from which any inference can
reasonably be drawn as to the accuracy
or otherwise of the statement, and in
particular to the question whether or
not the statement was made contemporaneously with the occurrence or
existence of the facts stated, and to
the question whether or not the maker
of the statement had any incentive to
conceal or misrepresent facts.
It was felt that it might have been
thought that the first Clause of the Bill
meant that where a document was
brought in it was to have undue weight,
and the object of the second Clause is
to make it certain that mere admission
is not to be taken as meaning that it
will be accepted as evidence. The
second sub-clause is also an important
one. There are certain classes of actions
in our courts where corroboration is
necessary, and this sub-section makes it
clear that the mere admission of documents of this sort is not in any way
to weaken the rule with regard to
corroboration.
Clause 3 refers to proof of documents
that need attesting. It says:-
.
Subject as hereafter in this section
provided. in any proceedings, whether
civil or criminal, an instrument to the
validity of which attestation is
requisite may, instead of being proved
by an attesting witness, be proved in
the manner in which it might be
proved if no attesting witness were
alive :
Provided that nothing in this
section shall apply to the proof of
wills or other testamentary documents.
The law as it stands at present is that
you must call the witness. That had its
origin in ancient days when a witness
could be called upon to defend in the
lists the truth of what he said in the
court. Nowadays, if a person is dead,
you can prove the writing of the document without actually calling the witness himself. That, however, is not to
apply in any way to wills or testamentary
documents.
Coming to Clause 4, at one time every
document produced in court had to be
fully proved. Then came a time when
it was obviously impossible to call the
witness, and the rule was laid down that
if a document was a hundred years old
it proved itself. That was subsequently
shortened to thirty years, and now it is
proposed to limit it to twenty years,
which would seem a not unreasonable.
time. If a document is produced from
proper custody and it is twenty years
old, the Bill says it proves itself and you
need not call the attesting witness.
Clause 5 deals with Rules of Court,
and Clause 6 is the Interpretation
Clause. It say that "documents" include books, maps, plans, drawings, and
figures. Incidentally may I say that a
curious thing about this is that in courts
of law a tally which a stevedore gives
for the loading of a ship by making
nicks in a stick is a document. These
are the objects of this Measure, and I
ask the House to give it a Second
Reading.
Question proposed, "That the Bill be
read a Second time."
Mr. LESLIE: I am sure we have

Bill which has been given to us by the

the Common law, because I think I am
correct in saying the Bill deals exclusively with Common law matters. There
is only one reference to Statute, which is,
I think, in the Interpretation Clause, and
deals with the mere matter of making
Rules.
I was, if I may humbly say so, a little
amused at the manner in which the right
hon. Gentleman gave a Newcastle and a
golfing flavour to the deliberations on
certain cases. I cannot help wondering
whether he had in mind the old enemy
of some of us of more than 200 years ago.

dealing, not with goods, but with casks.
of beer. That is my recollection, and it

William Fowden, the younger, which is
a pretty old case, but I am sorry to say
I do not remember the case about the
baptism. Perhaps I ought to, but I

One is left with the impression that the
law student of the future will have a
good deal less common law to study than
some of us had in the old days, because
a great deal more of the law will be set
out for him in Acts of Parliament. I
do not know whether there are any
family solicitors left in Northern Ireland
like the one who, when presented with
Statute said, "I do not care about that.
It is Act of Parliament. Very bad law."
That practitioner preferred Common law,
which has a great deal to commend it.
I do not think there is anything in the
earlier part of the Bill to which exception
can be taken. I think it will simplify
the law and render it a little more easy
of comprehension. I could only wish that
one sub-clause in the Bill could be made
applicable to Parliamentary Committees.
There are Joint Committees sitting to
consider questions of divorce, and before
those Parliamentary Committees evidence
which has already been given before the
High Court has to be repeated. If it
should happen that there is a sort of
double divorce, a husband suing another
man for adultery with his wife, and subsequently the wife of the adulterer suing
in his case, the evidence would have to be
heard no fewer than four times. Provisions such as are contained in this Bill
would obviate that absurdity. But that
is likely to be dealt with in another way,
so I will say nothing more about it.
I was rather sorry to gather from the
right hon. Gentleman a sort of suggestion
that learned counsel in Northern Ireland
required to get their law from text
books. Text books, I was brought up to
believe, are all very well for the student,
but when a man passed from that stage
he ought to be able to write text books
and deliver lectures.
Mr. ROBB: Of course, I used text
book as including Reports.
Mr. LESLIE: One accepts
accepts that.
There is one thing about the right hon.
Gentleman's speech on which I should
like to lay stress. He reminded the

the same thing as proof. It is really
something in the nature of a
common
fallacy that if evidence is allowed to be
received the fact to which that evidence
is directed is to be regarded as an
established fact. As we were told this
afternoon, that is not so, and it cannot
be too widely known. I was very glad
to observe that although this Bill, when
it becomes an Act, will have been passed
approximately a year after the English
Statute, it will come into operation on
the same date. That is 1st September,
which is the date on which the English
Statute also operates.
Passing from that to the two Clauses
towards the end of the Bill, I desire to
speak on both with very considerable reserve, because I recognise that I am to
some extent getting out of my depth.
Clause 4 shortens the time necessary for
the acceptance of the document, and
provides that the existing term of thirty
years should be shortened to twenty
years, and should apply to both civil and
criminal proceedings. I have the greatest
horror-and I expect the right hon.
Gentleman has too-of taking anything
for granted in criminal cases, and I think
that before the House accepts that Clause
we ought to be given some further information as to the reasons why
why the
criminal part of it is, so to speak, brought
before us.
It is perhaps a little difficult to
imagine a case in which the thirty or
twenty years would make any difference
in criminal cases. I could not myself, in
such resources as I have available at
home, find any case in which the matter
had arisen, although there may be some,
and it is obvious that such a provision
might cut both ways. It might assist
the prosecution, and it might, and
perhaps more likely, assist the defence.
It is a matter of some importance, and
therefore, although without any clear
conviction in my mind as to which way
the provision ought to go, I will at a
later stage move to leave out "criminal"
in order that the House may be advised
of the reasons which caused those in
England to insert that in the Bill. Our
Clause follows the English Clause word
с
Evidence Bill--
Second Reading
THE SENATE
[Mr. Leslie]
for word, and that is an argument for
accepting it.
The other matter is, to my mind, a little
more difficult. We have in Clause 5 a
provision about Rules of Court, and it is
perfectly obvious that the intention is
that it should be limited to civil proceedings. It can scarcely be extended beyond
that in the wording before us, but I do
feel a little doubt as to what is the
position as regards Quarter Sessions.
That is, of course, the Quarter Sessions as
distinct from the Civil Bill Court, but
still dealing with matters which are
essentially of a civil character. I am not
very clear as to what the intention is, or
how it is to be carried out, and again,
with the same reason of getting further
information about that, I will put something on the Paper.
There is, as the House knows, a certain
amount of civil work done by Quarter
Sessions, and Quarter Sessions nowadays.
consist of the County Court Judge or
Recorder sitting alone, just as if he were
sitting in a Civil Bill Court, but the
distinction between a Civil Bill Court
and Quarter Sessions is still there in
theory, if not in practice, and we ought
to be clear about it. These are matters
of detail, but they are matters on which
we should have clear information before
we finally enact the Bill. That we shall
enact the Bill I have no doubt whatever,
and I also have no doubt that it will be
of great assistance
in
our judicial
administration.
Mr. MCALLISTER:
I am sure the
House will appreciate the great pains
with which the Leader of the House has
introduced this Bill, and the clear and
lucid manner in which he has explained
the provisions of what must appear to
the ordinary layman a most complex
Measure. With regard to what the
right hon. Gentleman said about text
books, that is a serious difficulty in
Northern Ireland. We have no text
books of the kind to which he referred
to enable us to put our finger on particular aspects of the law as it stands, and
to cases which have been decided and
Acts of Parliament that have been passed
dealing with particular matters. These
are books to which any lawyer, whether
barrister or solicitor, will go first for the
general principles of law on the question
he desires to look up. These books are
written by men who have spent probably
a lifetime specialising in these particular
subjects. They deal not only with Acts
of Parliament, but with cases decided
under those Acts, and I do say it is a
great disadvantage to all practising
lawyers not to have such books available
in Northern Ireland. That is particularly
so in the case of solicitors like myself
practising in provincial centres.
As regards the Bill generally, as far
as I can ascertain it has met with the
approval of the entire legal profession.
I think it will be a great advantage to
the public that they will now be in the
position of being able to put before the
courts all matters material to the issue
for the consideration of the court before
it is called upon to give its decision. The
courts will not in the future be prevented,
owing to certain technical difficulties,
from receiving certain classes of important
evidence. The House is indebted to the
right hon. Gentleman for the lucid and
satisfactory way he has introduced this
Measure.
Mr. ROBB: The case of the baptism
to which I referred is reported in Fourth
Carrington and Payne, the King against
Clapham. With reference to the application of the Rule regarding criminal
cases, the Bill was considered by a Committee of Judges in England, consisting
of Lord Justice Greer, Mr. Justice Roche,
Mr. Justice Langton, and Mr. Justice
Avory. Mr. Justice Avory took a large
share in the drafting of the Bill, and I
think that what would satisfy Mr. Justice
Avory on the criminal side ought to
commend itself to any ordinary lawyer.
With regard to the point about County
Courts, under the County Courts (Ireland)
Act, 1877, the Rules applicable to a
County Court can be applied, or do apply,
to both Quarter Sessions and ordinary
County Courts.
Mr. LESLIE: That is in the Act itself?
Mr. ROBB: Yes. I have not got it
here, but I think my recollection that it
applies to both is correct.

The following question stood on the
Order Paper in the name of Captain
Perceval-Maxwell:-
To ask the Leader of the House
whether the Government will use their
influence with all employers of labour
to ensure that no man who volunteers
to serve in the Military or Air Force
Units now being raised in Northern
Ireland will by so doing suffer any
disadvantage in employment.
.
Captain PERCEVAL MAXWELL:
When I put down this question I had
anticipated that a debate which is now
proceeding in the Imperial Parliament
would have been concluded. I understand that that is not so. This question,
of course, does not relate to any particular legislation that is going through
the Imperial Parliament. It is clearly a
matter within the ambit of Our own
Government, but its urgency has arisen
by reason of this legislation which is
going on in England, and until I know
the exact result of that legislation I am
not prepared to press this matter any
further. Therefore, I do not propose
to put the question this afternoon, but
I intend to raise. the matter later on
if it is not dealt with in another place.
ELECTRICITY SUPPLY.
The following question stood on the
Order Paper in the name of Major
Dobbs :-
To ask the Leader of the House
whether he can state. the number and
names of the various electricity supply
and electricity distribution companies
operating in Northern Ireland; whether
these companies are under the supervision of the Ministry of Commerce;
whether he can state the causes of the
breakdowns in the supply of electric
current at Christmas time, 1938, in
County Antrim; and what steps have
been taken to prevent a recurrence of
these breakdowns; whether the Ministry of Commerce takes periodic inspections of the voltage of supply in
various areas; and whether any steps
are being taken to correlate the various
electricity power stations in Northern
Ireland, so that in the event of damage
to any electricity power station, a
supply of electricity to the area affected
may be distributed with the least
possible delay from other
stations.
power
Major DOBBS: The importance of
electricity for factories and mills has
long been recognised, but I do not
think any of us really realise how very
important electricity is becoming in our
home life, in the homes of the poor as
well as in the homes of the rich, and in
ordinary every day matters. Since we
have had the grid system going it is used
in heating, cooking, cooling food in our
small refrigerators, in the dairy, in milking cows, in incubators and brooders, and
we even plug into the electric current
with our wireless. It is used for such
Oral Answers
Oral Answers
11 MAY 1939
opposite extremes as the operating
theatres in our hospitals and our roadside petrol pumps. I am sure we are
all very thankful that accidents from
electricity are now very rare owing to
the skill and attention to details paid
by manufacturers of electrical equipment.
I would like to point out, because I
do not think it is generally recognised,
the great hardship that is entailed when
there is a black-out, a breakdown in
the supply of electricity. It is not only
a hardship, but it is a matter of very
great annoyance. An up-to-date farmer
who lives near me a year or so ago lost,
owing to a breakdown in the supply of
electricity, between £10 and £20 worth
of young chicks in a brooder. Now,
instead of having the convenience and
simplicity of electric current to heat his
brooders he has had to go back to the
old-fashioned oil lamp. This is due
entirely to the breakdown in the
electricity supply.
With regard to the breakdown last
Christmas, there was the greatest inconvenience caused to many users. Various
homes suffered inconvenience, because
they were unable to cook the breakfasts
and so on. One farmer-and I expect
there were many others in the same
position-had the greatest difficulty in
getting his cows milked that day, because he milks by electricity, and the
breakdown of the supply prevented him
doing what he had been accustomed to
do. I understand that in one telephone
exchange the operators were obliged to
use matches to see the dials on their
instruments. To my lay mind, knowing
something about what is done in the
United States and Switzerland, these
breakdowns do seem to occur rather
more often than there is any need for.
With regard to the last part of the
question, the linking up of the various
electrical power stations, that suggestion
is probably not very important in time
of peace, because an ordinary breakdown
should not take very long to rectify, but
in time of war, when you might get the
whole of one power station completely
destroyed, you might save a great deal
of money and be able to keep things
going if the different supply companies.
were linked up. I do not for a moment
think that if the Belfast Power Station
was destroyed any of the small power
stations outside could do very much, but
if steps are taken beforehand, at any
rate small subsidiary supply stations
served by the Belfast Power Station
could be supplied by other power stations,
which would alleviate the damage done.
Mr. ROBB (Leader of the House):
Excluding a number of small nonstatutory undertakings there are seven
electricity companies with statutory
powers carrying on business in Northern
Ireland, namely:-
Antrim Electricity Supply Company,
Distribution
Antrim Electricity
Company, Limited.
Antrim Light & Power Company,
Limited.
Ballycastle Electric Light & Power
Company, Limited.
Glenarm and District Electricity
Supply Company, Limited.
Larne Electric Light & Power
Company, Limited.
Leithwood, Limited.
a
There are also four local authority
undertakings at Belfast, Londonderry,
Carrickfergus and Portstewart, respectively, as well as the undertaking
of the Northern Ireland Electricity
Board. All these undertakings operate
under statutory powers involving
certain measure of general control by
the Ministry of Commerce. For example,
the prior consent of the Ministry is
necessary to the construction of new overhead lines, and undertakers are under
obligation to render certain returns and
accounts to the Ministry. The Ministry
is not, however, entrusted with the task
of making periodic inspections or of
taking readings of the voltage of supply.
I am informed by the Antrim Electricity Supply Company that the breakdown
which occurred in County Antrim at
Christmas time arose through exceptional
weather conditions. A severe frost combined with the existence of fog and the
deposit of salt on certain insulators resulted in flashovers and, in some cases,
the breakdown of the insulators. Furthermore, the frost and fog, and later the
snow rendered difficult the patrolling of
the line, a circumstance which delayed the
carrying out of the necessary repair work.
Housing Bill—
Committee
THE SENATE
[Mr Robb]
I am assured, however, that the company
is taking every practicable step to prevent
a recurrence of breakdowns of supply.
As regards the correlation of the
electricity power stations, Belfast and
Londonderry are already linked by main
transmission lines and the question of a
more extended series of links between
existing systems is receiving attention.
The hon. and gallant Member will appreciate that the linking-up of the Larne
Generating Station with that at Belfast
would entail considerable capital expenditure of a non-revenue-earning nature.

CLAUSES 1 (Provision for payment
of grants to local authorities constructing
working-class houses), 2 (Submission of
re-housing schemes by local authorities),
3 (Provision of temporary accommodation
for persons displaced), 4 (Limit on
amount of rent of certain working-class
houses) ordered to stand part of the Bill.
CLAUSE 5 (Exemption of certain
working-class houses from rates).
A local authority shall [until Parliament otherwise determines] exempt
from payment of rates leviable by that
authority all houses in respect of which
a grant has been made to the authority
under section one of this Act:
Provided that the said exemption
shall not apply to any item of poor rate
raised to meet a charge in connection
with compensation for criminal injuries.
Mr. LESLIE: I beg to move, in Clause
5, to leave out the words "until Parliament otherwise determines," and to insert instead "throughout the period
ending on the thirty-first day of March,
nineteen hundred and fifty." The words
"until Parliament otherwise determines "
of course are meant to indicate that it is
not really the intention of Parliament
that the circumstances introduced by the
Clause should endure for ever, but in
practice I am going to suggest to the
Committee that you might just as well
say that you are postponing the bringing
of these houses under rate until the Greek
Kalends. I cannot conceive that Parliament will ever interpose to make houses
which were provided more or less at the
public expense for a particular class of
tenant rateable when they are not rateable from the commencement. I think
that, on the whole, it would be far better
to substitute a fixed term for relief from
rates, as it is apparently intended there
should be some relief given from rates.
Hitherto we have allowed premises to
be exempt from rates for different periods.
sometimes five years and sometimes ten.
We have never, so far as I know, gone
beyond that, and I do not think we ought
to go beyond a ten years' limit now. I am
assuming, of course, that leaving the
words as they stand the limit would exceed ten years, because I do not think
that Parliament would ever interfere.
There would be too much oppositionpartly from the people themselves and
partly from people claiming to speak on
their behalf. These houses, of course,
will become ten years old at different
times. That is undoubtedly so, but I
suggest that the local authorities ought
to know the length of their burden. They
should know definitely on what date they
may expect to receive rates in respect in
these houses.
Again, as regards the people who
occupy the houses, we know that tenancies
do not endure for ever. It may very
well be that the first occupants of houses
may depart from those houses after a
very short term of residence. The tenants
themselves are not necessarily the most
desirable of tenants, but that has really,
I think, nothing to do with the question.
But something that has to do with the
question is the objection to creating in
any municipal district a separate class of
house; that is the house which pays no
rates but yet confers on its occupier the
right of voting at the local municipal
elections. That is to say, you are creating for an indefinite period a class of
persons who will take their part in voting
for members of the local body but will
not bear any share of the expenditure
imposed by that local body. That is not
sound. It may be advisable for a very
limited period, but it is incumbent on
Parliament to limit that period definitely
and strictly.
Evidence Bill-
Committee
11 MAY 1939
There are other objections to keeping
houses available which are relieved of
rates, because there may be some competition for houses of that class and some
temptation to promote the applications of
particular tenants. That, I hope, will
not happen. We can justify that the
exemption from rates is to endure for
a period up to March 31, 1950, which
will be a full ten years after the last
of these houses has been erected, but I
do not think we can properly go beyond
that. Accordingly, I beg to move the
amendment on the Paper.
