The real crux of the situation comes
Does this temporary accommodation amount to a house for which a grant
is to be made under Clause 1. We have
got Clause 1 before us, and in sub-clause

in the exercise of its powers provides
houses for persons displaced or to be displaced," etcetera, and it seems to me that
it is while a person is going to be displaced
that he is going to occupy one of these
temporary buildings. Again, we say in

in calculating the cost of a house in respect
of which a grant is going to be paid the
cost of providing temporary accommodation is to be taken into account, and is to
be pooled amongst the houses.
On the Bill as it comes before us it
seems to me that it is the intention of
Parliament that rates should not be paid
on the temporary accommodation, but I
think it is necessary to provide that that
exemption should not continue until
Parliament otherwise determines, but
should cease when the temporary accommodation ceases to be used for the purposes of the Act. That is the difficulty,
or rather the collection of small difficulties
that I am bringing before the Committee
and it seemed to me that the addition of
the words I have put on the Paper would
meet the case. These words would enable
the rates on this temporary accommodation to be foregone so long as the temporary accommodation was being used for
the purpose for which it is provided but
no longer, and, accordingly, I beg to move
the Amendment.
Mr. ROBB: On the view I have formed
of the interpretation of this Bill, these.
houses for temporary accommodation are
rateable. The exemption from rates is
only in respect of new houses built by
grant under Clause 1. If that is the
correct interpretation, and I think it is,
I think the hon. and learned Member
will admit that his amendment is unnecessary. It certainly was not the
intention that temporary accommodation,
if rateable, should during its occupation
become derateable. For this reason the
Government cannot accept the amendment.
Mr. LESLIE: That amounts to such a
pronouncement as we can make here. of
the intention of the Bill. It is in effect
that the Government does propose that
this temporary accommodation, while
occupied by a person displaced from his
existing house and waiting to get into
a new rate-free house, has to pay rates on
this temporary accommodation.
Mr. ROBB: Someone has to pay the
rates.
Mr. LESLIE: And presumably it is
the occupier. The local authority can
consider that when dealing with the question of the rent to be imposed. Now
that we have got a clear definition I think
I may ask leave to withdraw the amendment. It has served its
purpose completely, because we have a definite pronouncement as to what the Bill is intended to meet, and I hope it will be
correct, if it is ever challenged.
Amendment, by leave, withdrawn.

CLAUSE 20 (Residence qualification
for tenants of certain working-class
houses).
as
Mr. LESLIE: I beg to move, to leave
out Clause 20. This Clause, which I am
going to invite the Committee to take
out, is, I should think, about
Draconic a Clause as has ever come before us.
It pronounces quite definitely
that a local authority shall not ever,
under any circumstances at all, accept
any person as tenant of a house provided
under any of the Housing Acts or the
Labourers Acts, or any enactments
amending those Acts, unless that person
has been seven years' resident in the
United Kingdom or was born in Northern Ireland. I am going to suggest
that that is a most Draconic Clause, and
altogether unreasonable and unnecessary.
I say it is a very distinct infringement
of the discretion of the local authorities.
It is for the local authority to say what
tenants are to occupy these houses, to
the cost of which the ratepayers have so
very materially contributed. It is not
for the Government which, in a house
costing £450, has put up the relatively
small sum of £150, to lay down a hard
and fast rule which the local authority
is not to be permitted to disregard.
The proposal is not limited to a house
built under this Bill. It is to comprise
all houses built under
any of the
labourers or working-class dwellings Bills
that we have ever passed, and not only
Bills that we have passed, but Bills
passed by the Parliament of the United
Kingdom before we took over Northern
Ireland. Some of those houses were
built out of what for the moment I will
call United Kingdom funds, money
which was provided from the United
Kingdom generally, including at that
time the whole of Ireland, and yet it is
now proposed to cut out certain people
who were part of that body from the
right to be accepted as tenants of these
houses.
Suppose it should happen, as it does
happen, that one of these cottages is

houses, however

been seven years in the United Kingdom.

CLAUSE 21 (Provision as to fair
wages clause in contracts for erection of
working-class houses.)
It shall be the duty of a local
authority by whom any house is
erected under the Housing Acts or the
Labourers Acts, or under any enactment for the time being in force
amending any of the said Acts,
whether with or without financial
assistance from the Government, to
secure-
(a) that a fair wages clause
complying with the requirements
of any resolution (passed by the
House of Commons of the United
Kingdom or the House of Commons
of Northern Ireland) for the time
being in force and applicable to
contracts with departments of the
Government of Northern Ireland, is
inserted in all contracts for the
erection of the house; and
(b) except in so far as the Ministry may, in any particular case,
dispense with the observance of
this paragraph, that all contracts
for the erection of the house are
submitted to the Ministry for its
approval.

first line of the Clause the words

or any
other person or persons." The National
Federation of Building Trade Operatives
have naturally been examining this Bill
very carefully, and they are very anxious
that all possible steps should be taken to
prevent any evasion of this Fair Wages
Clause being inserted in all contracts. The
members of this union have approached
me, and they think it would be more satisfactory if this Amendment was inserted in
the Clause, because these houses may be
built by persons other than local authorities or building associations, and it is
desirable that the fair wages Clause
should be equally applicable to such
persons.
Mr. ROBB: There are four Amendments down in the name of the hon.
Senator. The Government are prepared
to accept the second and third Amendments, the fourth is unnecessary, and the
Government cannot accept the first one,
which has just been moved. The reason
is this. While they are quite willing to
bind local authorities and housing associations who receive a grant to insert in contracts a Fair Wages Clause, they are not
prepared to say that private individuals
should be bound in the way this Amendment seeks to bind them. Where a person
undertakes to repair a house it might be
quite a simple job. It might be done by
the man's own labourer and the endeavour
will be to have the repair and reconditioning of houses done as quickly and
as cheaply as possible. As I say, while
the Government are willing to bind local
authorities and housing associations, they
are not prepared to fetter private individuals in regard to the contract he may
make about wages.

CLAUSE 5 (Rules of Court as to proof
by affidavit).
Rules of court may provide for orders
being made at any stage of any proceedings directing that specific facts
may be proved at the trial by affidavit
with or without the attendance of the
deponent for cross-examination, notwithstanding that a party desires the
attendance of the deponent for crossexamination and that he can be produced for that purpose.

beg to move, "That Standing Order No.
XXV. be suspended for this day's

Second Reading of the Matrimonial
Causes Bill."
Mr. LESLIE: I hope this is not going
to become a habit. There have been
signs of late that this practice of taking
the First and Second Reading together
is becoming rather too common, and I
should not myself have thought that this
particular Measure is a very suitable one
for exercising this particular procedure.
I do not myself propose to object, but if
any other Member does complain that
he has not had sufficient time I should
feel bound to support him. It ought to
be remembered that a full half of this
House at present has other occupations
of a Parliamentary nature, and it is
really very difficult to find time to
examine a Measure of this complicated
description in the short space of time
elapsing between a Friday and a Tuesday
afternoon.

place and has been in print for a considerable length of time, and it has, in

cannot remember the exact date on which

ago, and I have been able to read it

Question to Minister.
VOLUNTEERS AND EMPLOYMENT
The following question stood on the
Order Paper in the name of Captain
Perceval-Maxwell:-
House
To ask the Leader of the
whether the Government will use their
influence with all employers of labour
to ensure that no man who volunteers
to serve in the Military or Air Force
units now being raised in Northern
Ireland will by so doing suffer any
disadvantage in employment.
-
Captain PERCEVAL MAXWELL:
I am conscious of the fact that in asking
this question I am treading on delicate.
ground, because although the situation
which is visualised in this question arose
through forces which are compelling the
increase of military forces all over the
world, undoubtedly it has been brought
to a head by certain legislation which is
unmentionable in this House, but which
has been passed in another place. In
the course of the discussion about the
Bill which has been going through
another place the Prime Minister of
Northern Ireland paid a visit to England,
and when he came back he expressed the
hope, if I read his statement correctly,
that nothing that had happened in
another place would make any difference
to recruiting in this country. That is a
hope with which I am sure the majority
of the Members of this House will
concur.
But there are underlying considerations which, although they are connected
with legislation in another place, have
a very important bearing on the internal
economics of this country. I hope I shall
say nothing out of order if I say that I
am not concerned with the question of
That is a
conscription in any way.
military matter decided by the Parliament of the United Kingdom, but I am
concerned very much with the effect that
it may have on the internal economics of
Northern Ireland. If the military forces
are called up for any prolonged time a
situation may result as unhappily
resulted in the last war where those who
were loyal to the connection of Ireland
to Great Britain, and many who disapproved of that connection, joined up to
fight what they considered to be a
common danger.
I have in mind the fact that many of
those who joined up in the Irish Regiments were men whose political and
religious views were different from my
own, and I had the pleasure and privilege of serving beside them, and no one
could want better comrades in arms. But
there are, unfortunately, a number of
persons in this country, as in England,
who for various reasons prefer to remain
at home when they could join the military forces, but who are in a position to
take the places of those men who join
the military forces of the Crown. It is
not fair that those men who join the
military forces of the Crown should lose
their rights and endanger the welfare of
their dependents as well as their own
welfare when they return to civil life
without something being done to help
in this matter.
Moreover, there are in this country a
number of Reservists who, in common
with Reservists in England, are liable to
be called up to join the Colours for a
period of military training, and I imagine
that the training will last for six months.
at a time. Legislation has been passed
in another place which will ensure that
those persons who join up as Reservists
will obtain stamps for their insurance
159 Volunteers and Employment
THE SENATE

Matrimonial Causes Bil.
[Captain Perceval-Maxwell]
cards, and they will be protected under
heavy penalties from losing their employment. This question has been
drafted rather widely on purpose,
because in England the situation is rather
different to the situation in this country.
In England young men are being compelled to join up, and therefore it may be
argued that employers should be compelled to re-employ
In this
re-employ them.
country they are not being compelled to
join up, but are being encouraged to do
so by the Prime Minister and the Members of the Government collectively.
Therefore I think it is right that the
Government should take whatever steps
may be possible and necessary, including
the promotion of legislation if it cannot
be done by any other means, to ensure
that the men who join up will not be
prejudiced to their disadvantage in their
employment, and that their dependents
will be preserved from want.
Mr. ROBB: I am very glad that the

show their sense of public duty in this
matter.

amendment. They have passed the
Matrimonial Causes Bill and the

Margarine Bill.
Resolved, "That this Bill be now read
a First time." (Mr. Long.)
Ordered, That the Bill be read a
Second time on Tuesday, 6th June.
Matrimonial Causes Bill.
Resolved, "That this Bill be now read
a First time."-(Mr. Long.)
new
Mr. ROBB: I beg to move the Second
Reading of this Matrimonial Causes Bill.
The objects of the Bill are pretty clearly
stated in the memorandum on the face
of the Bill. The object is two-fold—
firstly to establish in Northern Ireland,
as has been established in England for
many years, that divorce shall for the
future be granted not as heretofore by
Act of Parliament but by a decree of the
courts. I may point out that this Bill
introduces practically nothing
except as regards matters of procedure.
Divorce has been known in the United
Kingdom for a great many years. For
a long time in the early ages the dissolution of a marriage was committed solely
to the ecclesiastical courts. These
ecclesiastical courts originally declared
marriages to be null and void upon the
grounds that were sometimes obscure, but
always went upon some fiction of the im- .
possibility of those who contracted the
marriage contracting such a marriage...
But gradually the ecclesiastical courts
moved away from that, and they granted
what was called a divorce a mensa et
toro-a separation of the parties during
their lives, no longer compelling them to
fulfil the obligations they had under-
161 Matrimonial Causes Bill-

24 MAY 1939
First Reading
taken, enabling them to live apart, but
not enabling either of them to marry
again during the life of the other.
Should either of them wish to marry it
was necessary to promote a Bill in
Parliament.
These Bills, few at first,
increased in numbers, until in the early
part of the 19th century they were fairly
common, but they had the one great disadvantage that they were very expensive.
They involved three steps-always two
steps and sometimes three steps. There
So
had to be an action brought in the civil
courts by one of the spouses against the
other, and if the spouse bringing the
action were the husband he had to bring
a second action, an action for criminal
conversation against the adulterer.
that you had, in fact, two preliminary
trials in one case, but always one preliminary trial, and then the matter came
before the Houses of Parliament, and
there in the. Houses of Parliamentthe cause was in fact tried by the law
lords. There the case had to be gone
through all again. There had again to
be witnesses called. The matters in controversy, if they were in controversy,
had to be proved, and all that meant
piling up expense.
Now in England in 1857 the matter
was simplified, because jurisdiction was
given to the courts to pronounce what
they have always been able to pronounce,
a judicial separation, but enabling them
also to pronounce a decree of divorce, as
it
was called a vinculo matrimonii,
which enabled the spouses to marry
again. When that legislation was passed
in Westminster it was in contemplation
that a similar Bill should be passed extending that jurisdiction to the Irish
Courts, but that was never done. When
this Parliament was set up there was in
force the old system whereby the courts
could pronounce a decree of divorce a
mensa et toro, amounting in effect to
a judicial separation. But if either of
the spouses wished to go a step further
and be freed from the bond of matrimony and enabled to marry again, it
was necessary to go before Parliament.
That was a very complicated and very
expensive procedure, and accordingly
this Bill is in the first place designed
not to introduce anything new into our
law in the sense of establishing the
principle of divorce. at all, but to simplify
the procedure and to commit to the
courts the power, or to confer upon them
the power of pronouncing a
pronouncing a decree a
vinculo matrimonii, which shall enable
either of the spouses to marry again.
That is the first part of this Bill. It
proposes to enact that for the future the
jurisdiction in divorce shall be conferred
upon the courts. Now in theory
Parliament, being able to do anything,
was not bound with regard to the.
grounds upon which it should act in
passing an Act of divorce. But when
Parliament delegates to a court powers
in this matter it is right and proper that
Parliament should lay down strictly the
events in which, the circumstances under
which, and the conditions on which a
decree of divorce should be made or may
be made. Accordingly, the Bill goes on
not merely to confer jurisdiction, but to
give the conditions under which jurisdiction shall be exercised, and accordingly,
after the first Clause conferring jurisdiction, we have Clauses setting out the
grounds upon which the jurisdiction
shall be exercised.
I may say with regard to these
grounds that they have had full consideration. A Royal Commission investigated this whole subject of divorce and
reported in the year 1912, and the causes
of divorce which are set out in this Bill
are the causes of divorce which that
Commission recommended should be
made grounds of divorce in England,
and they are the grounds of divorce, which
have been incorporated in the Act
recently passed at Westminster governing the procedure of the courts in
England. The details, I think, are
matters for Committee and not matters
for Second Reading, and I do not propose to enter into them here. There are
further Clauses in the Bill dealing with
other matters such as the custody of
children, the marriage settlement, the
payment of alimony, and they also seem
to me matters for Committee if they are
indeed in any way in dispute.
But upon the main principle of the
Bill, that is, the establishment of jurisdiction in the courts for the granting of
a full decree of divorce and the grounds
upon which that decree shall be made.,
these, I think, are the main principles
of the Bill and the things to be considered upon the Second Reading, as the
Bill is primarily designed to lessen the
expense of those
persons who wish to

Mr. JOSEPH CUNNINGHAM: I
would like to support the Second Reading
of this Bill. For many years I have
followed the controversy in the Press and
also in Northern Ireland on this matter,
and I really always thought it a very
great hardship that two young people,
who probably made a mistake early in
life by associating or being married to
one another, if they found the match unsuitable, should be condemned to live in
misery, and that there was no escape
from it. I certainly came to the conclusion that something should be done
to make it easier for these people to get
into a comfortable state of living and to
relieve them of the condition they contracted when they were probably young
and quite innocent. There is something
more so far as the working classes are
concerned. It was always looked upon
as a very costly thing to obtain a divorce
of the kind that was in operation, and
by this legislation it is made a little
easier for these people in such an unhappy plight to rectify the position and
obtain a divorce.
Furthermore, I would like to say to
the Member who last spoke that this
Bill does not require a, man or woman
to take a divorce. It places them in the
position of relieving themselves of an
unhappy position of which I have
spoken.
or
I do say that it is more Christianlike.
to enable a man and woman to rectify
the position than to live a life of sin
and misery for the rest of their days,
whether they be Roman Catholic
Protestant. In early life I remember
working alongside a man who belonged
to the religion my hon. Friend belongs
to. That man was brought up in a respectable family. He was a respectable
man himself, but in his early days he
had unfortunately become connected
with a woman.
That man, to my
knowledge, lived in a most miserable
state for the. whole of his life. He was
such a good-natured and good-humoured
individual he often joked about his unfortunate position. It is only fair that
there should be legislation at the disposal
of a man like that, so that he might try
if possible to improve his position and
get rid of the misery he had previously
gone through. That is one of the
reasons why I whole-heartedly support
this Bill. It is a good Bill, and I am
satisfied there are a considerable number
of people in Northern Ireland who are
only waiting for this Measure to be
passed, knowing that there is a possibility
of improvement in their conditions of
life. Therefore, I have much pleasure.
in supporting the Second Reading.
Mr. LESLIE: I venture to think that
on previous occasions, when matters relating to this subject have been under
discussion I have made my position clear.
I concur absolutely with what was said
by the Senator in front of me as regards
the absolute indissolubility of marriage
contracted under Christian auspices in
the proper manner. I adhere to the
opinion that no terrestrial court can
dissolve that marriage, but those are
matters which I am bound to say are
matters for the personal conscience of
the individual. Although I hold myself that marriage cannot be dissolved,
Matrimonial Causes Bill-

24 MAY 1939
I
I do not claim the right to dictate to

my own personal views, and, therefore,
I do not think it is right to debar other
people from taking advantage of such
liberty as the law of their country may
afford them. I do not agree with the

before us introduces nothing new.
cannot admit that matters introduced

Consequently, I shall not oppose the

am referring, of course, to the liberty of

a case in a class by itself; in fact, it is

I welcome the Bill on the whole,
because it is, as I think, the right hon.
Gentleman stated necessary, almost
certainly desirable, that if the thing is
to be allowed at all the grounds on which
it is to be allowed should be prescribed
by Parliament and not left-I was going
to say to the whim of a Parliamentary
Committee, but I ought to say, perhaps,
to the varying chances of a Parliamentary
Committee. As you will remember, it
was pointed out in those days there was
no certainty that a Parliamentary Committee would ever decide or be able to
decide on the continuously consistent
course, of action. If you have a Parliamentary Committee to-day it may be displaced to-morrow, and the opinions of
the first Committee may give place to
new opinions. There is no end to it.
It is surely more, desirable that Parliament should definitely prescribe the
grounds on which divorce, so-called, is
to be granted, and, therefore, the only
method we can follow is to take advantage of the powers given to us in the
Act of 1932 of conferring new power or
new jurisdiction on our own courts.
But
I think that power of conferring a
jurisdiction is limited to that, and we
should abstain from defining the duty of
the court. That, however, is a matter
of small importance at the moment.
I think this Bill must have been a
very troublesome, and very difficult Bill
to draft. I received my copy on
Friday evening last, and I found that
the time between Friday evening and
Tuesday morning was insufficient for me
to refresh my memory and to trace the
wording of the Bill back to the various
sources of origin. Hon. Members who
may have been able to move more quickly
than myself, or had more easy access to
the Library of the House, will have discovered for themselves that a great part
of this Bill is founded on a Statute
passed so long ago at Westminster as
1857, and they will have to trace it
through the Supreme Court of Judicature Consolidation Act, 1925, and Mr.
Herbert's Act of 1937 down to the
present day. I think they will have.
found the task not altogether easy, but
I do think that it is necessary that the
task should be undertaken if the proposals now before. us are to be understood.
Although I do not propose to deal this
afternoon with detail, I cannot refrain
from pointing out that reference to those
more or less musty Statutes to which
I have referred has convinced me that
as we are setting out or asking our courts
to set out on some new venture, it is
rather desirable that we should, at Committee stage, spend a little time in
examining whether there are not some
items of our inheritance, so to speak,
which we might well discard. The year
1857, is a long time ago. Some of these
matters I will draw attention to later on.
These matters, I am now speaking of
are all new matters. I shall, in due
course, direct attention to words and
phrases which, as I say, have come down
from 1857. In some cases some of these
have become almost meaningless if not
occasionally misleading. These. are
matters that we ought, I think, to consider when we are dealing with a very
important subject.
The only point I want to say a word

for
CLAUSE 20 (Residence qualification
tenants of certain working-class
houses).
A local authority shall not after the
passing of this Act accept any person
as tenant of any house provided by
the authority under the Housing Acts
or the Labourers Acts, or under any
enactment for the time being in force
amending any of the said Acts, unless
such person-
(a) has, during the whole of the period.
of seven years ending on the date
of the letting, been resident in
the United Kingdom; or
(b) was born at some place which is
situate within Northern Ireland;
Provided that this section shall not
apply in the case of a person who, in
consequence of the carrying out of any
scheme under the Housing Acts, is
dispossessed of a house in which he was
residing at the date of the passing of
this Act and for whom no alternative
accommodation is available.
Mr. LESLIE: I beg to move, at the
end of the Clause to add: "or in the
case of a person to whom the provisions
of paragraph (a), (c), or (d) of subsection (1) of section sixteen of this Act
apply."
This Amendment is an attempt on my
part to mitigate in certain possible cases
what I believe to be unintentional hardships which might be inflicted by Parliament as a consequence of the adoption of
Clause 20. It may be remembered that
when speaking on Clause 20 I described
it as a very Draconic Clause, because it
absolutely prevents a local authority from
accepting as a tenant a person who does
not satisfy the prescribed conditions of
having resided for seven years in the
United Kingdom or having been born in
Northern Ireland. That Clause, I may
remind the House, is one of the Clauses
in the Bill which will be necessarily
operative. Many of the beneficial
Clauses of the Bill will not operate unless they are set in motion by the local
authorities. We hope the local authorities will operate the Bill, but that is by
the way for the moment.
In consequence of Clause 20 as it
stands a local authority cannot accept
as a tenant a widow of the previous
occupant of the cottage if she herself has
not resided seven years in the United
Kingdom or been born in Northern Ireland. Consequently the result will be that
the woman, in addition to losing her husband, must necessarily lose her house.
There does not seem to be a loophole
left because the Clause is absolutely
mandatory that the local authority may
not accept this woman, if there is such
a woman, as a tenant. There is no continuity of tenancy in houses of this
when
description, and
the
tenancy
passes, as it frequently does, from father
to son or from husband to wife, a new
agreement is usually signed. At all
events, it is from the new tenant the rent
is collected. It may happen under this
Clause 20 that a widow may have to be
put out of her house. She is the person
dealt with in paragraph (a) of Clause
16 to which I referred. The other classes
of people are those referred to in paragraphs (c) and (d), still referring back to
Clause 16.
All that I am asking for these people
is that they shall be placed in no worse
position than they would be if they
were displaced in consequence of a rehousing scheme under Part I of the Bill.
I venture to think that this is a reasonable proposal and I hope it will commend itself to the House. There is, I
repeat, no obligation on the local
authority to accept these people as
tenants. All I ask is that
we shall
refrain from saying precisely and
definitely that they shall not accept them
as tenants. I think, as I have said, it
is a reasonable proposal, and I move
accordingly.
Mr. ROBB: I regret that I cannot
accept this Amendment. As the hon..
Senators will remember, the principle
underlying Clause 20 which it is now
proposed to amend, was fully debated on
the Committee stage of the Bill. It was
then decided on a motion to leave out
the Clause, that the Clause should stand
part of the Bill, and the House may,
Housing Bill-
Report of Amendments
THE SENATE
[Mr. Robb]
therefore, be deemed to have approved
of the principle of applying a residence
qualification. In this Amendment it is
now proposed to limit the application of
this principle by exempting certain
classes of persons-in addition to those
mentioned in the proviso-from the provisions of the Clause. The Government
has, as I stated, on the Committee stage
of the Bill, given this Clause special
consideration, and it does not feel able
to make any further relaxation. There
is, moreover, the difficulty that if any
relaxation is to be permitted, it must
be granted in accordance with some logical principle, and a suitable principle is
not easy to find. Thus the proposed
Amendment would have the effect of
exempting certain classes of nonagricultural labourers from the Clause,
whereas the Clause would still apply to
genuine agricultural labourers however
unsatisfactory their housing conditions.
Mr. J. G. Leslie
Although the cases the hon. and learned
Senator has suggested are, of course,
cases of hardship, the Government does
not feel able to limit the application of
the Clause further than is done in the
Clause itself.
Mr. LESLIE: The possible opening of
the door which my Amendment would
allow is so very narrow that I do not
think it could seriously interfere with
the general proposal of the Government.
However, I cannot for myself be a party
to the possible infliction of such a hardship as that I have mentioned, namely, the
exclusion of an unfortunate widow from
her house because she has not been seven
years in Northern Ireland. I am afraid
I must ask the House to express its
opinion.

Unemployment Insurance (Increase of
Benefits and Reduction in Contributions) (Agriculture) Order, 1939.
Mr. ROBB: I beg to move, "That the
Order in Council made by the Governor
of Northern Ireland on 30th March,
1939, under Section 65 of the Unemployment Insurance Act (Northern
Ireland), 1936, for increasing the weekly
rates of Agricultural Benefit, the rate
payable for a dependent adult and the
maximum amount of Agricultural Benefit
payable weekly to any one claimant, and
for reducing the weekly rates of agricultural Insurance Contributions payable by
persons over the age of 18 years and their
employers, be hereby approved."
Under Section 65 of the Unemployment Insurance Act (Northern Ireland),
1936,
His Grace the Governor may
apply to Northern Ireland by Order in
Council the provisions of any Order made
by the Minister of Labour for Great
Britain on the recommendation of the
Unemployment Insurance Statutory
Committee to amend the Unemployment
Insurance Acts in force there.
The Unemployment Insurance Statutory Committee in its annual report on
the financial condition of the Unemployment Fund of Great Britain as at the
31st December, 1938, found that the
agricultural account of the Unemployment Fund was, and was likely to continue to be, more than reasonably
sufficient to discharge its liabilities. To
dispose of the accumulated surplus and
to prevent a further accumulation the
Committee recommended-(a) increases
varying from 6d to 1s 6d a week in all
rates of agricultural benefit payable; for
example, the rate for a man, aged 21
and over, is to be increased from 14s to
15s a week; (b) an increase from 7s to
9s in the weekly rate. of benefit for an
adult dependant; (c) an increase from
30s to 33s in the maximum weekly
amount of benefit payable to any one
claimant; (d) reductions of 1d per week
for the next three years in the rate of
agricultural unemployment insurance
contributions payable by workers over
the age of 18 years and by their employers.
The Committee also recommended
that the reduction in contributions
should take effect from 3rd July, 1939,
and that the other changes should be
brought into operation if possible on the
30th March, 1939. With the approval
of Parliament, an Order giving effect to
these recommendations was made by the
Minister of Labour in Great Britain on
the 29th March, 1939, and the provisions
of that Order were made applicable to
Northern Ireland by the Order in
Council which is the subject of the
Motion now before the House.
accordance with the provisions of the
Unemployment Insurance Act (Northern
Ireland), 1936, the Order will cease to
have effect after twelve months unless,
before the expiration of that time, it is
approved by Parliament.
In

Question to Minister.
THE HIRE-PURCHASE SYSTEM.
The following question stood on the
Order Paper in the name of Mr.
Barclay :-
To ask the Leader of the House
whether the Government will consider
the introduction of legislation to
control the hire-purchase system, and
to give adequate protection against
unfair treatment to those that avail
themselves of this system.
Mr. BARCLAY: My reason for
putting down this question is to find out.
if something can be done by way of protection to working-class people, and
particularly young married couples, who
use the hire-purchase system. As hon.
Members are aware, these people have
not got large banking accounts to draw
upon when they get married, and they do
business with dealers engaged in the hirepurchase system who in some cases are
very friendly towards them, but in others
try to get as much out of the workingclass people as they possibly can. Last
year a Bill dealing with this subject was
brought before the Imperial House of
Commons. It was
a Private Member's
Measure, and after receiving its First
and Second Reading it was amended in
Committee, and read the Third time without a Division. In the House of Lords it
passed through its First, Second, and
Third Readings without a Division,
which proves to me that the Members of
the Imperial Parliament realised the
justice of the Bill, and the necessity for
doing something to protect the working
classes against the abuses of this particular form of trading.
A few weeks ago I had a conversation
with a lady who is in business in Ballymacarrett, and she told me of a young
couple she knew who got married and
partly furnished their house on the hirepurchase system. They got along pretty
well at first, the husband being in work,
and they were able to keep up the weekly
instalments for a time. When the first
contract was almost completed the
furniture dealer came along and invited
them to enter into a second contract.
He praised them for the way
they had kept up their instalments,
and they duly entered into the second
contract. When that was almost paid off
the husband unfortunately became out of
work. He was a shipyard worker, and
he was asked to stand down for a short
time. Senator Joseph Cunningham will
understand what I mean by standing
down. It means that when a ship is
finished and goes away to sea, if another
ship is not ready to take its place the
workers may have to wait a week, a fortnight, or a month. When this man was
asked to stand down he went to the
furniture dealer and told him his circumstances, and he said it would be all right.
But what happened? When this worker
was standing at the gate expecting every
hour to be called in to work, the furniture
dealer arrived at the home with his van,
and he took not only that which was not
The Hire-Purchase System 180
Question to Minister-
THE SENATE
[Mr. Barclay]
paid for, but that which was paid for.
When the working man came home that
night he found there was nothing left in
his house but the bed on which his wife
was lying with a young baby.
Working-class people want some protection against such a thing as that.
Under the English Act the powers of a
furniture dealer are limited in this respect. If a third of the price is paid
he cannot come and take the furniture
away if the purchaser defaults in his
payments, as he could previously. He
has to sue the purchaser before the county
court judge, who can either allow the
man to continue his payments when convenient or to retain furniture to the
value of his payments. As we have in
Belfast a very large working-class community, which doubtless does a great deal
of business by means of the hire-purchase
system, they should receive every protection. They should be kept informed
of how much they have to pay on a particular contract.
Under the Imperial Act, when a hirepurchase agreement is entered into the
dealer has to supply the buyer with a
list of the articles purchased, and the
amount he has to pay, while the purchaser has the right at any time to
demand from the hirer the amount of
the outstanding account. When the
purchaser has completed the first contract
the dealer is obliged to give him a clear
receipt. As far as I understand the law
as it is here, I have no personal experience of the hire-purchase system-a
dealer can add a second contract to the
first, and if the purchaser fails to keep
up his payments under the second agreement the dealer can seize the furniture
obtained under both agreements and clear
the house.
the
Mr. LONG: This question has been
under consideration by the Government,
and the present position is that
is that
Minister of Commerce is engaged in consulting trade and other organisations in
Northern Ireland as to the desirability
of introducing legislation on the lines of
that recently passed in Great Britain.
Mr. MCALLISTER: I rise to associate
myself in a few words with the object
of the question and the sentiments expressed by Senator Barclay. I do not
come
intend to deal with the merits or otherwise of the hire-purchase system. I am
sure it is very useful in a good many
cases, but there is a minority of harsh,
unscrupulous and unconscionable men
engaged in this business, and it is the
very poorest of the poor on whom they
prey. They
along with what
appears on the face of it to be the most
tempting offer of clothes and furniture
which the poor working man has not the
means of paying for at once. They get
him to sign a contract, and I have come
across several instances where the man
has fallen out of work and cannot continue to pay, whereupon the dealer sues
him in court and pursues him in every
possible way. While I cannot find words
strong enough to support Senator Barclay
in the views he has expressed, I should
be sorry to say that hire purchase as a
whole is not conducted
a proper
manner, but I do say there is a substantial minority who do abuse it, and it
is up to the Government to protect the
poor from this system of dealing by these
unscrupulous people.
in
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: I should like
to support what has been said on this
question. While there are many respectable firms engaged in this method of
trading there are also some of the biggest
blackguards unhung, and it is high time
that some action was taken against them.

Margarine Bill.
Order for Second Reading read.
Mr. LONG: Last year British manufacturers of margarine made application
for an Order in Council under the
Merchandise Marks Act, 1926, requiring
that imported margarine should be labelled
with an indication of its origin. The
application was favourably received and,
as from 1st November next, imported
margarine when retailed will have to bear
an indication of origin.
As the law now stands in Northern Ireland margarine for sale by retail must be
put up in a wrapper bearing the one word
"Margarine." No additional words may
appear on the wrapper. The purpose of
this Measure is to remove this restriction
so that the wrapper may bear an indication of origin as well as the word
"Margarine.' It is most desirable that
the law in Northern Ireland in regard to
commodities affected by the operation of
the Merchandise Marks Acts should be the
same as the law in Great Britain. Under
these Acts goods manufactured or produced in Northern Ireland are treated
exactly as are goods manufactured or produced elsewhere in the United Kingdom.
They are regarded as home produced, and
do not require to be marked in any way.
I beg to move.
Question proposed, "That the Bill be
now read a Second time."
Mr. LESLIE: This is a short Bill
and, as explained by the Parliamentary
Secretary, apparently not only a harmless but a very desirable Bill. But it so
happened that I read the Bill a day or
two ago, and I took the extreme step of
getting a packet of margarine to look at.
I found the margarine of a brand very
in
commonly sold in this country put up
a packet measuring 4 inches in
length. I cannot see how such a packet
is to bear the word "margarine" which
contains nine letters each to be a half
inch in length. That would require 4
inches. Since then I have seen in a shop
window another packet of margarine of
a different brand, and though I could
not measure it through the glass of the
window it was not 5 inches.
That put me to consider whether this
half-inch was length or width. You do
not describe a letter as being long. The
old phrase was square, and I believe the
expression "square" is used in relation
to letters on sundry boxes of eggs-the
hon. Member can inform me and I
think it is reasonable. But I do not
think it is reasonable to require nine
half-inch letters to be put on a surface
of 4 inches. Then we pass to margarine
cheese which contains 15 letters, and you
get into greater trouble. There are
other conditions as regards the outer
wrapper, and I am inclined to think that
on the whole we ought not to pass the
Bill this afternoon.
There is another point. The packets
I saw through the window both, I think,
contained a lot of printed matter beside
the mere word "margarine" of the Bill,
and it would be extremely difficult to
alter the Bill for the reasons the Parliamentary Secretary gave, as he wants to
keep on the lines of the Amendment
introduced into the Food and Drugs Act
last year. At the same time I do not
think we are entitled to require these
particular people to change their method.
of packing so as to have larger packets
in order to print this one word in halfinch letters.
I do not like to move that the Bill be
postponed, but perhaps it can be
arranged that the Committee stage shall
stand over for some time in order that
the Government may deal with this.
You really cannot prescribe 4 inches of
letters on a surface of 41 inches. I do
not oppose the Second Reading, but I
reserve action as to the Third Reading.
On Question, Motion agreed to.
Bill committed to a Committee of the
Whole House for Thursday next.

[Viscount BANGOR in the Chair.]
CLAUSE 1 (Extension of matrimonial
jurisdiction of the Supreme Court)
ordered to stand part of the Bill.
CLAUSE 2 (Grounds and procedure
for divorce).
A petition praying for the dissolution of a marriage (in this Act
referred to as a "divorce") may be
presented to the High Court (in this
Act referred to as "the court") either
by a husband or by a wife on the
ground that the respondent-
(a) has since the celebration of the
marriage committed adultery; or
(b) has deserted the petitioner
without reasonable excuse for a
period of at least three years immediately preceding the presentation.
of the petition; or
(c) has since the celebration of the
marriage treated the petitioner with
cruelty; or
(d) is incurably of unsound mind.
and has been continuously under
care and treatment for a period of
at least five years immediately preceding the
the presentation of the
petition;
and by a wife on the ground that her
husband has, since the celebration of
the marriage, been guilty of rape,
sodomy or bestiality.
The following Amendment stood on
the Order Paper in the name of Mr.
Leslie: In paragraph (b) leave out "without reasonable excuse.'
Mr. LESLIE: I have to ask that this
Amendment be allowed to stand over for
Report. I shall have to make the same
application as regards one or two other
Amendments. In fact as the House
knows they are very much interrelated,
and it is too early to discuss the matter.
Therefore I do not move.
Amendment not moved.
Mr. LESLIE: I beg to move in paragraph (c) to leave out from "cruelty
to the end of paragraph (d). This is, I
venture to think, the only controversial
point likely to be raised on the Bill. There
will be other points, matters really more
for discussion than anything else, but this
particular point is, I know, one which
will give rise to considerable difference
of opinion.
The Amendment is, in fact, to leave
out the provision in the Bill that divorce
may be granted on the ground of incurable insanity of one of the parties. The
expression is in paragraph (d) that the
respondent "is incurably of unsound
mind and has been continuously under
care and treatment for a period of at least
five years immediately preceding the presentation of the petition." My proposal
is to leave that out entirely so that insanity shall not be allowed to become a
ground for divorce.
I will
We all
Now there are two aspects of the question-two aspects to which I think this
Committee is bound to have regard. First
of all, there is the religious aspect and,
secondly, the practical aspect.
take the religious aspect first.
know the conditions of Christian marriage. We know that it is a necessary
part of the solemn compact then entered
into that the parties shall take each other
for better or for worse in sickness or in
health until death them do part. This
insanity is insanity, of course, acquired
after marriage. In the case of a party
being insane at the time of the marriage
we deal with that in a separate Clause.
This deals with insanity subsequent to the
marriage. That is, in fact, after acquired
sickness. It might be merely that class
of sickness described as senile decay. That
might happen. I do not think it can be
in anybody's mind that senile decay should
become a ground for divorce.
But it seems to me in any case-still on
that aspect of the question-that we
should be doing violence to the very deeprooted convictions of I should imagine twothirds of our people if we allow these
words to stand. I think we must remember, still on that ground, that the
proposal to allow divorce on the ground
of insanity differs from the other grounds
on which we are prepared to allow those
people who desire it to get divorce in this
way, that the other accused party to be
185 Matrimonial Causes Bill—
6 JUNE 1939

Committee
divorced has committed some matrimonial
fault. It may be adultery, desertion, or
other matters.
At all events a fault has been committed
and it is optional with the unoffending
parties as to whether they do or do not
set the law in motion. They are not
bound to ask for the relief and in any
event in any of these other cases before a
decree can be pronounced both parties.
must be heard. Where one of the parties
is insane that party cannot in the nature
of things be heard. I think the
insane party might suffer a good
deal during those lucid intervals which
do occur, I believe, in almost all cases of
insanity by the absence of the partner who
was taken originally for better or for
worse. I cannot help thinking that we
should be very wrong, indeed, if we allow
anything to go through this Parliament
which would tend to weaken the solemn
undertaking entered into at the marriage
ceremony. So much for the religious
aspect.
Let me turn to what I have called the
practical aspect. It is sometimes said
that people's lives are wrecked and so on.
I am afraid it is unhappily frequently the
case. But in the Bill relief cannot be
obtained for at the very least six years.
Insanity is to be incurable for five years.
If you allow another year for the period
leading up to the time insanity becomes
pronounceable and a few months' preparation for these legal proceedings you get
about six years. There must be, therefore, six years. Then a person having
divorced his or her partner cannot marry
entirely according to taste, so to speak,
because if the Committee will turn to the
proviso to Clause 8 they will see that it will
not be lawful for a man during the lifetime of the divorced wife to marry the
sister or half sister of the divorced
wife. That is to say, if the aunt
has come to the house to look after the
children, a very natural thing for the
sister of the unfortunate woman who has
lost her mind to do, the man cannot
marry her. And similarly in the case
of a divorced husband, the brother or
half-brother and so on. But it is the
first point, paragraph (a), to which I
desire to draw attention.
And now, still on the question of the
practical aspect, the insanity has to be
incurable. Who is going to proinnounce that the insanity is
curable? I am inclined to think that
you will find very few doctors who are
really competent specialists in brain
disease, and who will say in any given.
case that the state of things they find is
incurable. But if the decision as to
incurability must ultimately rest with the
judge who hears the petition the judge.
will have to form his own opinion if he
can, and he will have before him such
medical evidence as can be got. It
seems to me the petitioner has got to
have a doctor who will support the suggestion that the disease is in that case
incurable, and the judge has to be persuaded that that is the correct deduction
from the evidence of the doctor. That
is, it seems to me, a very difficult matter
and sufficient in itself to move us to
reject the proposal now before us.
In the Scottish Act of last year, as
the Committee. will remember, a different
view is taken. It is there provided that
confinement in an asylum or its equivalent
during a period of five years shall be
regarded as proof of incurable insanity.
In England they have not adopted that
view, and it is not proposed in the Bill
before us. We are left with this question of incurability absolutely at the
discretion of the court. I am afraid I
do not myself think that the ordinary
court is a very satisfactory tribunal to
pronounce definitely and in a manner
which would carry conviction to the minds
of all as to the question whether a particular disease is or is not curable either
with science at its present state of
advancement or in the future, when for
all we know greater advance will be made.
I, therefore, invite the Committee to
reject the words to which I have drawn
attention.
Mr. LONG: Parliament not infrequently imposes hardship upon one section.
of the population in order to benefit
another, and is generally moved to do so
for the reason that the sum of human
well-being is clearly increased thereby.
The position of a person married to a
lunatic is always pitiable, and when there
are young children who are the issue of
the union it is grievous indeed, particularly when the persons concerned are
of the working class.
A poor man left a widower with young
children, unless he puts the children into
Matrimonial Causes Bill—
Committee
THE SENATE
[Mr. Long]
an orphanage, has no resource, if they
are to have the benefits of a home, but
marriage, and normally he does in fact
marry again. Very often, as is pointed.
out in the. Report of the Royal Commission on Divorce and Matrimonial Causes,
such a man whose wife is in an asylum
for the insane is forced into an irregular
union with a woman who passes as his
housekeeper. Women in the same circumstances are also not infrequently driven
to immorality.
The Royal Commission enquired into
the likelihood of hardship to insane
persons. They found that the largest
class of insane married persons in the
United Kingdom was "composed of those
suffering from secondary dementia, of
whom it is said that they have so lost
the power of proper feeling that the fact
of divorce would make no painful impression on their minds or no impression
at all." The. Report also gives the
following statistics. The average percentage of recoveries in asylums is between
33 and 35 per cent. of the admissions;
of this percentage 10 may be expected
to relapse and 25 to remain out of the
asylums. Of those that recover only 2.1
per cent. recover who have been under
care, for over five years. As the Report
was made as long ago as 1912, I have
obtained figures from the Belfast Mental
Hospital, where out of 1,187 persons who
have been discharged as recovered in the
past ten years only 21, that is, less than
2 per cent., had been continuously
resident there for five years or over.
To my mind the retention in the Bill
of insanity as a ground for divorce is
justified in the interests of public morality
and the much greater hardship that is
now imposed by the marriage bond on
the spouses of insane persons than would
be imposed on a very few persons by
enabling this bond to be broken. The
point has been raised that to allow divorce
for insanity is departing from the principle that it should only be given as a
CONTENT
penalty for wrongdoing, for a breach of
contract. Surely this view of divorce is
incorrect. The main motive which has
prompted divorce legislation is the desire
to enable persons to obtain relief from
an intolerable situation. This was the
view taken by the Royal Commission. No
stigma of guilt can, of course, attach to
an insane person by reason of his or her
divorce. The Government cannot accept
the amendment.
Mr. LESLIE: Of course I regret extremely if the Government of Northern
Ireland is proposing to suggest, as, indeed, the Parliamentary Secretary does
suggest, that a subsequent marriage socalled by a party to a marriage whose
partner is still living is not immoral. Those
who take the other view consider, of
course, that no second marriage so-called
can make any difference whatever. That
is, perhaps, scarcely the point. There is
a most distinct difference in the point of
view. It is with a view of pointing to
that difference that I say the adoption of
this proposal in the Bill would do violence to the convictions of at least twothirds of the people of Northern Ireland.
I arrive at my two-thirds, of course, by
taking the proportionate number of the
various religious persuasions and assuming that the greater number of them hold
strictly to the principles of their respective Churches, and I added mentally a
good many people, not, perhaps, adherents
of the other two Churches, but who entertain the same views just as strongly as the
others. I think I could not profitably
enter into a detailed discussion of what
this holds without an expression of my
profound regret that it should have
emanated from the Government of Northern Ireland. I cannot withdraw the
Amendment.

Mr. LESLIE: I beg to move in subclause (2) to leave out "been accessory to,
or" ("been accessory to, or connived
at').
man
or
It seems to me that the words I
suggest should be left out in this case
are really meaningless. If a
woman as the case may be for convenience I will stick to the man-if a
man has in any manner been accessory
to adultery it seems to me that he has
most undoubtedly connived at it. I do
not think a man can be accessory to
adultery unless he was there to connive
at it. The history of the words is a little
curious. The words as they stand before
us appear in the English Act of 1937-
Mr. Herbert's Bill. They also appear in
the Act of 1857, and the Act of 1925,
the Consolidating Act, both of which
Acts we have had mentioned more than
once during the discussion. In each of
those two cases, the 1857 and 1925 Acts,
the words are introduced, so to speak,
by a similar phrase in what is here subclause (1) of this Clause. The words run
-I am quoting from Section 178 of the
Act of 1925:
It shall be the duty of the Court
to inquire both as to the facts alleged
and whether the petitioner has during
the marriage been accessory to or has
connived at or done adultery or not.
In these two cases the Court is deliberately instructed to inquire into these
matters, and it is provided that any con-

nivance and so on must have taken place

appreciate the object of that. The only

were really useless as being covered by
the expression "connived" and took them

I think they

Second Reading.

CLAUSE 26 (Jurisdiction in case of
husband's change of domicile).
Where a wife has been deserted by
her husband, or where her husband has
been deported from the United
Kingdom under any law for the time
being in force relating to the deportation of aliens, and the husband was
immediately before the desertion or
deportation domiciled in Northern Ireland, the court shall have jurisdiction
for the purpose of any proceedings
taken by the wife in a matrimonial
cause or matter, notwithstanding that
the husband has changed his domicile
since the desertion or deportation.
Mr. LESLIE : I beg to move after
"Where" at the beginning of the Clause
to insert it is alleged that."
""
I am rather in a difficulty about this.
This particular Clause has to deal with
two cases, the case where a wife has been
deserted or the case where a husband has
been deported from the United Kingdom.
The question of whether deportation happened or not is a simple question of fact,
and no difficulty arises on that question,
but where the wife has been deserted by
her husband it seems to me to be a mixed
question of law and fact, and that before
the court can exercise the power conferred
by this Clause it must arrive at a conclusion as to whether or not the wife has, in
fact, been deserted. Accordingly, it
seems to me that our best plan was to
insert at the beginning the words on the
paper, so as to make the Clause read,
"Where it is alleged that a wife has been
deserted by her husband" and so on.
That, to my mind, will give the court full
jurisdiction.
Mr. LONG: The jurisdiction of a
court to pronounce a valid decree of
divorce ordinarily depends upon the
domicile of the husband. A case may
occur where a husband domiciled in Northern Ireland deserts his wife and goes
abroad and acquires a new domicile after
the desertion, for example, in France or
the United States of America. The
object of the Clause is to give the court
in Northern Ireland jurisdiction to make
a divorce decree in such a case notwithstanding that the husband has changed
his domicile.
But the jurisdiction to grant a decree
of divorce in such a case must depend
upon whether the wife has in fact been
deserted. You cannot give the court a
jurisdiction based merely on the fact that
the wife has chosen to make an allegation
of desertion. The jurisdiction of the
court must depend on facts, not
allegations which one party chooses to
make.
on
The Clause, as it stands, follows
exactly the wording of the corresponding
clause in the English Act. With the
Clause as drafted in the Bill the husband
if he wishes to deny the desertion will be
entitled to enter an appearance under
protest in answer to the wife's petition
and to raise the point, if he wishes, that
he has not, in fact, been guilty of desertion. The Government cannot accept the
Amendment.
Mr. LESLIE: I confess I am not
altogether convinced, but if the Government are satisfied that the court will have
jurisdiction I do not think I need pursue
the matter.
Amendment, by leave, withdrawn.

The following Amendment stood in

Mr. LESLIE: We approach here
what is one of the difficulties, almost the
greatest difficulty in the Bill, to which I
referred at some length on the Second
Reading. I should like to say that I do
not propose to move the Amendment in
exactly the same words as appear on the
Paper. I propose to move :
(2) The expression "desertion,"
wherever in this Act occurring, means
desertion without reasonable excuse
for a period of at least three years, and
the expression "deserted" shall be
construed accordingly.
I leave out the words "wilful separation"
and substitute "desertion." The difficulty I am in all the way through is as
to whether the way I put the matter on
the Amendment Paper does express the
intention of the Bill. When we
talk of desertion and deserted
do we
always mean for period of three
years, or do we not? On the answer to
that question depends the fate of a good
many of the amendments which I suggested should stand over for Report. I
think we must really come to the conclusion one way or another.
a
Do we
mean desertion wherever we use the word
as enduring for three years?
Hitherto to get a decree a mensa et
toro you wanted desertion of two years,
and in England you wanted a desertion
of two years to get a judicial separation.
Now we require that it should be three
years for a divorce. In the Clause we
have just dealt with, the Clause giving
jurisdiction in the event of a change of
domicile, and so on, we get the word
"deserted" alone. We are given no
indication of how long that desertion is
to last. Whether we mean in the last
Clause that the action of a man simply
going away and leaving his wife chargeable to the parish last week would constitute desertion or not we do not know.
I think the Bill ought to make it plain
in some, form or other as to what we do
mean by desertion.
I do not want, and that is why I have
altered the two words in the amendment,
a definition of the term "desertion"
itself, because that is rather too technical
perhaps for a discussion here this afternoon. In Divorce Court proceedings the
term "desertion" has been discussed and
discussed and construed and construed
again and again, and I think one of the
best definitions I ever saw was given in
a legal book which gave about three lines
and then said: "This description is not
exhaustive." That is really where, we
leave it. We know that desertion means
withdrawal, not necessarily from a place
but from a state of things, and various
other matters.
I should hesitate a very long time.
before putting anything in a Bill, or
suggesting that anything should go into
a Bill, which attempted to define the
term. Defining it is one thing and delimiting it is another. We want in this
Bill some clear information as to what
the intention is as to the length of time
for which the desertion must be continued.
As it stands we have only got it once;
that is in the beginning, where we are
told it must be for three years. After
that the thing is left in the air. I think
we had better assume that it did mean
three years all the time. We wish now
to be informed what the proposal of the
Government really is. I beg to move..
Mr. LONG: The Government cannot

Finance Bill.
Order for Second Reading read.
Mr. LONG: The purpose of this
Measure is to effect the changes in
taxation announced by my right hon.
Friend, the Minister of Finance, in his
Budget statement.
Clause 1 provides that the rate of
motor vehicle duty shall be increased
from 15s to 25s per horse power on
private cars, and that a rather smaller
increase be made in the rates for motor
cycles. The proposed rates, which are
set out in the Schedule to the Bill, are
not to apply to commercial or agricultural vehicles.
Clause 2 provides for an increase of
10 per cent. in the rate of estate duty
leviable on estates the value of which
exceeds £50,000.
Clauses 3 and 4 are designed to prevent evasion of payment of estate duty
and of stamp duty.
Clause 5 exempts certain instruments
from the charge of stamp duty.
Clause 6 will enable a contribution to
be made from the proceeds of motor
vehicle taxation towards the cost of defence. I beg to move, "That this Bill
be now read a Second time."
Question proposed: "That the Bill be
now read a Second time.
""
Mr. LESLIE: I have. to ask a rather

2, dealing with the increase in Estate

exceeding £50,000 in value. I believe.
the Clause follows exactly the wording
of the Bill in the Parliament of the
United Kingdom, but when it was referred to in the, newspapers it was apparently stated that this increase in estate
duty did not apply to landed property.
I am very much inclined to wonder
whether the report was not made up
rather hastily, and the word "agricultural" got, so to speak, misplaced from
its relation to Clause 1 to the other. I
thought it was worth while calling attention to it, and asking the Parliamentary
Secretary to state at a later stage whether
I am correct in assuming that the. Clause
does apply to estates whether composed
of real or personal property.
Mr. LONG: I will make inquiry into
the matter, and let the hon. and learned
Member know at a later date.

Education (School-Leaving Age) Bill.

Mr. LONG: The sole object of this
Bill is to postpone for one year the
raising of the school-leaving age as proposed in the Education Act of 1938. The
Act was to have been effective as from
1st July next, but representations have
been made to my right hon. Friend, the
Minister of Education, that in some areas
it will not be possible by that date to
make satisfactory arrangements for the
education of the children who would be
kept at school.

Mr. LONG: In moving, "That this
Report be now received," I desire to
make brief statement in reply to
questions raised by Senator Leslie on the
Committee stage.
a
There are six amendments on the
Paper. There is no doubt that it would
be a great mistake, to depart from English
law and practice by attempting, with
doubtful prospect of success, to lay down
a comprehensive definition of "desertion."
The position may be analysed in this way:
In Clause 2 (b) three years is mentioned;
in Clause 5 (Proviso iii) the fact and
continuance of desertion would be a
matter for the court; in Clause 5 (Proviso
iv) the three-year period is implied; in
Clause. 7 the three-year period is implied;
in Clause 13 (3) the three-year period
is implied by virtue of Clause 13 (1),
because in the case of a judicial separation
the period of desertion must be the same
as that for a divorce; in Cause 14 (2)
the three-year period is implied; in
Clause 14 (3) the three-year period is
implied; in Clause 20 (1) the three-year
period is implied by virtue of Clauses 2
and 13 (1); in Clause 26 no period is.
mentioned, but if the proceedings were
for divorce or judicial separation the
three-year period would be required. In
any other proceeding the fact and continuance of desertion would have to be
proved to the court; but such other
proceeding would be unlikely, for example, restitution of conjugal rights,
which is the ecclesiastical remedy for desertion, would not seem to be of much use
in the circumstances contemplated by
this Clause.
Question proposed, "That this Report
be now received."

the little

schedule of the various methods in which

Bacon Industry Bill.
Order for Second Reading read.
Mr. LONG: I think it is perhaps desirable that I should preface my remarks
on this Bil by some account of the
circumstances attending its introduction.
As hon. Members are aware, a Bacon
Industry Act was passed by the Imperial
Parliament at Westminster a year ago
and is now in operation in Great
Britain. The Act had two objects. On
the one hand it proposed to increase the
efficiency of the curing trade by enabling
a rationalisation scheme to be introduced
under which a number of factories would
be closed down. The efficiency of the
curing trade depends very largely upon
the size of its factories. The trade is
undoubtedly one in which the advantage of diminishing costs is very
apparent with every increase in the size
of the curing unit up to a relatively
high level.
Secondly, the Act was designed to
secure an adequate supply of pigs for
the curing industry during the years in
which rationalisation was taking place.
This was to be achieved by means of a
guaranteed price to the producer independent of fluctuations in the cost
feeding stuffs.
of
As soon as the proposals under the
British Measure were announced urgent
representations were made to the Government to secure similar assistance for the industry in Northern Ireland. Only after
prolonged negotiations has it been
agreed, however, that a corresponding
Measure should be introduced in Northern Ireland. The object of the present Measure is to secure for producers
and curers in Northern Ireland a comparable measure of assistance to that
which is afforded in Great Britain, and
to ensure that no matter what developments take place in that country the
curing trade and pig producing industry
in Northern Ireland will be able to
maintain their positions in competition.
with these trades in Great Britain.
I now turn to the various provisions
of the Bill. The first part of the Bill
is concerned with the rationalisation of
the curing trade in Northern Ireland.
and would enable a factory rationalisation
scheme, to be prepared by the Ministry
of Agriculture in consultation with curers
and other interests affected such as representatives of workers employed in the
industry. The factory rationalisation
scheme cannot come. into operation until
it has been approved by each House of
Parliament, and all amendments or
modifications of the scheme or its subsequent revocation must equally be dependent upon the express wish of each House
of Parliament.
The second part of the Bill deals with
the system of pig supply which it is intended should in future operate in
Northern Ireland. There are two
features of this system of pig supply to
209 Bacon Industry Bill-

13 JUNE 1939
Second Reading
which I should call attention.
In the
first place producers are asked to enter
into contracts for the supply of pigs to
curers. In the present year this contract
would be for a relatively short period,
but in future the contract would be for
a period of twelve months. It is recognised, however, that in view of the very
large number of small producers in
Northern Ireland the contract system
cannot be left to operate alone, and
special provision has been made to deal
with the small producer who will benefit.
under all the provisions of the Bill if
he makes a registered agreement to supply
any number of pigs up to thirty to a
curer in any twelve months' period.
During the period up to the end of
December, 1941, the price to be received
by the producer under contracts or registered agreements would be guaranteed by
the Government. The initial price is 70s
per cwt., and slight reductions from this
price are made in the second and third
years. This price of 70s is related to
the cost of feeding stuffs, and would
apply when the cost of the standard
ration of feeding stuffs is 8s 6d per cwt.
The basic price per cwt. of pigs, 70s,
would move upwards and downwards in
accordance with upward and downward
movements in the price of feeding stuffs.
Since feeding stuffs constitute the principal item in the costs of pig production
the producer is guaranteed a price for
his pigs in terms of real value as distinct
from money values, and is assured of a
price which should prove remunerative
no matter what may occur to bacon
prices or to feeding stuff prices.
The guaranteed price is not materially
different from the price which producers
are obtaining at the present time, but
there would be no necessity in the future
for the system of trading levies which has
operated in the past, and consequently
the actual price to the producer
under the Bill would be higher than the
current price, and he would be safeguarded against all risks. This is particularly satisfactory in view of the way in
which even under existing conditions the
pig population of the country continues
to increase. Hon. Members may be interested to learn that the pig population
of the country was estimated to have increased by nearly 13 per cent. between the
first of April, 1938, and 1st April, 1939.
In order to enable curers to pay the
prices laid down in the Bill, provision is
made in Clause 12 for payments to be made
to them by the Exchequer. In certain contingencies also, set out in Clause 12, repayments would have to be made by the
curers. The object of this Clause is to
assure the curer a definite margin to cover
his curing costs irrespective of fluctuations.
in the price of bacon. The amount of the
payment to curers will depend upon the
amount of the payment in Great Britain,
and the purpose of the Bill in its financial provisions is to place both curers and
producers in Northern Ireland in exactly
the same financial position as curers and
producers in Great Britain during the
period up to the end of 1941.
The opportunity of the Bill has also
been taken to deal with certain miscellaneous matters in connection with the
pig industry in Northern Ireland. In
particular, it has been necessary in Part
IV of the Bill to modify somewhat the
powers of the Pigs Marketing Board
under the Pigs Marketing Scheme, so as
to enable the provision of the Bill which
I have already outlined to operate. It
would, of course, have been possible to
have effected these modifications in the
Pigs Marketing Scheme by means of an
amendment to that scheme, but it has
been felt desirable that the precise position
of the Pigs Marketing Scheme and the
Pigs Marketing Board should be set forth
in the Bill. Generally speaking, the
principal power of the Board which is removed is the power of the Board to require
all pigs to be sold only to the Board. It
is clear that this power could not exist
along with the system of contracts and
registered agreements provided for in the
Bill.
In view of the very large numbers of
producers who are interested in pig production, I feel sure that this House will
agree that this Measure with its assurance of a remunerative price during the
next two and a half years will be a further
important step in the direction of securing the general improvement in the condition of the agricultural industry in
Northern Ireland which we all desire.

Mr. MAGUIRE: I am very pleased
that the Minister of Agriculture is present to-day, and I am sure he would go
away disappointed if some criticism of this
Bill was not forthcoming. I desire to
refer to some of the Clauses in the Bill,
and also to seek information which may
avoid the necessity of my putting down
Amendments. When the Minister of
Agriculture introduced this Measure in
another place he boasted that this was
the first Bill enabling Northern Ireland
pig producers to be put on a par with pig
producers in Great Britain." There is,
however, some difference between the
English Bill and that now before this
House. In this Bill a producer may make
a contract with the Pigs Marketing Board,
but in this connection the Board is simply
in the position of a producer who makes a
contract. I refer to Clause 9, sub-clause (6).
Under the Great Britain scheme the
producer does not name the curer. The
Pigs Marketing Board select the curer.
It is left entirely to the arrangements
of the Pigs Marketing Board, and under
such contracts the producers are paid
2s per pig in addition to the contract
price in Great Britain. I do not see
that that applies in the Northern Ireland Bill.
The Parliamentary Secretary has just said
the producer here would be in exactly
the same position as the producers in
England. I maintain they are not.
There is no 2s per pig allowed the producer in addition to the contract price.
I may also go further and say the English
Board fully approve of the principle on
which the price formula is based. The
English Board are convinced that the
actual price is not sufficient to cover the
producer's costs. It is very significant
that since the Act was adopted in England on 1st December last, if you refer
to the March census you will find there
has been a very heavy decrease in pig
production in both England and Scotland. I think in England there has
been a decline of 8.2 per cent. compared
with March, 1938, and in Scotland a
reduction of 1.7 per cent. compared with
March, 1938. That is not the only disturbing point. There is another very
disturbing factor which the census disclosed. It is that the sow population
in England and Wales has decreased by
17,000, or 3.7 per cent. compared with
the 1938 census. That would mean a
less to the production industry in the
year of approximately 130,000 pigs.
From that you will see that the English
producer is not satisfied with the Bill.
I thought that would give a line to the
Ministry at home in order to amend the
Bill to suit the producer.
The English Pigs Marketing Board
have asked for a price to be placed of
12s 6d a score, or 70s per cwt. when
feeding stuff is 7s 6d. They are advocating that at the moment, and rightly
so, when you make the comparison between the cost of production and what
we receive for our pigs at present. The
basis is the cost of the weaner or small
pig £1 5s, overhead charges including
labour 10s. It is calculated it takes
7cwt. of meal to bring the pig up to
7 score weight or 150lbs. What we receive here on the 70s basis for the 150lbs.
pig is £4 13s 9d. There is the trade
and administrative levy deducted to the
amount of 4s 6d, leaving the net result
£4 9s 3d against £4 14s 6d, or a loss of
5s 3d per pig. I do not think there is
very much encouragement for producers
in Northern Ireland to increase their
flock.
Another point I would like information
on is transport, Clause 14, page 16. I
do not quite follow the provisions. The
Pig Industry Council does the transport
of the pig, and by the Pig Industry
Council paying for the transport costs
the saving in costs to the curer falls
back to the producer. I see no provision
in the Bill for allowances to producers
who deliver their pigs to the curers'
premises. In the English Act they have.
an allowance on 35 miles or over of so
much per pig. There is also a Clause in
the Bill which states that the Ministry
may refuse to register a contract or agreement if the transport involves a very
long distance from the curer's factory.
I would like to know what is to become
of the pigs refused by the Ministry for
contract or agreement.
Then according to the Bill contracts
are to be on a yearly basis. Formerly the
contracts in Northern Ireland-and it
was carried on for many years were for
three months' terms. During those three
months most producers found it fairly
difficult to keep up their supplies. I
myself found great difficulty on many
occasions. It is very difficult to forecast
for twelve months the supply of pigs not
