Sir BASIL BROOKE: I will endeavour
cover the points raised by Senator
Maguire. The first question was that of
contracts, that in the case of Great
Britain the contracts are made direct
with the Board, whereas here contracts
are made with the. curer. The reason of
that alteration is this. In England the
Pigs Marketing Board are not the owners
of factories. In this country the Pigs
Marketing Board are the owners of two
factories, Colin and Cookstown, and when
I met the curers, discussing the provisions of this Bill, they felt, and I think
felt quite rightly, that the distribution
of the pigs should not be in the hands
of a body which were themselves curers.
Therefore, that provision was put in to
safeguard the curers in the event of the
Board deciding to favour their own
factories as against the curers. I may
say the Board brought
brought considerable
pressure. to bear on me to allow the same
principle to apply. So far as the Ministry is concerned there was no objection
whatsoever, but I told the Board the
objections I found that the curers felt.
I believe they subsequently saw the
curers, who informed them that that
objection was there, and the curers could
not see their way to give way on the
point.
The hon. Senator raised another point,
namely, that the Board in Great Britain
felt that the guaranteed price of 70s when
feeding stuff was 8s 6d was not satisfactory, and they were pressing for an
alteration and better terms. Now that is
one of the reasons why I am so anxious to
get this Bill through. It is possible to
argue that a bigger margin should be
allowed. I think myself that even with
the lower price of feeding stuff-I think
at present it is somewhere about 8s 3d
-with this lower feeding price and the
lower price to the producer, the producer
is actually better off than he was before,
because, as the Parliamentary Secretary
has pointed out, there will be no trading
limits. Trading limits are completely
wiped out. At any rate, the producer is
to that extent better off than he was before. Although the hon. Senator did not
raise the point which has been raised elsewhere I think he had it in mind, and that
is that the price which the producer is
getting now under the old scheme may be
quite good. There is, however, every
probability that the price of bacon will
fall in future, and, therefore, unless this
Bill was brought in there would be no
guaranteed price for the producer, because
the hon. Senator realises--he has an interest in the business himself-by computing the price to the producer the price of
bacon is brought in. Under this scheme
the price of bacon is entirely eliminated.
I come back to the point of the necessity
of bringing in the Bill at the present
time. It is that alterations in the price
margins are to be made in England, and
if we do not get this Bill into operation

Agricultural Wages (Regulation) Bill.
Order for Second Reading read.
Mr. LONG: The purposes of this
Measure are clearly stated in the description of the Bill. They are to set up
machinery for the regulation of the wages
of farm labourers and to provide for their
holidays with pay. Northern Ireland is
a country of small farms and its farm
labourers are, in consequence, a scattered
class less able, perhaps, than any other
class of worker to combine to strike a
collective bargain regarding their conditions of employment and their remuneration.
I need hardly remind this House how
important it is that the remuneration of
farm labourers and their conditions of
employment should be the best that the
industry can afford. They play an
essential part in the working of our
greatest industry, and employment in that
industry must be made sufficiently attractive to them to counterbalance the attractions of town life.
Clause 1 of the Bill provides for the
establishment of an Agricultural Wages
Board for Northern Ireland to consist of
15 members. It is proposed that six
members shall be representatives of employers of labour in agriculture, six of
workers, and that three shall be independent members appointed by the
Minister of Agriculture. In fixing a
minimum rate of wages for agricultural
workers in any area the Board is required by sub-section 7 of Clause 2 to
take into account the economic position
of farming in the area, and also, as far
as practicable, to secure for ablebodied men such wages as are adequate
to promote efficiency, and to enable a
man to maintain himself and his family
in a reasonable standard of comfort.
Before actually fixing wages the Board
is required by sub-section 9 of Clause 2
to give notice of its proposals, so that
objections can be received. Such objections must be considered before the rate
of wages is finally fixed.
Clause 4 provides that agricultural
workers shall be entitled to six days'
holiday with pay in the year. Clause 5
is concerned with the enforcement of the
minimum wages fixed by the Board.
Clause 9 provides that the Minister of
Agriculture shall make an annual report
217 Agricultural Wages Bill--

13 JUNE 1939
Second Reading
to Parliament of the proceedings of his
Ministry and of the Board under the
Act. I do not think I need enlarge on
the other clauses of the Bill which are
concerned mainly with questions of
machinery and detail.
sure that hon.
Members are
I am
aware that machinery for the fixing of
minimum wages in agriculture has been
working in England and Wales since
1924, and that since 1936, when
similar provision was made for Scotland,
Northern Ireland has been the only part
of the United Kingdom where no such
machinery existed. The Bill is introduced with these precedents, and was
drafted upon the basis of the unanimous
report of a Committee set up about a
year ago by my right hon. Friend, the
Minister of Agriculture, to inquire into
the conditions of employment and the
wages of agricultural workers in Northern Ireland. That Committee included four representatives of the Ulster
Farmers' Union, four representatives of
the Transport and General Workers'
Union, and four members of Parliament.
I commend the Bill to this House. I
beg to move, "That the Bill be now read
a Second time."
Mr. LESLIE: It has very frequently
happened to Members of Parliament that
they have felt bound to do something that they did not perhaps particularly want to do themselves, and,
anyhow, they would infinitely prefer
somebody else should do it for them.
Hence it sometimes happens, as is going
to be the case this afternoon, at all
events, for the purpose of discussion of a
Bill which is not only a very important
Bill, but one likely to have very farreaching effects, that somebody should
take upon himself to move the
accustomed motion that the word "now"
be left out to be followed in due course
by the insertion at the end of the motion
"this day six months."
I rather particularly hesitate on this
occasion to make that motion, because I
am quite sure that one would at once
be charged with lack of sympathy with
the agricultural labourer. I do not
know that there is any foundation for
that. In regard to my own case, I am
inclined to think such a charge would be
singularly baseless. Sympathy with the
agricultural labourer must not blind us
to the fact that what we are invited to
embark on this afternoon is a new experiment. It is not entirely an experiment without precedent. Some of us
will remember that we tried it once
before after the passing of the Corn
Production Act, 1917, and I shall refer
to that again in a few moments.
experiment therefore has been
before as regards the labourer.
The
tried
He
For a moment I have to look back to
the history of the farm labourer in Northern Ireland. Thirty-five years ago in
my part of the country you could get an
ordinary farm labourer for 9s a week.
Let me go back for a century and a half
before that, to the time when Arthur
Young paid his visit to Ireland.
came amongst other places to my own
district in the North of Antrim. He
was good enough to express approval of
the wheat grown there, and he records
that the wages paid were 8d a day. He
also records that the wheat, of which
as I have said he expressed approval,
was produced at a loss. Since then, as
we know, wages have risen very
materially, but for the moment I will
leave it at that.
Let us look at the present history of
the farmer. What was the general experience of farmers in Ireland last year?
Did they get any return, generally
speaking, for the capital invested in the
farm? The farmer, I suggest to the
House, is in some ways about the most
helpless man as regards business affairs
that one can imagine. He is helpless
not because of inefficiency or anything
of that sort, but because he has absolutely no control over the prices of his
products. I imagine the only item of
farm produce which the farmer can control is milk, and as the House knows
there are reasons why the public interest
desires that, if there be profiteering, there
should certainly not be any in milk. As
regards other things such as oats, potatoes
and cattle the farmer is entirely at the
mercy of world prices. His best market
is across the water, but he has no control
over the importation of oats, potatoes or
cattle. Therefore he is committed to a
line of business over which he cannot
exercise control similar to that enjoyed
by the man engaged in manufacture.
One knows, of course, that there are
in existence subventions or subsidies
which do materially assist the financial

Second Reading
[Mr. Leslie]
We
side of the farmers' arrangements.
understand that these subsidies are likely
to increase; that is to say, new subsidies
are offered, but these new subsidies do
not seem to be unconditional nor do they
seem to be altogether unlimited. I mean
not so much unlimited in amount but
as regards the sphere within which they
can be enjoyed. Moreover, we have had

has got to risk his capital on the chance
that this particular alleviation of his

As to the statute history, so to speak,
of questions on this matter we have to
go back, judging from the text of the
Bill before us, to the Trades Board Act
of 1909. Some of this Bill appears
to have its origin there. We have to
pass from that through a rather long
series of statutory enactments. One of
them worth mentioning is the Corn Production Act I have already spoken twice.
about, and it was followed by Acts in
1918, 1920 and 1921, the first amending
the Trades Board Act, the second amending the Corn Production Act, and, finally,
the third repealing it. In 1923 we had our
own Trades Board Act and in 1924 we had
the English Agricultural Wages Act, to
which the Parliamentary Secretary referred. That was followed in 1937 by a
somewhat similar Act for Scotland, and it
would appear it is the Scottish Act, 1937,
rather than the English Act of 1924, that
is followed in the Bill before us. I would
like the Minister, whom we are pleased
to see, to confirm that.
Finally, we had an Act passed last year
dealing with holidays with pay. I am
not going to say anything about the holiday part of the Measure now before us. I
do not think that part is likely to arouse
any particular opposition because, perhaps, in most cases it will make very
little difference.
Coming back to the Corn Production
Act, the House will remember that the
requirement under that Act as regards
wages was not that the wage should be
adequate for the man to maintain himself
and his family, but it was to provide for
an able-bodied man wages of not less than
25s a week. That was so far as England
and, I think, Scotland were concerned.
But at the end of that Act, as was so
often the case in the statutes of those
days, there was a section dealing with the
application of the Act to Ireland, and the
reference to 25s a week was left out.
However, in 1920, according to a copy
of the Order, which I have fortunately,
perhaps, preserved, made by the Agricultural Board set up under the Act of 1917
in my part of the country a herd had to
be paid 36s a week, which was to include
Sunday. That was because my immediate
part of the world was in Group 1, as it
was called. The whole of Ireland was
at that time divided into groups 1 and 2.
I shall have to speak about that in a
minute or two because this question of
groups would appear to arise again in the
Bill before us except that they are going
to be called areas.
At the time this herd was getting 36s
a week the rates which one had to pay on
buildings, not land, were 61d in the £;
to-day in the same area they are 92d in
the £. In those days when you sold
butter, I find from reference to old books,
you got from 3s to 3s 6d a lb. for it. If
you sold potatoes you got 8s per cwt. for
them. That was in 1920. Butter is, I
believe, now selling at 1s and potatoes, if
there are any left, at 2s to 3s. Of course
this is a bad time of the year for them.
The district which I spoke of is the
Ballymoney rural district, and the rate
for the ordinary labourer was there put
down at 32s 6d. The neighbouring rural
district of Ballycastle came down to 30s.
Why that was done is not, perhaps, altogether quite clear. Ballycastle rural
district, as most of the House are aware,
contains a good deal of mountain and bog,
but Ballymoney rural district contains the
Garry Bog, adjacent parts of which have
been reclaimed. What I am leading up
to is that the definition of areas under
that Act of 1917 was not very carefully or
very satisfactorily performed, and one
hopes that if this Bill is accepted by the
House care will be taken that there is
some better distribution of the relative
areas.

Larne rural district was, I think, the
only case in the county where two electoral

as group 1. It seems to me if this Bill
goes through and if anything in the way
of areas comes up again for consideration
it will be necessary that much greater care
shall be taken in the examination of the
circumstances of the particular areas.
I have mentioned some of the prices

Britain and Northern Ireland as regards

the month of July, 1914, whereas in the

distinct fall so far as that goes in the
cost of living. I think I have said

that the difficulties of the Board would
be very great, and what I want the
House to consider is whether there is
any reasonable prospect that they will
be able successfully to surmount their
difficulties.
We are not told in the Bill when they
are, so to speak, to commence operations,
but we are given a hint that the Board
is to be set up as soon as may be. It will
be important again that their Orders
should be issued at a suitable period of
the year in order that the farmer may
have them before him when planning his
arrangements for the following year and
an idea of what wages he will be called
upon to pay; in fact, I am afraid I must

opportunity of reducing his staff if he
desires to do so. That, however, is more

I want to draw attention to the system
of issuing permits to men who are not
regarded as competent to earn a full
wage, that is the man who is not ablebodied owing to infirmity, or age, or
some other cause. I do not think any
Member of this House would look forward with pleasure to bringing up one
of his own workers before a Board and
saying, "Look at this man, he is infirm
and cannot earn a full week's wage.
How much am I to give him?" And
I do not think the man himself would
care to go before a Board and say, "I
am an old man. I cannot earn a full
day's wage. Please may I be employed
at a lower rate?"
I do not think that is a fair position
in which to place either employer or
employee. Something of the same nature
occurred in the days of which I am
speaking under the 1917 Act. As far
as I remember what happened was that
when a man was known to be infirm or
very old a certificate was issued-I
think by a local clergyman-and that
was sent to someone or other, and the
thing went on without any trouble. I
think we must make provision definitely
as regards age. That, again, would be
for a later stage.
Why is it suggested that the Board
should be set up? Is it with the
deliberate intention of raising wages?
The Title of the Bill does not say that.
It does not say that it is intended to
raise wages, but in any case I am not
sure that the raising of the wage
in any
particular industry is a very proper task
for a Government to undertake. Those
who were in this House a week or so
ago heard the Leader of the House (Mr.

to the building and repair of houses
erected by local authorities, the Government were not prepared to interfere to
require payment of trade union rates of
wages by a person who chooses to build
a house for himself or to repair his own
house. I do not think that is altogether
the intention of the Bill, nor do I think
the Bill will solve the problem.
were

thrown out of work.
Let me go back. Why do those men
stay there? Is not one of the attractions
of service on a large farm or place the
knowledge that the employment will last
through the whole year? That is so.
There is no putting off because the day
is wet, nor is there usually any reduction
in wages in the short days. The Bill, I
think, is not really directed definitely to
raising the rate of wages.
It does not
say so in the. Title, but I think it has
an ulterior object, and it is because the
Bill will not attain that ulterior object
that I believe we ought to hesitate before
we pass it. That ulterior object appears
very much more from the Report to which
reference has been made than from the Bill
before us. In fact, it is scarcely possible.
to discuss this Bill in a broad way without referring pretty constantly to the
Report, to which the Parliamentary
Secretary himself directed attention.
That Report was, as we have been told,
a unanimous Report, and the Committee
responsible were appointed by the right
hon. Gentleman to consider and report
upon the conditions of employment of
agricultural workers in Northern Ireland
and to make recommendations for the
establishment of machinery for the fixing
of agricultural wages and conditions of
employment. So it would seem that when
the right hon. Gentleman appointed that
Committee on 25th May, 1938, he had
already made up his mind that he was
going to set up machinery to fix agricultural wages.
He did not wait for the
Report. He directed the Committee
apparently to tell him how to do it.
The Report itself is quite a good
Report. I disagree with some of it,
particularly those paragraphs where the
Committee suggest that agricultural wages
ought to be raised in order that it may be
made rather more easy to administer the
unemployment insurance scheme. I cannot see myself that facilities for administration are any ground for casting additional expenditure on other people. That
is one thing I do not approve of in the
Report.
on
The Report also suggests that,
account of the height to which unemployment insurance for the agricultural
labourer has risen, the wages to some extent ought to be levelled up to the figure
he could get if he were spending his days
in idleness. Of course we know that is
the case at the moment. It is one of the
unfortunate results of the adoption of the
unemployment insurance system for agriculture in Northern Ireland, but the
Report does recognise it is one of its
best points that the ability of the farmer
to pay is a fundamental factor. That is
put down in the Report, and I think I
may fairly say that the Report indicates
that in the opinion of the Committee the
farmer would pay higher wages if he were
in a position to do so.
I do think that perhaps the Report lays
a little too much stress on the question of
wages as helping towards the ulterior
object in view, that is keeping men on the
land, the maintenance of the present
number of agricultural labourers on the
land, and the encouragement of the
young person who would normally be an
agricultural labourer to resort to that
method of employment. I think that is
225 Agricultural Wages Bill-

13 JUNE 1939
Second Reading
the ulterior object of the Bill before us,
to maintain the present agricultural
population on the land, and to encourage
the young people to go on the land. The
Report also suggests-and indeed recognises that it is not only the young
labourer who is forsaking the land; it is
the farmer's son and the farmer, and it
very properly suggests that every endeavour should be made-and it pays due
regard to the efforts that have been made
in the past-to improve the position of the
farmer in order that he, in turn, may be
in a position to improve the position of
his worker. That also, I think, is to the
good in the Report.
There are other factors moving towards
this drift to the town. There is the
woman of the house. What does she want?
She wants what she can get in the town
and cannot get in the country-she wants
light and water. The boys usually
want amusement, but in their more or
less serious moments I think they want
chances of promotion; they want something to look forward to. They have
seen their grandfathers and their fathers
as agricultural labourers, and they know
that if they start as agricultural
labourers, as agricultural labourers in all
probability they will die, and that is
not an attractive prospect. I do not
think the few shillings a week extra
which can possibly be squeezed out of
the more or less prosperous farmer is
going to make any headway against that
deadly evil.
We have to recognise that the country
cannot compete with the town, either
as regards wages or the general
amenities of life. Paterfamilias knows
that. By paterfamilias I mean the agricultural labourer who has arrived at
years of discretion, and who has
probably burdened himself with a wife,
and, not perhaps always with discretion,
with a family. At all events, he has a
wife and family. and he is not as big a
fool as one might be disposed to think.
What does he want? I say that what
he really wants, in addition to living
out in the fresh air and the comparative
independence of a farm labourer's lifebut that is not in danger-is continuity
of employment. He does not want to
go on the dole-I speak of the man who
has arrived at years of discretionalthough he knows quite well that his
unemployment benefit during its currency
will bring him rather more money than
he can make by work. He does not want
that, he does want regular occupation; he
wants what I have called continuity, that
is, steady work throughout the year.
That, I am afraid, under an agricultural board which has the effect of
rising wages materially, he would not
get. I think the Committee in this
Report admit that; in fact, they do.
They say quite definitely there is a
change coming, that the practice of a
man staying where he is hired by the
year, in theory permanently week by
That
week, is going to come to an end.
is attributed in the Report partly to the
influence of unemployment insurance
and partly to other reasons. One of the
other reasons is decrease in tillage. If
the Agricultural Wages Board, when set
up, do in fact raise wages to a point
which will irritate the farmer, the
putting of men off will increase. There
will no longer be such temptation as
still remains-although temptation is
not a very good word-such inducement
as still remains to keep men at work on
the old wages during the shorter days,
and also to find work for them to do
during what would otherwise be a slack
period. That will receive a more severe
blow in the future than it has in the
past, and, consequently, if I am right,
that what the man really wants is continuity, we shall lose there, and we shall
also incidentally, I think, reduce the
amount of tillage in the country.
It is a curious fact-I do not know
whether the House knows it-I was very
much surprised to hear it a few weeks
ago that during the past year the
amount of tillage in Northern Ireland
has actually risen by 40,000 acres. There
have been ups and downs, but the
general trend has been downwards. I,
therefore, hope that the. House will pause
before giving any inducement to the
farmer to pay his men off. I think the
best thing we can do for the agricultural
labourer at the moment is to continue
our efforts to improve the condition of
the farmer, in order that he may be able
to pay
better wages, and to leave the
natural trend of the price of labour to
its own devices. At present it is distinctly on the up grade; wages are rising
without the assistance of the Agricultural
Wages Board, and I am not very much
impressed by the suggestion in the Report
227 Agricultural Wages Bill-

THE SENATE
Second Reading
[Mr. Leslie]
that the Agricultural Wages
Wages Board
would further assist in that direction.
What I am impressed with is this, the
great desirability of keeping work open
for the man. I think that is the best
thing we can do for him, and it is because
I am afraid that embarking on an agricultural wages scheme will not help in
that direction that I invite the House
to pass over the Bill for the present time.

I remember the time when the wages

to this part of the country. Very often
across the water political capital was
made of the wages paid to the linen

the amount paid to similar workers in

paid wages similar to the wages paid in

firms have passed the cost on to the

they used to do, if not better, and the
workers are a great deal better off too.

wisely by suggesting that the improvement in the condition of the agricultural
workers should be carried out by means
of this agricultural wages scheme. This
is not imposing on the. farmer a wage
that it is impossible for him to pay; it
is asking the Wages Board to consider
the position in the different districts, and
to recommend a wage that the employer
will be able to pay and that the employee
is entitled to receive.

the farmers are helpless, I maintain that

Simply because the poor fellows can do

They have had continuity of employment, but they have been existing in a
terrible state, obtaining just a bare existence, not enough for healthy men and
women to keep themselves and to rear
families. That is not by any means
creditable even to the farming community.
I should like to see the agricultural

deplorable.

pure air, healthy occupation and everything that gives long life to the individual.

The trouble is you cannot compare the

agricultural labourer with that of the
worker in the shipyard. After all wages
must have some relation to the value of
the product, and the value of the product in the shipyard is very much more
than that of the product of the farm.
Consequently, if you are doing it on
these lines, you are practically saying
to the farmer that he should invest more
capital, machine his farm, sack some
employees, and pay the rest a decent
wage. That is what I am afraid of.
The administration of this Act will be,
to my mind, a very important matter.
I feel a little bit nervous as to the
composition of the Wages Board. I
know it is appallingly difficult to form a
Board to work this kind of thing. I
suggest to the Minister that what he is
really going to ask that Board to do is
a very difficult, delicate and complex
piece of administrative work. Is a
Board of the character he suggests really
the right instrument? I am not saying
what it should be. I should like in
dealing with such a delicate matter to
have a Board composed entirely of impartial experts and without any representation of either employer or employee. Whether that is possible I do
not know, but I should like the Minister
of Agriculture, when he replies, to devote
some time. to that question, because I
think success or failure of this experiment.
which personally I welcome, will turn
almost entirely on the ability of the
Board to carry out a difficult task and
to keep the balance as between employer
and employee.

Sir BASIL BROOKE: I should like
to deal with one or two of the points
raised. Senator Leslie will, I am sure,
realise he always reads a Bill most carefully-but some other Members may not
realise that the Bill has been introduced
as the result of the unanimous report
mentioned by my hon. Friend, the Deputy
Leader of the House. During the past
few years, I think since 1934-I am not
very sure of the date-there has been
an increase of 60 per cent. in the output
of agricultural products in this country.
I have used that argument in another
place, and it has been thrown back at
me that the reason for the increase is
the spirit of hope which always permeates
the farmer. I am quite, sure that there
would not have been an increase of 60
per cent. unless the position was of such
a nature that hope was not the only
thing that caused the farmers to increase.
their output. There is, of course, the
inducement which they receive in the way
of subsidies, and also the better marketing conditions. They have benefited to
the extent of about one million pounds
through derating, and something like
£800,000 as a result of subsidies.
The House may be. interested to know
that under the new Oats and Barley
Subsidy Payments, Northern Ireland will
benefit to the extent of nearly half a
million pounds. A payment is being
made by my Ministry, and a further
payment will be made during the year,
bringing the total subsidy up to somewhere about 30s 6d an acre. I am
merely pointing out that fact to the
House, but it does not mean that I am
not fully aware of the difficulties which
the farmers have to contend with.
For the benefit of those hon. Members
who are, perhaps, better acquainted with
the industrial side than with the agricultural end-Sir Roland Nugent mentioned it I may state that the farmer
has several things to contend with.
may take on a considerable number of
hands for the hay harvest, and may have.
to keep those men for a month above the
time required owing to the weather.
He
Those men will, perhaps, be engaged in
cleaning up the place, but the farmer
does not receive any visible return for
their labour. Although one, can compare
agriculture in some things with industry,
there are other points in which there is
no comparison. You cannot compare
agriculture with industry.
I feel that when the Wages Board is
set up it will find there is a considerable
amount of understanding between the
employers and the employees. My experience and, I think, the experience of
most people is that it is really more of
a partnership between the farmer and
the labourer than the strict term employer and employee. Those hon. Members who have read the Report will find
that manifest in it.
Perhaps the Clause which all discussion
hangs on-I do not remember the number
of it at the moment-is that instructing
the Board to consider the economic position when fixing the amount of wages.
That is really the hook on which to hang
the whole of this discussion, because it
does not exist-I think I am right in
stating this-in the English, Scottish or
Welsh Agricultural Wages Acts. It means
that the Board must take into consideration the economic position in the area in
which it will be working.. It is naturally
very difficult for me to give an undertaking or to commit the Board in any way,
and I should be very reluctant to do so.
That direction is in the Clause, and as the
Deputy-Leader of the House has pointed
out when a wage is fixed there will be an
opportunity given to everybody who wishes.
to do so to make representations. I am
convinced that there are very few farmers
in this country who are not anxious to
pay their employees as much as they can,
but, as I have pointed out, the difficulties
are very great.
Mr. CUNNINGHAM: Where are the
farmers who are anxious to pay good
wages?
Sir BASIL BROOKE: I am satisfied
that most of them are anxious to pay
good wages if they consider the financial
position is such that they can. I suppose
it is human nature to resist paying out
more money than is necessary, but I believe the partnership feeling does exist.

in bargaining with the Ministry on the
other side to see that the farmers in this
country get equal treatment with those in
England and Scotland I would be seriously
handicapped if it could be thrown at me
that the labourers here were not receiving
the same wages and that we had no Wages
Board. That, perhaps, is a selfish point
of view, but it is one that those interested
in agriculture should bear in mind. During the past few years there has been a
tremendous amount of negotiation, and I
am convinced that if a Wages Board is
not set up it will increase our difficulties;
in fact, it will make it very difficult to
negotiate at all.
There is, in addition, the Government
policy of going step by step in regard to
all social measures. That was the reason
the unemployment scheme for agricultural
labourers was introduced. It created a
difficulty which, perhaps, hon. Members
here have not met, but which I have
encountered in Fermanagh. It is this.
When a farmer goes to the labour exchange to apply for a worker the onus
of stating the wage to be paid is placed
on him. That is a most unpleasant thing
for a farmer to have to do. Many of them
have told me that they would rather the
wages were fixed for them so that when
they go to the exchange they will know
the wage they have to pay, and they are
prepared to pay that wage. I think it is
quite a reasonable thing. None of us likes
to have to go in and ask for a man and
then have to state a wage which the man,
perhaps, would rather not accept.
The
responsibility for stating the wage rests
upon the farmer.
I do not think that Senator Sir Roland
Nugent need fear very much. I agree
with him that this Board will have to
work in a very tactful manner, because
it is quite conceivable that the Board
could very easily upset the good relations
which already exist between the labourer

clear understanding of the difficulties of
both sides. It would be somewhat difficult to set up a Board which would be
composed of appointed members. I think
the representatives of labour and the
representatives of agriculture would feel
they should have the right to express an
opinion. They are in a better position
to state the facts and keep the Board
wise on all that is going on. Then there
are three independent members appointed
by myself, as in other schemes already
in operation. I think it would be an
unfortunate thing if this scheme was not
adopted, because a similar scheme already
exists in other parts of the United
Kingdom, and it would be very
fortunate if this scheme were not applied
here.

Mr. LONG: In moving "That this Bill

Question proposed, "That the Bill be
now read the Third time."
Major DOBBS: I am sorry I was not
able to be present in this House on
Thursday when this Bill was read a
Second time, but I should like to be
allowed to make a few remarks upon the
Bill.
Clause 1 increases the licence duty on
private motor-cars. The increase is serious,
a matter of 66 per cent. I have heard
various reasons put forward, honestly and
quite seriously, for this increase. The
first is that by reducing the number of
large motor-cars used the authorities hope
to conserve the quantity of petrol in these
islands, so that if war breaks out more
petrol would be available for the fighting
services in the country. The fallacy of
this argument is obvious, for if the consumption of petrol be curtailed then by
the law of supply and demand, the
demand being less, the supply will also be
less, and we should find the available
supply in the country less rather than
greater. The argument would be sound
if oil were
a natural product of these
islands. It is to a very slight degree, but
not to any appreciable extent.
A second reason put forward is that
the. Government wish to reduce the orders
for new motor-cars, so that the motor-car
manufacturers will be willing to turn to
the making of munitions instead of cars.
This reason also is erroneous. I am quite
certain that motor-car manufacturers, if
they were approached, would at once take
up this work without any coercive legislation. Besides, if war comes, immediately, whatever is the production of
munitions, that production must be increased, and the motor-car manufacturer
with his skilled workmen, his up-to-date
equipment, and his organisation, becomes
at once an immense potential source of
supply.
In the last war most motor manufacturing firms were turned over to the making
of munitions, but it took eighteen months
and more before, they were in full swing.
You cannot at once turn a machine
making, for instance, the complicated
gear mechanism of a motor or car engine
into making, say, the screwed and
threaded breech-block of an anti-aircraft
gun. But there is no reason why much
should not be done to earmark every
machine-tool in the British Islands and
allot to that machine-tool its very special
work in war. It would cost thousands,
but I am sure it would be money well
spent, and I sincerely hope that this
matter has the very serious consideration
of the Minister of Supply. The
true reason for this Bill is that money
is wanted. I am very glad they have
not tried to put anything more on the
income-tax.
Mr. LESLIE: Hear, hear.
Major DOBBS: That is pre-eminently
a war tax. It was used for many
previous wars-the Napoleonic Wars,
the South African War and the
Great War. It remains still the
tax for war, although in the last few
years it has been used very much for
social services. When I first heard that
the horse-power tax was being increased
I considered how best I could get my own
car tax down and so pay no more than at
present. I thought it an unjust tax.
But when I realised that the money was
wanted for armaments, not for war, but
to ensure peace, and so that our nation
might act as proctector of the smaller
nations, then my views were changed,
and I, like many others who have heard
the real reason for this tax, have decided
to do all in my power to pay the increased
tax. I do wish that the reason for this
tax could be broadcast through the
country, that is the real reason.
School-Leaving Age Bill 238

In Clause 2 we come to estate duty. I
think the increase in estate duty on large
estates is to be deprecated. It seems to
me that in the case of a residuary legatee
this small tax may make a great
difference to him, and may make what
the testator hoped to leave to that man
disappear almost entirely. I do wish
that the Leader of the House were here,
and that he would make a speech on the
reasons for this Bill, and I would like to
hear him leave aside his usual wellbalanced arguments and his cold-blooded
logic. I should like to hear him speak
imperially and try to put enthusiasm into
us all. Then the reason would get out
to the people of Northern Ireland, and
they would, I am sure, loyally support
this very necessary tax.
is

capital value. If things go on as they

a very easy thing. Like my

deprecate the increased and increasing tax
on estates of deceased persons. You cannot get away from it that the State is
taking capital and spending it as income.
When the Harcourt duties were first introduced it was pointed out that if a man
wanted to leave his estate as he found it
all he needed to do was to insure his life.
But a man may enjoy his estate and go on
in years, and he cannot insure his life
every time the chancellor varies the duties.
The Imperial Chancellor of the Exchequer
and our own Chancellor must consider
whether this taxation has not passed
bevond due bounds. For the moment it
is impossible to oppose the Third Reading
of the Bill.
On Question, Motion agreed to.
Resolved, "That the Bill do now pass."
(Mr. Long.)
And a message was sent to the House
of Commons to acquaint them that the
Senate have agreed to the said Bill without any amendment.

Agricultural Wages (Regulation) Bill.

CLAUSE 1 (Constitution of Agricultural Wages Board)-
1.-(1) For the purposes of this Act
there shall be established, as soon as
may be after the passing of this Act,
a body to be called the Agricultural
Wages Board for Northern Ireland (in
this Act referred to as "the. Board''),
which shall consist of the following
persons:
Three members appointed by the
Minister of Agriculture, of whom
such one as the said Minister may
designate shall be the chairman.
of the Board;
Six members being representatives of
employers in agriculture and appointed by the Ulster Farmers'
Union and other prescribed organisations (if any) in such manner,
and according to such method of
distribution as between the several
organisations, as the said Minister
may from time to time determine;
Six members being representatives of
workers in agriculture and appointed by the Amalgamated
Transport and General Workers'
Union and other prescribed organisations (if any) in such manner,
and according to such method of
distribution as between the, several
organisations, as the said Minister
may from time to time determine.
(2) the members of the Board shall
hold office for such term and subject
to such conditions as the said Minister
may determine.
(3) The Board shall, subject to such
regulations as may be made under this
Act, have power to regulate their procedure and to appoint committees
consisting of members of the Board;
and six shall be the quorum of the
Board.
(4) The Board may act notwithstanding a vacancy in their number.
I beg to
Sir ROLAND NUGENT:
move in sub-clause (1), third paragraph,
to leave out the word "Six" ("Six members') and insert instead thereof the word
"Two." I move this Amendment in the hope
that on further reflection the Minister of
Agriculture, whom we are delighted to see
here again, will consider the advisability
of improving the composition of the proposed Agricultural Wages Board. I
think it will be generally agreed that the
success or failure of this Bill when it
becomes an Act will depend almost
entirely on the successful functioning of
that Board. I am rather inclined to
think that the Minister has possibly been
misled by the success of Boards of this
composition in dealing with other problems, and that he has simply taken the
form of Board that has worked well in
some of the Marketing Acts and put it
straight in here. I very much doubt
whether the regulation of wages and the
regulation of prices present exactly the
same problem.
There is a long history of different
forms of negotiation and organisation
dealing with wages, and out of that
history three types of successful machinery
have emerged. The first is the ordinary
straightforward collective bargaining
which, of course, is answerable in an industry which is not well organised. The
next step to that is a committee with an
equal number of representatives of both
sides and an independent chairman. That
is practically negotiating machinery, and
functions in that way, and it certainly
has been extremely successful. It depends,
however, for its success largely on the
personality of the chairman. If he is a
strong and tactful man that form of
machinery functions extremely well, but
if he is not it functions extremely badly.
We have examples under the Railway
Acts of a committee functioning well
under a particular chairman, and when
he retires it functions very badly under
the succeeding chairman. Obviously
this Agricultural Wages Board is not of
that type.
Finally, there is what I may call, for
want of a better word, the arbitration
type of machinery. Both sides, employers
and employees, meet and put their case
before impartial members and, failing
agreement, the decision of the impartial
members carries the day. I think it is
that type of committee at which the Bill
245 Agricultural Wages Bill-

20 JUNE 1939
Committee
aims, but it has not provided for it. What
I am very much afraid will happen if this
proposed Board is established is what so
often happens in wages matters-that
sooner or later an issue will arise on which
both sides are so far apart that the ordinary methods of compromise will not
bring them together. If that happens
the unfortunate chairman and the nominated members will be in a very uncomfortable position. They will have to vote
with one side or the other, and while the
risk may be small and not serious in the
matter of the regulation of prices, it
might be a very serious matter, indeed,
in the case of the regulation of wages.
I do not think the Amendment is
perfect, and I put it down only in the
hope that the collective wisdom of the
Committee might arrive at something
better. I think it is wise to give the independent members the power of carrying
their will in suggesting a middle solution
of the problem, and I am very much
afraid that if you do not provide for that
in some way you will sooner or later find
a deadlock on that Board, and if you do
it might be a very serious matter. Take,
for instance, a case where the employers'
representatives suggest that the weekly
wage in a particular district shall be x
shillings a week, and the employees side
argue that it shall be x shillings. Neither
side will budge, and the unfortunate
nominated members will be in the position.
of having to vote either for x or x. They
will not give anything else, and it would
be
very unfortunate if that should
happen in the matter of the regulation
of wages.
I am perfectly prepared to withdraw
my Amendment if I receive a promise
that between now and the Report Stage
really serious thought will be given to the
possible alteration of that Wages Board.
the

very small meeting of the Board on

The reason I put this Amendment on the
Paper is partly because I am impressed as
all of us are who live in the country, and
if one may say so who know something
about the question of the relations between the agricultural labourer and the
employer, with the importance of the
occupation of a house as regards the
subject generally. Now when the Corn
Production Act, 1917, was in operation.
the arrangement made by the Board
about assigning the value of a dwellinghouse was stated in this way.
The question was the amount at which the benefit
or advantage might be reckoned in calculating wages, and the requirement was:
The house or house and small garden
attached thereto not exceeding a statute
rood in area, where the employer pays
the rates and taxes and keeps the house
in repair, the weekly value shall vary by
agreement from 1s to 1s 6d in the pound
of the weekly wages payable to the workman.
The
It does not seem that that method of
calculation is the right way to approach the
matter. I have myself at home one small
house which is valued for poor-law purposes at £8 a year, and I have others
valued at 35s. The man who lives in
the £8 house pays a small rent for it, a
rent very much out of proportion to what
I got for the house when it was let.
other man in the 35s valuation does not,
as a matter of fact, pay rent at all. At
all events, the houses there vary from £8
to 35s poor-law valuation. I do not think
you can deal with it by 1s or 1s 6d in the
pound on the man's wages. Again, I also
pay the rates, and the rates in my particular area, which were 61d in the pound
in 1920, are now 92d, that is to say, they
have gone up 50 per cent. The rents I
charge for my cottages have not gone up
in the same proposition. As a matter of

fact they have not been raised at all,
because it was always accepted that the
owner of the house paid the rates and

Mr. McALLISTER: If this Clause is
allowed to remain in the Bill I can
visualise very many puzzled minds.
amongst laymen trying to determine what
is really its meaning. It seems to me on
the face of it that certain powers are
conferred on the Board of fixing the rate
of wages and of the value of certain
advantages, but it also semes to me that
the effect may be entirely changed by
vesting certain powers not specifically
stated in the Bill in the Ministry of Agriculture. As you know, and as the House
knows, I have always protested as strongly
as I possibly can against this method we
have adopted in Northern Ireland of putting in a Clause empowering the Minister
to prescribe Rules and Regulations which,
as I have stated before and state again,
are frequently more extreme and more
drastic and far-reaching than this House
would tolerate or would ever have passed.
Apart from that general protest, I do
protest against this particular Clause. It
may cause a great deal of litigation to
determine what are the powers of the
Ministry and what are the powers of the
Board. I say the Ministry should not
interfere in this question at all. This is
a question entirely for the Board to fix
minimum wages and to fix the amount of
the advantages
advantages which certain local
labourers get. There is no doubt about
it that if the Ministry prescribe Rules
and Regulations and intermeddle under
this Bill and under this particular Clause
as they have done under other Bills, then
this Bill will certainly not carry the confidence of many of the public. That is
not my mind alone. You have only to
lift the paper day and daily to see every
farmers' organisation protesting against
this method of vesting in the Ministry
certain powers which when the Bill was
passed the people never for a moment
thought would be exercised.
In the Pigs Marketing Board you had
the same thing and the same, system. No
farming organisation, no farmer, no
Member of Parliament, and no Member
of this House ever dreamed for a moment
that in the Bill as passed they were
giving the Minister power to destroy that
Bill and by innuendo make charges
against the members of that Board, many
of whom I know to be men of the highest
possible probity of character and honesty,
whom nothing would prevent from pursuing what they believed to be their duty.
I wish to protest with all my might
against this system. I have given you
one concrete instance where undoubtedly
in the minds of the public-not only
the farming community but the entire
public-the principle and spirit of the
Pigs Marketing Act was violated by the
Ministry. No one ever dreamed for a
moment that the Ministry would ever
take on themselves to interpret that Act
in a way that quite destroyed it. It was
a popularly elected Board, elected by the
people, and having the confidence of the
people. What were these elections held
for? The public were not told that the
Minister by a stroke of the pen could
wipe out the Board.

Committee
the passing of this Act and that such

the said provisions of this Act."
This is another point I mentioned on
the Second Reading. I am very much
concerned as to what is to happen to old
men. I have made my own man enter on
his old age at 65 years. Under the Corn
Production Act, as I told the House the
other day, a man could fill up a form and
apply to be graciously permitted to work
at a lower rate than the statutory wage
on account of his infirmity; in fact, he
had to hold himself up as one of the
afflicted and ask the permission of the
Board, in whose selection he had no part
and no confidence whatever, to allow him
to work at a lower rate. Apparently it is
this barbarous state of things that is contemplated in the Bill before us. I do not
see why we should not give a permit
to a man whose age is obvious and
can usually be proved. In fact,
in the case of a man of 65 years
you can prove it though not usually by
looking at the register of births, because
the registration of births in Northern
Ireland scarcely goes back far enough to
be a wholly reliable guide as regards many
of 65 at present; but the man of 65 is
usually getting the old-age pension. I
think we may take it that the man who
is getting his old-age pension is 65, and
that settles it. I do not see why that
man should have to get a special certificate
to enable him to be employed at a lower
wage.
If that man is in employment he wants
to stay in that employment, and if he
has got a house he wants to stay in that
house. If you give any encouragement
at all to the employer to put that man
out of his employment he will probably
have to go out of his house as well, and
where is he to get any other house?
is an old saying that no man over 60
should change his house as it is bad for
health. I see the greatest difficulty in
the country if a man of 65 is to be put
out of his cottage. Where is he to get
another? I do not think that there should
It
be any inducement to an employer to
part with the man. If the employer does
not want to part with him and the man
is content to stay as he is, why in the
name of goodness in this 20th century
cannot we leave him to do so instead of
imposing regulations to make him apply
to the Board? It is rather ridiculous,
and it seems to me my Amendment is the
way to do it, and this is the place in the
Bill to put it in. There is a possibility in
spite of what was said by Senator
McAllister of the Minister dealing with
it, to some extent, by regulation. The
words in the Bill appear to be absolute,
and I do not think there is any getting
round them. It is better if the Committee desire to afford this saving grace to
old men of 65. It is necessary to put
something in the Bill, and that is the
purport of the Amendment.
SIR BASIL BROOKE: I am sorry
that I cannot accept the Amendment.
If it was put in the obvious tendency
would be for the farmer to employ a
man of 65, because he would get him
at a lower rate than a man under 65.
I know Senator Leslie objects to that.
There is a provision in the Bill whereby
exemption can be given to men of a
certain age, either if they are crippled
or infirm or over a certain age. I do
not quite see the objection the hon.

not a disgraceful thing for a man who
has reached the age of 65 to apply and

than the fixed rate in the area. I think
that is the machinery that should be

is an inducement to a farmer not to
employ the younger man.
I think you
would get exactly the thing the hon. and

is an increase of the drift to the town
of able-bodied men. I am afraid I could
not accept the Amendment.

(a) has not been employed by any

(b) has not been in receipt of agricultural benefit under the Unemployment Insurance Act (Northern
Ireland), 1936, as amended by any
subsequent enactment;
shall be deemed
employment."
to be continuous
Mr. ROBB: It is proposed to insert the
Amendment on the Paper, but not in
exactly the form in which it is there.
Difficulties in administration in the second
paragraph have been pointed out, and,
accordingly, I move the Amendment in
the following form:-
"(7) For the purposes of this section
periods of employment with the same
employer separated by intervals not
exceeding in the aggregate eight days.
in any period of two months during
which the worker has not been employed
by any other employer shall be deemed
to be continuous employment."
Paragraph (b) is left out. During the
discussion of the Bill in another place
it was pointed out that a farmer might
deliberately evade his liability to give a
worker one day's holiday with pay in
each period of two months by discharging
that worker for one. day before the end
of his two months' continuous employment and then re-engaging him. The
Minister of Agriculture promised to consider this matter and to put down an
amendment designed to prevent an employer evading his responsibilities with
regard to paid holidays for his workers
in such a manner. This amendment is
designed as far as is reasonably possible.
to secure this object. It provides that
isolated days of unemployment during
any two months' period shall not debar
the worker from receiving one day's
holiday with pay from his employer, provided that these intervals of unemployment do not aggregate eight days in two
months, and that during all those days
the worker has not been employed by
any other employer. The effect of this
provision is that a worker who has been
employed with the same employer for a
period of two months with the exception
of eight days shall be deemed to have
been in continuous employment with that
employer for the period of two months,
and will be entitled to receive one. day's
holiday with pay from that employer.
On the other hand, the right of the
worker to a paid holiday will have been
lost if on any of the days in the two
months on which he did not work for his
employer he was employed by any other
person. This amendment is designed as
far as possible to secure that objective.
It provides for isolated days of unemployment. I beg to move.
Mr. LESLIE: What is to happen in
the event of an employee being sick depends very much on where the employee
happens to live. If he is an out-worker
and does not live on the farm he simply
does not come to work, and probably the
farmer does not know whether he is ill
or is taking a holiday without going
through the formality of asking leave
first. If, on the other hand, the man
is living in a cottage on the place it is
not unlikely that somebody will get to
know he is ill. Are days of absence.
through illness to count against a man's
holidays or not? I think this is as good
a place as any other to ask that question.
Mr. ROBB: An employee absent
through illness does not cease to be employed. He. is still in employment though
he may not be working.
Mr. LESLIE: Then the day he is
away ill is not a holiday?
Mr. ROBB: No.
Mr. LESLIE: He can be sick, and
absent for a week, and then get his
holiday.
Amendment agreed to.

part of the Bill.

Sir ROLAND NUGENT:

I beg to
to leave out sub-clause (2) of

Agricultural Wages Bill-
Committee
20 JUNE 1939
This is yet another sample of the vicious
practice which seems to be creeping into
the Statutes. It is, of course, a complete
and absolute violation of the liberty of the
subject and of British justice, as it has
always hitherto been conceived. Under
the sub-clause a man is to be deemed
guilty until he has succeeded in proving
himself to be innocent. What is the excuse for doing that? As far as I can
make out it is pure laziness on the part
of the people who are to administer the
Act. Clause 7 provides for an army of inspectors, and gives them an array of
powers, and unless we are to assume that
those inspectors are either grossly incompetent or disgracefully lazy this Act
should be enforced in the ordinary
manner. In other words, assume that a
person is innocent until he has been
proved guilty. This Amendment, which
I shall press, affects the liberty of the
subject.
Mr. McALLISTER: Would the hon.
Gentleman read the Amendment?
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: The Amendment is to leave out sub-clause (2).
Mr. MCALLISTER: Of what Clause?
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: Clause 5. I
propose to leave out:-
In any proceedings against a person
under this section it shall lie with that
person to prove that he has paid wages
at not less than the minimum rate.
Unless he can prove he is innocent, he is
assumed to be guilty.
Mr. LESLIE: If the hon. Senator
opposite carries out his intention and does
press this matter to a Division, in the
event of his being unable to convince the
Minister, I shall certainly vote with him.
I should, perhaps, remind the Committee
that a Clause of this description appears
in various Acts. You will find it in the
Scottish Act of 1937, the English Act of
1924, the Corn Production Act of 1917,
and the Trade Board Act of 1909.
should like to read a passage from the
Trade Board Act of 1909, but before
doing so I would remind the Committee
of what I said to the House on the Second
Reading, that a farm is not a factory,
and that there was not very much use
in proceeding as if it were. The passage
in the Trade Board Act runs like this:-
I
It shall be the duty of every employer
in a trade to which a minimum rate is
applicable to keep such records of
wages as are necessary to show that the
provisions of this Act are being complied with as respects persons in his
employment.
The throwing of the onus of proof on the
party charged is, of course, as the hon.
Senator has pointed out, contrary to
British justice. As a matter of fact, the
presumption of innocence is one of the
pillars of the Constitution. Occasionally
Parliament has seen fit to depart from
that, but I do not think that much good
ever came of it. I cannot, however,
imagine a more unfavourable soil for introducing a matter of this kind than the
question of farm labourers' wages. Is it
really to be supposed that a farmer who
takes on a casual hand, and, perhaps,
employs him for a day in the hayfield and
pays him off in the evening is going to
obtain a formal receipt from that man?
It may be that the majority of casual
labourers are unable to write their names
in acknowledgment of the receipt of
money. In spite of the precedents which,
I am sorry to say, are to be found in support of this sub-clause, I feel that this
practice ought not to be continued, and
I shall support the Amendment.
Mr. MCALLISTER: I rise to support
the Amendment. It is a recognised
principle of law that a man is presumed
to be innocent until he is proved guilty.
In this Bill there is the presumption that
a man is guilty until he has proved his
innocence. I think it is perfectly
absurd. Would a farmer in Northern
Ireland keep books showing the specific
time when he employed a man and the
wages he paid to him, particularly
during the seed time and harvest? I
think anyone could see the utter
absurdity and unfairness of this Clause.
In this connection I may point out
that there has sprung up in Northern
Ireland a system of allowing extra costs.
A man is fined one shilling by a Resident
Magistrate, but the R.M. finds that he
is compelled to give 15s, 20s, or 25s extra
costs in respect of a witness employed by
the Ministry. I think that out of those
extra costs the Ministry could very well
afford to take upon themselves the onus
of proof in matters of this kind. If
take the case of a court of summary
you
Agricultural Wages Bill--
Committee
THE SENATE
[Mr. McAllister]
jurisdiction which is sitting day by day,
you will find that the amount of extra
costs imposed amounts to a huge sum in
a year. If a farmer discharges the unfair onus of proving himself innocent of
a charge he will get no extra costs. The
dice are loaded against him. I do protest against this system of placing the
onus of proof upon the defendant, particularly on a farmer who does not keep
books and who has no time to keep books.
This kind of thing is cropping up very
frequently in Northern Ireland, and if
we are going to allow the Ministries to
continue this system we will find that,
contrary to every principle of justice,
a man will have to prove his innocence
in every case.

of the Minister to accumulate the evidence

highest principle of justice-that a man

wage, the only way the farmer can prove

cannot see any other way out of the

Suspension of Standing Order.
Mr. ROBB (Leader of the House): I
beg to move "That Standing Order No.
XXV be suspended for this day's sitting
for the purpose of taking the Second
Reading of the Civil Defence Bill."
move this as the matter is one of great
urgency, and in order that operations
may begin and steps be taken under it.
I
Civil Defence Bill-
Second Reading
6 SEPTEMBER 1939
It is hoped the House will be agreeable.
to passing the Bill through all its stages
to-morrow afternoon in order that it may
get the Royal Assent on Friday.
On Question, Motion agreed to.
Message from the Commons.
The Commons have passed the Civil
Defence Bill to which they desire the
concurrence of this House.
Civil Defence Bill.
Resolved, That this Bill be now read
a First time."-(Mr. Long.)
Mr. ROBB: I beg to move the Second
Reading of this Bill. I recognise that
Members have not had an opportunity of
studying it and, indeed, no person has
had an opportunity of really studying it
in detail.
Much of the power of this Parliament
to legislate on defence matters is derived
from the Civil Defence Act passed by the
Parliament at Westminster, which was
not on the Statute Book when this House
rose. As hon. Members are aware, considerable progress has, however, been
made with many of the matters dealt with
in this Bill and the Government must,
therefore, ask Parliament to ratify those
steps. I propose to explain very briefly
the nature of the provisions of the Bill.
Local authorities already have power to
provide public air-raid shelters, subject
to the inclusion of their proposals in
schemes approved by the Ministry of
Home Affairs under the Air-Raid Precautions Act, 1938.
Part I of this Bill, which deals with
public shelters, will facilitate the provision of such shelters in any area which
the Ministry is satisfied is particularly
liable to attack. In these areas the local
authority will be able to strengthen suitable buildings to provide public shelters.
and to take over those portions of buildings which have been adapted as shelters.
Safeguards are included giving persons.
interested in properties affected the right
of appeal to the Ministry, and providing
for compensation for loss and damage
caused by the strengthening works and
for restoration when the need for the
shelter is passed. Local authorities in
such areas will also be empowered to construct shelters on a road or adjacent to
a road subject to suitable safeguards for
the interests of gas, water and electricity
undertakers and the occupiers of land and
buildings adjoining the site. In cases
where it can be shown that air-raid
shelters cannot reasonably be provided in
factories and commercial buildings the
local authority may undertake the provision of public air-raid shelters for the
employees and on such terms as to payment by the employer as may be agreed.
Part II of the Bill would empower the
Ministry to specify compulsory areasthose which must be regarded as most
vulnerable in which occupiers of factories and owners of commercial buildings
housing more than 50 workers must provide shelters for them. Machinery is
provided for adjusting the rights and
contributions of all parties interested in
the premises and provision will be made
for Government grants on the same scale
as in Great Britain for shelters put in
hands before 31st March, 1940. The
rate of grant will be 5s 6d in the pound.
It is the Government's intention to restrict for the present the area in which
the provision of shelters for workpeople
will be compulsory to what is called the
Belfast Harbour Estate and a few adjoining streets.
Provision is also made for Government
grants on the same scale to employers
(with certain specified exceptions) who
of their own accord have provided shelters
or put the work in hands before the same
date, 31st March, 1940, either within the
compulsory area or within voluntary
areas to be specified by the Ministry.
That is, the Ministry of Home Affairs.
It is intended that these voluntary areas
shall be approximately the land area
contained in a triangle having at its
angles the towns of Larne, Lisburn and
Donaghadee, and the County Borough
of Londonderry. This part of the Bill
seeks also to impose the duty of seeing
that their employees are properly trained
in air-raid precautions on all persons
who employ more than 20 persons in any
factory premises or in any commercial
building.
Part III of the Bill lays upon local
authorities the duty of erecting domestic
shelters in cases where the materials for
their erection are provided by the Government under the Air Raid Precautions
Act of 1938. The Government have
Civil Defence Bill-
Second Reading
THE SENATE
[Mr. Robb]
decided, in view of the difficulty of
obtaining steel shelters of the kind used
in England, and in view of the fact that
the nature of the subsoil in a great part
of the Belfast area would not allow of
the sinking of such shelters, that brick
structures should be provided.
pro-
The
intention of the Government is that
should these shelters be provided in an
area extending fanwise from the Harbour
to Tillysburn, Chichester Street, Oxford
Street, the shelters will be
vided free of charge in the
area in
connection with all houses of a valuation of less than £13. When shelters
have been erected over this area persons
living in Belfast who wish to be supplied
with them will also have them provided
gratis, again provided that the valuation
of their house is not more than £13.
Part IV of the Bill deals with public
utility undertakings (that is, railways,
canals, docks and harbours, gas,
electricity, and water undertakings),
and seeks to place them generally in the
same position as other employers in regard to shelters for their ordinary employees, and in addition makes special
provision to ensure that steps are taken
to maintain essential services.
vision will be made for special grants up
to 50 per cent., 85 per cent., and in some
instances 100 per cent. of the approved
expenses so incurred. The Ministry of
Home Affairs has already approved in
the main the schemes submitted by the
three principal railway companies.
Pro-
Part V deals with the obscuring of
lights. I need say little on this subject,
as steps to secure this have already been
taken. I would, however, direct the
attention of the House to the proposal
that Government grants up to 50 per
cent. of the approved expenses should be
paid to factory occupiers or public utility
undertakers who are required to take
special measures to camouflage their
premises or to screen furnace glare and
glare of that sort.
Part VI of the Bill makes provision for
the medical and surgical treatment of
war casualties.
The local authorities, under their
schemes under the Act of 1938, are
pressing on with their arrangements for
rescue and first-aid work. Many,
perhaps most, of the injured have to be
treated in hospital, and Part VI of the
Bill would enable the Ministry of Home
Affairs to make provision in hospitals by
the transfer of patients, provision of
equipment (and, if necessary, of buildings), the training of nurses and other
means.
Much preliminary work has already
been done, thanks very largely to the
ready response made by the medical profession and the hospital authorities and
to the keenness of the many volunteers
who have come forward for the Nursing
Service which is being formed and
trained.
Part VII deals with various matters.
First of all with evacuation. As
Members will be aware steps have
already been taken to carry out the
surveys and make the local arrangements
which are necessary before any evacuation from the danger area could be
undertaken.
There has been an excellent response
from persons who have volunteered
accommodation for persons sent out of
Belfast. But Belfast contains such a
high percentage of the total population
of the Province that the voluntary response is inadequate to deal with the
situation which would arise if evacuation became necessary, and accordingly
this part of the Bill provides for compulsory powers of billeting.
That
The last two parts of the Bill deal
only with definitions. I should say that
where compensation is payable tribunals.
will be set up and an arbitrator appointed
by the Minister of Home Affairs.
arbitrator will assess compensation after
giving all the persons interested proper
notice and an opportunity of being heard.
I have not gone into this Bill in great
detail. It is a mass of very complicated
but necessarily detailed provisions. I may
say on behalf of the Government that
they suspect-I will not say they are
sure that some of these details may
require amendment, but they ask
the House to pass the Bill in
its present form on the. undertaking that such defects as experience may discover will be remedied by
one or more amending Bills in the future.
But the urgency is such that they ask
that the. Bill should be passed by tomorrow afternoon.
Civil Defence Bill-
Second Reading
6 SEPTEMBER 1939
Question proposed, "That the Bill be
now read a Second time."
Captain PERCEVAL-MAXWELL:
I do not think that any Member of this
House will take it upon himself to delay
the Second Reading or the passage of
this Bill. The situation which now
confronts us is so terribly serious that
no Member could lightly delay this important measure. At the same time,
there are one or two matters which I
think should be pointed out on the
Second Reading in order that the Government may be in a position to give them
consideration before they make mistakes
which it may be difficult to remedy once
they are mada.
I notice that this Bill provides for
public shelters, but it does not make any
provision for the distribution of gas
masks in rural areas. I understand that
a great many gas masks have been distributed in places like Belfast and Larne,
but I think that if you go into the streets
of Belfast to-day you will find that about
two-thirds of the people who are likely
to be gassed do not live, in Belfast or
Larne. They come into Belfast for the
day in the
course of their business
or duties, and the areas from
which they come are supposed to
be free from any danger. Yet if
there was a gas attack on Belfast the
hospitals would be full of people from
areas that are considered to be out of
danger, simply because these people, in
the course of their duties, have to be
in a dangerous area.
As far as I know there is no provision
by which anyone outside the area of
Belfast or Larne can obtain a gas mask.
Many people in the North of Ireland may
be called upon to go over to England or
Scotland in the course of their duties,
and yet they will arrive in England without a gas mask. I am told that in
England and I think it should be so in
this country-it is considered to be not
only a question of self-preservation, but
a public duty for people who arrive in
England and people who are in England
in a dangerous area to be in possession of
a gas mask all the time. I go through
the streets of Belfast, and so far I have
seen one gas mask carried by a civilian.
Yet if there was an attack the hospitals
would be full of gassed people, and
A.R.P. arrangements would be put out
of joint simply because the ordinary
person walking about the streets of
Belfast has no protection whatever.
I would now like to refer to Clause 49
of the Bill. I saw this Bill only ten
minutes ago, but it occurs to me that this
question of evacuation must be very carefully considered, if it is to be carried
out at all. It appears that this evacuation scheme is to be worked on similar
lines to those in England.
I think anyone who knows the condition of houses
in England and rural houses in Northern
Ireland would be struck by the fact that
the two classes are very different. It should
be very carefully considered whether a
great many of these houses in Northern
Ireland are suitable for town dwellers
who have been brought up all their lives
in conditions which the rural dweller has
not known. Some of the cottages are
very small and the rooms are very small.
I understand the rule in England is that
there should be one inhabitant for every
habitable room in the house, and every
room in the house, including the
kitchen, is supposed to be habitable
except the scullery. Why the kitchen
should be included and not the scullery,
I do not know, but I am told that that
is the rule. I do not think it would be
wise to consider that every room in some
of these houses in the poorer parts of
this country is habitable.
to
Again, there is the question of the
division of the sexes and the question
of tuberculosis. I do not think it should
be left entirely to the householder to
decide whether he has a room suitable
for children or not. That is a matter
for the public health authorities
decide, and I think there would be a very
grave danger of spreading disease if the
householder alone were to decide that.
Then again, practically every village in
England has some sort of water supply,
but there is practically no village in
Ireland that has a water supply, unless
it happens to be a fairly large one or is
on the main line of the Mourne scheme.
Most houses rely on wells, and in a
summer like this most of the wells are
either dry or so shallow that a great
many of them are not fit for drinking
purposes. If the population of Belfast
were suddenly thrown on the rural
districts I think it would create a very
serious situation, and the water supply
Civil Defence Bill-
Second Reading
THE SENATE
[Captain Perceval-Maxwell]
would certainly not hold out if there was
any sort of a drought. I have no doubt
that when I read this Bill through there
will be other points that will occur to
me, but I raise these matters now so that
the Government may have time to consider them.
Mr. LESLIE: Like other Members, I
listened with attention and interest to
the speech delivered by the right hon.
Gentleman the Leader of the House, in
moving the Second Reading of this Bill.
I think we are all agreed that the right
hon. Gentleman discharged a task which
was not too easy with his accustomed
vigour and clarity, but I feel I am not
alone in thinking that this is an occasion
on which this House would like to have
had at its counsels the right hon. Gentleman, the Minister who will be responsible for carrying out this Bill
if it becomes an Act. I think it is
unfortunate, perhaps, that the Minister
of Home Affairs is not here this afternoon, because, for one thing, it is obvious,
on even a most cursory examination of
the Bill, that great powers are sought
by the Minister of Home Affairs, powers
which will put a very severe demand on
the personal capacity of the Minister in
charge. They will demand of him the
utmost measure of concentration and of
promptitude in decision. Given that, I
see no reason why the somewhat extraordinary, and necessarily extraordinary,
powers sought for in the Bill should not
be worked successfully.
What is required to make the Bill a
success, of course, will be, first of all, the
goodwill of the public of Northern Ireland; secondly, their exercise of that
quality which they so predominantly
possess, the quality of commonsense; and,
thirdly-though I am not sure I should
not have put it in the first place their
sense of patriotism, for, if I may digress
for a moment, if ever there was a war
that is a people's war it is the war in
which we are now engaged. But in
order to ensure that goodwill it is necessary, as we know from our experience
in this part of the world in general, and
Northern Ireland in particular, that you
should keep compulsion in the background. It is necessary that those powers
should be exercised with the utmost exhibition of the qualities I have mentioned,
commonsense and goodwill.
The Bill before us, as I understand,
is based for the most part on the Civil
Defence Bill, which is now an Act passed
by the Parliament of the United Kingdom. We must remember on this
occasion, as on others, that Great Britain
is not Northern Ireland, and indeed the
implication of that was given a few
moments ago by the hon. and gallant
Gentleman opposite. There are conditions in Northern Ireland which are not
altogether on parallel lines with those
across the water, and it is no use proceeding in measures extraordinary or even
ordinary as though that did not exist.
The Bill itself is not, if one may use
the expression, intended to be an effective
instrument in the active prosecution of
the war. It is intended, as its Title
indicates, for the protection of the civil
population, a point, of course, of very
great importance. At all events, it is
not quite in the same position as a Bill,
which could not be promoted in this
Parliament, for the more active prosecution of the war; that is, for the taking of
the offensive which is necessary to success.
The Bill would seem to have been prepared to some extent before war actually
broke out. Consequently its phrasing
here and there is, perhaps, a little hard
to follow in the light of the present
circumstances, but I do not imagine the
House will be inclined to attach any importance to that. We should have liked
to have had this Bill before us six weeks
ago, but I understand that that was impracticable, not on account of any
failure in energy on the part of any
Member or official of this Parliament or
Government, but because, until the
Imperial measure had passed, we were
not in a position to legislate even to the
extent to which this Bill seeks power to
legislate. The House will remember,
and perhaps will consider me somewhat
impertinent for reminding it, that our
powers in relation to matters arising out
of a state of war are very strictly
limited by the Statute of 1920, which
we sometimes call our parent Statute.
We have very limited powers in that
respect. In this House, of course, we
have other limitations, one of which I
am afraid I shall have to mention in a

of the second issue embodying amend-
