everything that requires, or seems
require, alteration in the Bill can be
dealt with by an amending Bill or by a
succession of Bills, which those of us who
remember the course of the last war will
be only too ready to expect and in due
course to accept.
to
We must ask ourselves to-day in any

spoken. The right hon. Gentleman will,

In dealing with this Bill and, indeed,

Northern Ireland and what do not. The

powers recently conferred. Some of

Kingdom in the sense in which we use the
word and some only to Great Britain. We
ourselves when this Bill passes will
shortly be firing off Regulations and
Orders with very considerable rapidity.

use

in

our

Now, taking a broad view of the Bill,
the first question that seems to arise is
whether it is intended, or rather to what
extent it is intended, that the Bill shall
be regarded as a permanent addition to
our Statute Book. Passages in the Bill are
obviously suggestive of temporary currency and other portions of the Bill might
very well remain part of our Statute
Law. We hope and we believe that after
the conclusion of the present war there
will be no more wars which will require
provisions of this class. It seems to me
that is the position we have to keep
before us in examining the Bill, and we
have at the same time to remember again
the obvious difficulty of making any
definite pronouncement on the subject at
the moment.
We do not want, I imagine, to press
the Government to say to-day whether
or not any provision is intended to be
of permanent or, as I have said, of
ephemeral currency, but at the same, time
we have to remember that it is our duty
to maintain something in the nature of
the control of Parliament over such wide
powers as we are. asked to give in the
Bill. We have a provision in Clause 66

representative of both Houses, to assist

The first point to which I would like

at a later stage than the first Clause,
in the tenth Clause to be accurate, we do
make a reference which suggests that
when we use the word "area" we use it
as not going below a district, that is to
say, we seem to suggest, except in the

an Order made must apply to a whole
area, not, let us say, to a townland or
something less than an
Whether
that is a matter that would cause any

I do not think any serious difficulty may

area.
In Clause 6 we have provisions for the
restoration of designated premises. I do
not think that the decision as to restoration should in any way rest with the
local authority concerned. Therefore, I
think that in the appropriate place, that
is in paragraph (a) of sub-clause (3), the
words "they think" ought not to appear.
The next point arises in, amongst
other places, Clause 14, and it deals with
the powers of the court. It is provided
in this Clause, as in sundry others, that
where an Order is not complied with the
court may fix a reasonable time within
which it is to be complied with. I am
not at all certain that in this case and
throughout the Bill, although again this
is a case which can easily be dealt with
later, we do not want to say the court
may fix it by Order. The point may
appeal to the right hon. Gentleman. Ä
difficulty arose the other day as to
whether the power given to a court of
summary jurisdiction was not subject to
appeal. That, of course, raises sundry
points of summary jurisdiction on which
I need not detain the House.
Another point arises on Clause 20 in
the original Bill, and that is a matter
which I am afraid you would not have
allowed me to raise in Committee. It
relates to the fact that no grant shall be
paid in respect of an air-raid shelter
unless the shelter was provided before the
end of March, 1940. That is one of the
passages in the Bill which seem to point
to its being of an ephemeral character.
The difficulty that occurs to me is that
the passage occurs in the part of the Bill
Civil Defence Bill-
Second Reading
6 SEPTEMBER 1939
-Part II-which is only to apply in the
area laid down by the Minister, and it
seems to me that as we cannot forecast,
amongst other things, the duration of
the present war, and, as the Minister
may have occasion to declare certain
areas to-day and additional areas in six
months' time, it may be that this
limitation of contribution to 31st March,
1940, may prove a difficulty. It was,
therefore, in my mind to suggest in the
Committee Stage that we should leave
out those words, but I think you would
not have allowed me to do so
on the
ground that it might cast additional expense on the public. It is, however, a
case pre-eminently to be considered by the
Government and dealt with by subsequent legislation if the question should.
unfortunately arise. I think I have made
it fairly clear that the necessity may very
well not arise until after the 31st of
March of next year.
Again, on page 23, we have a provision
that a person upon whom a notice is
served may, within fourteen days, appeal
to the Ministry and so forth. That is
where, to some extent, I think, the Bill
breaks down. These periods of fourteen
days are thrown about very properly and
very fairly in order to give a man time
and so on, but circumstances may very
well arise in which the public interest
would not allow an interval of fourteen
days before an air-raid shelter
was
erected. This is one of those matters it
is hard to deal with in a Bill, but the
urgency of the public interest might, I
think, be fairly met by the exercise of
goodwill on the part of the person concerned. That is one of the reasons why
I lay stress on the importance of the
goodwill of the public.
In Part III of the Bill as it was-I
think Part IV now-dealing with public.
utility undertakings, we have, I think,
for the first time the expression "hostile
attack." That occurs throughout the
Bill, and it does here and there give me
a little difficulty as to whether it is intended to limit it to attack from the air,
or whether attack from the sea has been
considered. That, again, is a point which
I do not think need detain us, but it is
a matter to be borne in mind, because
various provisions occur in the Bill dealing with this question of hostile attack
as though it was limited to attack from
the air.
When we come to obscuration of lights
and camouflage there is a curious expression which is not, I think, very clear.
Part IV of the original draft provides
that it shall be the duty of the occupier
of premises to take measures to secure
that, in the event of war, "throughout
any period of darkness, " and so on. I
rather wonder what we mean by a period
of darkness. Do we mean an artificial
period of darkness prescribed by order,
or do we mean a period of actual darkness such as we call black midnight? If
we only mean that we stand to be
confronted with light that we cannot put
out, that is the light of the moon. I think
it is rather an unfortunate phrase. What
do we mean by it?

Now I come to Clause 43 as it wasthe hon. and gallant Gentleman beside
me tells me that it is Clause 49-dealing
with the evacuation of the civil population. I trust I may be forgiven for saying that during the course of a
somewhat lengthy and lengthening experience
I cannot recall any matter on which a
greater spate of absolute nonsense has
been written and spoken than on this
question of the transfer of the civil
population in the event of war. In the
opinion of the public the powers are
merely intended to secure the evacuation
of children. The evacuation of children
may be, perhaps, a very important
matter, but the Clause goes a great deal
further than that. It speaks of the
transference of the civil population.
It is readily conceivable, though I
hope it will not happen, that you might
have such wholesale destruction of some
town or other, not necessarily Belfast, as
to require the transference of the whole
population, or rather what is left of it.
It is a very wide Clause, and it depends
for its success on the goodwill of the
people. The hospitality of the people of
Ulster is well known, and I think
deservedly well known. They may be
led, but they will not be driven, and I
do not think any compulsory power of
billeting would meet with success.
In England, I understand, they have
provided that unaccompanied children
are only to be sent to people who have
volunteered to receive them. They have
also provided for an appeal from what
I might call the billetee. That does not
appear, and could not very conveniently
appear, in the Clause before us.
That
The
is a
matter for administration.
Clause does not deal definitely with any

Ministry intend to deal with, matters in

I have spoken of might very well be of

On this Clause I come to the point
which seems to me most difficult and to
require immediate amendment. If the
House will look at sub-clause (7) of
Clause 43 they will find that the expression "local authority" means a local
authority within the meaning of the Act
of 1938, and includes the board of
guardians of a poor-law union. The Act
of 1938 did not include poor-law guardians. The difficulty that arises on this
Clause is this. A poor-law union in
Northern Ireland is not necessarily
limited to a rural district council. It
may include one or more urban district
councils. In my own case the Ballymoney
Union includes the Ballymoney Rural
District Council and the Urban District
Councils of Ballymoney and Portrush.
am quite sure there are many others in
the country. In such cases the question
will arise which is the local authority?
Is it the board of guardians which comprises the whole thing, or have the urban
districts, components of the union, any
independent authority? Whether you
can deal with that or not without special
legislation I confess I am not sure.
If the House looks at Clause 45, in the
first edition of the Bill, they will see a
paragraph in the first sub-clause allowing
the local authority to permit any other
local authority to use lands and buildings
and so on. That gives a pointer to the
fact that the local authorities are expected
to act in unison. Again, if you look at
Clause 51-the marginal note is, "Directions to local authorities to discharge
functions with respect to air-raid precautions"-you will find that it points in the
same direction, and still more I think
does Section 3 of the Air Raid Precaution
Act of 1938 point to the intention that
local authorities should act together. But
that does not get away from the fact that
if you are going to have a definite

Major DOBBS: I want to make one or

ourselves against. I quite agree that if
you go to London you want to have a
gas mask with you, but I do not think
the question of a gas mask here is so
very important. The enemy may use
high explosive, incendiary bombs and
gas. I think if they do bomb us
shall find that the percentage
casualties would be something in this
proportion:-95 per cent., high explosive;
3 per cent., incendiary; 2 per cent., gas.
we
of
I do not think it is fair to put out to
the population here that as long as they
have gas masks they are absolutely safe.
Personally I do not think they are. I
think high explosive is what we have got
to fear most here. You want to remember this. Whiffs of gas are most
unpleasant, but they are not deadly, and
unless the area is soused with the
gas
casualties will not be great. If there is
a wind blowing or if there are high explosives it will tend to blow that gas
away, and it is not so deadly as if there
a complete calm. You must be
soused with the gas. Personally I would
rather have a tin hat than a gas mask.
I say that perfectly seriously.
was
The right hon. Gentleman the Leader
of the House said something about camouflaging or screening furnaces. A little
time ago I asked a relation of mine, the
chairman of one of the oldest of the
glass works in England, certainly the
second largest, what he was doing with
regard to his furnaces. I spoke to him
as a very shrewd man. Besides that, he
bombed Germany in the last war. I said
to him, "What are you doing about
camouflaging your furnaces?" He said,
"Well, if we get a direct hit of course
that settles it, but our furnaces are
spread over a certain area of ground.
Therefore, if they are bombed there is
every chance of a great number of those
bombs doing no damage. But," he said,
"you started your question with regard
to the atmosphere in Germany and the.
atmosphere here. The atmosphere in
Germany is really very much clearer than
the atmosphere in or around Birmingham
or the Midlands, and the way I should
like to camouflage my furnaces is this: I
should try as we have no difficulty in
doing to put up clouds of smoke from
these furnaces-glass furnaces or smelting
furnaces. If, when there. is an air-raid
warning they put up clouds of smoke there
is going to be the greatest difficulty for
any enemy to locate us." Naturally I
a great supporter of this Bill.

authorises the Ministry of Finance to

be issued after 31st March, 1940, and
any sum issued is to be repaid not later

20 SEPTEMBER 1939
THE SENATE.

Wednesday, 20th September, 1939.
The Senate met at a Quarter after
Three of the Clock, Mr. SPEAKER in
the Chair.
Papers Presented to Parliament.
Ministry of Labour:

Regulations (Northern Ireland), 1939.

Minute assigning Powers and Duties to

Laid before the House (pursuant to
Act), and ordered to lie on the Table.
Question to Minister.
HOARDING OF PETROL.
The following question stood on the
Order Paper in the name of Major Dobbs:
To ask the Leader of the House
whether he can state what steps are
being taken to prevent the illicit
storing and hoarding of petrol.
Major DOBBS: We have got some very
important business before us, and I am
sure it would be the wish of the House
that I should not waste any time in
amplifying my question. I will, therefore, merely ask the question as printed
on the Paper.
Mr. ROBB (Leader of the House):
Every effort has been made by broadcast
and Press announcements to bring to the
notice of the public the fact that, subject
to certain minor exceptions, it is unlawful
to hoard petrol or to store it elsewhere
than in the fuel tank of a motor vehicle.
The police are carefully watching the
situation, and
prosecutions are
being brought where offences can be
detected. Furthermore, it has been
arranged that during the present week
supplies of petrol to retailers will be
restricted to the normal weekly peacetime amount.
I cannot emphasise too strongly the.
desirability of strict obedience to the law
in this matter, not merely because the
hoarding of petroleum spirit may seriously
interfere with the supply required for
essential services, but also on account of
the very grave danger of fire which is
incurred in the presence of quantities of
petroleum in unsuitable places of storage,
and in insecure containers.
Business of the House.
Resolved, "That Standing Order No.
XXV. be suspended for this day's sitting
for the purpose. of taking all stages of
the Agriculture (Emergency Provisions)
Bill when it is brought from the House
of Commons."-(Mr. Robb.)
Message from the Commons.
The Commons agree to the Agriculture
(Emergency Provisions) Bill, to which
they desire the concurrence of this House.
Agriculture (Emergency Provisions) Bill.
Resolved, "That this Bill be now read
a First time."-(Mr. Long.)
Mr. ROBB: I beg to move, "That
this Bill be now read a Second time."
The object of this measure is to enable
the necessary war extension of tillage to
be carried out as swiftly as possible.
The powers of the Secretary of State for
Home Affairs in regard to agriculture in
Northern Ireland have been delegated to
my right hon. Friend, the Minister of
Agriculture.
It is considered essential that the
tillage campaign should be conducted.
under the direction of a single authority,
and the Minister, therefore, has no
power to delegate the functions and
duties laid upon him.
In England and Wales the Minister
of Agriculture and Fisheries, acting with
similar powers, has appointed a War
Agricultural Committee in each county,
and has appointed the staff of these committees. My right hon. Friend prefers
to work through the existing county
agricultural committees but, as he cannot delegate his powers, provision has
had to be made for transferring the
officers of the committees to the staff of
the Ministry of Agriculture.
Agriculture Bill—
Second Reading
THE SENATE
[Mr Robb]
The pension and other rights of the
officers so transferred will be preserved,
and when, after the emergency period,
they return to the employment of the
county committees, their service under
the Ministry will count as service under
the committees.
The Bill also provides that, for the
period of the emergency, the Ministry
may appropriate the proceeds of the ld
rate levied by county councils for agricultural purposes.
The Minister of Agriculture expects.
to receive very valuable assistance from
the county committees in the prosecution of the food production campaign,
and the committees will still award
premiums for bulls, boars, rams, etc.
They will thus continue to control the
spending of sums considerably greater
than the proceeds of the 1d rate.
The importance of increasing the production of food in this country in time
of war cannot well be exaggerated, and
the Government are convinced that our
farmers will do everything in
power to achieve this end.

Mr. LESLIE: I am sure the right hon.

measures which may be considered advisable, after mature consideration, for the
purpose of ensuring the additional production of foodstuffs and other essential
materials-by which I conclude that flax
is mainly intended-in Northern Ireland during the continuance of what is
described as the present emergency. But
I do not think our natural willingness to
do all in our power to assist ought to
lead us to abandon our function of
examining the various proposals laid before us, and with that object in view I
propose to address the House on one or
two matters, partly perhaps in order to
give the Minister of Agriculture an opportunity of defining the position and giving
to some extent the explanation asked for
by the hon. Senator in front.
The Bill itself is a little difficult to
follow, and I think we must remember
that it was first issued to Members who
sit in another place before the Order
made by the right hon. Gentleman had
been promulgated. I am referring to the
Order made in pursuance of powers
conferred on him by the Home Secretary
in London and which we have seen in the
newspapers, but which, so far as I know,
has not been directly communicated to
the Members of this House. In this
connection I asked the other day that
any Orders made under the Defence of
the Realm Regulations should be
circulated to the Members of this House
automatically, and no doubt in this
particular case steps will be taken hereafter to circulate Orders to farmers likely
to be concerned.
As regards the Bill itself, I am afraid
I must say it is a little bit vague, but
taken in conjunction with the Order subsequently issued, I think we can rely
again on that common sense to which I
referred the other day to ensure that no
serious difficulty will arise regarding the
interpretation or application of the Order.
Agriculture Bill-
Second Reading
20 SEPTEMBER 1939
I do not think myself there will be any
wield
serious necessity to
what is
commonly called the big stick in this connection, because I believe that farmers
generally throughout Northern Ireland
will be only too ready to assist. Again,
we must not press that willingness too
far.
Let us look at it from the various
points of view. First of all, the Bill
itself as it comes before us this afternoon
contains no provision for the maintenance
of the control of Parliament. It contains no provision for compensation,
makes no suggestion as to the reconditioning of land, and provides no power
of appeal. Whether any of those things
are necessary or not must, I think, depend in this case on circumstances.
Ordinarily one would be disposed to lay
very great stress on each and all of them.
Again, we have apparently the suggestion
that most of the work is going to be done
by the county committees of agriculture,
but I presume I am right in assuming
that the assistance of the officials of the
Ministry will be constantly available.
If I may take a personal instance, I am
myself ploughing up some land I did not
mean to plough. It seems to me very
questionable indeed whether it is not
good wheat land, and I should very much
like some representative of the Ministry
to go over it with me and tell me what
he thinks about it.
Again, I have, as many others have,
a good deal of grass land which would
certainly benefit by ploughing, but I
have not myself the facilities for undertaking that work. I can, and propose,
to meet the strict terms of the Order
issued, but in my own case and I am
quite sure in many other cases-there is
a great margin of possibilities beyond
that which could be turned into
actualities if someone else would undertake the job. It seems to me that there
is a line which might be pursued by the
right hon. Gentleman, perhaps in consultation with the county committees. At
all events, I think there is a great deal
of indifferent grass land in the country
which could profitably be turned, for this
year only, into corn.
That brings me to the other point. I
think that the right hon. Gentleman
really hopes to get 250,000 acres more
tillage out of Northern Ireland and I
understand the tillage area of Northern
Ireland is only about two and a half
million acres. That is one-sixth part of
the tillage which it is expected to get in
England and Wales. I understand that
in England and Wales they propose to
get another one and a half million acres,
and Northern Ireland, as we have been
told so often before, does not exceed in
size one of the counties in England, the
name of which I need not mention. At
all events in Northern Ireland, in comparison with England and Wales, the
percentage of effort which it is hoped to
make effective is very high indeed, and I
cannot but think myself that in order to
get that quarter of a million acres and
I do not believe it is impossible; I think
it is quite practicable-you will have to
have recourse, rather heavy recourse, to
grass land.
I mean by that land which is not
relatively temporarily in grass as part
of the ordinary economy of the farm,
because, after all, your ordinary farm
rarely exceeds fifty acres.
But you
have
farms exceeding fifty acres, and it is on
those larger holdings that the extent of
grass land is usually proportionately
greater. A great deal of it is comprised in demesnes, and in a great
many instances the demesne Owner
has not the facility for doing any
large amount of ploughing. If full use
is to be made of that, if land capable
of being ploughed and bearing crops is
to be brought under cultivation, some
special provision will have. to be made.
A man may plough the minimum laid
down, but, speaking generally, he is not
likely to decide to be bound by that
minimum. He is quite prepared to do
anything more that can be successfully
accomplished, but he will need something
in the nature of a scheme, and over that
scheme the right hon. Gentleman's
officials will have to cast a friendly but
careful eye.
Let us see for a moment what is asked
of the farmer as regards this extra
ploughing. The extra land to be ploughed
is grass land. It must either have been
in grass for seven years or must not have
been out for seven years. A man will
be entitled to £2 an acre for the ploughing, and when the crop is gathered he
will also be entitled to so many times
the difference between the assessed price
and the actual price, based not on the

Major DOBBS: Twenty odd years ago,
under somewhat similar circumstances, I
got a tractor and ploughed up a lot of
land. In the last ten years I have been
laying it down in grass. I am quite
prepared, if it is necessary and if it
can be done, to plough up every single

important point, and that is that we
cannot make bricks without straw. There
is no surplus of horses in this country.
There is no surplus of tractors, and if we
want to plough up 250,000 extra acres
we shall want something like 1,400
tractors. At the present moment you
cannot get a tractor in Belfast. Is there
any possibility of getting the ordinary
light tractors? It is not much good if
we cannot get these tractors until March
or April next year.
We want them now,
to make it possible to plough anything
like, another 250,000 acres.
Does the Minister of Agriculture propose to control the crop that is to be sold?
If you plough up grass land the natural
thing to do is to put in wheat, then oats,
and then roots. That is what is done in
England, and we might very well do it.
here. Supposing that this year everyone
puts in wheat, next year oats, and the
following year roots, you are going to
have in 1940, if the harvest is good,
thousands of tons of wheat, the next year
oats, and the next year potatoes, and
roots to feed cattle. Therefore, I think
the Minister of Agriculture does require
to exercise some control in that matter.
We shall want an immense amount of
seed, tons and tons and tons. Is it
possible to get that amount of seed? It
will also be used in England, Scotland,
and Wales.
Then again, there is the question of
artificial manures. We shall want artificials after we have grown our wheat
and oats. We shall have. practically no
farm manure, and, therefore, we must
have artificials, otherwise we shall not get
a crop worth taking out of the ground.
There is one other small point in regard to
artificials. I do not know what it is like
in the Minister's part of the country, but
in mine, if we plough up old grass land
we immediately come across our friend
the wire worm. I know that can be dealt
with by Kainit, but will there be
sufficient? Can his Department tell us
of a better method? Whatever you do
do not recommend rolling the ground, because that means more man power.
As I understand the Bill, the person
who is responsible for ploughing is the
occupier. It would be much better if
they called him the landlord. Take my
own case. Practically all my land is let
by agreements on the eleven months'
system, and I can get that land in March,
April, and so on. I am perfectly happy
about it, because I have already made
arrangements to take back some of it.
The question arises, do you want compulsory powers to take that land back
into your own hands? I do not think
anyone who owns land is going to allow
any one farmer who is renting his land
under an agreement which has only a few
months to run to plough it up.
It may
not be well ploughed or seeded, and if it
is not the owner of that land is going to
have trouble in the future.
Then there is the question of the £2
bonus. That was started at the
beginning of the year, and I understand
a man could only get the bonus if his
land was bad land and not good land.
Now you propose to give the bonus to
everybody who ploughs land that is
capable of growing good crops. That is
different to the idea of six months ago
when the first bonus was given. You
have now extended the time until 31st
Agriculture Bill-
Second Reading-
THE SENATE
[Major Dobbs]
December. At the present time, if a
man can get a tractor, he may be able
to plough his land up and get the bonus
of £2 for every acre he has ploughed.
His neighbour may be unable to get a
tractor and he may not be able to start
ploughing until next March or so.
I think it would be very hard that because one man can get a tractor now or
in a few days' time he should be able to
get the £2 for every acre he ploughs, but
the man who is unable to get a tractor
and will have the same crop next year will
not get the bonus. Therefore, I should
like to ask whether it would be possible,
so that there shall be no favouritismfor want of a better word-amongst
farmers, to continue the payment of that
£2 bonus until 1st April next year.
Next door to me is a farmer who has
some very fine cows. If I mentioned his
name the Ministry of Agriculture would
know to whom I refer. He said to me,
"I have got to plough up a certain
amount of land. I am afraid I shall
have to get rid of my cows and shall not
be able to supply Belfast with the
quantity of milk I have been supplying."
I said, "I think there must be some
mistake, for surely the milk you are
supplying is just as important as if you
cut up your grass land and turned it
into wheat and oats." I think the question of the milk supply is very important.
and should receive the careful consideration of the Ministry. I am sure it will,
but the fact should go out to the country.
Then, again, is the question of beef
important? I should like to be told
about that. If we cut up all our grass
land or a very big proportion, naturally
we cannot feed the same number of cattle
as we did before. I hope the right hon.
Gentleman will be in a position to reassure me on these various points.
Mr. MCALLISTER: There is just one
aspect of this Bill I would like to stress.
We are at one with the Minister of Agriculture in his desire to produce as much
food as possible. This is a very short
Bill, and it cannot possibly cover all the
matters that will arise in connection with
this tillage scheme. I have always
strongly opposed the principle of allowing
a Ministry to make rules and regulations
under any Act passed by this Parliament without first giving proper notice
to Parliament of their intention to do so.
I should like the right hon. Gentleman
to appreciate that Parliament should
retain
substantial control of the
very
legislation, under which this scheme will
be administered, and I hope he will give
an assurance to the House that all rules
and regulations made under this short
and hastily conceived measure will first
be circulated to Members of Parliament.
The Bill itself could not possibly cover
one-fifth of the matters that will arise in
the administration of the scheme, and I
trust the Minister will give the House the
assurance I have asked for in respect of
rules and regulations that it may be
any
found necessary to issue.
Further, in view of the fact that this
Bill is being passed with the greatest
expedition, I should like an undertaking
that if, from time to time, we hear of
substantial grievances on the part of
farmers and come to this House with
those grievances the Ministry will
give them sympathetic consideration.
This is particularly important, because
there is no appeal from the Ministry. If
this scheme is to prove a success the
Ministry must have the confidence of the
farming community. If the Minister,
instead of, as is usual, laying the Rules
and Regulations on the Table of the
House in dummy form, so that no one
ever sees them, takes the House into his
confidence, it will greatly minimise the
risk of grievances arising under this
measure.
The MINISTER OF AGRICULTURE
(Sir Basil Brooke): I think, perhaps, it
would be of some interest to the House
if I indicated very roughly the lines on
which we propose to go. Before doing
so I should like to answer the specific
questions that have been addressed to
I am in some difficulty in giving an
affirmative answer to the point Senator
McAllister has just raised, because the
powers under which the Tillage Order has
been made have been delegated direct
from the Secretary of State to the
me.
Ministry. Therefore, there may be
occasions when I would not be in a
position to place before the House an
Order which had been issued. It might
be issued one day and put into force the
next day. Therefore, I would not like
to say definitely that I will lay any such
Order on the Table, because circumstances might arise where I could not
Agriculture Bill-
Second Reading
20 SEPTEMBER 1939
do so.
So far as I can do so, however, I
will keep Members informed of any
regulations that are issued, and I should
be glad if Senators would keep me informed of any grievances they may hear,
because the only way we can get through
these difficult times is by team-work and
doing everything we can to ease difficulties that may arise.
Various Members raised
questions
about the bonus of £2 an acre. I do not
think Senator Leslie realised that that
payment of £2 only applies up to 31st
December next.
Mr. LESLIE: Oh, yes, I did. Perhaps
I did not make it clear.
Sir BASIL BROOKE: That has been
extended from the end of October to
the 31st December, and is, of course, no
part of this tillage scheme at all. Several
hon. Members raised the point, which is
a very important one, of grass land used
for dairy cattle and other purposes. As
regards this question, I think we must.
take a lesson from the last war. Although
I knew very little about farming then, I
do know that there were more complaints
about the phrases "tillage" and "Tillage
Order" than about anything else. Acres
of land were ploughed up which were
not of the slightest value when they were
ploughed. I suggest that the right phrase
to use is "increased production" rather
than "increased tillage." It must be
realised that there are certain places
which if ploughed up would yield no
result whatever. On the other hand, if
fertilisers were applied certain crops would
be grown which were better than before.
The intention is that in areas which are
considered unsuitable for tillage purposes
the farmer will be able to use fertilisers
and thus improve production.
Senator Major Dobbs raised the question of the division of crops. There are
various areas which are. suitable in that
respect, and I think that will solve itself.
There may in the end have to be a
general direction, but I hope not. The
main point that hon. Members have
raised is the question of how the 250,000
acres are going to be ploughed.
The
intention of the Ministry is that some.
1,400 tractors will be brought into use
in this country, and there are various
methods by which we propose to use those
tractors. The most probable and the
most suitable way is to allow farmers to
have these. tractors. I should mention
that all new tractors are now the property
of the Government, except for the odd
tractor that is in store. All other tractors
belong to the Government.
The intention is that a tractor shall
be given to a farmer on easy payment
terms, provided the farmer guarantees
to plough, we will say, one hundred acres.
That is the condition in England. If
he does not plough that number of acres
the tractor can be taken away from him.
I think it is obvious that if a man merely
ploughs twenty acres and then puts the
tractor in the shed or uses it for some
other purpose he is not carrying out the
main scheme of tilling his land. The
other method we intend to employ-and
I think it will only be used to a fairly
small extent is intended to fill up the
gaps where there are not sufficient
tractors. We intend to train a certain
number of men and girls in the North-
West School and the agricultural
schools at Cookstown and Greenmount,
and when trained they will go round the
country in teams as required. So much
for the tractor organisation.
As to the machinery it is proposed to
set up, we intend to take over the present
staffs of the county committees of agriculture. The leading official will be the
executive organising officer, and under
him will be various assistant officers. By
taking over these officials we hope to get
all the information necessary with regard
to tillage and the number of tractors
and quantity of feeding stuffs and
fertilisers required. I think that is as
good an organisation as we can get for
this purpose. As far as possible we will
keep the men who have been working in
a particular area in that area for the
duration of the emergency. As you will
have noticed, contrary to what has been
done in England, the committees of
agriculture here are to continue.
In England they have been superseded
by a committee appointed by the Minister
of Agriculture. I felt that over here
there was a very close link between the
committees and the agricultural community. These committees have rendered
very valuable service in the past and they
know the country and the conditions,
and I thought it would be a loss to the
country if they were superseded. They
are in a position to know the peculiarities
and difficulties of the areas in which they
F
Agriculture Bill-
Committee
THE SENATE
[Sir Basil Brooke]
function, and to advise the Ministry as
to what should be done.
So far as the labour position is concerned, that is a difficult problem. If
this House is good enough to pass the
Agricultural Wages Bill, I think the
position will be relieved, but in addition
we intend to train a certain number of
girls, as has been done in England. I
know there is a prejudice here against
using women on the land. That prejudice existed in England, but if there
is a shortage of labour there is no other
means of making it up, and I think
women land workers will prove to be an
asset later on. The intention is that
they should receive about a month's
training in various agricultural schools.
At the
Another important question raised,
which I find it difficult to answer, was,
what is going to be the position after the
war? The only thing I can say is that
the present barley, oats, and wheat
scheme will continue, and therefore
farmers have this safeguard, which they
had not got after the last war.
same time, I am sure hon. Senators
realise that any change may take place.
It is impossible to say what may happen.
There is no difficulty as regards
fertilisers. There is a division in my
Ministry which is looking after feeding
stuffs and fertilisers, and there will be no
difficulty as far as one can see.
We should all appreciate, and the
farmers of this country should realise,
that we are living, and are bound to go
on living, in very difficult times. As I
said yesterday in another place not only
may there be difficulties but even breakdowns in our schemes, but if we only
realise that one of the greatest
catastrophes in history has taken place.
and all work together as a team, I think
we will succeed in our task.

Mr. LESLIE: I suppose I may take

offiicals, on the lines he has indicated.
As it is increased production which is to
be aimed at, a good deal of information
might be given as to the amount of
tillage a man might do. For instance,
if you are dealing with an increase in
production you rule out some of those
more or less wooded fields which can be
ploughed but which would
not pay.
During the last war I went to one of
the public parks which were ploughed up.
There was a plantation with a notice
showing when the trees were planted. You
looked at the trees, the date, and the land,
and you were not surprised to hear that
the land had been ploughed up as an
example and not with a view to getting a
crop. We do not want to do that sort of
thing again, and it is important that the
Ministry should not rush blindly at the
amount of grass areas shown on the map
without due regard to the condition of
the land itself.
Sir BASIL BROOKE: For the purposes of machinery, the Tillage Order
will actually apply to all land, but it
is obvious that common sense must be
used, and instructions will be sent out in
a common-sense way.

Bill.

Unemployment Assistance (Emergency
Powers) Bill.
Order for Second Reading read.
Mr. ROBB (Leader of the House): This
Bill is designed to enable provision to
be made for persons in need as a result
of war conditions. The benefits accruing
to persons already receiving assistance
will not be curtailed, and it will be
possible to assist those who to a substantial extent have been deprived of
their normal means of livelihood.
The Bill also enables present procedure
to be modified, as under conditions which
may arise it may not be possible to apply
the provisions of the Unemployment
Assistance Scheme in their entirety..
Power to make amendments in procedure by means of regulations is taken
in order that delay may be avoided in
meeting a sudden change of circumstances.
Such regulations must first be approved
by the Ministry of Finance and have the
approval of Parliament within twelve
months after the date of their coming
into force.
Regulations to modify the existing
scheme will not apply to those sections
of the 1934 Act which relate to the assessment of allowances on the basis of need
or to the way in which resources are to
be dealt with.

been made by the Parliament at Westminster. I beg to move.

those in another place than for us, and if
there is any hurry about them—it occurred
to me it might be desirable to get these
Bills quickly-it might be a case in which
the House might refrain from dealing in
Committee-with this Bill and the one
succeeding it on the Order Paper.

Unemployment Insurance (Emergency
Powers) Bill.
Order for Second Reading read.
Mr. ROBB: This measure is intended
to enable the Ministry of Labour to carry
on the duties devolving upon it in connection with unemployment insurance under
the changed conditions now obtaining
and under the more difficult conditions
which may obtain in time of war.
It is not possible to foresee what
difficulties might arise and it is
necessary, therefore, that the unemployment insurance scheme should be as
flexible as possible. The Bill gives the
Ministry of Labour power to modify the
Education Bill-
Second Reading
21 SEPTEMBER 1939
scheme by regulations which must have
the approval of the Ministry of Finance,
and be placed before Parliament.
The Ministry cannot alter rates of
benefit or of contribution to the
Unemployment Insurance Fund by these
regulations. The principal changes
contemplated are the suspension of certificates excepting or exempting persons
from liability to insurance and adjusting
benefit periods in view of the possibility
that records may not be available.
Persons now on benefit will continue
to draw the amounts now authorised and
the Bill provides that benefit conditions
will also be relaxed in favour of persons
taking in refugees and in favour of
persons claiming dependants benefit.
In this case also the provisions of the
Bill are similar to those passed by the
Parliament at Westminster. I beg to
move.
On Question, Motion agreed to.
Bill committed to a Committee of the
Whole House for Tuesday next.
Education (Evacuated Children) Bill.
Order for Second Reading read.
Mr. ROBB: The purposes of this Bill
are clearly defined in its title. Briefly,
it is necessary that the Measure should
be on the Statute Book in order that if
elementary school children normally
resident in Belfast have to be sent to
areas less likely to be attacked from the
air their education need not be interrupted.
Clause 1 lays upon education authorities
in reception areas, that is
that is areas to
which children evacuated from Belfast
would be sent, the duty of providing
proper facilities for their elementary
education. The Bill is not concerned at
all with secondary or technical or higher
education.
Clause 2 enables education authorities
and voluntary school managers in reception areas to recover from the education
authority in the evacuating area the
amount of the expenditure properly incurred by them on the elementary
education of the evacuated children they
have received. It is reasonable to
suppose that the expenditure of the
Belfast Education Committee which is,
of course, the only authority at all likely
to evacuate its children, will be increased if the conditions envisaged by
the Bill should obtain.
Provision is therefore made in subclause (3) of Clause 2 that any additional
educational expenditure, over and above
the approved estimate of the authority
for its financial year, incurred under the
Bill by reason of the altered conditions,
will rank for a two-thirds grant until
the total educational expenditure of the
authority reaches the amount of the return on a rate of 1s in the pound, and
when that amount is exceeded then a
grant amounting to 50 per cent. on a
rate of the additional expenditure will
be made.
I may say that the Lord Mayor of
Belfast asked me how this provision compared with the provision made in
England, and I am glad to be able to say
it is exactly the same. There is a proviso
that the amount of the approved estimate be not less than that of the approved
estimate for the current financial year.
The remaining sub-clauses of Clause 2
are consequential upon sub-clauses (1) and
(3).
managers,
the
Clause 3 provides for the redistribution
of teachers in the event of the evacuation

scheme being carried into effect.

education
authority as manager of provided and
transferred schools, of schools in the
evacuated area would have to prepare a
scheme assigning the teachers in their
employment to the schools which their
children were to attend. Some of the
teachers would be assigned to schools in
the reception areas and some would remain to teach those children whose
parents did
did not wish them to be
evacuated.
The assignment scheme of the Belfast
authority and the assignment schemes of
most of the voluntary school managers
would, I am assured, be ready for

as evacuation was completed, but it may
well be that in the stress of these times
some managers of voluntary schools would
be unable to prepare a scheme in which

authorities in the area to which the
children had been sent. Assignment

Under Clause 4 a teacher will not
change his employment by reason of hist
assignment to a school under the
authority of a different manager whether
an education authority or a voluntary

Moreover, where
teacher assigned to a school under a
different manager from his own leaves the

appointed by the manager of the

would seem to the Ministry to be a reasonable time the manager of the receiving
school may make the appointment and in

evacuated school. We do not anticipate

there is provision to get
to get over
difficulty.
that
Clause 4 also provides that in their
relations with teachers assigned to them
the managers of receiving schools shall be
regarded as the agents of the managers
of evacuated schools. The Clause further
provides that where a vacancy for a
teacher exists in a school in an evacuation
area or in a receiving school the vacancy
shall normally be filled by the appointment to the position of a redundant
assigned teacher if such a teacher is available.
We may find that the number of
teachers required in the country after
children have been evacuated is not equal
to the number of teachers in the Belfast
area. When a vacancy occurs it might
well be that there is a redundant teacher
in the employment of the Belfast Education Authority. In that case provision is
made that that redundant teacher may
be assigned to fill the vacancy instead of
a new appointment being made.
Clause 5 enables the Ministry to make
such regulations as may be necessary for
the proper carrying into effect of the
Act. There can be no question that provision must be made for the education of
children in the event of their evacuation,
and I feel that this measure will provide
a flexible instrument to assure that their
education will be continued with the
minimum of delay and friction. I beg to
move, "That this Bill be now read a

pleased I am that our Government is not
acting in any rash way concerning the
evacuation of school children. I for one
think the last thing that should be done
is to separate the children from their
parents and send them to new homes and
new districts. From the experience
already gained across the water it is
quite clear that evacuation there has not
worked satisfactorily. We find mothers
and children returning to districts from
which they have been evacuated, and
there is a disposition on the part of some
local authorities to re-open schools in the
evacuated areas. People in the reception areas are also very dissatisfied with
the class of children that is being forced
upon them. In some cases children are
not suitable for the homes to which they
have been sent. We know there are
some parents who keep their children very
clean, and there are others who keep
them even more clean, and it must be a
terrible hardship on some people to have
billeted on them children who are not
fit to go into those homes.
I hope our Government will not make
any move in this direction until it is
absolutely necessary. We in this part of
the United Kingdom are in a very
Education Bill-
Second Reading
21 SEPTEMBER 1939
favourable position, and I think it will
be a long time before we see any bombing 'planes over Northern Ireland. It
is not at all likely that they will come
here first. The Germans have so many
friends in Ireland that I do not know
how they would pick out the loyalists
from the others, and that is another
reason why they would be dilatory in
coming to this part of the country.
I wish our Government had not followed
the Imperial Government so quickly in
their black-out scheme. I cannot see any
particular reason for the drastic blackout that is in operation in Northern
Ireland.
Mr. SPEAKER: I am sorry to interrupt the hon. Member, but that has no

Mr. ROBB: I can assure hon. Members
that the evacuation scheme will not be
put into force until the Government is
satisfied that it is necessary.
With regard to school accommodation, about
which Senator Barclay spoke, that, of
course, is one of the difficulties. The
way it has been met in England has been
that in some districts a school building
has been used for two shifts, a morning
and afternoon school. In some cases it
might be necessary to find other provision, such as hiring a hall.
With regard to the question of foisting a teacher on an unwilling manager,
the Bill provides that the assignment
scheme should be made as far as
practicable in consultation with the receiving managers. In case of disagreement the Ministry will act as a sort of
umpire or referee or negotiator, and, if
necessary, the assignment scheme can be
altered by the Ministry to fit any
particular hard case brought to their
notice.

Housing Bill-
Second Reading
THE SENATE
Housing (Additional Powers) Bill.
Order for Second Reading read.
Mr. ROBB: This is not purely a War
Measure. As hon. Members are aware,
important military establishments were
being set up in the Northern Ireland area
before the outbreak of the war, and, if
only in view of the importance of the
Province as a recruiting area, I think it
is clear that these posts will remain when
the war is over. Of course with the war
expansion of the armed forces the housing
of men
of the three services and of
civilians employed about military depots.
will be a bigger task during the war than
after it, but nevertheless this is a measure
which may well remain upon the Statute
Book in time of peace. The need for
housing accommodation in one area is
already acute, and the urgency with
which the powers sought in the Bill are
required are largely due to the needs of
that area although we are informed by
the military authorities that similar
powers will probably be needed by local
authorities in other areas. I should like
to add that the provisions of the Bill
were considered in close co-operation with
the local authority to which I have referred.
The local authorities to whom additional powers will be given under this
measure are the urban and rural sanitary
authorities. Urban authorities have at
present power to provide houses only for
persons whose wages fall below a certain
level and rural sanitary authorities may
only provide houses for persons within the
statutory definition of "agricultural
labourer."
The persons for whom houses will be
required, mostly civilian employees, and
in certain instances married members of
the armed forces are able to pay an
economic rent and the Bill requires that
an economic rent shall be charged. The
only risk, therefore, to the local authority
is that a depot in connection with which
they had provided houses should close
down or have the number of its personnel
considerably reduced. If a local authority
feel that it would be unwise to build
without some guarantee of continuity of
revenue from their houses the Ministry
of Home Affairs with the approval of the
Ministry of Finance may guarantee to
find the difference between the economic
rent and the rent which the authority is
able to obtain up to a limit of 40 per
cent. of the loan charges. In cases
where such a guarantee is given the
Ministry of Home Affairs will exercise a
greater control in regard to the carrying
out of the building scheme. That is the
main part of the Bill.
I should draw attention to Clause 7 of
the Bill which deals with a different
matter. It amends certain sections of
the Labourers Acts relating to a revision
of the rents fixed for labourers' cottages.
Section 29 of the 1906 Act which lays
down the procedure to be followed has
been found to be unnecessarily complicated and an undertaking was given by
my right hon. Friend the Minister of
Home Affairs to the rural councils some
time ago that it would be simplified. As
this Bill has offered a suitable opportunity
it is proposed to substitute the simpler
procedure set out in this Clause. I beg
That the Bill be now read
to move,

a Second time."

Mr. LESLIE: I do not see in practice

Mr. J.
CUNNINGHAM: May I
ask if the Leader of the House
to
could give me any information as
to whether there is any possibility
of the lighting restrictions being
moderated to any extent? In Belfast particularly it is being carried
on far too strictly. When you take
into consideration the number of fatal
accidents that have occurred in the
streets and how difficult it is for the
people to move about the city after dark
you will recognise how necessary it is
that something should be done
moderate restrictions to some extent. I
was surprised to learn that even in
France, which is much nearer danger
than we are, they have not the same
black-out as here. I cannot see the particular necessity for it here. We are told
we must carry on as well as possible during
the time of this terrible war. A considerable number of workers are working
on three shifts and knock off work after
ten o'clock. It is most difficult for them to
get home from their work. If it was
only for their sake I think something
should be done. People who live in the
country are accustomed to dark nights,
but we in the city are only accustomed

Lighting Restrictions
THE SENATE
[Mr. Joseph Cunningham]
to artificial light, and it is difficult for
us to walk about in the dark.
Then, again, the police are very
troublesome to business people. No definite instructions have been given to the
police. One policeman wants this thing
done and another wants something else
done. The Specials come along and they
require something further, and the airraid wardens say something else is necessary to be done. Something could surely
be done to relieve terrible trouble existing
in the city. Furthermore, we must
recognise it is very depressing on the
citizens. How on earth it is going to
work when the day gets dark at 4-30 or

if the Leader of the House has any influence on these air-raid wardens. They
go about at night and some have cracked

not think that should be practised in

Major DOBBS: I hope it will be
realised-it is realised by the authorities
-that the war has only just started. If
there is bombing it will be very terrible.
I do think though the people in Belfast
are suffering from darkness at night
everything must be done to help the
military authorities. I hope the people
of Belfast will put up with any inconvenience in helping them. As to the
breaking of panels of doors that is a very
serious thing. Many of us here have
been in villages where there really was
not a stone standing because the war was

hope that danger will be realised.

I
Sir CRAWFORD MCCULLAGH: 1
was sorry to hear Senator Barclay say
that some of the air-raid wardens have
been doing something they should not
do. I can speak with some experience as
far as Belfast is concerned, and I can
assure the House I have not had one
complaint yet about the conduct of the
air-raid wardens in the city.
I quite agree it would be a good thing
if we had some more light, but it must
be borne in mind that we are falling in
line with what has been done across the
water. Senator Cunningham in his
speech said there was not much danger
of an air raid in Belfast. I cannot
possibly hold that view. Why is Belfast
mentioned in the German broadcast every
week? Why is the statement made that
the harbours of the Kingdom are to be
attacked? That is official from Germany.
Do you think Belfast is going to be let
off free? I hope so, but, believe me,
you will never know there is going to
be an air raid until fifteen minutes before
it is made. It will come suddenly.
Then if the authorities have not taken
full precautions what is going to happen?
Who will be blamed? I admit, and so
will every Member of this House, that
there were a great many wise people who
told us before the declaration of war that
there was going to be no war. They
professed to know all about it. I asked
them how did they know, and I found
they knew nothing about it. There are
people in Belfast to-day who will tell
you there will never be an air raid. Ask
them how they know. Oh, they know all
about it! Those people would be the
very first to blame the authorities if they
had not taken every precaution to protect
the citizens. Therefore, I am one of
those who take the view that we have, to
treat the matter seriously. We have to
be prepared. I admit inconvenience is
caused to people, but I hope we will not
have more inconvenience to suffer before
the war is over. Let us treat everything
as we find it. Let us support the autho
rities in doing what is right, and I am
certain then that no matter what happens
we will have no regrets.
Mr. ROBB: I would like to say in
answer to Senator Barclay that if he has
any definite information of misconduct by
air-raid wardens I would be very glad
if he would give it to the. authorities.

Question to Minister.
PERMITS FOR TRAVEL TO
ENGLAND.
For
Major DOBBS: The points I want to
raise. in this question which I have tabled
are, to my mind, very serious and important. It is hard for me to keep that
question in its proper perspective.
the sake of brevity and of clearness I
should like to be allowed to illustrate.
my question with a hypothetical case,
but a case founded on facts, which facts
can be very easily substantiated.
Yesterday morning at 11 o'clock Mrs.
Smith of Carrickfergus received a telegram to say that her husband was very
seriously wounded, and was in hospital
in London. After the first shock she got
on the telephone to the steamship company to book a cabin for last night.
The
clerk said that would be all right, but
reminded her that she must have a permit,
and that permit could be got from the
police.
So Mrs. Smith went down to the
constabulary barracks at Carrickfergus,
and asked for a permit. The police were
very helpful, and gave her, not a permit,
but an application form for the granting
of that permit. Here is one of those
forms. First, particulars of passport or
identity document. I believe I am right.
in saying that at the present time there
are no identity documents. Still, particulars of passport. But the passport is
not sufficient. You must have a permit
as well. If you have not got a passport
then you must supply with your application for a permit two photographs. Of
course there is no mention of these photographs in the application. Then there
is your name in full and also your
nationality.
I will not go through all the form.
On the other page you have the date of
departure, route, port of embarkation,
and one strange, thing-state fully the
object of the journey. Evidence in
support of the statement should be produced. And then it goes on with the
other information that has to be found.
When Mrs. Smith filled up that application she asked for her permit. The police
told her she could have it in ten days.
Remember that her husband was
dangerously wounded, and she wanted
desperately to get over to London to see
him. Mrs. Smith was born in Carrickfergus, and has always lived there. She
married a Carrickfergus man, and both
these families are. very highly respected
and well known in the neighbourhood.
But she was told she would have to wait
ten days.
Let us see what happens to that
application-after all it is only an
application. It went from the police
barracks in Carrickfergus to Larne,
which takes about 24 hours to the
District-Inspector in Larne. Did the
No.
permit come back?
The application went from the D.I. in Larne to
Waring Street, Belfast, the office of the
Inspector-General of the Royal Ulster
Constabulary, and then it went over to
the Ministry of Home Affairs. You
might say you wonder it did not go to
Scotland Yard. I do not think it does
go there.
Then it came back. The
thing seems to be incredible.
Mrs. Smith, as I have said and I
want to press this point is well known.
Both families are very respectable. She
has been known for years, but it is going
to take ten days to get a permit not to
go to Germany, but to England. We
have to realise that not one of us can
go over there without a permit, and
that permit is hard to get. If you want
a passport you require two photographs.

Oral Answers
26 SEPTEMBER 1939
Mrs. Smith is treated as a criminal. I
suppose I shall be told, "Well, she
might have been a spy!" A suspected.
spy! A woman well known and a
family well known. Last night she
must have been sobbing her heart out.
In a
Another point I want to make.
a very few days' time our Army on the
Western Front will be getting into grips
with the Germans, and there must be
train loads of wounded officers and men
coming back to England. Do you mean
to tell me that mothers, husbands and
sisters of those wounded men will have
to wait 10 days before they can get a
passport? My own wife is half frantic.
She has got a boy over there and he may
go out any day. She wants to go over
to see him before he goes out. The
whole position is pathetic; it is
monstrous; it is ridiculous.
I beg to ask the Leader of the House
whether anything can be done to
simplify the method of obtaining permits
to travel to England and to return to
Northern Ireland.
Mr. LESLIE: Before the right hon.
Gentleman answers that question may I
ask him what is perhaps a supplementary
question whether the Government
really appreciate the fact that the people
in Northern Ireland want it understood,
and claim that it shall be understood,
that Northern Ireland is part of the
United Kingdom, and accordingly people
in Northern Ireland are not prepared
to brook any restriction of their
right to visit other parts of the
United Kingdom? They are equally
determined that those in the other part
of the United Kingdom who want to
come to us shall be equally unrestricted.
Of course we are aware there must be
some sort of restriction. But anything
of the kind indicated by my hon. and
gallant Friend-an application taking 10
days to get through-is to our minds
simply absurd. We hope that will be
impressed on the responsible authorities,
whether on this side of the water or on
the other.
My hon. and gallant Friend quoted
one instance. I can think of another.
I know of a comparatively young married
woman in England whose children at the
moment are in Northern Ireland. Her
husband at the moment is under orders
to join his battery in England. At any
minute he may go. She may not be able
to accompany him, and she may not come
here. Surely it is not proposed that it
should take ten days to get a permit,
because at the moment she does not know
when she will want a permit. Such
points as that should be carefully borne
in mind, and all possible steps should be
taken to see that there is no delay and
no hindrance put in the path of those
who want to pass from one part of the
United Kingdom to another on lawful
occasions.
Major BOYLE: When he replies I
hope the Leader of the House will tell us
whether the Government will make
arrangements with, I presume, the Home
Office in England for the freer passage of
persons holding passports. I hold a
passport to enable me to move between
countries, but in order to get to England
I have to give ten days' notice. Great
inconvenience is at the present time being
caused by that restriction. We here are
supposed to be part of the United
Kingdom. We have free passage between
Northern Ireland and our neutral neighbour, Eire. We can go freely between
here and Dublin as often as we like, yet
we cannot go to England without giving
ten days' notice and obtaining a special
permit. I hope the Government will
make some arrangement to facilitate
travel in this respect.
Mr. ROBB (Leader of the House):
The Members of the House are, of course,
aware of the circumstances under which
this restriction was originally imposed.
Major DOBBS: No, we are not.
Mr. ROBB: I do not want to discuss
the origin of this system of permits at
the present moment, but it is inevitable.
that the institution of emergency restrictions, such as the travel permit system
between Great Britain and Northern
Ireland, should cause a certain amount
of inconvenience, and even hardship, in
its early stages. The Government is,
however, satisfied that the present method
whereby, in effect, travel permits are
obtained through the medium of the local
police barrack of the area in which the
traveller resides, is the most effective
method of securing the desired objects,
and at the same time the most convenient
to the public.

have regard to the essentials of the
scheme-namely, the accurate identification of travellers and the restriction of
unnecessary cross-Channel traffic.

be given covering a number of journeys
within a specified time will make an

people, but it does not entirely cover

special cases a special permit will be
granted. That, however, will be a very
exceptional thing. In ordinary cases it
is necessary that there should be some
method of identifying all travellers, and
in the absence of passports photographs
are essential, and, I think, cannot be
dispensed with.

and to promote a scheme whereby, let us
say, a man and his wife in Northern
Ireland, with a son and daughter across
Unemployment Assistance Bill- 350
Oral Answers
26 SEPTEMBER 1939
the water, may all of them send in an
application, accompanied by two photographs, permitting unlimited journeys.
for a period of one year. I do not know
whether the right hon. Gentleman intended to go as far as that, but that
seems to be a case which would meet
almost any occurrence that is likely to
happen.
Mr. ROBB: I regret that I personally
am not specially acquainted with the
method of issuing these permits, but I
will bring before the responsible authority
the complaints that have been made in
this House to-day. At the same time I
am assured that the period of ten days is
not necessarily enforced in special cases.
If any person can show a special reason
for wanting a permit in a hurry it will.
be granted, but I cannot say within what
time. It would very much depend on
where the person was. The Permit
Officer is resident in Belfast, and, of
course, a person in the country would
take some time to get in touch with him.
However, I am assured that in special
cases permits will be issued within a
reasonably short time, and I would
further emphasise that if a person anticipates the possibility of having to make
journeys he can obtain a permit that will
cover a period of time.
The question of whether there should
be any permit or not is a very difficult
one, and it is, perhaps, not one that
comes within the scope of this Parliament as the system was imposed under
the emergency legislation of the Imperial
Parliament. At the same time, as the
administrative officer is resident in this
area, I shall endeavour to bring before
the Ministry of Home Affairs the complaints made in this House and see how
far matters can be expedited.

PERMITS FOR TRAVEL TO
ENGLAND.
Mr. LONG: I beg to move, "That this
House do now adjourn until Thursday
next at Ten minutes after Twelve
o'clock.'
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: In view of
the extremely unsatisfactory answer given
by the Leader of the House to the question of the hon. and gallant Senator
(Major Dobbs) I should like to move:
That this House views with great
displeasure the inconvenience being
caused by the present permit system,
and urges that the Government will
settle this matter immediately.
I have already pointed out that in normal
times a passport, which is more important
than any permit, can be obtained in 24
hours. I can only assume that the
present delay is due to the incompetence
of those in charge of the issue of these
permits or else they are scandalously
short of the necessary staff. I would
point out also that one may pass freely
from this Province into the Free State,
which is a neutral country, and when
there one could go to the German Consul
and convey information for an enemy
country and then return without a permit.
I cannot go from here to Great Britain
without a permit. I may not use a passport which will enable me to get to any
other part of the world. This is an
unsatisfactory state of affairs, and
should like to move that this House expresses its dissatisfaction and desires the
Government to set matters right.
Mr. SPEAKER: The Motion before
the House is that the House do now
adjourn. The hon. Member will have to
move his motion as an Amendment.
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: I do so.
Mr. SPEAKER: Would the hon.
Gentleman put in a copy of the Amendment he wishes to move?
Sir ROLAND NUGENT indicated
assent.
Mr. J. CUNNINGHAM: I know the
difficulty that exists with those who wish
to travel from Belfast or any other part
of Northern Ireland to England. Those
workers sent across the water find it very
difficult to get back again as they require
a permit to do so. I think we should
recognise that this is an extraordinary
time. Not only have we a very big war
on us at the present time, but there was
trouble in England prior to the war
breaking out. This, I think, was the
cause of these permits being required.
Mr. LESLIE: No.
Mr. J. CUNNINGHAM:
that was the origin of it.
Mr. LESLIE: No.
I think
Mr. J. CUNNINGHAM: The British
Government were compelled to deport a
number of people from England who
were creating a considerable amount of
trouble, and rather than have these
people going backwards and forwards
they introduced the permit system. We
should take that into consideration without pressing the Government to go too
hastily in the matter. We should give
them time to examine the complaints
made to-day, and I am sure the matter
will be put right.
Adjournment-
Permits to England
THE SENATE
Mr. GLENDINNING: In my opinion
the assurance the Leader of the House
has given that he will bring the matter
before the proper Department and have
it thoroughly inquired into should satisfy
us.
Mr. LESLIE: It seems to me, with due
deference to Senator Joseph Cunningham,
that the existence of an extraordinary
emergency is not any excuse for an
exhibition of extraordinary incompetence
and extraordinary lack of consideration.
(Hon. Members: Hear: hear.) I do
not for a moment believe-I should not
like to believe that the introduction of
something in the nature of disturbing
regulations under the Emergency Acts
caused by the European war is being
used in order to facilitate the extirpation of the Irish Republican Army. If
it was desired to take special steps regarding that matter I should imagine.
that, in accordance with Constitutional
convention, those steps would be taken
completely and openly on that ground,
and they could have been defended on
that ground, but the present position is
altogether owing to the European war.
The details have not been communicated
to Northern Ireland or to the Parlialiament of Northern Ireland which really
is entitled to a voice in all these matters.
We are thus left in the present unsatisfactory condition.
I must repeat that in my view nothing
will meet the case except the issue of
permits to suitable applicants to travel
from Great Britain to Northern Ireland
or from Northern Ireland to Great
Britain which could be applied for in
anticipation of an emergency which may
or may not arise and be valid for a
relatively considerable period. That is
the only way I can think of in which
you can meet the case of a person who
desires to make a sudden journey. These
journeys, if necessary at all, are likely
to become necessary at extremely short
notice. Some of us may remember that
during the late. war, when a man had
the misfortune to be seriously wounded
in France, and it was thought desirable
his wife or other relatives should visit
him, a telegram was sent from the War
Office to the intending visitor, and that
telegram acted as a passport all the way
through. Some of us also may remember
that in the year 1916, when there was
more than a trivial disturbance in Dublin,
it was decided to issue travel permits
for passage between Northern Ireland and
Great Britain. The permit I had was
to travel from Great Britain to Northern
Ireland, and that was valid for a year.
I turned up my old permit the other
day. I found it at the bottom of a box
when looking for my foreign passport.
I do not recollect that there was any
trouble in obtaining that. It does seem
to me if these two photographs are
necessary we should be told authoritatively
by our own Government at once that this
is what is required, and it should go
out from this House what steps are to
be taken by the citizens of Northern
Ireland who may want to travel to Great
Britain at short notice and of the formalities which they must go through so
that there will be free transit between
Northern Ireland and Great Britain.
That seems to be the only thing the House
can expect to meet the situation.
Sir CRAWFORD MCCULLAGH: I
for
one regret that this Amendment
should be pressed to a vote and a definite
pronouncement made on the question. I
think that the assurance. given by the
Leader of the House that the matter will
be investigated and if possible made right
should be accepted. A good deal has
been said on this matter. I admit it is
a great hardship. I will give you a
little experience I had a few days ago.
A lady who lives in England and was
here on holidays at a seaside town wrote
to me stating that she wanted to return
home immediately for family reasons, but
when she made application to the police
was informed that she would have to wait
ten days. She wanted to see if I could
do anything to help her. I immediately
found out the gentleman concerned-I
think his office is in Waring Street-and
gave him the particulars of the case.
asked, "Do you recommend her?" and I
said, "Yes, certainly." I said I would
write to him stating that I knew her to
be a perfectly fit and proper person to
cross, and he said, "She will have her
permit to-day." That shows that there
is another side to the question. While
I admit that delay is certainly something
that should not take place, particularly
in view of the fact that in a few days or
weeks we may have many people called
across to see their friends after a big

may have grounds for knowing exactly

their towns, have to wait ten days before
they can get their permits.
I strongly
deprecate the use of what is called backstair influence.

Finance (No. 2) Bill.
Resolved, "That this Bill be
read a First time." (Mr. Robb.)
now
Finance (Entertainments Duty) Bill.
Resolved, "That this Bill be
read a First time." (Mr. Robb.)

now
Resolved, "That this Bill be now
