Finance (No. 2) Bill.
Mr. ROBB: The first of these Bills is
the Finance Bill (Northern Ireland) (No.
2) Bill. It gives effect to the statement
made in the Budget. It is divided into
two parts the increase of Estate Duty
arising on death in respect of estates over
£10,000 and £50,000, and the other
Clauses deal with an attempt to stop
evasions that have been from time to
time made with regard to death duties.
I do not know what is the feeling of
other Members of the Senate, but I
know my feeling in approaching any
question of death duties is one of trepidation, and I am delighted to get rid of
it, I may say with regard to evasions that
an interesting illustration was given both
in the English House of Commons and
in another place in Northern Ireland.
It was this: Supposing a man had an
estate of £100,000. He is getting on,
and has a son, and he thinks it would be
a good thing if he could avoid death
duties. The father lends the son
£50,000. Now, if that transaction
happens three years before the man's
death I should say he gives the son
£50,000 that £50,000 escapes death
duties. The next step is that the son
lends that £50,000 to his father, who
pays five per cent. interest. The father
pays that £2,500 a year to the son and
the son uses that amount by taking out a
policy of insurance upon the life of the
father. The father dies. The result is
that the father owes the son £50,000,
and that being a debt due from the
estate, no duty is payable on that
£50,000. And the same £50,000 which
he gave the son more than three years
before death also escapes death duties.
The result of that pretty little transaction is that the father's estate escapes
duty from the £100,000.
That is a very ingenious thing. I
wish I had discovered it, but I did not.
At any rate, it came under the notice
of the revenue authorities in England,
and provision is made or attemptedwhether it will be successful or not no
one knows-to get over evasions of that
sort. There are other cases given to me,
but they are SO complicated that,
frankly, I do not understand them, and
I do not propose to try to explain them
to the House. That is the sort of thing
the remaining provisions of the Bill are
to meet. I beg to move.
Question proposed, "That the Bill be
now read a Second time."
measure
Mr. LESLIE: I do not suppose that
the House will reject a
of
this kind which has one way or another
escaped the vicissitudes of another place,
but I must point out that it was impossible to follow the example given by the
right hon. Gentleman. He seemed to
speak of a loan from the father to the
son.
Mr. ROBB: I started by saying a loan
from the father to the son. It was a
gift from the father to the son, followed
by a loan from the son to the father.
2 NOVEMBER 1939
Finance Bill

Government Loans Bill
in any way alter the law. They are
introduced, and after having been read
the Second time here and in another
place, they are sent to a Joint Committee of the two Houses to go through
them to see if in any particular they do
not conform to the law as it exists at
the moment, and then they are subsequently passed. I do not propose to
deal with these in any way, only just
to state, as I have already stated, that
these Bills gather into one Act of Parliament all the various provisions with
regard to entertainments duty that are
scattered about a number of Acts of
Parliament. It is purely for the matter
of convenience this is done. The Bill
has been thoroughly examined already.
I beg to move.
Mr. LESLIE: I see. That makes it
different. An absolute gift was quite
safe. I pass from that. No doubt there
have been people in the past who
exercised ingenuity, perfectly legiti
mately, in escaping any situation they
did not want. I leave that. The only
point I wish to stress is that since the
days of Sir William Harcourt there have
been persistent and repeated attacks on
accumulated capital in the form of death
duties. The result is very apparent in
England. A lot of country estates are
being disintegrated and divorced from
the possession of the original owner.
The same thing is happening to some
extent in Northern Ireland. Proposals
of this kind will accentuate that. I do
not myself look forward to the future
state of a country which is deprived of
people with a certain amount of estate
in the country, whether the estate is in
terms of land or in terms of money.
We have since 1894 had many experiences of increases of death duties,
but I do not remember a case where we
heard of a decrease. Whether, when
the present emergency subsides, something of that sort will be done I do not
know. I repeat the protest I made in
this House before against the growing
practice of the State of taking the
capital of a deceased man and spending
it as income of the State. I am sure it
is unsound, and I do not think it will
inure to the benefit of the community.
As the Bill has passed another place,
I do not propose to object to it to the
point of dividing the House. I do think
it is a most unfortunate step in the
advance on what I consider to be the
downward road.
On Question, Motion agreed to.
""
Moved, That the Bill be committeed.' (Mr. Robb.)
On Question, Motion negatived.
Ordered, That the Bill be read the
Third time on Thursday next.
Finance (Entertainments Duty) Bill.
Mr. ROBB: The other two Bills are
consolidation Bills. I will take the
Entertainments Duty Bill first. I should
explain to the House that these are
consolidation Bills only. They do not

Government Loans Bill.
Mr. ROBB: This Bill is in exactly

Measure. It is a consolidation Bill,

CLAUSE
proceedings).
5 (Penalties and legal
(6) Notwithstanding any enactment prescribing the time within
which proceedings may be brought
before a court of summary jurisdiction,
proceedings for an offence under this
section may be brought either within
twelve months from the date of the
commission of the alleged offence, or
within three months from the date on
which evidence sufficient in the opinion
of the Ministry of Agriculture to
justify a prosecution for the offence
comes to its knowledge, whichever is
the longer, and for the purposes of
this provision a certificate, purporting
to be signed on behalf of the said
Ministry as to the date on which such
evidence as aforesaid came to its
knowledge, shall be conclusive evidence
thereof.
Mr. LESLIE: I beg to move, to leave
out sub-clause (6). When speaking on
the Second Reading, I mentioned that
there were two Clauses which seemed to
me to be rather objectionable. One of
those Clauses has been omitted from the
Bill by a very decisive vote in this House
during the summer months, and the
second is the one to which I now want to
direct attention. The sub-clause before
us-sub-clause (6)-was not in the Bill
as it was originally presented in another
place. It was something in the nature.
of an afterthought, I presume, on the
part of the Ministry, and I am afraid I
must say it is not an example of the old
adage that second thoughts are best,
because I think the sub-clause would
have been very much better left out of
the Bill.
No clause of the same class appears in
the English Agricultural Wages Act of,
I think, 1924, nor can I find anything
like it in any of the Trade Board Acts.
I do find something like it in the Unemployment Insurance Consolidation Act of
1936, but the circumstances where it
appears there are entirely different from
the circumstances as presented to us
to-day. In the case of the Unemployment Insurance Act, the same Clause
deals with such matters as the nonstamping of cards, which does, of course,
impose something in the nature of continuing damage on the person whose
cards ought to have been stamped. In
this case there is nothing of that nature
at all. The transaction is done and done
with.
I do not like, in any case, what I
might describe as altering the ordinary
normal law for a special case, and that
is, in the case of an offence triable before
courts of summary jurisdiction. a period
of six months after the alleged commission of the offence is to be the period
during which a prosecution may be
brought. I do not think there is any
particular justification for departing from
that standard in the present case, the
more so as nothing of the sort occurs, as
far as I know, in the Trade Boards Act,
and it is hard to see why the Ministry
should differentiate against the farmer.
It is not the sort of thing the farmer
likes. He does not like being irritated.
He would prefer, if he is allowed, to
regard the Ministry as his protector and
friend, rather than his enemy always
Report of Amendments
Agricultural Wages Bill-
2 NOVEMBER 1939

seeking to pop at him from behind a
bush. That renders this particular subclause distinctly unpopular.
After all, what is the reason for it,
and what is the effect of it? The effect
is apparently to extend the time during
which a prosecution may be brought to
twelve months. If the Clause limited
itself by saying that for special reasons
it might be desirable to extend the six
months limit to twelve months, that
would be one thing, but it does not stop
there. It goes on to say that a prosecution may be brought within three months
after the matter comes to the notice of
the Ministry of Agriculture. That hangs
the liability round the farmer's neck for
all eternity. As I reminded the House
on the Committee stage, we are dealing
with a criminal prosecution; we are not
dealing with a civil action. Apparently
the suggestion is that for practically all
time this charge is to hang over the head
of the luckless farmer who probably does
not keep very many accounts at all.
Passing from that, I must point out to
the Minister of Agriculture-and I am
very glad he is here to hear me say itthat he has a very responsible duty as
Minister of Agriculture, but he has not
the responsibility or duty of his
Majesty's Attorney-General. It is for
the Attorney-General to say whether a
prosecution should be launched, not for
the Minister of Agriculture. The
Minister, under this Bill, has no power
to prosecute, none at all. If you left
the thing like this, what would happen
would be that in the event of a prosecution, if it was more than twelve months
old, the unfortunate court would be
embarrassed by the knowledge that the
Minister had already expressed
opinion, and the Minister would not be
in the box. The position is, I think,
absurd. You cannot have the court,
even a court of summary jurisdiction-as
I used the word "embarrassed' I do not
an
think I can improve upon it-embarrassed by a matter of that kind.
Under this Bill the Minister has no
power of prosecution at all, and therefore I am afraid the knowledge of the
Minister is of no importance whatever,
and has nothing whatever to do with the
case. It is like the works of a watch.
The fact that before a prosecution after
twelve months, the court must know
that the Minister has, so to speak, prejudged the case and expressed an opinion
on the propriety of prosecution cannot
fail to have a disturbing influence. It is
a thing that ought not to be done.
The right thing to do is for this
House to expunge the sub-clause
altogether and rely for the proper
observance of the Act on the rather substantial powers conferred on the
Ministry's inspectors under Clause 7. I
think there are ample powers there to
see that the Act is complied with. I
cannot agree that this particular subclause ought to be inserted in the Bill,
and I can only regard it as having been
inserted, as I say, as an afterthought
Ι
in another place without due consideration of its various implications.
Question proposed, "That sub-clause
(6) be left out."

personal opinion is that the Summary

for a prosecution to be brought, but the

should be amended in the way I have
suggested if
if the hon. and learned

Report of Amendments
The following Amendment stood on
the Order Paper in the name of Mr.
Robb:
At the end of Clause 5, to add the
following new sub-clause :-

a
(12) It shall be the duty of every
person who employs a worker in agriculture, in any case to which
minimum rate of wages is applicable,
to keep such records of time worked
and of wages paid as are necessary to
show that the provisions of this Act
are being complied with as respects
that worker, and if such an employer
fails to do so he shall be liable, on
summary conviction, in respect of each
offence to a fine not exceeding two
pounds and to a fine not exceeding one
pound for each day on which the
offence is continued after conviction."
Mr. ROBB: The House will remember
that on the Committee stage of this Bill
strong exception was taken in this House
to the form of sub-clause (6) of Clause
5, which threw upon a person summoned
under the Act the onus of proving himself innocent. There were constitutional
objections, and they were stated SO
vigorously that the Government accepted
them. At the same time the Department was very anxious that there should
be available for the inspectors of the
Ministry some record of what the farmer
had done with regard to the payment of
wages to any employee who came within
the scope of the Act. Accordingly, it
has been suggested that it could best be
done by amending the Bill in the form.
which I have put down on the Order
Paper. The particulars required are
quite simple-the time a man works and
the wages he is in fact paid. Any person ought to be able to keep records of
that sort. The House will see that if
an offence is committed the penalty is
not extravagant. It is £2, although it
goes on to say that, after conviction, if
a man continues to fail to keep records,
he will be fined £1 for each day on
which the offence is committed. Those
particulars will, the Department thinks,
satisfy what they need in order to see
that the Act is carried into effect. It
ought not to be difficult for any person
to supply these returns. They are as
simple as can be made.
Mr. LESLIE: Having regard to subsection 4 of section 7 of the Trade Boards
|
Act, 1909, as amended by the Trade
Boards Act, 1918, it is very difficult to
oppose the inclusion of some words of
this character. The point is, you are
the
applying, rightly or wrongly, to
farm provisions hitherto confined to the
factory. Well, for reasons that appeal
to both Houses of Parliament more or
less it has been decided to do that, but
the fact remains, and it cannot be
altered, a farm is not a factory. It is
not run on the same lines, except
theoretically according to a certain leaflet
issued by the right hon. Gentleman's
Department
years ago. But,
speaking generally, you cannot run a
farm as you would run a factory. Hence
it happens that some of these details
which
and learned
the right hon.
Gentleman has spoken of as being simple
are not such as could be compiled by anybody. I am afraid I do not think that
will work out in practice.
some
The great difficulty I have as regards
the proposed new sub-clause is the fact
that this Bill applies to piece-work and
deals with, amongst other things, perquisites as well as wages. I do not think
any farmer could possibly be expected
to keep a record of the amount of time
a man spends over piece-work. A
farmer, if he employs people at all, has
possibly 50 acres to cover. Many a
50-acre farmer does not require labour
at all. The farmer has not time to go
round watching how long a man takes
for cutting a hedge which is set to him
as piece-work. Moreover, it is not hedge.
cutting only is set. There is the question
of field drains. A farmer having a field.
drained will probably look at it several
times but he cannot stand over the man
and see how many hours he takes over
it. It is not practicable.
I am afraid, on the whole, there are
difficulties about the proposal, although
I am not for the reason I mentionedthe reference to the Trade Boards Actinvincibly hostile to something of this
nature. The proposed Amendment refers
to such records as are necessary.
is going to be the judge of necessity?
Who
Mr. ROBB: Ultimately the court.
Mr. LESLIE: It depends very much
on the word court. If the court consists
of a country magistrate, a man who

Report of Amendments
country point of view, there probably

think, on the whole, it is quite a fair
burden to put on the farmer. I showed
this particular clause to two farming
friends of mine. They came to see me
on a matter which will interest the right
hon. Gentleman. They came to discuss
with me the terms on which they could
take a rough field of mine for breaking
up in order to grow corn. When we had
settled that, I showed them this Clause
and asked what they thought about it.
Both said they did not object to a fixed
wage. The Bill deals with a minimum
wage. They objected to anything which
required the farmer to keep elaborate
books, because, they said, they do not do
it. These people were relatively large
farmers. They do not keep books like
that.
You have in Clause 7, to which I
referred a few minutes ago, power for the
inspector, if there is one, to go and look
at wage sheets and get the information
he wants. He probably will get it, but
I do not think that we would be wise in
inserting in the Bill anything requiring
the farmer to keep what will be relatively
elaborate books. These books will be
elaborate. It is simple enough to put
down the man's name, the time worked
and the wage. What I do myself is, I
put down the man's name and the
amount of wage for so many days, and
leave it at that.
I do not go into the
question of hours. At the same time,

paid. I think that would meet all
reasonable requirements. I do admit
that the requirements on the whole are
not unreasonable, but I do not think
they are quite workable.

Questions to Minister.
PRICE OF PETROL IN STORAGE
TANKS.
Mr. MAGUIRE: I beg to ask the
Leader of the House-
Whether many commercial users of
petrol who possess storage tanks for
which they pay a licence are obliged
by the present regulations to obtain
supplies of petrol from dealers at a
price higher than that paid for petrol
supplied in bulk to a storage tank, and
whether the Government could make
representations to the proper authorities to have the regulations altered, so
that the owners of petrol storage tanks
might enjoy the advantage which the
installation of these tanks was expected
to afford to them.
The reason I have put this question on
the Paper is that at the present time
business people in a small or medium
way are prevented by the restrictions
from purchasing petrol in bulk quantities. The condition of the Petroleum Board
is that they do not supply quantities
under 200 gallon lots. Merchant users
in a large way who have got high allotments, over 200 gallons per month, are
enabled to purchase in bulk lots. A good
many people for many years past have
installed on the premises petrol storage
tanks for use in their own business, not
for sale.
Just to give an instance of what does
take place, I have in mind one firm.
Prior to the restrictions their consumption was something like 280 gallons per
month. This was cut down to 97 gallons
per month, that is, one-third of the
normal consumption. Firms in that
position are prevented from purchasing
petrol in bulk quantities, and they are
compelled to purchase from outside
dealers at a very much increased price.
The Petroleum Board have refused to
allow such traders to add the different
months together for instance, the 97
and 97 would bring it to practically 200
gallons. They have vouchers for that
quantity, but the Board refuse to allow
them to join one month to the other in
order to get the bulk price. I feel this
is a great grievance and traders in a
large way have an advantage over
traders in a medium and small way.
feel the Government should give way to
some extent and that the Petroleum
Board should allow such traders to join
two months together in order that they
might gain the bulk supply of 200
gallons per month.
I
The petrol tanks were installed by the
firm I refer to over thirteen years ago
and they have been buying petrol in
bulk during that period. Since the restrictions they have been compelled, at
great inconvenience, to go outside and
purchase the quantity of petrol from
merchants and pay a higher price.
Another point is that holders of petrol
tanks have to pay a licence to the Government and the tanks are of no service
if the holders are not allowed to
use them. I would suggest that the
Petroleum Board should allow traders in
such a way to link up one, two or three
months together in order to get the bulk
supply. They have vouchers for a certain.
number of gallons. They cannot put it to
use in a dual way. They must be used
on one occasion. I do not think any
Government would be taking any risk in
making this slight concession. That is
the point I wish to put in the question.
THE SENATE
Oral Answers

vehicles so as to enable them to obtain
delivery in bulk, but the hon. Senator
will appreciate that the Government
could not ask that the normal trade procedure should be altered in the interest
of a few consumers.
I would like to emphasise again that
the matters raised in this question are not

of trade custom.

to enable distributors to effect economy
in delivering supplies. and is as necessary

obtain the ration granted for each car
from any retailer in the same manner as
private motorists obtain their petrol.

be filled from such supplies.

DAMAGE BY RABBITS AND RATS.
The following question stood on the
Order Paper in the name of Mr. Samuel
Cunningham:--
To ask the Leader of the House
whether he can state if any steps have
been taken, or will be taken, to reduce
the number of rabbits and rats in
Northern Ireland, so that the loss
caused by these vermin to crops, etc.,
may be reduced as much as possible,
and whether, in view of the action
taken by the Minister of Agriculture
in Great Britain, he, proposes to
arrange any similar campaign to that
which has commenced in Great Britain
on 6th of this month.
Mr. S. CUNNINGHAM: I would
like to remind hon. Senators that this
question has been before the House
previously, but I do not know that anything has been done in the matter. At
the present time there is a Rat Week
campaign in progress in England, where
the Ministry of Agriculture seems to
realise that a great deal of harm is done by
rats. I understand it has been estimated
that in England fifty million pounds
worth of damage is done annually by rats
and mice. The Ministry of Agriculture
has asked the farmers to sacrifice their
half-fed pigs by killing them now instead
of finishing their fattening in order to
provide more food. I am not going to
criticise that, because the Ministry must
have good reasons for making such a request, but they should not overlook the
fact that perhaps fifty million pounds
worth of wheat, oats, potatoes and other
crops is being used by rats and mice. As
far as I know these vermin are of no
value. I do not think that even their
skins are worth anything.
With regard to rabbits, the last Parliament in England passed an Act for reducing the number of rabbits. If a man has
rabbits on his ground and they are
injurious to his neighbour's crops he
must reduce them. In England they are
realising that rabbits are a perfect curse.
I think I am right in saying that six
rabbits consume as much grass or food
as a sheep, and when we realise how many
thousands of rabbits there are the
amount of food they must consume is
immense. I think it would be better to
have sheep rather than rabbits feeding
on this food.
When travelling on the railway I
noticed a cutting in the railway embankment honeycombed with rabbit holes. I
do not think that rabbits are of any use
to the railways as far as sporting rights
are concerned, and it would be a good
thing if the Ministry of Agriculture
asked the railways to fumigate these
warrens with the object of reducing the
number of rabbits. If the Ministry
would devote some attention to this
matter it might be the means of saving
a considerable amount of food, and it
might also enable us to have finished pigs
and pork sooner than we have at present.
Mr. BARCLAY: I would like to support what Senator Cunningham has said.
From experience I know that rats are
very destructive. Two years ago I myself had an invasion of rats. They got
into the potatoes, and had I not got
them in time they would have devoured
all the potatoes I had in my garden. In
three weeks I destroyed thirty rats.
agree with the right hon. Gentleman, who
raised this question, that something
should be done to exterminate these
vermin in Northern Ireland, and
especially in the city of Belfast. Round
about the docks there must be a
tremendous loss of foodstuffs owing to the
presence of rats. I remember that
during the last war there was an invasion
of tens of thousands of rats along the
water's edge at the docks and the shipyards. and, except for an occasional
attempt, I never heard of anything being
done to destroy them.
I know that rabbits also are very
destructive little animals, although they
are very nice and are now the subject of
a popular song called "Run, rabbit,
run. At the same time, I think the
best thing to do would be to shoot them.
I have seen from thirty to forty perches
of oats growing, and for a distance of
two or three perches from the ditch
where the rabbit burrow was there was

not a blade of corn growing. The farmer

Mr. ROBB: May I say that the
responsibility under the Rats and Mice
Destruction Act, 1919, rests with the
local authorities, and it is the intention
of the Ministry of Home Affairs to issue
a circular drawing the attention of the
local authorities concerned to their
powers and duties under this Act. The
Ministry does not consider that there is
any need at present for a similar campaign to that which is taking place in
Great Britain this week.
The position in Great Britain as regards rabbits is that a recent Measure,
the Prevention of Damage by Rabbits
Act, 1939, gives powers to local authorities in Great Britain to require the
prevention of damage by rabbits. If the
occupier fails to comply with a notice
served upon him he is liable on summary
conviction to a fine not exceeding £25
and to a further fine not exceeding £5
for each day on which failure continues
after conviction. The Ministry of Agriculture in Northern Ireland is not, howin the fortunate position of
ever,
possessing such powers, as the Act does
not apply to this part of the United
Kingdom.
I should add that the Ministry of
Agriculture is conscious of the fact that
much valuable food is wasted by the
depredations of vermin and will consider
carefully what steps can best be taken
within the somewhat limited powers
which exist at the moment. If only we
had a Pied Piper the problem would be
easily solved. A gun licence costs 10s,
and that fee has not been altered, I
believe, for the last sixty years.

Mr. LESLIE: We are approaching
what I might describe perhaps as the
final stage of the launch of what was
described on the Second Reading as a
somewhat new experiment. It may be
remembered that I suggested on the last
stage that perhaps the House would like
to consider whether, in view of the
altered circumstances since this Bill was
read a Second time, some time for the
commencement of the operation of the
Act should be inserted in the Bill. I
took advice on that subject, and, on the
whole, I came to the conclusion that perhaps there was no more object in doing
anything of that nature, the reason
being that any action of that sort might
be taken as being to some extent mere
opposition to the enactment of the
385 Agricultural Wages Bill-

9 NOVEMBER 1939
Third Reading
Measure which had been discussed and
considered by both Houses, and would,
perhaps, not serve any useful purpose.
As a matter of fact, of course there is no
date in the Bill itself for commencement,
but it is implied in the Bill that the
preliminary operations must necessarily
take some time, and that before
any
Order of the new Agricultural Wages
Board can become operative some weeks,
and probably months, must necessarily
elapse. During that period it will be
possible for the Board to take account of
the prices for agricultural produce which
by that time will have been fixed. We
saw, for instance, in this morning's
paper that in England it is proposed to
raise the datum line for the oats subsidy from 8s to 9s a cwt., which means
raising the price from 1s a stone to
1s 1d. Of course the Board will be able
to consider whether such a price is likely
to be an inducement to the farmer in
Northern Ireland to grow oats, and
whether it will afford any opportunity
for increasing his expenditure.
It is not unlikely that before the time
comes for the Board to promulgate its
decision-and it will be remembered that
any decisions of the Board can only have
effect from some date subsequent to the
promulgation of the Order-there is no
retrospective effect as regards the Orders.
-it may be that the various rates of the
County Cour.cils will have been struck,
and those rates, I am afraid, are not
unlikely to have some bearing on the
rent of the house and the amount to be
allowed for the rent of the house which
is to be admitted as an advantage to the
labourer in the debit account as against
his employer. These things will have
arrived nearer a settlement than they
are at present.
When these questions of price and
perhaps of rates are decided, I do not
think there will be anything left as regards the perquisite question except the
amount to be contributed in respect of
board and lodging. That is a most important matter, because it is on that one
point that many people thought it
would have been wiser to do what the
Bill has not done. That is to fix
minimum wage at a comparatively low
rate, say 20s per week, to be paid in
cash, and to leave all the other benefits
to be dealt with by the Board or by
agreement as subsidiary matters. That
a
does not really belong to the Bill now
before us, but I do not think, on the
whole, the House would be well advised
to delay the launching of this Measure.
One can only hope that the result will
not be such as to militate against the
purpose of the Bill, which, as I understand
it, is to keep people working on the land.
If the Agricultural Board accepts certain
counsels thrown out to them in the Press
and lends itself to what I may call immoderately imposed demands, then the
Bill will be a failure, but if the Bill
will help to keep people on the land that
is a consummation much to be desired.
Whether the Bill will meet with success
or not I do not know, and I do not
venture to prophesy, but I do not see any
occasion to oppose the Third Reading of
the Measure.

itself that the Clause reproduced in the
Bill before us did in fact represent the
existing law. The Committee did not
inquire in any way as to what were the
merits of the situation. We did not
inquire as to whether this or that provision was a satisfactory provision. What
we did inquire into was whether that
provision was or was not already provided for by statute. We came to the
conclusion that this particular subclause did represent the existing law.

Control of Employment Bill.
Order for Second Reading read.
Mr. ROBB: The object of this Bill
may be told quite briefly. It is to ensure
In
that skilled workers who become unemployed and whose services are urgently
needed by vital war industries should be
placed in employment in these industries.
rather than in work which is not
essential in the national interest. I
should like to stress the words "skilled
workers' who become unemployed.
the Bill there is no measure of industrial compulsion. It would apply
only to unemployed workers in certain
classes of skilled trades and certain
classes of war industries. It would not
affect the position of men in employment.
They cannot be compelled to leave employment with which they are satisfied,
nor can they be compelled to remain in
employment which they desire to leave.
I should fancy the House will not disagree with the general proposition that
vital war industries at the present time
must have first claim on any surplus of
suitable skilled labour which becomes
available. To make this claim effective
the Bill proposes that the Ministry of
Labour shall have power-and unrestricted power as I will show subsequently to make orders prohibiting,
except by the Ministry's consent, employers to whom the order applies from
advertising vacancies for and from
engaging or re-engaging any worker to
whom the order relates. Where consent
is given the employer will make his own.
arrangements with the worker. He will
not be compelled to make the engagement
through the employment exchange.
Before the Ministry can refuse to
accede to the engagement of a worker to
whom the order applies, the Bill provides
that the Ministry must first be satisfied
that alternative employment in more
essential work is available for the worker
and must notify him of that alternative
employment. Should the worker be dissatisfied with the Ministry's decision he
may appeal against it to the court of
referees, and if his appeal is upheld the
court may award him compensation for
any loss he has sustained as a result of
the consent being withheld. Such compensation is to be paid under regulations
by the Ministry, made with the approval
of the Ministry of Finance. I said the
make orders was not an
power to
absolute one, because all orders made
under the Bill must be submitted in
draft for consideration of a committee
representative of the employers and
workers affected by the order. After
consideration by the committee the order
would then be made by the Ministry and
come into effect at once, but as soon as
the order is made it must be laid before
both Houses of Parliament, and with it
any report made on it by the committee,
and either House can annul the order by
resolution within the statutory period.
The Bill also makes provision for keeping outside the scope of the Bill arrangements which trade unions have in
existence for placing their members in
employment provided that these arrangements are operative in accordance with
directions received from the Ministry.
The other provisions of the Bill are supplementary. They provide for the
appointment of inspectors to enforce the
provisions of the Bill. I might add that
it is not expected that it will be necessary to appoint additional inspectors, as
for this purpose the Ministry will make
use of its existing inspection staff. I beg
to move.
391 Control of Employment Bill-

THE SENATE
Second Reading
Question proposed: "That this Bill be
now read a Second time."
Mr. JOSEPH CUNNINGHAM: I
would like to support the Second Reading of this Bill. It is very often said
that we just muddled through the last
war and won. Undoubtedly there was
considerable confusion during the earlier
part of the war. Men were allowed to
enlist and change employers, and when
at the end of a short time it was found
they were absolutely necessary for shipyard and engineering work, particularly
engineering work, they were brought
home from the trenches. In view of that
I think it is very satisfactory to find that
the Government at Westminster and the
Government here are making arrangements to prevent the confusion I have
referred to. It is necessary that skilled
men in the shipbuilding, engineering and
aircraft trades should be kept in sufficient
numbers to carry out essential work.
Men may be
very necessary in the
trenches, but there are men better fitted
to serve there than the men I have
mentioned whose services in the workshop are of more value to the country
than they would be in the trenches. In
supporting the Second Reading I repeat
that it is very satisfactory that such
arrangements should be made to prevent
the confusion that occurred on the last
occasion.
Mr. LESLIE: The Bill is in its way
an interesting Bill. It is obviously a
Bill brought in to meet a difficulty which
may possibly arise in Northern Ireland,
but which does, I am afraid, exist to
some extent across the water. I do not
know at the moment there is any great
need for the Bill in Northern Ireland,
but one must admit there is the possibility that that need may arise, and may
arise very suddenly at a later date.
A question which I think the Second
Chamber should consider is whether the
Bill is unnecessarily wide, whether it is
sufficiently circumscribed, or whether it
goes as far as may be necessary. I need
scarcely point out that there is nothing
in the Bill which deals with the question
of enlistment in His Majesty's Forces.
Any provision of that sort would be beyond the powers of this House. But
there are other possibilities we ought, I
think, to consider. If the House will
turn to Clause 4, sub-clause (1), dealing
with inspectors appointed under various
Acts I think they will get a very fair
index of the sort of thing to which the
Bill is intended to apply.
I think the indication there as to the
inspectors to be employed pretty well
covers the whole ground it is intended
to cover. That is all very well, but
there is the question as to whether the
Bill can be stretched further. I do not
think, for instance, the Bill can be
stretched to apply to the case of a man
who advertises for a butler or a cook,
because butlers and cooks do not belong
to organisations in regard to which there
is a possibility of representation, and it
is unlikely an order will be made. I do
not think domestic service comes into it,
and therefore I am inclined to think that
although agriculture might conceivably
be covered by this Bill we ought to insert a few words stating that any order
made in respect of agricultural workers
should only be made after consultation
with the Minister of Agriculture.
The reason I make mention of this is,
as I am sure the House knows, because
of the fact that there are measures before
Parliament at the moment, and indeed
there are measures which Parliament has
passed, dealing with the various powers
of the Minister of Agriculture as regards
tillage and undertakings given to
assist as regards labour and one
thing and another. It might conceivably
be thought that it might be necessary to
employ some machinery of the nature
adumbrated in this Bill in connection
with agricultural labour, and if that is
so the Ministries of Labour and of Agriculture ought certainly to work in
common. But that is a matter strictly
for the Committee stage.
I have also a little difficulty as regards
the first sub-clause of Clause 5. Clause 5,
if my information is correct, underwent
some ameliorative treatment at the
hands of the Committee in another place,
and the words at the end of that subclause, I believe, were added after the
Bill had received its Second Reading.
The words I refer to are:-
.. there shall be laid before Parliament a copy of any report made by a
committee constituted in accordance
with the provisions of this Act which

In Clause 9 it is stated that the Bill is to
continue in force until such time as the

is the cause of the passing of the Act,
at an end. We are not told anywhere in

point is that these Acts may endure for
some indefinite period, and I think it is
a matter for consideration whether each
Bill should not contain a reference as to
what the emergency is which calls it into
operation.
Again, as regards that, there is no
question in this Bill as to its duration.
The Bill is only to continue in force
during what we know, or think we know,
is the course of the present war, but that,
I think, is left a little in the air and
ought to be corrected. I do not think
there is any reason on the part of anybody to object to the Bill. It is not
going to impose any hardship on anybody,
because there is protection all through, as
the right hon. Gentleman indicated. But
there are those two points. The first is
as to whether some special provision
ought not to be made to safeguard the
agricultural worker and the demands for
an agricultural worker, and the second
is what is the emergency with which we
are dealing. The second point is, of
course, for the future.

it

the measure, this is in the form employed
in all this war legislation, and it is in
so many Bills that it is hardly worth
while altering it in one Bill unless we
start to alter it in all.

Housing (Emergency Powers) Bill.
Order for Second Reading read.
Mr. ROBB: On the outbreak of war
the Imperial Government, as one of their
emergency measures, introduced a Bill
empowering local authorities to make fit
for housing purposes buildings damaged
either by enemy action or damage caused
by repelling enemy action. The present
Bill is similar in scope.
This Bill has
been the subject of a circular which the
Ministry of Home Affairs has sent to
local authorities, and therefore I do not
propose to deal at length with the
manner in which local authorities will be
expected to carry out the duties imposed
on them by this Bill, but I think it is
desirable that I should state briefly the
necessity for the measure. It will be
readily realised that if extensive damage
should occur to house property by air
bombardment or any other enemy action,
or by defensive measures, especially in
working-class areas where no alternative
accommodation is available, it would be
essential that immediate repairs should be
carried out in respect of buildings which
by such action had been rendered unfit
for habitation.
But it might be that the person owning
the buildings or the person responsible
for putting the buildings in repair
would be either unable or unwilling to
incur the expense of making the premises
fit for habitation, and to overcome
this difficulty the Bill is introduced.
It empowers the local authorities in such
Housing Bill—
Second Reading
THE SENATE
[Mr. Robb]
circumstances to carry out the essential
works. Before they can do that they
must give at least fourteen days' notice
to the person who has control of the
property and they must take into consideration any representations he may
make. It might happen that it might
be necessary to carry out immediate
repairs to safeguard the health of the
occupants of the building and to prevent further damage. Accordingly, provision has been made for carrying out
emergency repairs without prior notice.
where the local authority think that
course absolutely necessary.
The other aspect of the Bill to which
I would draw attention is the financing
of the cost of repairs. In the first
instance, of course, the cost of these
repairs will fall upon the local authority
that does them, and Clause 3 provides
that loans will be available to local
authorities from the Ministry of Home
Affairs on such terms and conditions as
the Ministry of Finance approve for this
purpose. The local authorities are not
to be asked to repay these loans or any
interest until after the termination of
hostilities, and they will not be entitled
to demand from the owners any expenses
incurred in carrying out the necessary
repairs until the end of the present
emergency.
The other provisions are consequential
upon the main objects of the Bill. They
apply the machinery of the Planning
and Housing Act, 1931, which was designed to deal with houses demolished
for reasons of public health for the purposes of this Bill. The Bill is a short
one, and those are its main provisions.
Its object is that if people are dispossessed of their dwelling-houses by action
due to the war the local authority may
come in at once, or almost at once. They
may, in the first instance, get a loan
from the Ministry of Home Affairs and
recover it subsequently from the owners,
but not until the war ends.
Question proposed: "That this Bill be
now read a Second time."
Mr. LESLIE: Again I have to make

that it is difficult to look at it
prospectively in view of what may happen, and to provide against occurrences
which may or may not, and probably will

not,

this
are

what the intention in this case is in the
event of let us say, a more or less successful air raid demolishing houses in a
particular area. We know it will be
necessary to take exceptional measures in
order to meet an occurrence of that kind.
I am simply speaking on the point as
to whether we ought not to be told what
the emergency really is, and whether this
Bill is intended to operate beyond the
present war. We are not told in the war
damage definition clause definitely whether
war damage is to be damage caused
during the present emergency or whether
it is to be extended further. That is
the only point in the observations I am
making. I do think that in all these
Bills we ought to confine ourselves a little
more strictly and a little more definitely
as to what position we are dealing with,
whether the actualities before us at the
moment or whether the possibilities of
the future.
Major DOBBS: One or two of these
Bills with which we have been dealing
this afternoon will not have a Committee
stage. I should like to ask whether
there will be a Committee stage on this
Bill.
Mr. ROBB: Yes.
Major DOBBS: I prefer to speak on
the Committee stage.
On Question, Motion agreed to.

Trade Boards Bill-
Second Reading
15 NOVEMBER 1933
THE SENATE.

Wednesday, 15th November, 1939.
The Senate met at a half after Two of
the Clock, Mr. SPEAKER in the Chair.

Ministry of Labour:
Contributory Pensions (Joint Committee) Regulations (Northern Ireland),
1939.
Laid before the House. (pursuant to
Act), and ordered to lie on the Table.
Trade Boards Bill.
Order for Second Reading read.
Mr. ROBB (Leader of the House):
This Bill is intended to bring the law
concerning trade boards into line with
that in Great Britain by two alterations.
At present, before a trade board can be
set up, or before even an existing Order
can be modified, it is necessary to make
a Provisional Order followed by a confirming Act of Parliament. It is
proposed to substitute for these Provisional Orders a system of special
Orders, the procedure for making which
is set out in the Schedule to the Bill.
Under this Schedule before, say, a new
trade board is established, the Ministry
of Labour must give notice to the persons
likely to be affected. They are given an
opportunity of presenting any objections.
they may have, and, if the. Ministry does
not think fit to amend the Order it must,
unless the objection is clearly frivolous,
direct a public inquiry to be held. At
that public inquiry the objectors may
appear either personally or by counsel.
When this has been done the Special
Order has to be laid on the Table of each
House. It is then open, of course, for
discussion, and it may be annulled if
either House thinks fit to take such
action. This procedure preserves parliamentary control and gives all reasonable
safeguards to the interests concerned.
The other amendment is to authorise
the appointment of three independent
members to a trade board-instead of
one, as at present-where the Ministry
thinks that change advisable. A trade
board numbers from six members-three
on each side-up to 24 members-12 on
each side-and it is felt that with the
introduction of more complex industrial
legislation such as, for example, holidays.
with pay--the duties of a chairman of
trade boards are becoming heavier than
ever, and that in the larger boards, where
controversial matters are more apparent,
some extra support would be of assistance
to him in discharging his difficult and
possibly invidious duties.
My
May I say that I personally acted for
many years as a member of a trade board,
sometimes as an ordinary appointed
member and sometimes as chairman of a
trade board, and I never had any experience of a trade board upon which there
was only one appointed member.
experience was that in the difficult questions that sometimes arose three persons
having interests entirely independent of
the two sides of the trade board were not
too many. I am quite satisfied that in
many cases it would be an advantage to
have three such members instead of one.
These are the two matters with which the
Bill deals, and I beg to move.
Question proposed, "That the Bill be
now read a Second time."
Mr. LESLIE: As regards what one
might describe as the second part of the
Bill, that part which the right hon.
Gentleman dealt with at the conclusion of
his speech, I do not think there is anything that can be said by way of objection. Almost anybody who has had experience of boards or councils of one sort
or another will be very ready to accept
the suggestion that the number of nominated members should be increased.
As regards the first part of the measure
I am afraid I must say that the Bill itself
and the speech made by the right hon.
Gentleman do not seem to me to be convincing. It seems to me that if we pass
what is the first Clause of the Bill we
shall be weakening the control of Parliament, and that definitely ought not to be.
The fact that they have apparently done
so in Great Britain does not seem to me
to affect the question because, after all,
the Parliamentary arrangements in Great
Britain are rather different to what they
are in Northern Ireland. However, those
are matters which can properly be developed at a later stage, but, as at present
Teachers' Salaries Bill- -
Second Reading
THE SENATE
[Mr. Leslie]
advised, I adhere to the opinion that the
first proposal would, in fact, weaken the
control of this Parliament and that, I
think, we should not do. As regards the
second part of the Bill there is no objection, and, therefore, I do not propose to
oppose the Second Reading.
On Question, Motion agreed to.
Bill committed to a Committee of the
Whole House for to-morrow.
Teachers' Salaries and Superannuation
(War Service) Bill.
Order for Second Reading read.
Mr. ROBB: This Bill is designed to do
for the teachers and other persons who
come within its scope what has been done
for teachers in Great Britain in the matter
of safeguarding their salary and superannuation rights whilst they are rendering
war service during the present national
emergency.
As regards salaries, a distinction has to
be made between public elementary school
teachers who, though in the employment
of their school managers, receive their
salaries from the Ministry, and teachers
and other persons who are paid by the
managers, committee or governing body of
the school in which they are employed.
As regards public elementary school
teachers the Bill provides that any teacher
who, with the permission of his manager,
undertakes war service shall be regarded
as on leave of absence whilst so serving.
The effect of this is to keep his post open
for him. Further, if the amount of his
war service pay be less than the amount
which he would have been paid if he had
remained in his teaching employment, the
deficiency may be paid to him.
In the case of persons whose salaries are
paid by their employers-that is, fulltime teachers in preparatory, intermediate, secondary and technical schools
and teachers, officers and servants
employed in the two Belfast training colleges-it is provided that where, with the
permission of his employer, any such
person engages in war service, his
employer may make up any deficiency
which there may be between his war service pay and the remuneration which he
would have received had he continued to
serve in his civil capacity.
The Bill also provides that in such
cases the Ministry may pay to the authorities of the school concerned any increment grant which would have been
payable by the Ministry in respect of the
absent teacher if he had remained in his
teaching employment. I should, perhaps,
explain that the only direct payments in
respect of any such teacher that the
Ministry pay is his increments, and this
Bill gives power to the Ministry to continue to pay those increments although
the teacher is not for the moment
employed in teaching.
In the case of unemployed teachers who
undertake war service, and persons
accepted for admission to training colleges
who undertake war service on or before
completing their course of training, and
who subsequently enter or re-enter upon
full-time teaching service in Northern Ireland, it is provided that the period of war
service may, in due course, be reckoned
for increments of salary as if it had been
teaching service. An exception is made,
however, where a student has not completed his training before joining up. In
this case, following what is done in England
we do not propose to reckon either for
increment or for pension any period of
war service rendered before the date on
which his course of training would normally have been completed.
As regards superannuation, the Bill
provides, in general, that credit for superannuation purposes may be given in respect of war service which under the
earlier provisions of the Bill may be
reckoned for salary or increment purposes.
It also makes clear in what circumstances
pension contributions are payable in respect of such service, but it exempts from
the payment of such contributions a person
whose war service pay is less than the
amount of his civil pay, and to, or in
respect of whom, no deficiency payment
is made by his employer. I beg to move.
Question proposed, "That the Bill be
now read a Second time."
Mr. LESLIE: I imagine the whole
House is in sympathy with the general
purpose of this Bill, and I rise only to
draw attention to a small matter to which
in ordinary circumstances I should have
drawn attention in Committee. I shall
have to explain to the House why it is
I cannot take that course to-day. The
point is that there seems to me to be a

Unemployment Insurance Bill.
Order for Second Reading read.
Mr. ROBB: This differs from the other
Bills in that it is not a war emergency
Bill. It deals with a variety of matters,
and the Ministry of Labour have circulated a memorandum which perhaps
relieves me from the necessity of a detailed explanation of the provisions. The
memorandum goes through the Bill
Clause by Clause, and I assume Members
have read it. I should, however, point
407 Unemployment Insurance Bill-

THE SENATE
Second Reading
[Mr. Robb]
out that the Bill corresponds to the Unemployment Insurance Act passed by the
Imperial Parliament in July last and is
needed to keep our Unemployment
Insurance practice uniform with that of
Great Britain. Much of the Bill deals
more with what I might call matters of
machinery rather than principle. The
chief exception is Clause 1 which introduces to unemployment legislation the
word "holiday. Decisions by the
umpire, who, as the House knows, is
called upon to interpret the Unemployed
Insurance Acts, have defined what are
known as "customary or recognised
holidays" which are
are not counted for
purposes of benefit. The growth of the
holiday-with-pay movement has led to the
emergence of holidays which have not yet.
crystallised into the state of being "recognised or customary." The Unemployment Insurance Statutory Committee,
which is a committee which considers
from time to time various points which
may arise and makes recommendations,
has recommended that the Ministry of
Labour should be given power to define
what are holidays, and that insured persons on holiday should not be treated as
being unemployed. Clause 1 gives effect
to that recommendation.
I should like also to make a special
reference to Clause 15 under which, at
the end of twelve months from the date
on which this Bill becomes law, long
hirings in agriculture will cease to attract
rebates to agricultural insurance contributions. Both here and in Great
Britain it has been found that the cost
of giving rebates exceeds the amount of
the rebates themselves. In a year £170
was paid out in rebates in Northern Ireland, and it cost over £200 to do it.
Having regard to the small amount at
issue it was thought that the continuance
of the scheme could not be justified.
There are concessions to insured persons in the inclusion of additional classes
of persons for whom dependents' benefit
may be paid. The Ministry is empowered.
to make payments to juveniles attending
courses of instruction for persons under
the age of 18. Clauses 10, 11 and 13
bring unemployment insurance law into
line with health insurance practice.
In short, this is an omnibus Bill which
aims at placing unemployment insurance
in relation to holidays upon a definite
and satisfactory basis, and at effecting a
number of minor amendments where experience has shown that existing law and
practice are open to improvement.
Question proposed, "That the Bill be
now read a Second time."
Mr. TAYLOR: I would like to know
if the Government would give favourable
consideration to the granting of a winter
allowance to unemployed insured persons,
in receipt of unemployment insurance
benefit, to alleviate the hardships now
existing, in view of the increased cost of
living, due to the war-time conditions
now prevailing. The amount of benefit
they are now receiving is wholly inadequate, in view of the increasing prices
of the necessaries of life, especially food,
coal and light, for a man to maintain his
home, wife and family on the present
scale of benefit.
Arising from the dislocation in trade.
and business and the restricted activities
of some of the larger industrial concerns
during the first few weeks of the war, a
great number of men and women have
lost their employment, many of whom
are ineligible for military service and are,
therefore, dependent on unemployment
insurance benefit to maintain their homes.
This, of course, was reasonably possible.
prior to the outbreak of war, but now
that the long-expected war has come upon
the world, with the resultant increase in
the cost of living, I submit it is the duty
of the Government to
see that a fair
degree of comfort is attainable for those
unemployed insured workers, who through
the exigencies of the war, and no fault
of their own, are compelled to live on
insurance benefit.
Of course it was only to be expected
that the cost of living would go up
following the outbreak of war, and that
the figure will continue to rise, as the
war goes on, is obvious, though one may
hope it will not be at the same rate or
to the same extent as in the last war.
Then the cost of living went up and up,
until when the war officially ended the
figure was 120 per cent. above that of
July 1914, and retail food prices were up
125 per cent.
The Government in their wisdom
recently extended the scope of the Unemployment Assistance Act, whereby un-

National Health Insurance and Contributory Pensions (Emergency
Provisions) Bill.
Order for Second Reading read.
Mr. ROBB: This Bill is a financial
Measure arising out of the National
Health Insurance and Contributory
Pensions (Emergency Provisions) Act
passed by the Parliament of the United
Kingdom on 3rd September last. That
Act deals with matters arising out of the
special conditions created by the present
emergency in so far as they affect National
Health Insurance and Contributory Pensions. The National Health Insurance
Scheme is constituted on a
United
Kingdom basis, and the Contributory
Pensions Scheme is closely interlocked
with it. The necessary amendments
were, therefore, embodied in the United
Kingdom Act covering both
Britain and Northern Ireland.
Great
The sole purpose of the Bill is to
provide for the appropriate grant from
the Exchequer of Northern Ireland in
relation to the new charges imposed on
the Health and Pensions Schemes
Northern Ireland by the amendments
made by the United Kingdom Act.
in
These amendments are substantially the
same as those made in the last war. They
provide for the continuance in insurance
of persons engaged in war occupations
who would not be insured under the
general scheme of the Acts; they restrict
the payment of health insurance benefit
in any case in which a pension or allowance is paid from Government sources for
injuries received as a result of the war;
and they provide that health insurance
benefit or contributory pensions may be
paid on the best evidence available if
records have been destroyed as a result
of the war.
In respect of the war occupations
brought into insurance or kept in insurance by the United Kingdom Acts,
the contributions payable will not be at
the full rates, but at the rates which are
payable under the general provisions of
the Acts for members of His Majesty's
forces.
It is necessary to provide that approved
societies shall not suffer financial loss in
consequence, and a State grant is therefore payable on the difference between
the actual contribution paid and that
which would be payable under the
ordinary insurance provisions.
The cost of the Bill is hard to estimate,
but I do not think it will be very large
even if there should be widespread destruction of records. I am sure the
House will agree with the wisdom of the
proposals for the continued payment of
health insurance benefit and contributory
pensions in every conceivable contingency.
I beg to move, "That the Bill be now
read a Second time."
On Question, Motion agreed to.
Bill committed to a Committee of the
Whole House for to-morrow.

Mr. JOSEPH CUNNINGHAM:
would like to ask the Leader of the House
if he is in a position to give us any
further information regarding the distribution of petrol in Northern Ireland.
We have received a circular from the
Northern Ireland Private Motor Lorry
Owners' Association, and if the statements contained in that circular are
correct we are certainly not being treated
as we should be. Evidently motor lorry
owners in England are in a much better
position as regards petrol supplies than
those in Northern Ireland. I think
everyone in Northern Ireland, irrespective
of what political views he holds, is
anxious that our services should be on
the same level as those in England.
Therefore I think the right hon. Gentleman should give us some information as
to whether there is going to be any improvement in the distribution of petrol
to bring the supplies granted to motor
lorry owners in Ulster up to the standard
of the supplies given across the water,
and, if not, what are the reasons for the
differentiation.
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: I am very glad
this question has been raised again. I
think it is very important to point out
to the Leader of the House and to the
Government that we in Northern Ireland
are not getting the same privileges and
advantages as they are in England and
Scotland. We have got to pay the same
taxes and rates, and therefore we ought
to be on an equal footing with the man
across the Channel. But when it comes
to a question of giving supplemental
allowances of petrol we are told we will
get fifty per cent. of what the man across
the Channel is getting. According to my
information, a man in England with a
6 to 7 ton lorry is allowed 168 gallons per
month, whereas in Northern Ireland such
a man is allowed only 90 gallons. I say
that is not right or fair, and I trust that,
if our Government is not in a position
to place motor lorry owners here on the
same footing as those in England, they
will put the case before the Imperial
Parliament and demand their rights and
the rights of the citizens they represent.
Mr. MAGUIRE: I wish to support

month, the Petroleum Board should be

to purchase in bulk quantities as formerly.
If the Government would agree to that
it would assist traders to some extent.
Adjournment-
Commercial Petrol Supplies
THE SENATE
Mr. ROBB: This question was before
the House on the last day we met, and
it was subsequently raised by the circular
which I presume. every Member of this
House received this morning or yesterday morning.
Two points should be carefully remembered in connection with this matter.
First, petrol is rationed so that supplies
shall be available for (a) the defence of
the Realm; (b) the efficient prosecution
of the war; and (c) the maintenance of
supplies and services essential to the life
of the community. Secondly, with the
exception of a negligible minority, goods
vehicles in Great Britain are under
Government control.
the
Vehicle owners in Great Britain were
required to form their vehicles into
groups of about 100 vehicles and to place
each group under a manager who decides
how, when, and for what purpose,
vehicles in his group shall be used. He
uses the vehicles indiscriminately to carry
for the business of the owners, and also
uses them to carry for the public, giving
public business priority over private
business. There is no such Government
control in Northern Ireland and goods
vehicles, consequently, remain in the sole
management of their owners. At a conference with representatives of owners of
goods vehicles in July last it was clearly
indicated to the Ministry of Home Affairs
that owners did not favour the adoption
of any such system as that in operation
in Great Britain.
Because of the great difference which
exists between the way in which vehicles
are controlled and used in the two areas
it is obvious that petrol rationing in
Great Britain and Northern Ireland must
be on entirely different bases and that no
true comparisons can be made between
what is allowed for a vehicle in one area
and for a similar vehicle in the other. In
Great Britain an allowance is not made in
respect of any particular vehicle in any
group, but an allowance is made to the
group manager for the work of the entire
group, and any supplemental allowance
granted is also to the manager for the
whole group, but only for public work.
This grouping system ensures that the
work of the owners of the vehicles is done
economically, and that the vehicles are
available without delay to supplement
public transport.
In Northern Ireland each vehicle remains in the control of its owner, and the
basic or supplemental ration granted is
in respect of the vehicle and its owner's
business alone, basic ration being
granted for each vehicle according to its
unladen weight. In deciding whether
any supplemental allowance shall be
given the Petroleum Officer has to determine how far each vehicle is used on work
which is essential to the life of the
community. For example, munitions,
food agriculture, the fuel industrythese are all essential services, Businesses
of other types may not be essential, but
the bulk of the work for which goods
vehicles are used must be of a semiessential nature.
The petroleum officer has dealt with
about 8,400 goods vehicles and he has
treated 2,500 or 30 per cent, as being
essential and 5,100 or 61 per cent. as
semi-essential, and the remainder, the 9
per cent. residue, as non-essential. The
allowances given for the semi-essential
class are twice the basic ration allowance
for non-essential vehicles. Turn to the
circular which has been issued. I have
numbered the paragraphs for my own
convenience. First of all I would say if
this represents the views of the association
they seem to be under the impression
that there is a glut of petrol. Such is
not the case. They should be able to
appreciate that as the war has scarcely
started the future must be very uncertain. They deal with the allowances
to the Transport Board. The Board is
said in this circular to be receiving 85
per cent. of pre-war consumption. It is
very difficult to estimate whether that is
a fact or not. But assume it to be a
fact. Then if it were so, the Board is a
public utility undertaking. It is a
common carrier and is obliged to take
whatever merchandise is offered to it,
whereas the private lorry owner has no
such obligation. He can and does use
the Board's vehicles when it suits him.
The Board is, therefore, in a very different position from that of the lorry
owners. Yet it has been rationed in
petrol and is being treated in the same
manner as a business undertaking whose
business is essential to the life of the
community, and dealing with merchandise gives priority to what is essential.
Further, the rationing of petrol for
private cars must have sent additional
Adjournment-
Commercial Petrol Supplies
15 NOVEMBER 1939
passengers to the Board's omnibuses,
because those deprived of the use of their
cars cannot all travel by train. When
the petroleum officer told the Northern
Ireland Private Lorry Owners' Association that public transport must be used
to the greatest possible extent he merely
emphasised the principle underlying
petrol rationing and the transport control
which is in operation in Great Britain,
namely, that public interest must precede
private interest. Public transport with
its public duties and responsibilities is
obviously in a different class from private
transport, and the suggestion that petrol
rationing is being used to drive traffic to
the Transport Board is simply a variation
of the complaint made in Great Britain
that it is being used to drive traffic to
the railways.
In paragraphs 2 and 3 this circular
makes a comparison between the amounts
granted in Great Britain and those
allowed in Northern Ireland. These comparisons are really useless, because, as
I have pointed out, the conditions are
entirely different. We have pooled
transport in England and you have not
got pooled transport here. Supposing we
take the figures they give here. As I
pointed out earlier, lorries in Northern
Ireland have been divided into three
classes, essential, semi-essential, and nonessential. If you take the figures in this,
and I have the figures for all the weights
that are put here, but I will just work
out two of them. I did not work out the
6 and 7 ton lorries the hon. Senator
referred to. But take the unladen weight
of half a ton to one ton lorry. As set
out here the monthly ration in England is
24 gallons. In Ireland lorries of that
weight are divided into three classes. It
is quite true as this table points out in
this circular that the monthly ration for
the non-essential motor lorry is 15 gallons
as against 24, but that 15 gallons only
applies to 9 per cent. of the total number
of those lorries. The essential lorry of
that weight, of which there are 30 per
cent., on the average is allowed 45 gallons
monthly. The semi-essential lorry, of
which there are 61 per cent., is allowed
30 gallons, so that if you work it out over
all the lorries of that weight, as against
the ration of 24 gallons in England there
is a ration in Northern Ireland of 33.45
gallons.
Now if you take the only other one I
have worked out, the 4 and 5 ton lorry,
table 120 gallons in
it says in this
England and 65 gallons in Northern
Ireland. But while it is quite true that
65 gallons is the basic ration for nonessential motor lorries of that weight,
there are only 9 per cent. of these lorries
non-essential. There are 30 per cent. of
them essential, and that 30 per cent.
are allowed 195 gallons monthly.
The semi-essential, of which there
are 61 per cent., are allowed 130 gallons
a month, and as against the ration in
England of 120 gallons the average in
Northern Ireland is 143.65. It does
seem to me that the private lorry owner
in Northern Ireland is at no disadvantage
on the average in respect of his petrol
ration under the present scheme.
Now with regard to the question of
bulk deliveries in paragraphs 8 and 9 of
this circular. It reads as follows:-
We are also to state that it is understood that the arrangements in force
across the Channel regarding deliveries
of minimum bulk to any commercial
consumer are as follows.
It then sets them out. The same
arrangements are in force in Northern
Ireland. In paragraph 9 they go on to
make the suggestion that the pool board
should give discount in respect of 100
gallons. The present figure is 200. As
I pointed out last day this is a trade
regulation. It was made for the purpose
of economy, and the regulation is that
this discount is only given to people who
have tanks to take 200 gallons at a time.
As I pointed out, that trade regulation
has got nothing to do with the Government.
The discount is given because of
the saving in carrying, and that was given
for the purpose of economy, and if it is
wasteful to deliver lots of less than 200
gallons, surely in war-time it is more
essential than ever that we should avoid
wastage.
The last matter dealt with in the
circular is the question of Road Fund
grants which is touched on incidentally.
The Road Fund is the product of taxes on
motor vehicles. Now, because of petrol
rationing and the increase in the tax on
private cars from 15s to 25s per unit of
horsepower, the increase in income-tax,
the increase in the price of petrol, and
other reasons, it is estimated that at least
Adjournment—
Commercial Petrol Supplies
THE SENATE
[Mr. Robb]
75 per cent. of the private cars will not be
used after 31st December next. The
greater part of the revenue of the Road
Fund comes from the tax on private cars,
and it is received during the last three
months of the year. Consequently, the
Road Fund revenue for the coming financial year will be much reduced, and so
much reduced that when the costs of
administration and of the commitments
already entered into are discharged, there
will not be anything in the Road Fund
to hand out to anybody.
That has been intimated to the local
authorities, and the highway authorities
have been advised to limit their activities
to such maintenance work as will prevent
deterioration. Unfortunately they will
still have the pre-war omnibus and horse
traffic, the most destructive on the roads.
I hope I have convinced the House that
the bulk of these complaints are
sound. If there are any small matters
not interfering with the general system
which Member of the House would
any
like to bring before the attention of the
Ministry of Home Affairs I shall be very
glad to do so.

Local Government Staffs (War Service)
Bill.
Order for Second Reading read.
to
Mr. ROBB (the Leader of the
House): This Bill is very similar to the
Bill dealing with teachers undertaking
war service, which was before the House
yesterday. Its main
is
purpose
provide that local authorities or any
other public body which the Ministry of
Home Affairs may determine to be a
local authority for the purposes of the
Bill-may be in a position to ensure that
any of their employees undertaking war
service shall not suffer financially on that
account.
In the first place, it enables local
authorities to pay to or on behalf of
their employees undertaking war service.
the balance of their civil pay; that is to
say, if an employee's war service pay is
less than what he would have received
if he had continued to serve in his civil
capacity the local authority may make
good the difference.
In the second place, the Bill preserves
the pension rights of any employees
who were serving in a pensionable
capacity, and provides that the period
of
war service of an employee
will be aggregated for superannuation
purposes and reckoned in the same
manner as service in his civil capacity.
I beg to move, "That the Bill be now
read a Second time."
On Question, Motion agreed to.
Bill committed to a Committee of the
Whole House for Tuesday next.

Essential Buildings and Plant (Repair of
War Damage) Bill.
Order for Second Reading read.
Mr. ROBB:
The purpose of this Bill
is to provide for the repair of buildings
and plant damaged by war and considered
to be essential to the welfare of the civil
population. It does not apply to buildings used solely for housing purposes,
such buildings being covered by the
Housing (Emergency Powers) Bill already
introduced into this House.
The decision as to whether action
should be taken for the purpose of
repairing buildings or reinstating plant
would rest with the appropriate Ministry.
Thus the Ministry of Education would
initiate action in regard to schools and
the Ministry of Commerce in regard
to essential industrial or commercial
buildings.
Before a loan is made the appropriate
Ministry must be satisfied that the
damaged plant or building is used for an
essential purpose, that the work may be
done at a reasonable cost, that no other
suitable building is available for the
same purpose, and that the owner of the
damaged building cannot for financial
reasons carry out the repairs himself.
or
Once it is established that the carrying out of the work is essential to the
community, the appropriate Ministry,
subject to the approval of the Ministry
of Finance, may lend to the owner of the
building, whether local authority.
otherwise, the necessary amount. If
the owner does not make any move in
the matter, the Ministry concerned may
by notice require the local authority to
undertake the work and may make a
loan for the purpose.
The Measure, which is based on
similar emergency legislation passed by
the Imperial Parliament, is designed to
ensure that the community shall not be
deprived of the use of essential buildings.
and plant as a result of war; and the
Bill should therefore commend itself to
the House. I beg to move, "That the
Bill be now read a Second time."
Question proposed, "That the Bill be
now read a Second time.”
