CLAUSE 1 (Substitution of special for
provisional orders).
Question proposed, "That the Clause
stand part of the Bill."
Mr. LESLIE: As the Committee will
be aware from the Order Paper, I am
attacking this Clause, and suggesting it
should be expunged, which is the same
thing as inviting the Committee to vote
against the proposal that the Clause stand
part of the Bill. The Clause I am attacking appears to me to be based on the
United Kingdom Act of 1918, the Act
which amended the original Trade Boards
Act of 1909. Now, the Committee will
perhaps remember that that Act of 1918,
which, of course, applied to Northern Ireland, was one of those Statutes selected
for unfavourable comment in Lord
Hewart's work, which was entitled "The
New Despotism." That book Lord
Hewart, Lord Chief Justice of England,
felt bound to write and publish on the
ground that the power of the bureaucracy
was increasing, and in his view it was a
Trade Boards Bill---
Committee
THE SENATE
[Mr. Leslie]
matter that ought to be checked. Amongst
the Statutes which he selected, as I say,
for unfavourable comment was this one
Statute of 1918.
I need scarcely remind the Committeein fact I am going to lay stress on it in a
few minutes-that that Act of 1918 was
very much indeed before this Parliament
when the Act of 1923, which it is proposed
to amend to-day, was passed. I do not
myself recollect that this Act of 1918 received any special attention in Sir Leslie
Scott's Committee which dealt with
Ministerial powers. The Committee I
am speaking of, this Committee may remember, started under the chairmanship
of the late Lord Donoughmore, and owing
to his withdrawal the chairmanship was
taken on by Sir Leslie Scott, and it was
under this name the Report was issued.
That Report was issued, of course, subsequent to Lord Hewart's book, and also
subsequent to the Northern Ireland Act
of 1923.
I must come back to our Act of 1923,
because that Act did in, fact, repeal the
Imperial Acts then in operation, both the
Act of 1909 and the amending Act of
1918, and it made the provision which
the Committee will observe exists in
Section 2. Section 2, I may remind the
Committee, is, in the Bill before us, the
Schedule, so to speak, for repeal. Section
2 of the Act of 1923 said that "The
Ministry may submit to Parliament for
confirmation any provisional order made
by it in pursuance of this Act, but no
such order shall have effect unless and
until it is confirmed by Parliament." It
goes on to say that any Act confirming a
provisional order may be repealed, altered
or amended and so on. That is the law
at the moment, and that is what it is
sought in this Bill to alter.
The Bill before us proposes the substitution which is before the Committee. The
Bill proposes, in effect, that the Ministry
may make this Order, a Special Order,
after going through sundry performances,
I was going to say, but after taking sundry
steps, and a Special Order of the Ministry
is to become effective when it has been
laid on the Table of both Houses, and
if either House within the Statutory
period, which is five sitting days, raises
any move to annul the Order, annulled
it shall be. But that does not ensure the
same control of Parliament as exists under
the procedure provided for in our Bill of
1923, which requires that the Provisional
Order shall receive actual confirmation by
Parliament before it becomes effective.
I am afraid I have to say that I regard
this proposal as a very distinct and a very
objectionable example of the attempt to
further impose bureaucratic control on the
community as a substitute for control by
Parliament, and, after all, control by
Parliament is what this country is entitled
to expect, and that is the control which
I think it is due to this House, whether
in Committee or in the House, to maintain. Let us look back a little to what
happened in 1923 when the Bill, part of
which it is now proposed to repeal, was
before those who sat here and in another
place. I am sorry to say that many of
those who took part in that debate during
the months of October and November,
1923, are no longer with us.
We are fortunate in having one right
hon. Gentleman now who took part in the
debate, in the person of the present
Leader of this House. We have not got
Mr. McGuffin, Mr. Donald, or Mr. Coote,
all of whom addressed those who sit elsewhere on the Bill. Nor have we got
Colonel Woods, who represented one
Division of Belfast-I am afraid I forget
which-and to those gentlemen I must
for a moment refer again. The Minister
of Labour in those days was the present
Minister of Finance. The Parliamentary
Secretary was the same gentleman who
has now become right honourable.
Honourable he always was, but he now
occupies the position of Minister of
Labour.
Let us see what Mr. Andrews said when
he introduced the Bill to the House. First
of all I may remind this Committee, as
Mr. Andrews reminded that House, that
the Trade Boards Bill of 1923 was
founded on the Report of the Committee
presided over by your distinguished predecessor, the late Marquess of Dufferin and
Ava, and the report, which I have in my
hand, was a unanimous report. Incidentally that report included a paragraph, the
effect of which was that in the Bill then
before Parliament we should revert to the
Imperial practice as laid down in the Act
of 1909, and not adopt the new practice
suggested by the Act of 1918, to which I
have previously directed the attention of
the Committee. That Act, I may repeat,
Trade Boards Bill-
Committee
21 NOVEMBER 1939
in effect provided for the proposal which
it is suggested we should now set up in
substitution for the procedure which previously existed. The report of the Committee was:-
That the procedure to be adopted by
the Minister of Labour in the setting
up, suspension, or withdrawal of a
Trade Board shall be by the making of
Provisional Orders as provided in the
Trade Boards Act of 1909.
That was the direct report, and the
unanimous report of the Dufferin Committee. Mr. Andrews, when introducing
the new Bill to the attention of the House,
stated quite definitely:-
As regards the Bill I come in the
first place, of course, to Clause 1, subsection (1). It provides for the application of the Act to any specified trade
by Provisional Order instead of by
Special Order, as has been the custom in
the past.
The Dufferin Committee
came to the conclusion that the Provisional Order system was more in keeping with the fitness of things than
establishing Trade Boards by means of
Special Orders.
I may mention that the right hon.
Gentleman was supported by an hon.
Member representing one of the Divisions
of Belfast, who is now Parliamentary
Secretary of the Ministry of Labour, and
what that hon. Gentleman said was:-
I am glad that under this Bill he
intends to revert to the Provisional
Order procedure, the same as under the
1909 Act, in spite of the fact that the
Committee set up by the Imperial Government recommends that they should
retain the present power of applying
Acts by Special Order.
So there was no doubt whatever, I may
say, in the mind of the Government of
the day as to the advantages of bringing
in the system which this afternoon this
Committee is asked to vary. Those quotations are from the Second Reading debate
in another place. They have the same
procedure as we have. After a Bill has
been through the Second Reading it goes
through Committee. In the Committee
on this occasion the gentleman to whom I
have referred, the hon. and gallant
Member who is no longer with us, Colonel
Woods, moved quite definitely to leave
out the words "Provisional Order" and
I
insert the words, "Special Order."
need not deal further with that, but let
us see what was said. Mr. Donald hoped
the Government would not accept the
amendment. Mr. Grant rose to oppose
the amendment. Mr. Andrews said:-
The Government are not in a position
to accept this amendment. In the first
place it would cut right across the
report of the Dufferin Committee. Besides, speaking personally as Minister
of Labour, I very much prefer that
the House should have this power.
In fact, the opinion entertained in 1923
was that the course which is now in operation under the Act of 1923 was infinitely
more democratic and infinitely better than
the course introduced by the Imperial Act
of 1918. I do not know whether I need
elaborate any more the point that the
control of Parliament, which I hold to be
essential, is much better exercised by
making the Order of the Ministry come.
to Parliament for confirmation, and before it comes into operation, than the
other way about. We had experience
last year of a small confirmatory Bill on
one of these Orders, and I never heard
that it gave any particular trouble. There
does not seem any object in doing away
with the Parliamentary stage. In the
event of differences you have got to hold
an inquiry, and fees have got to be paid
to the inquirer, but the Schedule does
not tell us where the money to pay these
fees is to come from. I can only suppose
they are intended to be defrayed as part
of the expenses of the Ministry, as to
which there is some provision in the Act
of 1923. At the same time Parliament
has not, as regards the Bill before us,
made any direct provision for the payment of those fees. I do not think these
fees would be any less than the fees incurred by members of the Joint Committee who consider these Bills.
But the objection, or one of the great
objections under present conditions to
proceedings in this way by Special Order
is this. If a Special Order is made and
it is attacked in one House or the other,
it necessarily happens that the Government proceeds to defend the Special Order.
It could scarcely be otherwise. Then if the
matter is persisted in it goes to a vote,
and this is a party vote. In this House
we do not trouble much about matters
of a purely party character. In the other
Trade Boards Bill-
Committee
THE SENATE
[Mr. Leslie]
place which is differently constituted, and
working under different conditions to
some extent, they do, and so it would
happen that the Order would be confirmed
by virtue of a Government vote.
If you
send the Bill to a Parliamentary Committee, which is a non-party committee,
you get a different atmosphere altogether.
You get the brains of both parties, whoever may be represented on this Committee, and you get a purely impartial
tribunal on a matter which is certainly
one not of party politics. That is a much
better way.
As things are at present an Order supported by the Government in another
place is passed-I was going to say almost.
irrespective of the merits of the position.
You have not got that Parliamentary
control under that system of procedure
which you have got if you send
Bill
your
to a Committee representing both Houses.
What is the difference with England?
I think it is, perhaps, true that before
1918 the Imperial Parliament might have
sent their Bill first to a Committee of the
House of Commons, and, secondly, to a
Committee of the House of Lords. That
meant doing the work twice over. We do
not do it. We send it to a Joint Committee and there is no trouble. I must
insist on moving this amendment, for it
is up to this House to maintain full Parliamentary control, and to put forward
such obstacles as it can to an extension of
bureaucratic control which the country
does not want, and which was decidedly
rejected by the information before it in
1923. I beg to move.
Mr. ROBB: Frankly this seems to be a
storm in a tea-cup. At present as the
law stands before a new trade board can
be set up the Ministry makes its Order.
The steps are numerous. First of all
there is a draft order which is published.
It is circulated to all interests, and objections are invited. After the objections
have been dealt with in some way or
another a provisional order is made. After
that a Bill has to be drafted; it has to be
prepared and printed, and go through
First and Second Readings in both Houses
and it must go before a Committee. It
then comes back for its Third Reading.
That has been found in practice-the only
example we have had in this country has
been one Bill-to be a very cumbersome
practice. It is urged in its favour that
it preserves the control of Parliament over
the actions of the Government in making
this order.
In answer to the quotations which the
hon. and learned Senator has given from
the debates in 1923, I may say that this
Bill in its present form received clear
support from both sides of the House in
another place. The present proposed procedure is that the Ministry shall announce its intentions to make an order
either for the establishment of a new
trade board or for the amendment of the
scheme for an existing trade board by
bringing in a few more people or excluding a few more people. Then it must,
according to the Schedule, bring the provisions before the attention of the people
who are primarily concerned-the
employers and the employed. If objections are taken and these objections are
not settled then an inquiry is held before
some person appointed by the Ministry,
but not in the employment of the Government, at which both sides can be heard.
After hearing evidence and receiving the
report thereon the Ministry can make an
order. Then the order is laid before
Parliament. The hon. and learned
Senator's point is this, that the control of
Parliament is being abrogated. This
provisional order-Special Order it is
called in this Bill-must be laid upon the
Table of each House of Parliament, and
if the Houses of Parliament do not like
it they can stop it. That is a much less
cumbersome procedure.
After an experience of 16 years another
branch of this Parliament commended the
provisions of the present Bill. They are
provisions that have been working in
England, Scotland and Wales all these
years. It is quite true that in 1923 this
Parliament at a time when the trade
board system was not so well known, and
not so well recognised a feature of our
industrial policy as it is to-day preferred
that the order should be the subject of investigation by Parliament before it became
operative, but now conditions are entirely
changed. Everyone in touch with industry
knows the intention, scope and activities
of trade boards. There is nothing new
about them now. People are accustomed
to them, and the method of making an
order under the Trade Boards Act as proposed in this Bill is definitely less cumbersome.

but the objection will be heard not by

that, I think, is not the same thing.
agree that it may be more convenient

although I am not very sure from what
the right hon. and learned Gentleman
told us that we have had much experience
of it. I rather gather he admitted saying
that the case I cited-the Bill of last
year was the only Bill we have had
since 1923.
The hon. and learned Senator talked
about the fees that would have to be paid
to persons coming before the inquiry, but
if a Parliamentary Committee was to do
any good and hear objections the same
sort of fees would have to be paid if an
objector wished to oppose. The system
now proposed is a much simpler one.
does not in any way abridge the rights of
those persons definitely interested in the
matter by way of objection to the proposed order. They have the same right
of objection-the same opportunity; in
fact, a better opportunity of bringing
their objections before the notice of the
Ministry and the Government, and, indeed, for that matter before the notice
of Parliament if necessary than they
have under the Parliamentary Committee.
system. It is much simpler. It is a
system that is working perfectly well in
England and has commended itself to
both sides in another place. The Government is not prepared to accept the
amendment.

CLAUSE 7 (War service as contributing service).
*
For the purposes of the Education
Acts (Northern Ireland), 1923 to 1939,
and the Superannuation (Teaching
Services) Acts (Northern Ireland),
1926 to 1938, and of any scheme made
under the said Acts (hereafter in this
Act referred to as "the scheme'),
where any person-
*

*
(b) having been employed as a teacher
in contributing service but being
temporarily out of employment as
such, undertakes war service and
subsequently re-enters contributing service; or
*
*

*
-*-
*
Mr. ROBB: I beg to move in paragraph (b) to leave out "and subsequently
re-enters contributing service; or."
It has been brought to my notice that
the words which I propose to leave out
would prevent the payment of a death
gratuity in the case of a teacher temporarily out of employment when he joined
up and who died whilst on war service.
The Clause requires that he should go
back into the service before anything
could be paid, and, consequently, I move
that these words be omitted. They were
inadvertently inserted in the Clause.
Amendment agreed to.
Clause, as amended, ordered to stand
part of the Bill.

(a) where school contributions

Mr. ROBB: I beg to move in subclause (2), paragraph (a) after "and"
("applicable in his case and") to insert
"a school contribution equal to."
The object of the insertion of these
words is to make it clear that the 2 per
cent. is a school contribution. Towards
the pensions of teachers there is a contribution paid by the teacher and a contribution paid by the school, and this
sub-clause provides that
where school contributions were payable in respect of the contributing
service last rendered by him before
undertaking war service
the sum to be paid is the amount equal
to the sum of the teacher's contributions
and the school contribution of 2 per
cent. of his salary. This is to make it
quite clear that if a man comes to get
back money he is not to get back money
in respect of the school's contribution,
but only in respect of his own contribution.
Amendment agreed to.
Clause, as amended, ordered to stand
part of the Bill.

CLAUSE 12 (Interpretation).
In this Act, unless the context
otherwise requires, the following expressions have the meanings hereby
assigned to them, that is to say:-
*
*
*
*
*
*
*
"War service pay," in relation to a
person engaged in war service,
means his pay (including marriage,
family and other similar allowances) in respect of his war
service.
Major BOYLE: I beg to move to
leave out "similar". I suggest that this
word be left out as on the Second Reading
the Leader of the House informed us, in
reply to a question by the hon. and
gallant Gentleman opposite, that he was
not aware what the allowances were but
they would be settled by the Ministry.
He was sure they would be sympathetically settled. I am
sure every Member
of this House is anxious that they should
be sympathetically settled, but I think
what these allowances are should be made
clear. There are a great many allowances. The words of the Clause are, I
expect, taken from the British Act, but
the cases do not appear to me to be
similar.
In Great Britain the people who are
joining the Army are compulsorily doing
so, whereas in this country they are
voluntarily doing so. I think that makes
a difference. These people who are joining
up are doing so for their country without considering their own futures. They
are giving up their careers, et cetera, and
I think they should be treated generously.
This does not really only apply to people
in this Bill because civilian employers are
anxious to treat their employees in the
same manner. Applications are coming
in to the military authorities daily to
know what these allowances are. Therefore, I think it very important that they
should be set out clearly.
There are
a considerable number of
allowances and they vary. People get
them according to their employment,
their rank, or their age. There are
marriage allowances, lodging allowances,
ration allowances, fuel and light allowances, field allowances, disturbance allowances, service allowances, dependants'
allowances, and many others, and it is
difficult to say which are similar and which
are not as set out in the Bill. The lastmentioned allowance is a very wide one. It
may include your mother, your sisters, your
divorced wives, and it may also include

allowances are meant in the Bill.

CLAUSE 11 (Power to require evidence
at inquiries).
Question proposed, "That the Clause
stand part of the Bill."
Mr. LESLIE: Yesterday afternoon.
we concerned ourselves a good deal with
discussions to some extent on the consistency of governments and to a greater
extent with the constitutional principle
of the preservation of the direct control
of Parliament. This afternoon we are
still rather at the moment on the consti-
H
451 Unemployment Insurance Bill

THE SENATE
Committee
[Mr. Leslie]
tutional tack, and the point that seems
to me to be before us in connection with
this particular Clause is the liberty of
the subject. The liberty of the subject,
I need scarcely tell the House, is a fundamental principle of our constitution, and
it is a principle which this House ought
if the necessity arises to be singularly
keen in supporting.
What does the clause before us seek to
do? What is its history? I am very
much afraid there is something in the
nature of this clause to be found in the
National Health Insurance Act, 1936,
passed by the United Kingdom. There
is certainly something in Section 290 of
the Local Government Act, 1933, of the
United Kingdom, which does not apply
to Northern Ireland. But however that
may be I could not for myself agree to
adopting in Northern Ireland anything
of this nature.
The effect of this clause would be in
effect to put the person appointed by the
Ministry on the same level as, let us say,
a magistrate or a judge of the High Court
or his court of inquiry, whatever it may
be, on a level with a Parliamentary Committee which has power to send for
persons and papers. I do not think it
can be gravely suggested that merely because the Ministry thinks some inquiry
ought to be held the holding of that
inquiry is necessarily of such great urgent
public importance as to justify an
encroachment on the ordinary law of the
land which comprises the liberty of the
subject.
It is suggested that a man should not
be required to go more than 10 miles
unless his fare is paid. In that respect
the man would be rather better off than
the ordinary juror who is summoned to
attend in Belfast for the County Antrim
Assizes. Our experience is that the complaints of jurors, however justified, are,
at all events, clamant and frequent.
People do not like to be summoned for
jury service. They will like still less to
attend an inquiry ordered at the instance
of one or other of the Ministries.
cannot see any justification in the clause
before us for imposing this liability under
penalty to attend an inquiry when, so
far as I know, there is no serious difficulty
if an inquiry on a matter of urgent public
necessity has to be held, in securing the
necessary evidence.
I
I must add that speaking from a good
many years' experience I do not remember a case in which evidence compulsorily acquired was of the same value as
evidence voluntarily tendered. We are
not given in this Clause any indication of
the sort of inquiry contemplated, and,
therefore, there is a little difficulty in
comparing it with such inquiries as one
might expect to be set on foot under the
1933 English Act to which I referred. But I must leave that. It is a
perfectly general power that is suggested
here, and I am afraid I must suggest to
the Committee that it is a power which
Parliament ought not to grant. Accordingly I must vote against the inclusion of
the clause.
Mr. ROBB: The object of this clause
is to bring unemployment insurance into
line with health insurance. There are
from time to time applications for the
formal decision of the Ministry on points
with regard to unemployment insurance,
and in respect of the unemployment end
of those inquiries there is no power to
summon and swear witnesses. There is
power in connection with health insurance, but not in connection with unemployment insurance, and it is only for
the purpose of putting the unemployment
insurance scheme and inquiry on the same
footing as the health scheme and inquiry.
In Section 161 of the Health Insurance
Act it is provided sub-section (2):
Any person appointed in accordance
with any regulations made under this
Section for the purpose of holding an
inquiry and reporting to the Minister
may by summons require any person to
attend at such time and place as is set
forth in the summons, to give evidence
or produce any documents in his
custody or under his control which
relate to the question to be determined
and may take evidence on oath and for
that purpose administer oaths.
Provided that no person shall be required in obedience to such a summons
to go more than 10 miles from his place
of residence unless the necessary expenses are paid or tendered to him.
It is in order to put in the unemployment insurance scheme the provisions
already applied to health insurance
scheme that this clause is introduced. It
must be obvious that where you have a
Minister of Ministry dealing with two
453 Unemployment Insurance Bill--- 22 NOVEMBER 1939
Committee

perience of litigation. All the facts and
all the relevant documents ought to be
available before the Ministry can come
to any decision. It is for the purpose of
making all these facts and documents
available that this clause is inserted. It
is an advantage to have the same code as
exists in connection with health insursubjects, so allied as unemployment insurance and health insurance, where the
results of an inquiry in one are very often
relevant to the question arising in the
other scheme, it is awkward and anomalous to have the power to summon under
one Act and not have power to summon
under the other Act. It is in order to
bring the two codes into agreement that
this section was introduced.
Mr. LESLIE: I am very much afraid
that that answer boils itself down to the
suggestion that having done wrong once
you ought to do it again. I do not think
there is any overpowering necessity to
assimilate the two codes. I can quite
recognise inquiries under health insurance
might be inquiries into excessive sickness
or an inquiry into the typhoid outbreak
in or about the borough of Croyden.
There is nothing said in the clause before
us as regards the right to swear a witness.
I rather think that was given in another
section.
Mr. ROBB: I believe it has power to
summon and swear.
Mr. LESLIE: There is power to
swear. I think that is as far as we
ought to go. I do not like any proposal
that a man can be compulsorily brought
from Londonderry to Belfast to attend an
inquiry instituted by the Ministry, even
if his expenses are paid, and I like almost
less bringing a man from Cullybacky to
Ballymena when his expenses would not
be paid. I do not know what the opinion
of the Committee is, but I think it would
be better that we should not pass this
clause. It is going too far altogether.
Mr. ROBB: There is one point that I
think will appeal to the hon. and learned
Senator, and it is this. The only right of
appeal from a decision founded upon an
inquiry of this sort is to the court of
appeal. Consequently it is of the highest
importance that all the relevant facts
that can be obtained should be brought
out at the inquiry. It would be most
unsatisfactory to have to go to the court
of appeal upon a case stated in a matter
of this sort, and to have to tell the court
of appeal that, while there was other
evidence that might have been given,
there was no power in the tribunal to get
that evidence. That is a point that must
appeal to anyone who has had any exance.
With regard to the question of expenses, persons travelling less than ten
miles would get their travelling expenses,
but only when travelling over that distance would they get a further viaticum.
The
Mr. LESLIE: As regards the latter
point, that is not what the Bill says. As
regards the earlier point, if this collection of all the evidence available was a
collection by a court of law I should be

suggestion is that this collection should

Mr. JOSEPH CUNNINGHAM: Even
at the present time it is quite a common
practice for the Ministry to send a postcard to a claimant asking him to attend.
Certainly he is not summoned, although
if he fails to attend he may be summoned. If a statement made by a

by his employer in most cases the Ministry

CLAUSE 14 (Power to extend Unemployment Insurance Acts to excepted
employments).
Question proposed, "That the clause
stand part of the Bill."
Mr. LESLIE: I beg to move the
deletion of this clause. It is a clause
which, for reasons that I think I may say
I gave the House in Committee during
the last few days, I am not disposed to
accept, because it seems to me that it is
another clause to give the Ministry power
to do something which it could not do at
present without the authority of Parliament. What we have got here is a provision that the Ministry of Labour, after
consultation with the Ministry of
Finance, may by regulation provide for
certain inclusions. We may take it that
the Ministry of Labour represents the
employed person, and the Ministry of
Finance represents the interest of the
McHugh, Mr. J.
McLaughlin, Mr. T.
Sinclair, Colonel T.
Treasury. The interest of the employing
person is, perhaps, left a little in the
air.
The public are becoming accustomed to
the fact that the Ministry of Labour
regards the employer of labour as public
enemy No. 1. I do not think the
employer likes that very much, but it is
borne in upon him so frequently that he
has to recognise it as a fact. Apart from
that, there is the same objection as I
have voiced recently-the objection to the
Ministry doing anything fresh which at
present it cannot do without the more
direct authority of Parliament.
The people in Part 2 are given, of
course, in the Act of 1936, which I have
not got before me. I have only got the
Act of 1934, and since 1934 there have
been a good many alterations. Private
gardeners were in the Schedule of the
1934 Act, and they are not there now. I
think we must leave it on that broad
question as to whether this clause is not
conferring power on the Ministry, after
consultation with the Ministry of
Finance, to do something which at present they cannot do without the more
direct authority of Parliament. Subject
to anything the right hon. Gentleman
457 Unemployment Insurance Bill-
22 NOVEMBER 1939

Committee
may have to tell us he may be able to
go rather more into detail than I have
had time to go-I am not disposed to
support this Clause either.
Mr. ROBB: This is really a very
harmless little clause, although it looks
bad.
Mr. LESLIE: It looks wicked.
Some
Mr. ROBB: Its only object is to put
right what is a very small difficulty.
Under Section 3, sub-section (2) of the
Act of 1936, power is given to the
Ministry of Labour to exclude from Part
2 of Schedule 1 a class of person.
cases have arisen where the Ministry was
clearly of opinion that, while the whole
class of persons should not be excluded,
there were particular individuals in that
class who should be excluded. All that
this Clause does is to enable the Ministry
to say that particular persons shall become insurable, although they do not say
that the whole class to which that person
or persons belong is to become insurable.
Cases have mainly arisen in connection
with two classes of people, theatre attendants and sextons of churches. Where a
man is a sexton of a church he may do
only one or two hours' work in addition
to his time on Sunday, and that class of
person is not insurable. But there are
cases of particular individuals whose work
as sexton of a church is so constant that
really they ought to be insured. On the
other hand, there are theatre attendants,
some of whom are employed only three or
four hours in the evening on certain days
of the week. On the other hand, there
are persons who work in cinemas and who
are employed all day long, and obviously
they ought to be included. The Ministry
at present has power to include as insurable a whole class of persons. If they
want to make an order they have to make
one that all sections, for instance, should
be insurable, and they do not want to do
that. They want in this clause power to
say that particular individuals belonging
to a class are really properly insurable.
That is all the difference that this clause
makes in the law. At the present time
the Ministry can transfer the whole class,
but they cannot transfer particular individuals, and that is all that this clause
gives them power to do. I think it is a
reasonable thing.
Mr. LESLIE: I am quite sure that I
am right in assuming that the regulations
to be made will have to be laid on the
Table of the House in the ordinary way?
Mr. ROBB: They will.
Mr. LESLIE: That being so it is with
great pleasure that one receives an explanation which one can accept, and
accept in a gracious spirit. I ask leave
to withdraw the amendment.
Amendment, by leave, withdrawn.
Clause ordered to stand part of the
Bill.

that we have to remember that these rates
of insurance may rise. What will happen
when the war comes to an end it is impossible to foresee. We do know that there
must be a considerable disturbance as regards unemployment one way and
another. We cannot forecast that, and
we hope it is not in the very distant
future.
Then, again, there is the fact that we
are very anxious, I think, to encourage
men, to continue to work on the land, and
slight as it is this provision is, at all
events, a slight inducement to enter into
a contract which will endure for some
months. I think that is an inducement
we ought to maintain, at all events, for a
few more years to see whether it is of
any value or not. If experience shows in
three or four years' time that this proposal is not of any great advantage it
may be simply left to expire; but the
time has not yet come for direct interference by Parliament. I do not think we
should disturb the existing arrangement.
The right hon. and learned Gentleman
described this Bill as an omnibus Bill. I
suggest that this particular clause is a
clause that may wait for a bus.
Mr. ROBB: As the hon. and learned
Senator has said, in 1936 this concession
was given by way of inducement to
farmers and agricultural labourers to
make long-term hirings. It has failed,
and that is why the clause is here. There
are 30,000 insurable persons in agriculture in our area, and only 600 are on
long-term hirings. In respect of this 600
out of 30,000 rebates to the amount of
£170 are given. It costs £200 to make
these rebates. It is felt by the Ministry
that the inducement has failed. It is
costing more than it is worth, and the
sooner it stops the better. I may say
that this matter came before the Unemployment Insurance Committee that sits
for the United Kingdom investigating
unemployment questions that turn up
from time to time, and they recommended
that this change should be made and the
concessions withdrawn. It has been
withdrawn in England and we propose
doing the same. There are ample grounds
for doing it.
Mr. LESLIE: The point was that when
Lord Charlemont introduced the Bill he
drew attention to the fact that this proposal obtained to a much greater extent
in Northern Ireland than in England or
even Great Britain. It obtains to some
extent in Scotland. The whole point
between us is whether sufficient time has
been allowed for it to be decided that this
experiment, to adopt the right hon.
and learned Gentleman's phrase, has
failed. I myself do not think we have
had quite enough time and with the incidence of the war coming in we ought to
wait a little longer. I have not heard
anything from Members representing
other parts of the country. I can only
speak for myself. Ought we to take
away this right whatever be its value or
ought we not?
Mr. ROBB: The Government say it is
not worth while at all.
Mr. LESLIE: I do not think it is for
the Government to say it is not worth
while. It is for the employed and
employers between them, and that is
what one would like to hear.
Mr. ROBB: If you take the employers
and employed between them
you have
30,000 to 600. Is not that majority
sufficient for anybody?
Mr. MAGUIRE: My opinion is that
this condition is not generally known to
farmers in the area. Take my own case.
I employ a good many men on long term
and I have never made any application
yet. I think most farmers are not aware
of this condition, and if it was generally
known greater advantage would be taken
of it. I believe we should give more
time in order to test the matter.

Agricultural Returns Bill.
Order for Second Reading read.
Mr. ROBB: Our agricultural statistics
in Ireland date back to 1847, the year
of the great famine. The Government
of that day found such statistics
necessary in order to secure a more or
less accurate picture of the food production of the country in what was at that
time a position of considerable peril.
Since 1847 our statistics in Northern
Ireland have only been interrupted for
three years, so that for now nearly a
century our agricultural returns have
been a peculiar and characteristic feature
of our rural life, and the police
enumerator has become quite a familiar
and friendly visitor to our farmers.
To-day, almost a century since the
beginning of our agricultural returns, an
emergency makes it necessary to introduce
a Bill to make those returns statutory.
This does not mean that our farmers are
to be called upon to do anything that
they were not doing for nearly ninety
years. It does not mean that each individual farmer will be called upon to
perform onerous and intricate clerical
operations. The method of collection of
statistics will remain as at present. The
police enumerators will continue their
friendly visits, but, with the furnishing
Agricultural Returns Bill-
Second Reading
23 NOVEMBER 1939
of information imposed on everybody as
a statutory duty, the Government will
now be certain to have a true and correct
picture of our agriculture in all its varied.
phases. They will be able to estimate
the effect of its policy to decide what
change in policy or what new policy it is
desirable to apply to any particular
branch of the industry, and a very important matter-to have information
necessary for the proper enforcing of the
Tillage Order.
It has been suggested that this
measure should be a purely emergency
measure and should only remain in
operation for the duration of the war.
But I might be allowed to point out that
our subsidy payments on barley, oats and
wheat depend to a large extent on these
returns. For instance, the oat subsidy
decreases in proportion to the excess area
above a certain acreage in any particular
case. Now these agricultural returns
at
provide the information. as to that
acreage. Again, and this must apply
with particular force during the war, it
is essential that the Government
almost any time should be able to give a
fairly accurate forecast of the acreage
under any crop and the likely yield of
that crop. This can only be done through
our agricultural returns.
Turning to the Bill itself, Clause I
specifies the returns which will in future
be placed upon a statutory basis. In
this the Government are only asking the
farmers in Northern Ireland to do exactly
the same thing as farmers in England
and Scotland have been doing since the
year 1925. The returns will, with one
exception to which I will refer in one
moment, be treated with the same degree
of confidence and secrecy as has prevailed
in the past, and the Bill makes special
provision to that effect.
Clause 2 does make special provision
that during the present emergency the
returns may be used at the discretion of
the Ministry in connection with their
tillage and increased production campaign
and SO will facilitate the proper
distribution of agricultural machinery,
implements, seeds, fertilizers and feeding
stuffs.
In conclusion, may I say that I am
certain that this House will recognise the
necessity that the Ministry of Agriculture should possess the fullest possible
information regarding every detail of our
agricultural industry, particularly at the
present time. And I hope that experience will show that this information
will prove equally useful in forwarding
the interests of our entire agricultural
community when the war comes to an
end. It is a short Bill and deals with
only one subject. I beg to move.
Question proposed, "That the Bill be
now read a Second time."
Mr. LESLIE: I am quite sure I am
not speaking entirely for myself when
I say we regret the absence this afternoon of the right hon. Gentleman, the
Minister of Agriculture. Some of us
had looked forward rather confidently to
his presence to assist in our deliberations.
Mr. ROBB: May I say that if I had
known there was any such anticipation
I would have asked him to be here.
Mr. LESLIE: At all events he is not
here, and I confess myself disappointed
that he is not. The Bill itself as it
comes before us is to my mind a very
difficult Bill. It is a Bill which I do
not think the House ought to accept in
its present form and, therefore, to keep
myself in order, I must move that the
word "now" be left out of the right
hon. Gentleman's motion with a view,
of course, of adding "this day six
months" at a later stage if we find it
necessary to proceed to a division on
what may be described as the main
Question. I think I ought to make
that motion. I imagine the debate will
occupy some time and range over a
somewhat varied field.
I have said the Bill is a difficult Bill,
and it is difficult in this way. If the
Bill was designed purely for war purposes
-the right hon. Gentleman tells us it
is not I am sure that none of us would
be disposed to offer anything in the
nature of opposition to any provisions it
contained which could be shown to be
likely to be of advantage. I say likely.
We should not have asked for very much
proof. But if the Bill is to be an enduring Bill it becomes necessary to
examine it much more closely.
Again, the principle of the Bill seems
to be to rely for the future on information compulsorily extracted. In the
past the bulk of the information asked
Agricultural Returns Bill
Second Reading
THE SENATE
[Mr. Leslie]
for or adumbrated in the. Bill before us
has been obtained voluntarily and without any difficulty whatever. As the
right hon. Gentleman said, the police
investigator has become a familiar and,
I may say, a welcome figure.. He has
always been given, so far as I know, as
much information as he required, and if
the information was not exhaustive at
the moment he has always been encouraged to go and get it for himself.
on
As investigations are conducted in the
country the constable is usually a local
man. He goes to so-and-so and says:
"How much turnips have you got?" The
farmers says: "I have got that field down
there." The investigator may go
and say: "What is the area of it?" The
owner probably does not know and he
says to the investigator: "Go and look
at it." The investigator does go and
look at it, and probably he is a good
hand at estimating the extent of the field,
or he may go to the barracks and look at
the ordnance map to get the information.
So there is no difficulty whatever in
obtaining, by the voluntary method, the
information required.
Now it is proposed to substitute compulsion, and one of the objections to that
seems to me to be that so long as you rely
on voluntary information you are prepared to accept the best information you
can reasonably get, but directly you
begin to require a man under penalty to
fill up a form, make a statement or give
information, it puts that man to some
extent on his guard. He wants to look
round a little more carefully and see
what are the grounds on which he bases
his estimate of the circumstances of the
case. I propose to show in a minute or
two that the man is not unlikely to be in
extreme difficulty in giving accurate
answers to some of the questions it is
proposed to ask. For instance, a man
with a farm of some size may,
let us
suggest, have one field in grazing which
he does not want for his own purposes
and which he has set to somebody else
to graze.
You come to that man-if you
accept the suggestion that the owner of
the farm is necessarily the occupier-and
ask for information as to the number of
livestock there. The man does not
know, and says he does not know. I am
speaking from experience, and I can understand that he might not know. He has
let the field for grazing, and he will not
know how many beasts are on it on the
prescribed day. You are coming within
six weeks of the prescribed day, and it
is putting an impossible burden on that
man. You will not get accurate information in that respect.
Passing from that to some of the other
things, the right hon. Gentleman reminded us that agricultural statistics had
been furnished practically regularly since
1847, the year of the famine. He said
there had been an interval of three years,
but he did not tell us what those three
years were.
Mr. ROBB: I do not know.
Mr. LESLIE: If they were the years
I am thinking of the right hon. Gentleman would have known. He has pointed
out that the year 1847 was the famine
year, a year of great trouble and
emergency, and he rather suggested-I
hope he was completely wrong-that the
present period is a time
of equal
emergency. The reason I hope he is
wrong is that I hope we are not in the
position of a beleaguered city, hard put
to it for food. I am sure the right hon.
Gentleman shares that aspiration also.
Between 1847 and the present time there
have been four years of difficulty, the
four years 1914 to 1918, the years of the
Great War. I cannot myself believe
that the emergency of to-day is any
greater than it was during those four
years. The food position in the United
Kingdom was infinitely worse during
portion of that period than it is at
present, and infinitely worse than we
hope it will be in the future. I do not
think there is anything at all in that
part of the argument.
Then again, the right hon. Gentleman
alluded to the Tillage Order. The
Tillage Order, so called, is made under
the Defence. of the Realm Statute. I
cannot see that, on the face of things,
it has anything to do with the Bill
before us, which is not brought as a war
measure. It is brought as a general
measure, applicable relatively for all
time. If the Bill has any special relation
to the Tillage Order we ought to be told
so, and we are not. That is one of the
reasons why I am going to say later that
the Bill seems to have got itself into a

I think I told the House before, based

Those two measures are mixed up with

what are called cereal subsidies depend,

ordinary legislation passed
passed by the

under which growers of certain cereal

for some oats grown by myself, but the
question of those subsidies is again apart
from the Tillage Order. These cereal
subsidies are part of the, peace-time legislation at the moment.
Whether those cereal subsidies will
continue under war-time legislation we
do not know. The general opinion of
the country is that they will not, and
that they will remain, so to speak, on
the slate, but will not be called upon,
because they depend at present on the

generally thought that the average price.
of the cereal crop during 1940 and subsequent years will rise above that level to
which the subsidy is applicable. However
that may be on this point definitely concerned with the Bill before us the fact
remains that the cereal subsidies for the

and they are being paid on the
information furnished and obtained
voluntarily, not compulsorily. I do not.
think there is anything whatever in that
argument.
It has been found possible hitherto to
get the necessary information-I agree it
is necessary-voluntarily, without relying
on compulsion. If we are to rely on

Bill as being a permanent measure and
not a purely war-time measure, it seems

become, perhaps, an effective instrument.
I am afraid I have digressed a little
owing to some of the things said in the

come to look at the Bill as a permanent
measure; that is to say, as a measure
which is not solely intended for wartime conditions. If the Minister of
Agriculture-whom I am glad to say has
now joined our counsels-is able to assure
us that he will accept an amendment in
Committee to make this Bill a war-time
measure, with, of course, power at the
end of the war to adopt so much of it for
permanent use as may seem necessary,
the greater part of the objection will
disappear. Until that is done we have
to examine the Bill most meticulously
and almost line by line, because there are
things we could not accept as a permanent addition to our Statute Book, but
which we might gloss over very easily if
it was a war-time measure.
In any case, there would be some
slight alteration necessary in the Bill,
although I do not think they would give

seems to be a notion outside to regard

Those are matters which the public will

mean by "occupier," whether you mean

is the conacre tenant, and if you really

necessary that you should give a definition of "occupier" which would include

man may have a very large area of land
and use it for grazing, and if you really
want to know how much livestock is on
the land that is the man you have to

he will not know.

weeks it does not seem to me to argue
a very true appreciation of the facts of
the situation. You have such things as
fairs and markets in Northern Ireland.
You have also, usually, town parks
immediately outside the town where
those fairs and markets are held. Some
of those fields are let to a cattle dealer,
and in that field or fields he, so to speak,
parks his cattle for a day or two before
the market.
If you happen on the prescribed day

your information as regards livestock is
likely to be distinctly inaccurate, and if
wait for six weeks after the preyou

going to get the facts you ask for.

do.

The Ministry may require

all the information you require voluntarily. If you ask for it compulsorily
you are going to put the man's back up
and he is not going to help you. At all
events, if the farmer is required to give
the information more than once in a
year he is almost put to the necessity
of engaging a clerk-at least every big man
will be. He has to get an office for his
clerk. Your ordinary farmer cannot build
a room on the roof to take in an extra
staff. Farmers are only limited to a
number of days in the week, and they
will not have time, after carrying out
their other duties, to do all the work

The MINISTER OF AGRICULTURE
(Sir Basil Brooke): There are two phases
in this Bill. There is first of all the
actual war measure and the Tillage
Order which is involved in this scheme,
and further the peace time necessity for
a compulsory census. I was not, unfortunately, here to hear all the hon.
and learned Senator's criticisms on this
matter, but I gave an assurance elsewhere that the method which has heretofore been used by the farmer in giving
information to the R.U.C. would be
continued under the present scheme. It
would not be expected, as some hon.
Members in another place thought
possible, that he himself would have to
compile the returns of all the fearsome
details named in the scheme. The
method will continue just as at present,
but it will be. compulsory instead of
voluntary.
There is another point which I think
is important. The census in this country
has been in existence, I think, for
twenty years longer than it has been in
England. It originally started, if I
remember aright, at the time of the
famine, and it has been voluntary up to
the present time. In England it is compulsory. Therefore, it was not necessary
for them to bring in the same type of
measure we are introducing now.
We are placed in this position. May
I take the war reason first. That is the
most important. Hon. Members will remember that in the Tillage Order it is
laid down that a farmer shall plough 10
per cent. over his 1939 arable area, and
that he shall plough a minimum of onefifth of his total arable holding. Unless
that information can be given accurately
it is obviously an easy thing for the
Tillage Order to be evaded. It is to be
presumed to a great extent, not completely, that the information which has
been given up to the present has been
fairly accurate. I heard in another place
Members saying the information was completely inaccurate. As far as we know
it was, on the whole, fairly accurate.
becomes much more important here where
it is necessary to see that every acre that
can be ploughed is ploughed. That is
as far as the tillage campaign is concerned.
It
It goes further than that-there is the
war measure. Hon. Members will
realise that in the production of food it
may be necessary to allocate certain feeding stuffs in a certain way. It is inadvisable here to say anything more on
that subject. I am sure hon. Members
will understand what I mean. It is
necessary to find out what classes of feeding stuffs are retained in certain holdings. There may be certain farmers who
have a very large stock and therefore do
not require further supplies. There may
be some who have nothing at all left on
their farms and they will require to be
supplied. There may be the question of
the distribution of fertilisers. There
again in order to get an equitable distribution it will be necessary to find out
where the fertilisers are lying-whether
one particular district is short and one
particular district has a surplus, because
during a war it is necessary-and I think
it is a right thing that the Government
should as far as they can see that every
individual gets assistance much as
possible.
as
It is quite conceivable that certain.
farmers may have had a long view and
stored a considerable amount of fertilisers,
but if there is a shortage in the remainder

by team work, by everybody working together in order to enable us
to get
through the difficult times we are going
to have.
The hon. and learned Gentleman
raised the question of sub-clause (2) of
Clause 2, which states:--
For the purposes of sub-section (1)
of this section any agricultural
statistics collected by the Ministry
before the beginning of the period of
the present emergency shall be deemed
to be returns made under section one
of this Act.
The only reason for the insertion of that
sub-clause is the fact that at the present
moment there are no figures whereby the
10 per cent. increase in tillage can be
made unless the information given at the
last census is used for this purpose. That
is the intention of the sub-section.
Perhaps I have made my point quite
plain to the hon. and learned Member.
Mr. LESLIE: Does that mean that
for whatever purpose you like you are
going to use information which you
obtained on the understanding that you
would not use it?
Sir BASIL BROOKE: Yes.
Mr. LESLIE: It does?
Sir BASIL BROOKE: Yes, for the
purpose of the Tillage Order, which is a
war measure. There is no other means.
The hon. Member, I think, shakes his
head at that. Since that census was
taken war has broken out. There was
no compulsory census prior to this Bill.
The only information the Ministry can
work on is voluntary information which
was given before. If sub-clause (2)
were not in it it would be perfectly futile
to talk of increasing tillage by 10 per
cent., because there would be no knowledge of what it was before. Therefore,
that information must be used owing to
the war.
Mr. LESLIE: Then you must march
through Belgium?
Sir BASIL BROOKE: I should not
care to put it that way.
Major DOBBS: May I ask what
the right hon. Gentleman is
census
referring to?
Sir BASIL BROOKE: The voluntary returns which in the ordinary course
of events have been made hitherto.
Those figures will be used in making the
calculation as to the increased tillage
required on an individual's holding.
Major DOBBS: The right hon. Gentleman was not referring to any particular
census but to the last few ones.
Sir BASIL BROOKE: The ordinary
yearly census. I think what the hon. and
learned Gentleman objected to was that
this information was confidential information. Indeed, it always has been confidential information. He objects to the
fact that it is now being used in a way
which is not necessarily confidential.
The reason it is being so used is that it
provides the only figures of which the
Ministry are in possession and on which
they make any calculation for instructing
a farmer as to the amount of increased
tillage he has to do.
I have dealt with the war side of this
measure, and I will now turn to the
other point which the hon. and learned
Member raised, the question of whether
it would be necessary to continue it
during peace.. The House and the hon.
Gentleman are, I am sure, aware that
for some considerable time there have
been subsidies paid on wheat, oats, and
barley. I am talking now strictly of
peace-time conditions. The House is
also aware that should the oats acreage
exceed a certain acreage then the assistance. SO far as oats is concerned is
reduced. That means that it is necessary
from that point of view alone that we
should know how many acres of oats are
grown in this country. As I say, in
England and Scotland that information
is compulsory. So far the British
Government have accepted the voluntary
information which we have given them.
I think we would be placed in a very
difficult position at the end of the war if
Agricultural Reurns Bill-
Second Reading
THE SENATE
[Sir Basil Brooke]
this Bill was abolished and they said:
"We accepted your voluntary information in the past. You then made it
compulsory. Are you going back on it
and making it voluntary? We may
perhaps doubt the figures you have given
us.
""
There is another point which I am sure
the House will appreciate. It is impossible to formulate any policy unless
you know what the condition of affairs
is. One policy which I myself have been
very anxious to see fructifying is an increase of the cow population in this
country. The increase has been very
slow, and I have been criticised. I
admit it has not gone on as well as I
hoped it would, but still we have made
progress. I will touch on that for a
moment. The system we have adopted
is assistance to the creameries, and by so
doing we hope gradually to build up an
increased cow population in this country.
Unless the information was available as
to whether we were succeeding in our
policy it would leave the Minister and
the Ministry in the dark as to what steps
should be taken to overcome the lack of
progress.
That is just one example. Then there
is the question of the spinning trade.
They want to know each year the flax
area and what flax is likely to be
available. If there is not sufficient they
can then make their arrangements in
other places to get more. Therefore,
from the peace side we are not moving
out of step with Great Britain. From the
subsidy point of view I think it is
essential we should have it, and from my
comparatively short experience in the
Ministry I know that unless one had
some information that one could rely on
it would be impossible to formulate a
policy. But I will say this to the hon.
and learned Member-I gave this
assurance in another place--if at the end
of the war any hon. Member in this
House or elsewhere feels the Act is being
a burden or is not of assistance the
Government will give it every consideration, but for the moment it is essential.
I want to assure the House that without
this Bill I cannot work the tillage
scheme. In my opinion in peace time
it is also necessary.

There is one thing I strongly object
to in regard to this Bill.
I agree with
what the hon. and learned Senator (Mr.
Leslie) has said with regard to it. He
said that for the purposes of this Bill
the returns given by a farmer are to be
used directly in any prosecution against
that particular farmer. That is not as
it should be. That is practically putting
a man in the dock on his own evidence,
evidence which he has been made to give
himself before any prosecution is brought
against him. We all know perfectly
well that most farmers in the country
know the number of fields they own, but
could not say the exact acreage of each
field. A great many farmers would not
be able to give the acreage under crops.

28 NOVEMBER 1939
THE SENATE.

Tuesday, 28th November, 1939.
The Senate met at Half-past Two of
the Clock, Mr. SPEAKER in the Chair.
Papers Presented.
Ministry of Home Affairs:
The Goods Vehicles (Amendment)
Regulations (Northern Ireland), 1939.
Ministry of Labour:
Factories (Laundries-Overtime) Regutions (Northern Ireland), 1939.
Laid before the House (pursuant to
Act) and ordered to lie on the Table.
Essential Buildings and Plant (Repair
of War Damage) Bill.
Order for Third Reading read.
Resolved, "That this Bill be now read
the Third time."--(Mr. Robb).
Resolved,
"That this Bill do now
pass." (Mr. Robb).
And a message was sent to the House
of Commons to acquaint them that the
Senate agree to the said Bill with an
Amendment, to which they desire their
concurrence.
Liability for War Damage (Miscellaneous
Provisions) Bill.
Order for Second Reading read.
Mr. ROBB: This is a short Bill and
similar to an Act passed by the Imperial
Parliament. It really is not controversial. Briefly, the Bill is designed to
ensure that a bailee, that is to say, one
in possession of goods without actually
owning them, shall not be liable for such
goods in his possession which suffer loss
or damage as a result of war. The
various clauses of the Bill cover goods
possessed under hire purchase agreements, goods on approval, or on "sale
or return" and certain goods in the
custody of an innkeeper.
The Bill on which this is based gives
statutory effect to the recommendations
that were made by the Committee on
Liability for War Damage, which
reported on this problem early in September last. The passage of this Measure
will ensure that the public is given
adequate protection in war time in the
cases which the Bill is designed to cover.
I beg to move.
Question proposed, That the Bill be
now read a Second time.
Mr. LESLIE: There is no objection
that could possibly be taken to the Bill,
but there are one or two small matters
which I will mention in order that we
may consider whether one or two
verbal alterations might be made, preferably after Third Reading in order to
save time. It is a very trifling matter.
First of all, I should like to draw
attention to Clause 4 of the Bill, dealing
with possible exemptions under the laws.
relating to excise. I conclude that the
Government is quite satisfied that that
Clause is within our powers. As the
House knows, we have difficulties in
that respect.
There are some matters
we can deal with and some we cannot.
I presume the Government is content we
can pass that particular Clause.
Another curious little point arises and
that is the protection given to a man as
regards the compulsory production of
goods which have been lost by war action.
That is all right, but the protection also
extends to goods damaged by war action.
It is a little difficult at first sight to see
how the revenue is adequately protected
in that way. It is not a question I propose to raise if the right hon. Gentleman
is satisfied that this Bill follows word for
word the Imperial statute. If it does
we shall pass over that point. Early on
in the Bill, I think it is the first Clause,
liberty is given to a bailee to put goods
in what he considers a safer place--if he
considers they would be safer from
damage. Surely we ought to say safer
from war damage? Again, in the ensuing
Clause we are giving protection from the
obligation imposed by contract, custom
and so on. We do not say anything
about the obligation imposed by law. I
am thinking of rule of law rather than
statute.
491 Execution of Trusts Bill

THE SENATE
Gas Undertakings Bill
[Mr. Leslie]
It does seem to me we want to insert
the two words "by war" in the first
Clause, sub-clause (2), and I am inclined
to think the two words "by law" in the
second Clause. But these are such
purely verbal matters that if the right
hon. Gentleman agrees they can very
well stand until the Third Reading and
not detain the House in Committee.
Mr. ROBB: I agree.
On Question, Motion agreed to.
Bill committed to a Committee of the
Whole House for to-morrow.
Execution of Trusts (Emergency
Provisions) Bill.
Order for Second Reading read.
Mr. ROBB: This is a Bill similar to
one passed during the last war, and it is
intended to deal with this sort of a position: By rule of law, a trustee cannot
delegate his duties to somebody else. He
must perform them himself. The reason
for that is obvious. A man may appoint
another person as trustee because, he has
faith in his integrity and judgment. But
it may very well happen that a trustee or
other class of person dealt with in the Bill
is abroad on war service. In that event
he is not here to do the things that as a
trustee are confided to him. Accordingly,
this Bill makes provision that a trustee
going abroad shall have power to appoint
some other person to perform the trustee's
duties while he is away. It applies to
certain other classes of persons, personal
representatives and tenants for life under
the Land Acts. The principle is the
same throughout the classes of cases.
It does make one limitation of the
power to appoint another person in your
place as trustee, that is where there are
two trustees, "A" and "B," and they
are both going on war service, they
cannot both appoint the same person as
deputy. A man appoints two trustees
because he likes to have two people
exercising judgment in any particular
matter. In order that his intention in
that respect shall not be defeated, it is
provided in the Bill that where there are
two trustees both going abroad, each of
them must appoint a separate trustee.
unless the person to whom powers is given

trust company.

Mr. LESLIE: Again a simple and
useful Bill. It is based, the right hon.
Gentleman told us, on two Acts which
passed the Imperial Parliament during
the last war. One of those Acts was
passed in 1914 and the other in 1915,
and the curious will find them as
Chapters 13 and 70 of the appropriate
regnal years, for practical purposes, 1914
and 1915. Only one point occurs to me.
If the House will allow me, I will endeavour to deal with it in the same way as
I suggested in the last Bill, by the addition of a word or two after Third
Reading. The point is, we are setting up
in sub-clause (6) of Clause 1 something
in the nature of what is called a fence.
I think perhaps we ought to take that
fence a little. further, in order to give a
definite indication that we do not intend
to interfere with the validity of anything
done under any other power. I do not
think there is any fence of the description in either of the two Acts I have
referred to. That again is a very small
matter and can be appropriately considered before we actually pass the Bill.
We need not trouble with it at an earlier
stage.

Gas Undertakings Bill.
Order for Second Reading read.
Mr. ROBB: This is a short Bill dealing
with two points in connection with gas
undertakings. If a gas undertaking has
been set up by special Act of Parliament
which incorporates the Companies Clauses
Consolidation Act, 1845, it is bound by
the provision in section 85 of that Act,
that no person holding an office of profit
under the company shall be capable of
being a director of the undertaking. In
an undertaking carried on by an ordinary
limited company appointed under the
Companies Act no such provision would
have applied. But that provision still
binds those undertakings set up under
special Act of Parliament incorporating
the Companies Clauses Consolidation Act.
Agricultural Returns Bill--
Committee
28 NOVEMBER 1939
I may say that in England many years
ago the restriction was removed, but it
still persists here. It so happens that in
the City of Derry they have a gas undertaking which is bound by this restriction,
and at the present time they have a Board
of whom there is only one active at the
moment, one being ill, and two others
being on active service, and they desire
to appoint the manager a director of
the company. There is no real objection to that. They have themselves.
faced with this difficulty. But it may be
other cases may arise presently or in the
future.. By request, the Government has
introduced this part of the Bill.
a
The second part deals with a different
matter altogether. Clause 2 refers to
local
the procedure under which
authority can seek powers to establish a
gas undertaking. As the law stands at
present an Urban District Council has
the choice of applying to the Ministry of
Home Affairs for a Provisional Order, to
be later confirmed by an Act of Parliament under the provisions of the Public
Health (Ireland) Act, or of applying to
the Ministry of Commerce for a Special
Order under the provisions of the Gas
Regulation Act, 1920. The two Departments would prefer that gas should come
under the control mainly of the Ministry
of Commerce, the same as electricity.
Accordingly, this Clause will limit
authorities in future to the second alternative, that is, procedure under the
Ministry of Commerce rather than the
Ministry of Home Affairs. The matter
is not at all contentious. It is to facilitate the Departments. I beg to move.
On Question, Motion agreed to.
Bill committed to a Committee of the
Whole House for to-morrow.

Mr. LESLIE: I beg to move, at the
beginning of Clause 1, to insert the
words, "During the period of the
present emergency." If the Committee
decide to accept this Amendment the
Committee will take a very long step to
meet the desires expressed, I may say,
in both Houses of Parliament. They
will also take steps which will mitigate
very much, if indeed they do not entirely
remove, the objection on the part of the
farmer to being dragooned. That is the
farmers' objection-to being required
now to do by compulsion what, to some
extent at all events, he has hitherto done
voluntarily. The farmer does not really
understand why he is to be compelled in
this matter. I am sure he would accept
compulsion during war time as a war
measure without anything like the same
objection he would feel and voice, when
he gets the chance, in respect of a
Measure intended to endure permanently.
Most of the Clause, to my mind-I do
not say the really most important part
of the Clause, but most of the Clause,
perhaps, is only tolerable during war
time as a war emergency. The Clause
differs very materially from the law in
England as laid down in the Agricultural Returns Act of 1925,
1925, to
which reference was made by the
right hon. Gentleman, who introduced the Bill, and the right hon.
Gentleman, the Minister of Agriculture,
who spoke upon it afterwards. The
Agricultural Returns Act of 1925, the
United Kingdom Statute, did not apply
to Northern Ireland, and it differed
from the Bill now before us in the
manner which I am now about to
specify.
Agricultural Returns Bill-
Committee
THE SENATE
[Mr. Leslie]
In the first place, the 1925 Act said
that these returns might be required
annually. The Bill before us says "at
any time or times." That is, the Bill
would give the Ministry power to
catechise the unfortunate farmer week
by week instead of once a year. That is
the first difference. Secondly, there is
some difference as regards the period
allowed. The Act of 1925 gives a man
definitely twenty-one days to get in his
return after service of the notice. The
method of dealing with that in the Bill
is somewhat different. I need not lay
stress on it because, with this Amendment, it would really make no practical
difference.
The Imperial Act does not ask any
questions about agricultural machinery,
nor does it require information as regards seeds, fertilisers, or feeding stuffs
held by a farmer. That is rather a
material difference, and it is a point on
which the farmer may be ready to help
you as a war-time measure. He will
recognise the necessity of the Ministry.
being in possession of that information
as
a purely war-time measure, but he
does not recognise the right of intrusion
into his private affairs during peace
time.
Again, the Imperial Act makes no
requisition as to information on the subject of poultry. That may be, perhaps,
because the keeping of poultry was not
such an important matter in Great
Britain in 1925 as it is in Northern Ireland in 1939. At all events, the word
poultry does not appear in the Imperial
Statute. This same Act of 1925 limits
itself to a day. The information will
be asked for in respect of a day, not for
a period ending with such a day, and
that may give farmers some little
difficulty.
The Act of 1925 does not apply to
holdings of less than one acre in extent.
There is no such saving in the Bill before
us. The. Act of 1925 requires that Regulations made are to be laid in draft before
Parliament before they are to become
effective. That is not the same as the
Bill before us. It provides that Regulations shall be made, and shall be good
unless they are objected to. I consider
that all those variations could, as I say,
be put up with cheerfully as a war-time
measure, but they are not acceptable as
a peace-time permanent arrangement.
If the Government insists on maintaining the permanent character of this Bill,
I can only say that what they will be
doing is this. They will be pressing
through Parliament, both Chambers of
which are attenuated by absence of
Members on war service, and the sittings
of which are necessarily somewhat abbreviated owing to war-time conditions, a
Bill which will not be a popular measure
if it is passed as it stands. If it is
amended in the way I suggest, and in
other ways we will come to later on, it
may become a measure which will be
accepted and worked cheerfully, whereas
on the contrary it will not be accepted in
a cheerful spirit. I beg to move the
Amendment standing in my name.
The MINISTER OF AGRICULTURE
(Sir Basil Brooke): I regret that I cannot
accept this amendment. I appreciate the
arguments which the hon. and learned
Senator has put forward, but there is this
point to be considered. Certain information is required now which was not
required when the Act was passed in
1925, as, for instance, information relating to oats, wheat, and barley.
Again, the pig scheme was not functioning in 1925, and a whole host of information is now required which was not
required at that date, in order that this
Government and the Imperial Government can decide what action should be
taken. I think the hon. and learned
Senator is quite agreed that from the
emergency point of view this information
is necessary.
I think that is obvious to
everybody.
It is as regards the application of the
Act during peace time that there is a
difference of opinion. I quite appreciate
all that the hon. and learned Senator
has said with regard to these. Rules and
Regulations, which farmers object to. It
is a very natural objection, and it is an
objection which everybody has a certain.
amount of sympathy with. At the same
time, the farmers have got to face. this
fact; that either they have got to put up
with a certain amount of bureaucracyif I may use that word-and get Government assistance, or else be allowed to be
complete masters in their own house and
get no assistance. I do not think it is
possible for the Government, which con-
Agricultural Returns Bill—
28 NOVEMBER 1939
Committee
trols the fund from which public money
is distributed to the farmers, to allow
that money to be distributed unless there
is a certain amount of supervision.
An example of what I am referring to
has occurred quite recently. There has
been a rise in the price of milk. It
would be quite wrong for the Government to say that the price of milk should
be a statutory price if the Government
did not at the same time ensure that the
consumer was getting the article it was
laid down that the farmer shall produce.
Therefore, if there is to be assistance,
there must be a certain amount of official
control, although I admit there is an
objection to it. We would much prefer
that agriculture should stand on its own
feet without the supervision it now has
to undergo.
It may interest the Committee to know
that each year we are dealing with some
50,000 applications for subsidies under
the oats, barley, and wheat scheme, and
the total amount of money involved is
no less than £450,000. I am sure the
Committee will realise that if there is
any laxity in making returns, or if wrong
information is given, it would be a most
serious thing for the Government responsible for handing out such a vast amount
of public funds to the farmers, unless
the Minister was in a position to stand
over the request he made to the. Imperial
Treasury for those payments. I believe
that in the past, when the furnishing of
these returns was on a voluntary basis,
they were probably accurate enough. I
do not suppose the returns were
pletely accurate, but I think it is very
necessary now, with these very large sums
involved, that a complete and definite.
check should be placed on them.
com-
The farmers must really face the
position that if this Amendment is
accepted and the furnishing of these
returns was not made compulsory when
the war is over, there would be a very
serious risk of the Treasury saying,
"Very well, if you are not prepared to
make your returns in the same way as
we on this side are doing you must find
your own money." I do not think the
making of these returns is going to be
onerous. We
are merely making the
return compulsory, whereas formerly it
was voluntary. As regards the other
matters on which information is required, I will give an undertaking to
the Committee that at the end of the
war, if these details are not required,
they will not be asked for from the
farmers. They are included in the Bill
at the moment because they may or may
not be required.
