Mr. LESLIE: I beg to move, to leave
out sub-section 2.
This is an Amendment which seems to
be of real importance. I am sure nobody in this House wants to interpose
any difficulty whatever in the way of
anything that is necessary for the prosecution of the war, but that I am afraid
is not altogether the point in this case.
It will be remembered that speaking as
regards this particular sub-clause on the
Second Reading I asked the right hon.
Gentleman, the Minister of Agriculture,
directly whether it meant that the
Ministry propose to take power to use for
another purpose information which it
had undertaken to use for only one particular purpose, and the answer, I regret
to say, was in the affirmative. The effect
of the sub-clause is to give the Ministry
unrestricted power to use for such purposes as it thinks necessary information
which is only given on the distinct understanding that it should not be used.
except for the specific purpose of the
compilation of statistics.
Something of the sort, but yet very
different, was done in the United Kingdom
Wheat Act, 1932. Some returns issued
under the Act which the Committee has
already heard of this afternoon, the Act
of 1925, were directed to be disclosed to
the Wheat Commission. That Commission may be described as an ally of the
Ministry of Agriculture, but disclosing
the figures to them for their purpose is
not quite the same thing as giving unrestricted power to use them as set out
in the Bill. It is all right, of course,
to take this power as regards the new
returns that sub-clause (1) deals with. As
we are introducing a new arrangement
we can insert without impropriety such
conditions as may seem convenient at
the present juncture. That is quite in
order, but you want to do more than
that.
You want to go back on returns
already furnished in confidence and you
seem to take power to break that confidence. If you once do that in an individual case you are not for one thing
going to assist yourself in future when
you come for more returns, and you are
going to destroy that confidence which
ought to subsist between this community.
and the Ministry who are supposed to be
in charge of its interests. I rather
gather, I am sorry to say, that there
was some idea on the part of the Government that if this Clause was passed
they would be able to use in a prosecution for breach of the Tillage Order
information which had previously been
given in confidence. I do not know that
that was the intention. I hope it was
not. It is rather objectionable in itself
in theory and I do not think it would
be even of great value in practice.
cannot see myself any court accepting or
attaching much value to evidence of that
description.
I
However, the broad point which is
before the Committee this afternoon is:
Must you break confidence in this
matter? We have had Tillage Orders
before. One was made, as we all remember, under the Third Schedule of
the Corn Production Act, 1917. That
Act provided for cultivation of land in
Great Britain and Ireland. The arrangements were somewhat different, but it
was not necessary in 1917 to do anything
of this kind. There was no power taken

This emergency is of a most serious
nature. We have complete confidence

overcome the menace of the U-boat and

maintained." I wonder would that meet
his wishes. I am very much inclined to
think he would go as far as that as I
do not believe he wants to use this information in support of a possible prosecution. I am sure that those advising
him will tell him that in a court of law
it would not carry any weight.
We must remember that there is a
difference well known to those concerned
in official departmental matters between
information communicated in confidence
and information communicated under the
seal of secrecy.
A Government department will recognise the difference at
once. Papers which are absolutely secret
are usually sent about from room to room
in a locked dispatch box, while confidential papers are not treated, shall I
If the right hon.
say, so reverently.
Gentleman would agree to put those
words in on the Report stage I think
perhaps the Committee would not be
disposed to insist on what my hon. and
gallant Friend (Major Dobbs) referred to
as an academical point, although I cannot myself agree that a question of a
breach of faith is purely a matter for
the scholars.
Sir BASIL BROOKE: I will consider
the matter, but I think it will be very
difficult to see exactly where a confidential
matter would cease. Suppose that, acting on the previous returns, a farmer
proceeded to plough an area which was
less than the 10 per cent. which he had
returned, and after investigation it was
found that he had made a wrong return,
I do not think that farmer should be
allowed to get off by ploughing less. I
do not know whether the hon. and learned
Gentleman follows.
Mr. LESLIE: Yes, I follow, but I do
not think the right hon. Gentleman has
got it quite right.
Sir BASIL BROOKE: The purpose of
this sub-clause is to see that the 10 per
cent. over and above the previous year's
tillage, or one-fifth of the total area, is
ploughed. You may have had inaccurate
returns in the voluntary case.
Are you
going to allow that man quietly to get
off with less than his 10 per cent. or his

CLAUSE 3 (Interpretation).
:-
In this Act, unless the context
otherwise requires, the following expressions have the meanings hereby
assigned to them, that is to say:
"Agricultural land" includes forest
land, bog land, land used as
grazing, meadow or pasture land or
orchard, and land used wholly or
mainly for the purpose of the
trade or business of a marketgardener, nurseryman or horticulturist, or for the purpose of breeding or keeping poultry;
Mr. LESLIE: I beg to move in Clause
3, second paragraph, to leave out
"forest land, bog land."
These words do not occur in the Agricultural Returns Act of 1925, and I
cannot see any reason why they should
occur here. The information that is
being sought under Clause 1 does not
seem very applicable to forest land or
Mr. J. A. Long
Mr. T. McLaughlin
Rt. Hon. J. H. Robb
Mr. H. Taylor
bog land, and if you must use the words,
I must ask what you mean by forest
land. Do you mean woodland, or what
is to be found in a Scottish deer forest?
I do not know why the words are put
in here. They do not occur in the
Agricultural Returns Act and a good
many other agricultural statutes. I do
not think we want them here, and therefore I move to leave them out. If they
are kept in, they may be a cause of possible trouble..
Sir BASIL BROOKE: Probably the
reason these words are in this Bill and
not in the English Act is that forestry
comes under my Ministry, whereas in
England it is under the Forestry Commission. Again, this is a war question.
Hon. Members are, probably aware that
at the present moment we are going round
most of the forestry areas- -I am not
speaking from the deer forest point of
view, but of the proper tree-bearing areas
-making a census of the trees that are
available. That information may be
necessary, and I do not think it will cause
any great trouble to supply it. Even at
the present moment timber is of great
509 Agricultural Returns Bill---

Adjournment
importance, and it will be of even greater
importance as the war goes on.
As to bog land, quite a lot of it in this
country can be converted into good agricultural land, and there is a very
interesting experiment-indeed, I may
say it has passed the experimental stage
-going on at Verner's Bridge, where a
considerable seed potato trade has been
started in a peat country. Information
as to bog land may conceivably be required. If it is not required it will not.
be asked for.
Mr. LESLIE: I see that the right
hon. Gentleman is anxious to retain
these words. Would it be better not to
say "forest land" but "woodland," if
we mean that? We could make the
change at a later stage. I do not desire
to press the Amendment.
Amendment, by leave, withdrawn.
Mr. LESLIE. I beg to move,

this Amendment.

CLAUSE 1 (Power to delegate execution of trusts).
Mr. LESLIE: I beg to move, at the
end of Clause 1 to add the words, "and
nothing in this Act shall so operate as
to prejudice or affect the validity of anything done in execution of such other
power.' This is another matter of, I
think, relatively small importance. What
occurred to me was this; that as we have
gone so far as to insert a kind of protection, for what it is worth, by power
of delegation outside the ambit of the
Bill, whether we ought to go a little
further and insert a protection for the
validity of anything done in execution.
.
of power conferred outside the Bill.
Whether we put these words in or not,
it will make no difference whatever, but
as we have put in some words giving
protection up to a point, it is a question
for a moment's consideration whether we
should add these words or leave them
out. Again, it is entirely a matter in
which we should be guided by the advice
of the right hon. Gentleman, he having
had time, to consider the matter.
Mr. ROBB: Again, may I say that I
consulted the learned Attorney-General
on this point, and neither of us could
see that the words made any difference.
If they do not make any difference, it is
not necessary to insert them.
Mr. LESLIE: I am glad the matter
has been looked into. That is all that
was required, and I ask leave to withdraw the Amendment.
Amendment, by leave, withdrawn.

PROROGATION OF PARLIAMENT.
Mr. SPEAKER: I have it in command
from his Grace the Governor to read
forthwith in this Chamber to the Senators
and Commons of Northern Ireland therein
assembled his Grace's Proclamation
proroguing this present Session of Parliament.
Resolved: "That the Gentleman
Usher of the Black Rod be conmanded to inform the Commons that the
Proclamation proroguing the present
Session of Parliament is to be read
forthwith, and inviting the Commons to
attend in order to hear the same."—(Mr.
Robb.)
The Commons, being at the Bar, with
their Speaker, the Proclamation was read
to both Houses of Parliament by the
Speaker of the Senate (in pursuance of
his Grace's command), as followeth :
---
Proclamation
End of Session
6 DECEMBER 1939
BY HIS GRACE THE GOVERNOR
OF NORTHERN IRELAND.
A PROCLAMATION
For Proroguing the present Parliament
of Northern Ireland.
WHEREAS it is provided by section
eleven of the Government of Ireland Act,
1920, as modified by and in pursuance of
certain enactments amending the said
Act, that the Governor of Northern
Ireland shall, in His Majesty's name,
summon, prorogue and dissolve the Parliament of Northern Ireland:
AND WHEREAS it is desirable that
this present Parliament of Northern
Ireland should be prorogued to the
nineteenth day of December, one thousand
nine hundred and thirty-nine:
NOW I, JAMES ALBERT EDWARD,
DUKE OF ABERCORN, Knight of the
Most Noble Order of the Garter, Knight
of the Most Illustrious Order of Saint
Patrick, Governor of Northern Ireland,
by this Proclamation do publish and
declare His Majesty's pleasure that the
said Parliament be, as from the reading
of this Proclamation in the Parliament of
Northern Ireland, prorogued to Tuesday,
the nineteenth day of December, one
thousand nine hundred and thirty-nine.
AND I do hereby further declare His
Majesty's Royal Will and Pleasure to be
that the said Parliament shall on the
said nineteenth day of December, one
thousand nine hundred and thirty-nine,
assemble and be holden at Stormont for
the despatch of divers urgent and
important affairs, and the Senators and
Members of the House of Commons of
Northern Ireland are hereby required to
give their attendance accordingly.
Given this sixth day of December, one
thousand nine hundred and thirty-nine.
(Signed by his own hand),
ABERCORN.
GOD SAVE THE KING.
End of the Second Session (opened
